CASE_ID: 072
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Interstitial Nephritis

ALIASES:
AIN
drug-induced nephritis
allergic interstitial nephritis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 45-year-old woman presents with decreased urine output, flank pain, and a diffuse maculopapular rash that began 10 days after starting omeprazole for reflux.
2. She also reports low-grade fever and mild joint pain; she has no history of kidney disease.
3. Serum creatinine has risen from a baseline of 0.8 to an abnormal value over an abnormal value; CBC shows peripheral eosinophilia at 12%.
4. Urinalysis shows sterile pyuria (WBCs without bacteria), hematuria, and WBC casts; urine eosinophils are detected on Hansel stain.
5. Renal ultrasound shows bilaterally enlarged, echogenic kidneys without obstruction; fractional excretion of sodium is 3.1%.
6. Kidney biopsy reveals interstitial infiltration with lymphocytes, plasma cells, and eosinophils with tubulitis; omeprazole is discontinued and a course of prednisone is initiated with gradual recovery of renal function.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common drug causes: NSAIDs, PPIs, penicillins, cephalosporins, sulfonamides, rifampin, diuretics.
- Classic triad (fever, rash, eosinophilia) is present in only 10-30% of cases.
- WBC casts and sterile pyuria on urinalysis; eosinophilia/eosinophiluria are supportive but not required.
- Treatment: discontinue offending drug; corticosteroids may speed recovery.

CASE_ID: 073
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Renal Tubular Acidosis

ALIASES:
RTA
distal RTA
proximal RTA
type 1 RTA
type 2 RTA
type 4 RTA

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 35-year-old woman with a history of Sjögren syndrome presents with muscle weakness and cramping; she was found incidentally to have a low potassium on routine labs.
2. She denies diarrhea, vomiting, or diuretic use; she has no other medications.
3. Labs show potassium an abnormal value, bicarbonate an abnormal value (normal anion gap metabolic acidosis), chloride an abnormal value, and normal creatinine.
4. Urinalysis shows a urine pH of 6.8 despite systemic acidosis (urine pH should be less than 5.5 in normal acidification).
5. Urine anion gap is positive (+8), confirming impaired urinary ammonium excretion consistent with distal tubule dysfunction.
6. Renal ultrasound reveals bilateral nephrocalcinosis; she is diagnosed with type 1 (distal) RTA from Sjögren-related tubulointerstitial nephritis and started on oral sodium bicarbonate and potassium citrate.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Type 1 (distal) RTA: failure to acidify urine distally; urine pH >5.5 despite acidosis; hypokalemia; nephrocalcinosis and nephrolithiasis (calcium phosphate stones).
- Type 2 (proximal) RTA: failure to reabsorb bicarbonate proximally; Fanconi syndrome; hypokalemia.
- Type 4 RTA: hypoaldosteronism (DM most common cause); hyperkalemia; normal anion gap.
- Causes of Type 1: Sjögren, SLE, amphotericin B, lithium.

CASE_ID: 074
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hemolytic Uremic Syndrome

ALIASES:
HUS
thrombotic microangiopathy
STEC-HUS

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-year-old girl presents with 3 days of bloody diarrhea after eating at a fast food restaurant; she is now oliguric and pale.
2. Her parents report she stopped urinating yesterday and has become increasingly lethargic.
3. On exam, she is pale and irritable with periorbital edema; blood pressure is elevated at 118/an abnormal value; no petechiae or purpura are present.
4. Labs show hemoglobin 6.8 g/dL, platelets 42,000, creatinine an abnormal value, LDH 1,an abnormal value, and haptoglobin is undetectable; peripheral smear shows numerous schistocytes.
5. Stool culture grows Shiga toxin-producing Escherichia coli O157:H7; ADAMTS13 activity is normal (distinguishing from TTP).
6. She is managed with supportive care including blood transfusion and dialysis; antibiotics are withheld given evidence they increase Shiga toxin release; she recovers renal function after 3 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Triad: microangiopathic hemolytic anemia + thrombocytopenia + acute kidney injury.
- Most common cause in children: STEC (Shiga toxin-producing E. coli O157:H7) following bloody diarrhea.
- Distinguished from TTP: HUS has prominent renal failure, normal ADAMTS13; TTP has prominent neurological symptoms, severely reduced ADAMTS13.
- Avoid antibiotics (increase Shiga toxin release) and antimotility agents.

CASE_ID: 075
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Von Willebrand Disease

ALIASES:
vWD
von Willebrand disorder
platelet-type bleeding disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old woman presents with a history of heavy menstrual periods since menarche requiring iron supplementation and two episodes of prolonged bleeding after dental extractions.
2. Her mother and maternal aunt have similar bleeding histories; her father is unaffected.
3. On exam, she has no petechiae, bruising, or joint swelling; mucosal bleeding (epistaxis, heavy menses) is the predominant pattern.
4. Labs show a prolonged bleeding time and prolonged aPTT with normal PT; platelet count is normal at 220,000.
5. Von Willebrand antigen level is 28% of normal (low); vWF activity (ristocetin cofactor assay) is 22% of normal; factor VIII activity is 35% of normal (mildly reduced, as vWF is a carrier for FVIII).
6. Genetic testing confirms type 1 von Willebrand disease with an autosomal dominant inheritance pattern; desmopressin (DDAVP) challenge test shows a good response with doubling of vWF levels.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common inherited bleeding disorder; autosomal dominant (type 1 most common = partial quantitative deficiency).
- Mucocutaneous bleeding pattern: heavy menses, epistaxis, easy bruising, prolonged bleeding after dental procedures.
- Prolonged bleeding time + prolonged aPTT + normal PT + normal platelets.
- vWF carries factor VIII (explains mildly low FVIII); vWF also mediates platelet adhesion to collagen.
- Treatment: DDAVP (releases vWF from endothelial stores) for type 1; vWF concentrate for type 3 or surgery.

CASE_ID: 076
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Lymphoblastic Leukemia

ALIASES:
ALL
acute lymphocytic leukemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 5-year-old boy presents with a 3-week history of fatigue, pallor, bone pain particularly in his legs, and recurrent fevers.
2. His parents note he has been limping and refusing to walk; he has had two nosebleeds this week.
3. On exam, he appears pale and unwell; cervical and axillary lymphadenopathy is present; the spleen tip is palpable an abnormal value below the costal margin.
4. CBC shows WBC 68,000 with 82% blasts on differential, hemoglobin 6.4 g/dL, and platelets 18,000; LDH is markedly elevated.
5. Bone marrow biopsy shows replacement of normal marrow with 94% lymphoblasts; immunophenotyping confirms B-cell precursor ALL (CD10+, CD19+, TdT+).
6. Cytogenetics reveal hyperdiploidy and ETV6-RUNX1 fusion (t(12;21)), which are favorable prognostic features; he is enrolled in a pediatric ALL clinical trial with induction chemotherapy achieving complete remission at day 29.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common childhood malignancy; peak incidence ages 2-5.
- Presents with bone marrow failure (anemia, thrombocytopenia, neutropenia) + lymphadenopathy + organomegaly.
- B-cell precursor ALL: CD10, CD19, TdT positive.
- Favorable prognosis: age 1-10, WBC <50,000, hyperdiploidy, ETV6-RUNX1.
- CNS prophylaxis (intrathecal chemotherapy) is essential; overall cure rate >90% in children.

CASE_ID: 077
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Chronic Lymphocytic Leukemia

ALIASES:
CLL
B-cell CLL
small lymphocytic lymphoma
SLL

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man is referred after routine CBC shows a WBC of 42,000 with a markedly elevated lymphocyte count; he is entirely asymptomatic.
2. He has no fever, night sweats, or weight loss; he feels completely well and is confused about why he needs further evaluation.
3. On exam, he has non-tender bilateral cervical, axillary, and inguinal lymphadenopathy; splenomegaly is palpable an abnormal value below the costal margin.
4. Peripheral smear shows numerous small, mature-appearing lymphocytes with characteristic smudge cells (fragile lymphocytes broken during smear preparation).
5. Flow cytometry shows a clonal B-cell population positive for CD5, CD19, CD20 (dim), and CD23 - the classic CLL immunophenotype.
6. FISH panel shows deletion 13q14 as the sole abnormality, which carries a favorable prognosis; he is observed with watchful waiting given early Rai stage 0 disease.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common adult leukemia in the Western world; most patients are asymptomatic at diagnosis.
- Smudge cells on peripheral smear are pathognomonic.
- Immunophenotype: CD5+ (T-cell marker aberrantly expressed on B cells), CD19+, CD20 (dim), CD23+.
- Prognosis by cytogenetics: del(13q) best, del(11q) intermediate, del(17p)/TP53 worst.
- Treatment: watchful waiting for early disease; BTK inhibitors (ibrutinib), venetoclax for progressive disease.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 078
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Pneumocystis Pneumonia

ALIASES:
PCP
Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia
PJP

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 31-year-old man with HIV who has not been on antiretroviral therapy for 8 months presents with 3 weeks of progressive exertional dyspnea, dry cough, and low-grade fevers.
2. He reports being unable to climb a flight of stairs without stopping to rest; he has lost 15 pounds.
3. On exam, respiratory rate is 26/min; oxygen saturation drops from 95% at rest to 84% with minimal exertion; lung auscultation is surprisingly clear.
4. LDH is markedly elevated at an abnormal value; CD4 count returns at an abnormal count; chest X-ray shows bilateral perihilar ground-glass opacities.
5. High-resolution CT confirms bilateral diffuse ground-glass opacities with a central predominance; beta-D-glucan is markedly elevated.
6. Bronchoalveolar lavage with methenamine silver staining reveals cysts and trophic forms of Pneumocystis jirovecii; IV trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole is started along with prednisone given a PaO2 of 58 mmHg on room air.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common AIDS-defining opportunistic infection; occurs at CD4 <200.
- Subacute dyspnea, dry cough, hypoxia (worsens with exertion) in HIV with low CD4.
- CXR/CT: bilateral diffuse ground-glass opacities; elevated LDH; lung exam often clear.
- Diagnosis: BAL with silver stain or direct fluorescence; beta-D-glucan elevated.
- Treatment: TMP-SMX; add prednisone if PaO2 <70 or A-a gradient >35.

CASE_ID: 079
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Cryptococcal Meningitis

ALIASES:
cryptococcosis
Cryptococcus neoformans meningitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 29-year-old man with HIV and a CD4 count of 22 cells/uL presents with a 2-week history of progressive headache, fever, and neck stiffness.
2. He is not on antiretroviral therapy or prophylaxis; he has been homeless and reports significant stress.
3. On exam, he is febrile at 38.8°C with moderate nuchal rigidity; fundoscopy shows papilledema bilaterally.
4. Lumbar puncture shows elevated opening pressure of an abnormal value H2O; CSF shows 18 WBCs (mostly lymphocytes), protein an abnormal value, and glucose an abnormal value (serum an abnormal value).
5. India ink staining of CSF shows encapsulated yeast with wide polysaccharide capsules; CSF cryptococcal antigen titer is 1:2048.
6. He is started on induction therapy with liposomal amphotericin B plus flucytosine for 2 weeks followed by consolidation with fluconazole; serial therapeutic lumbar punctures are performed to manage elevated intracranial pressure.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause of fungal meningitis in AIDS patients; occurs at CD4 <100.
- Subacute meningitis with elevated ICP (papilledema, hydrocephalus); opening pressure often markedly elevated.
- India ink stain: encapsulated yeast (halo appearance); CSF CrAg highly sensitive and specific.
- Treatment: amphotericin B + flucytosine (induction) → fluconazole (consolidation and maintenance).
- Managing elevated ICP with serial LPs is critical to prevent vision loss and death.

CASE_ID: 080
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Rocky Mountain Spotted Fever

ALIASES:
RMSF
Rickettsia rickettsii
spotted fever

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 9-year-old boy presents in July with 4 days of high fever, severe headache, myalgias, and a rash that began on his wrists and ankles and is spreading centrally.
2. His family recently returned from a camping trip in North Carolina; his parents recall removing a tick from his scalp 8 days ago.
3. On exam, temperature is 39.9°C; a petechial and maculopapular rash involves the palms and soles and is spreading to his trunk.
4. WBC is 8,200 with left shift; platelets are 68,000 (thrombocytopenic); sodium is an abnormal value; LFTs are mildly elevated.
5. Serology for Rickettsia rickettsii IgM is positive; skin biopsy of a petechial lesion with immunofluorescence staining confirms rickettsial organisms within endothelial cells.
6. He is started immediately on doxycycline with clinical improvement within 48 hours; the treating physician notes that doxycycline is the drug of choice even in children despite tetracycline class concerns.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Rickettsia rickettsii transmitted by Dermacentor (dog/wood) tick; Southeast and South Central US.
- Classic triad: fever, headache, rash; rash classically starts on wrists/ankles and spreads centrally, involving palms and soles.
- Lab findings: thrombocytopenia, hyponatremia, elevated LFTs.
- Treatment: doxycycline (drug of choice for all ages including children); do not wait for confirmation - delay increases mortality.
- Serology confirms retrospectively; start treatment empirically based on clinical picture.

CASE_ID: 081
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Septic Arthritis

ALIASES:
infectious arthritis
bacterial arthritis
joint infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old man with rheumatoid arthritis on methotrexate presents with 2 days of acute worsening pain, swelling, warmth, and redness in his right knee that is far worse than his usual joint symptoms.
2. He had a minor skin abrasion on his right leg an abnormal value ago while gardening; he is febrile at 38.7°C and unable to bear weight.
3. On exam, the right knee is markedly swollen, warm, and exquisitely tender; range of motion is severely limited by pain.
4. Joint aspiration yields 60 mL of cloudy, yellow-green synovial fluid; cell count shows 92,000 WBCs with 94% neutrophils.
5. Gram stain of the synovial fluid shows gram-positive cocci in clusters; synovial fluid glucose is markedly low relative to serum.
6. Synovial fluid culture grows methicillin-sensitive Staphylococcus aureus; he is started on IV nafcillin and undergoes arthroscopic joint washout with clinical improvement over 5 days.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause: Staphylococcus aureus in adults; Neisseria gonorrhoeae in sexually active young adults.
- Monoarticular joint: acutely hot, swollen, painful with markedly elevated WBC (>50,000) = septic arthritis until proven otherwise.
- Joint aspiration is essential for diagnosis and treatment.
- Risk factors: immunosuppression, RA, prosthetic joints, IV drug use, skin infection.
- Treatment: IV antibiotics + surgical drainage; do not aspirate prosthetic joints without orthopedic surgery involvement.

CASE_ID: 082
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Polymyalgia Rheumatica

ALIASES:
PMR
polymyalgia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 71-year-old woman presents with a 6-week history of severe bilateral shoulder and hip girdle pain and morning stiffness lasting more than 2 hours that is preventing her from raising her arms above her head.
2. She has difficulty dressing herself in the morning and getting up from a chair; she reports fatigue, low-grade fevers, and a 10-pound weight loss.
3. On exam, active range of motion of the shoulders and hips is markedly limited by pain; there is no muscle weakness, atrophy, or tenderness to direct palpation of the muscles.
4. ESR is an abnormal value/hr and CRP is markedly elevated; ANA, RF, anti-CCP, and CK are all negative or normal.
5. MRI of the shoulders shows bilateral subdeltoid and subacromial bursitis with glenohumeral synovitis but no rotator cuff tears.
6. She is started on prednisone 15 mg daily with dramatic improvement in pain and stiffness within 72 hours - a response so rapid it is itself diagnostically supportive of PMR.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common inflammatory rheumatic disease in adults over 50; strongly associated with giant cell arteritis (occurs in ~15-20% of PMR patients).
- Proximal muscle aching and stiffness (shoulder and hip girdle) without true weakness.
- Markedly elevated ESR and CRP; CK is normal (no myopathy).
- Screen for GCA symptoms: headache, jaw claudication, visual changes - if present, use high-dose steroids immediately.
- Treatment: low-dose prednisone (15-20 mg/day); rapid response is characteristic.

CASE_ID: 083
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Psoriatic Arthritis

ALIASES:
PsA
psoriatic arthropathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old man with a 10-year history of plaque psoriasis presents with a 6-month history of pain and swelling in his right index finger and left ankle, along with lower back stiffness that is worse in the morning.
2. He also reports pain in the sole of his right foot that is worst with the first steps of the morning.
3. On exam, his right index finger is swollen in its entirety (sausage digit/dactylitis); nail examination shows pitting, onycholysis, and oil-drop discoloration in multiple fingernails.
4. X-ray of the hands shows erosions at the DIP joints with adjacent bone formation giving a pencil-in-cup deformity; sacroiliac joints show early erosive changes.
5. Rheumatoid factor and anti-CCP antibodies are negative; HLA-B27 is positive; ESR and CRP are elevated.
6. He is started on a TNF inhibitor (adalimumab) given his failure to respond to methotrexate; both the skin and joint disease improve significantly at 3 months.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Seronegative (RF and anti-CCP negative) inflammatory arthritis associated with psoriasis.
- Five patterns: asymmetric oligoarthritis (most common), DIP predominant, axial, symmetric polyarthritis (RA-like), arthritis mutilans.
- Dactylitis (sausage digit) and enthesitis (plantar fasciitis, Achilles tendinitis) are characteristic.
- Pencil-in-cup deformity on X-ray in severe DIP disease.
- Treatment: NSAIDs, methotrexate, TNF inhibitors, IL-17 inhibitors (secukinumab).

CASE_ID: 084
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Reactive Arthritis

ALIASES:
Reiter syndrome
Reiter's syndrome
reactive arthropathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old man presents with a 3-week history of right knee swelling and pain, dysuria, and bilateral eye redness that developed 4 weeks after an episode of diarrhea from eating undercooked chicken.
2. He denies prior joint disease or sexually transmitted infections; he has no skin rash currently.
3. On exam, the right knee is swollen and warm with a large effusion; bilateral conjunctivitis is present; urethral discharge is noted.
4. Urethral swab and stool PCR are negative for active infection but Chlamydia trachomatis IgM serology is positive; synovial fluid shows 38,000 WBCs with 85% neutrophils but no organisms on Gram stain or culture.
5. HLA-B27 testing is positive; sacroiliac joints show mild inflammation on MRI.
6. He is treated with NSAIDs for the arthritis; doxycycline is given for possible chlamydial trigger; his symptoms gradually resolve over 6 weeks with no permanent joint damage.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Seronegative arthritis triggered by infection (Chlamydia, Campylobacter, Salmonella, Shigella, Yersinia).
- Classic triad (Reiter syndrome): arthritis + urethritis + conjunctivitis/uveitis - "can't see, can't pee, can't climb a tree."
- Sterile synovial fluid (reactive, not infectious); HLA-B27 positive in ~75%.
- Treatment: NSAIDs; antibiotics only if active infection confirmed; most cases self-limited.

CASE_ID: 085
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Generalized Anxiety Disorder

ALIASES:
GAD
anxiety disorder
chronic anxiety

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old woman presents requesting help for persistent worrying that she describes as uncontrollable and affecting every area of her life for the past 2 years.
2. She worries excessively about her health, finances, her children's safety, and work performance even when there is no realistic threat; she cannot relax.
3. She reports chronic muscle tension, difficulty sleeping (lying awake with racing thoughts), irritability, fatigue, and difficulty concentrating.
4. She has had multiple normal medical workups including thyroid function tests, CBC, and cardiac evaluation; she does not use substances.
5. These symptoms have been present most days for more than 6 months and cause significant impairment in her work and family relationships.
6. She is started on sertraline and referred for cognitive behavioral therapy (CBT); after 8 weeks her worry and somatic symptoms improve substantially.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Excessive, uncontrollable worry about multiple domains for >6 months with at least 3 somatic symptoms.
- Somatic symptoms: muscle tension, restlessness, fatigue, difficulty concentrating, irritability, sleep disturbance.
- Distinguished from panic disorder (episodes vs. chronic), social anxiety (situational), specific phobia (single object/situation).
- Treatment: SSRIs/SNRIs (first-line); CBT (equally effective); buspirone; benzodiazepines only short-term.

CASE_ID: 086
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder

ALIASES:
PTSD
trauma disorder
post-traumatic stress

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old female combat veteran presents 18 months after returning from deployment with persistent nightmares of combat experiences, intrusive daytime flashbacks, and hypervigilance in public spaces.
2. She avoids watching war movies, driving past military installations, and refuses to discuss her deployment; she has stopped socializing with family and friends.
3. She reports chronic sleep disturbance, irritability, exaggerated startle response to loud noises, and emotional numbing; she feels detached from those around her.
4. She denies psychotic symptoms; urine toxicology is negative; she screens positive on the PCL-5 with a score of 58 (threshold >33).
5. These symptoms began within weeks of a traumatic event during deployment and have persisted for more than 6 months, causing severe functional impairment.
6. She is referred for trauma-focused CBT (Prolonged Exposure) and started on sertraline; she achieves significant symptom reduction after 12 weeks of therapy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Four clusters: intrusion (nightmares, flashbacks), avoidance (people, places, thoughts), negative cognition/mood, hyperarousal (startle, hypervigilance, sleep disturbance).
- Requires exposure to traumatic event + symptoms >1 month + functional impairment.
- First-line: trauma-focused CBT (Prolonged Exposure, CPT); SSRIs (sertraline, paroxetine) are FDA-approved.
- Distinguished from acute stress disorder (<1 month duration).

CASE_ID: 087
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Attention Deficit Hyperactivity Disorder

ALIASES:
ADHD
attention deficit disorder
ADD

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 9-year-old boy is referred by his school for evaluation after his teacher reports he is unable to remain seated, constantly interrupts classmates, and fails to complete assignments despite appearing bright.
2. His parents report the same behaviors at home since preschool; he cannot wait his turn, loses his belongings constantly, and is easily distracted by any noise.
3. On exam, he is fidgety, impulsive, and has difficulty sustaining attention during testing; cognitive testing shows normal intelligence with significant executive function deficits.
4. Vanderbilt Assessment Scales from both parents and teachers confirm significant inattention and hyperactivity-impulsivity symptoms in multiple settings for more than an abnormal value.
5. These symptoms began before age 12, are present in at least two settings (home and school), and cause significant academic and social impairment.
6. He is started on methylphenidate with gradual dose titration; at follow-up his teacher reports marked improvement in on-task behavior and assignment completion.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Symptoms must be present in two or more settings, onset before age 12, for at least 6 months.
- Three presentations: predominantly inattentive, predominantly hyperactive-impulsive, combined.
- Most common neurodevelopmental disorder; often persists into adulthood.
- Treatment: stimulants (methylphenidate, amphetamine salts) are first-line; behavioral therapy; non-stimulants (atomoxetine) if stimulants contraindicated.

CASE_ID: 088
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pemphigus Vulgaris

ALIASES:
PV
pemphigus
autoimmune blistering disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 55-year-old woman presents with painful oral ulcers that have been present for 3 months, followed by the development of flaccid fluid-filled blisters on her trunk and face over the past 6 weeks.
2. The oral lesions have prevented her from eating solid foods; the skin blisters rupture easily leaving raw, denuded erosions.
3. On exam, skin shearing with gentle pressure is positive (gentle lateral pressure on perilesional skin causes blister extension or new blister formation); multiple crusted erosions are present on the trunk.
4. Closer inspection and office evaluation narrow the differential skin conditions.
5. Direct immunofluorescence shows IgG and C3 deposits in an intercellular (fishnet/honeycomb) pattern throughout the epidermis.
6. Anti-desmoglein 3 antibodies are markedly elevated; she is started on high-dose prednisone and rituximab with gradual improvement over several weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Autoimmune blistering disorder; anti-desmoglein 3 antibodies (and desmoglein 1 in skin-predominant).
- Flaccid (easily ruptured) blisters + oral mucosal involvement; Nikolsky sign positive.
- Intraepidermal blistering (suprabasilar acantholysis) distinguishes from bullous pemphigoid (subepidermal).
- Direct IF: IgG intercellular (fishnet pattern) throughout epidermis.
- Treatment: systemic corticosteroids; rituximab has transformed management; can be life-threatening if untreated.

CASE_ID: 089
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Bullous Pemphigoid

ALIASES:
BP
autoimmune subepidermal blister

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. An 82-year-old man presents with intensely pruritic, tense fluid-filled blisters on his thighs, abdomen, and flexural areas that have been developing over the past 3 months.
2. He notes the itching preceded the blisters by several weeks; the oral mucosa is not involved.
3. On exam, multiple tense bullae and urticarial plaques are present on an erythematous base; the blisters are firm and do not rupture easily when pressed.
4. Closer inspection and office evaluation narrow the differential skin conditions.
5. Direct immunofluorescence shows linear IgG and C3 deposition along the dermoepidermal junction (basement membrane zone).
6. Anti-BP180 and anti-BP230 antibodies are markedly elevated on indirect immunofluorescence; he is started on topical clobetasol and doxycycline with gradual resolution over 4 months.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common autoimmune blistering disorder; typically in elderly patients.
- Tense (not flaccid) bullae; Nikolsky sign NEGATIVE; oral mucosa usually spared.
- Anti-BP180 (BPAG2) and anti-BP230 (BPAG1) antibodies targeting hemidesmosomes.
- Subepidermal blistering on biopsy; linear IgG/C3 at dermoepidermal junction on DIF.
- Distinguished from pemphigus vulgaris: tense vs. flaccid bullae; subepidermal vs. intraepidermal; oral involvement.

CASE_ID: 090
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Erythema Multiforme

ALIASES:
EM
target lesions
erythema multiforme major
erythema multiforme minor

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old man presents with a rapidly spreading rash and painful mouth sores over several days.
2. He recently had a self-limited lip lesion but denies starting any new medications.
3. Examination shows multiple symmetric ring-like lesions on the hands and forearms with limited mucosal involvement.
4. Several lesions have central blistering; oral mucosa shows erosions on the buccal mucosa and lips with less than 10% BSA skin involvement.
5. Skin biopsy shows lichenoid interface dermatitis with keratinocyte necrosis and lymphocytic infiltrate at the dermoepidermal junction.
6. HSV PCR from the cold sore site is positive; the rash resolves over 3 weeks with supportive care; he is started on suppressive acyclovir to prevent recurrent HSV-triggered episodes.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common trigger: herpes simplex virus (HSV); also Mycoplasma pneumoniae, drugs.
- Classic target lesions: three concentric zones (dark center, pale ring, red outer ring) on acral surfaces.
- EM minor: no/minimal mucosal involvement; EM major: mucosal involvement in 1-2 sites.
- Distinguished from SJS: EM has true target lesions, BSA typically <10%, associated with infection not drugs; SJS is drug-associated, more severe.
- Recurrent EM from HSV: prophylactic acyclovir prevents recurrences.

CASE_ID: 091
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Febrile Seizure

ALIASES:
febrile convulsion
fever-associated seizure

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A toddler is brought in after a brief episode of whole-body shaking during a fever.
2. The child was otherwise well earlier in the day before rapidly developing a temperature at home.
3. Examination shows a sleepy but arousable child with no focal neurologic deficits and a likely source of fever.
4. Blood glucose is an abnormal value; electrolytes are normal; no lumbar puncture is performed given her age, exam, and uncomplicated presentation.
5. She regains normal behavior within 30 minutes; her neurological exam returns completely to baseline.
6. Parents are counseled that simple febrile seizures (first occurrence, <15 minutes, generalized, no recurrence within 24 hours) carry a low risk of epilepsy and do not require antiepileptic treatment; fever control and reassurance are provided.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common seizure type in children ages 6 months to 5 years; caused by rapid temperature rise.
- Simple febrile seizure: generalized, <15 minutes, no recurrence within 24 hours, returns to neurological baseline.
- Complex febrile seizure: focal, >15 minutes, recurs within 24 hours - requires further workup.
- LP indicated if: <12 months, signs of meningitis, prolonged postictal state, complex features.
- Prognosis excellent: 2-3% risk of epilepsy (only slightly above general population).

CASE_ID: 092
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Hirschsprung Disease

ALIASES:
congenital aganglionic megacolon
Hirschsprung's disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A newborn has progressive abdominal distention and vomiting in the first days of life.
2. There has been minimal stool output since birth despite an otherwise uncomplicated delivery.
3. Examination shows a distended tympanitic abdomen and a tight anal canal on rectal exam.
4. Abdominal X-ray shows massively dilated loops of bowel with absence of gas in the rectum; contrast enema reveals a narrow aganglionic segment transitioning to a dilated proximal colon.
5. Rectal suction biopsy shows absence of ganglion cells in the submucosal and myenteric plexuses with hypertrophied nerve trunks, confirming the diagnosis.
6. He undergoes a pull-through procedure (Swenson or Soave operation) to remove the aganglionic segment with anastomosis of normally innervated bowel to the anus.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Failure of neural crest cell migration → absent ganglion cells in Auerbach and Meissner plexuses of the distal colon.
- Most common in the rectosigmoid region; associated with Down syndrome (trisomy 21).
- Failure to pass meconium within 48 hours of birth + abdominal distention = Hirschsprung until proven otherwise.
- Diagnosis: rectal biopsy (absence of ganglion cells) is gold standard.
- Treatment: surgical pull-through procedure removing aganglionic segment.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 093
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Pyloric Stenosis

ALIASES:
infantile hypertrophic pyloric stenosis
IHPS
pyloric stenosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 4-week-old infant is brought in for worsening vomiting after feeds over the past week.
2. The child remains eager to feed but has had fewer wet diapers and appears to be losing weight.
3. Examination shows mild dehydration and visible upper abdominal peristaltic waves after feeding.
4. Electrolyte studies suggest a metabolic disturbance from repeated vomiting, and abdominal imaging is obtained.
5. Abdominal ultrasound shows a thickened pyloric muscle with a wall thickness of 4.8 mm and a channel length of 19 mm, diagnostic of hypertrophic pyloric stenosis (normal: thickness <3 mm, length <14 mm).
6. After IV fluid resuscitation and correction of electrolytes, he undergoes a Ramstedt pyloromyotomy with complete resolution of vomiting and discharge home the following day.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common surgical cause of nonbilious vomiting in infants; peak at 3-6 weeks of age; more common in firstborn males.
- Projectile, nonbilious vomiting + hungry after vomiting (distinguishes from pyloric stenosis from other causes of vomiting).
- Hypokalemic, hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis from loss of HCl in vomit.
- Olive-shaped mass palpable in RUQ; ultrasound is diagnostic.
- Treatment: always resuscitate and correct electrolytes FIRST, then surgery (Ramstedt pyloromyotomy).

CASE_ID: 094
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Heparin-Induced Thrombocytopenia

ALIASES:
HIT
HIT type 2
heparin induced thrombocytopenia thrombosis
HITT

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 60-year-old woman who is postoperative day 8 from hip replacement surgery develops new swelling and tenderness in her left lower extremity.
2. She has been receiving prophylactic anticoagulation since the day of surgery without complications until now.
3. On exam, the left calf is swollen, warm, and tender; there are no skin lesions; her platelet count has dropped from 240,000 to 78,000 over the past 3 days.
4. Doppler ultrasound of the left leg confirms an acute deep vein thrombosis in the femoral vein.
5. The 4Ts score is 7 (high probability); a serotonin release assay is sent and PF4-heparin antibody ELISA is strongly positive.
6. All forms of heparin including line flushes are stopped immediately; she is transitioned to argatroban with platelet recovery over the following week.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- HIT type 2: immune-mediated; IgG antibodies against PF4-heparin complexes → platelet activation → paradoxical thrombosis.
- Onset: days 5-10 of heparin exposure; platelet drop >50% from baseline.
- Paradox: thrombocytopenia + thrombosis (HITT); do NOT give platelets (worsens thrombosis).
- Diagnosis: 4T score + PF4 ELISA + serotonin release assay (gold standard).
- Treatment: STOP all heparin; use non-heparin anticoagulant (argatroban, bivalirudin, fondaparinux).

CASE_ID: 095
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Neuroleptic Malignant Syndrome

ALIASES:
NMS
antipsychotic malignant syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 29-year-old man with schizophrenia was started on haloperidol 4 days ago and now presents with high fever of 40.6°C, confusion, and severe muscle stiffness.
2. His blood pressure is 178/an abnormal value with heart rate of 136 bpm; he is diaphoretic and poorly responsive.
3. On exam, he has generalized lead-pipe rigidity throughout all extremities; he is barely arousable to painful stimuli.
4. CK is 62,an abnormal value; WBC is 24,an abnormal count; serum creatinine is rising; urinalysis shows myoglobinuria with 4+ blood but only 2 RBCs on microscopy.
5. All antipsychotic medications are immediately discontinued; ICU transfer is arranged.
6. He is treated with IV fluid resuscitation, bromocriptine (dopamine agonist), and dantrolene (muscle relaxant) with gradual improvement over 10 days; the episode is attributed to dopamine receptor blockade from haloperidol.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Life-threatening reaction to dopamine antagonists (antipsychotics, metoclopramide, prochlorperazine).
- Classic tetrad: hyperthermia, lead-pipe rigidity, altered mental status, autonomic instability.
- Elevated CK → rhabdomyolysis → AKI; myoglobinuria on urinalysis.
- Distinguished from serotonin syndrome: NMS has lead-pipe rigidity (not clonus), gradual onset, caused by dopamine blockers.
- Treatment: STOP antipsychotic; aggressive cooling; IV fluids; dantrolene; bromocriptine or amantadine.

CASE_ID: 096
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Warfarin Toxicity

ALIASES:
anticoagulant overdose
warfarin overdose
supratherapeutic anticoagulation

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 78-year-old woman on chronic anticoagulation for atrial fibrillation presents with 2 days of blood in her urine, gum bleeding, and a large bruise on her thigh.
2. She recently started a new antibiotic for a respiratory infection without her anticoagulation being adjusted.
3. On exam, she has ecchymoses on her arms and thighs; there is gross hematuria; no acute external bleeding source is identified.
4. Labs show INR of 9.2 (target 2-3); CBC shows hemoglobin of 10.4 g/dL (baseline 12.8); the new antibiotic is identified as clarithromycin, a CYP inhibitor.
5. Her medication list confirms warfarin therapy; PT is markedly prolonged and corrects with mixing studies, consistent with vitamin K-dependent factor deficiency.
6. Warfarin is held; she receives IV vitamin K and 4-factor prothrombin complex concentrate; INR normalizes within 12 hours and bleeding resolves.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Warfarin: inhibits vitamin K-dependent factors (II, VII, IX, X, protein C and S).
- CYP2C9 inhibitors increase warfarin effect: clarithromycin, fluconazole, amiodarone, metronidazole, omeprazole.
- Reversal: vitamin K (slow, hours to day); FFP (faster, short-acting); 4-factor PCC (fastest, preferred for life-threatening bleeding).
- Supratherapeutic INR without bleeding: hold warfarin ± oral vitamin K depending on INR level.

CASE_ID: 097
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Preeclampsia

ALIASES:
pregnancy-induced hypertension
PIH
toxemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman at 34 weeks of her first pregnancy presents with a 2-day history of severe frontal headache, visual disturbances described as flashing lights, and swelling of her hands and face.
2. Her blood pressure in the office is 166/an abnormal value; her previous prenatal visits showed normal blood pressures throughout the pregnancy.
3. Urinalysis shows 3+ protein; spot protein-to-creatinine ratio is 0.6 (diagnostic threshold ≥0.3).
4. Labs show platelets 88,000, AST an abnormal value, ALT an abnormal value, LDH elevated, and peripheral smear showing schistocytes - consistent with HELLP syndrome.
5. Fetal heart tracing is reactive; biophysical profile is 8/8; estimated fetal weight is at the 8th percentile consistent with fetal growth restriction.
6. She is admitted; magnesium sulfate is started for seizure prophylaxis; IV labetalol controls severe blood pressure; delivery is planned within 24 hours given HELLP syndrome at 34 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Preeclampsia: BP ≥140/90 + proteinuria (or other severe features) after 20 weeks.
- Severe features: BP ≥160/110, severe headache, visual changes, RUQ/epigastric pain, thrombocytopenia, elevated LFTs, renal impairment.
- HELLP: Hemolysis, Elevated Liver enzymes, Low Platelets - a severe variant.
- Only cure: delivery; magnesium sulfate prevents eclampsia; antihypertensives for severe BP.
- Eclampsia: preeclampsia + new-onset seizures.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 098
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Endometriosis

ALIASES:
endometriosis
chocolate cyst
endometrioma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 29-year-old woman presents with a 5-year history of severe dysmenorrhea requiring her to miss work each month, deep dyspareunia, and increasing difficulty conceiving over the past 18 months.
2. She reports that her pelvic pain begins 5 days before her period and does not fully resolve until several days after it ends.
3. On pelvic exam, uterosacral ligament nodularity is palpated and the uterus is fixed and retroverted; adnexal tenderness is present bilaterally.
4. Pelvic MRI shows a an abnormal value left ovarian cyst with characteristic T1 hyperintensity and T2 shading (chocolate cyst/endometrioma) and thickening of the posterior cul-de-sac.
5. CA-125 is mildly elevated at 68 U/mL but is non-specific for endometriosis; fertility workup shows bilateral tubal occlusion on hysterosalpingography.
6. Diagnostic laparoscopy confirms stage III endometriosis with powder-burn lesions on the peritoneum, bilateral endometriomas, and adhesions; surgical excision is performed and she is counseled regarding IVF given tubal occlusion.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Ectopic endometrial tissue outside the uterus; most commonly affects ovaries, fallopian tubes, peritoneum.
- Classic triad: dysmenorrhea, dyspareunia, infertility.
- Chocolate cyst (endometrioma): endometriosis of the ovary; T1 bright/T2 dark on MRI.
- Diagnosis requires laparoscopy with biopsy; MRI is helpful but not definitive.
- Treatment: NSAIDs/OCPs for symptoms; GnRH agonists; surgical excision; IVF for infertility.

CASE_ID: 099
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Benign Prostatic Hyperplasia

ALIASES:
BPH
prostatic hyperplasia
lower urinary tract symptoms

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man presents with a 2-year history of progressive urinary hesitancy, weak stream, straining to void, and the need to urinate multiple times at night.
2. He also reports incomplete bladder emptying and dribbling at the end of micturition; he has had one episode of urinary retention requiring catheterization.
3. International Prostate Symptom Score (IPSS) is 22 (severe); digital rectal exam reveals a uniformly enlarged, smooth, rubbery prostate without nodules estimated at 60 grams.
4. PSA is 3.8 ng/mL (age-appropriate); post-void residual urine volume is 180 mL; urinalysis is negative for infection or hematuria.
5. Renal ultrasound shows bilateral mild hydronephrosis from bladder outlet obstruction; serum creatinine is 1.4 mg/dL (mildly elevated from chronic obstruction).
6. He is started on tamsulosin (alpha-1 blocker) and finasteride (5-alpha reductase inhibitor); given his hydronephrosis and prior retention, urology is consulted and TURP is discussed as a definitive option.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hyperplasia of periurethral transition zone of the prostate; caused by DHT (testosterone converted by 5-alpha reductase).
- Obstructive symptoms: hesitancy, weak stream, straining; irritative symptoms: frequency, urgency, nocturia.
- Treatment: alpha-1 blockers (tamsulosin, terazosin) for rapid symptom relief; 5-alpha reductase inhibitors (finasteride) reduce prostate size (take months to work).
- Complications: urinary retention, recurrent UTIs, hydronephrosis, renal failure.

CASE_ID: 100
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Retinal Detachment

ALIASES:
detached retina
rhegmatogenous retinal detachment

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old severely myopic man presents to the ED with sudden onset of floaters and flashing lights in his right eye that began 2 days ago, followed today by a dark curtain spreading across the lower portion of his visual field.
2. He had cataract surgery on the right eye an abnormal value ago without complications; he denies trauma.
3. On exam, visual acuity is 20/200 in the right eye; the relative afferent pupillary defect is present in the right eye.
4. Slit-lamp exam shows tobacco dust (pigmented vitreous cells - pigmented cells in the anterior vitreous), a sign of retinal tear.
5. Fundoscopy reveals a large superior retinal detachment with the retina billowing forward like a parachute and a visible horseshoe-shaped retinal tear at the 11 o'clock position.
6. Emergency vitreoretinal surgery (scleral buckle and vitrectomy with gas tamponade) is performed within 12 hours; visual acuity improves to 20/40 at 3-month follow-up.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Photopsias (flashing lights) + floaters = posterior vitreous detachment or retinal tear - ophthalmology emergency.
- Addition of visual field defect (curtain) = retinal detachment until proven otherwise.
- Risk factors: high myopia, prior cataract surgery, trauma, family history.
- Shafer sign (tobacco dust): pigmented cells in vitreous = retinal tear.
- Surgical emergency: scleral buckle, vitrectomy, or pneumatic retinopexy depending on detachment characteristics.

CASE_ID: 101
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Mitral Stenosis

ALIASES:
MS
rheumatic mitral stenosis
mitral valve stenosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old woman originally from Pakistan presents with a 2-year history of progressive exertional dyspnea, orthopnea, and fatigue that has worsened since her recent pregnancy.
2. She had recurrent streptococcal pharyngitis as a child that was never treated with antibiotics.
3. On auscultation, a loud S1 is followed by an opening snap, then a low-pitched mid-diastolic rumbling murmur best heard at the apex in the left lateral decubitus position.
4. The interval between S2 and the opening snap is notably short; chest X-ray shows straightening of the left heart border and double density at the right cardiac border.
5. ECG shows P mitrale (broad notched P waves in lead II) and new-onset irregular rhythm consistent with atrial fibrillation.
6. Echocardiography confirms thickened, calcified mitral leaflets with a mitral valve area of 0.9 cm2, severe left atrial enlargement, and a diastolic pressure half-time of 280 ms; she is referred for percutaneous balloon mitral valvuloplasty.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause worldwide: rheumatic heart disease from Group A Strep.
- Opening snap + mid-diastolic rumble at apex; shorter S2-OS interval = more severe stenosis.
- LA enlargement leads to AFib and risk of systemic emboli; pulmonary hypertension develops late.
- Treatment: balloon valvuloplasty for pliable non-calcified valves; surgical replacement otherwise.

CASE_ID: 102
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Ventricular Tachycardia

ALIASES:
VT
v-tach
sustained ventricular tachycardia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man with a history of anterior myocardial infarction 4 years ago presents with sudden onset palpitations, dizziness, and near-syncope lasting 20 minutes.
2. He is currently on metoprolol and aspirin; he has not had cardiac follow-up in an abnormal value.
3. On exam, heart rate is 172 bpm and regular; blood pressure is 88/an abnormal value; he is diaphoretic and pale.
4. ECG shows a wide-complex tachycardia at 172 bpm with AV dissociation (P waves marching through the QRS), positive concordance in the precordial leads, and a QRS duration of 160 ms.
5. He remains hemodynamically unstable despite IV amiodarone; synchronized DC cardioversion at 200 J terminates the arrhythmia.
6. Post-cardioversion ECG shows sinus rhythm with Q waves in V1-V4 from prior MI; electrophysiology study confirms monomorphic VT from scar-mediated reentry and an ICD is implanted.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Wide-complex tachycardia with AV dissociation = VT until proven otherwise.
- AV dissociation, fusion beats, capture beats, concordance in precordial leads favor VT over SVT with aberrancy.
- Most common cause: ischemic heart disease with scar tissue acting as reentry circuit.
- Hemodynamically unstable VT: immediate synchronized cardioversion; stable VT: amiodarone or procainamide IV.

CASE_ID: 103
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pericarditis

ALIASES:
acute pericarditis
pericardial inflammation

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old man presents with sharp pleuritic chest pain that began 3 days ago, is worse when lying flat, and is relieved by sitting forward and leaning ahead.
2. He had a viral upper respiratory infection an abnormal value ago that resolved on its own.
3. On exam, a three-component friction rub is heard best at the left sternal border with the patient sitting forward and exhaling; temperature is 38.2°C.
4. ECG shows diffuse saddle-shaped ST elevation in multiple leads with PR depression in lead II and PR elevation in aVR.
5. Troponin is mildly elevated at 0.8 ng/mL; echocardiography shows a small pericardial effusion without tamponade physiology.
6. He is started on high-dose aspirin plus colchicine; at 3-month follow-up he is symptom-free and the effusion has resolved; colchicine is continued for 3 months to reduce recurrence risk.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause: viral (Coxsackievirus B, echovirus); also bacterial, autoimmune, uremic, malignant, post-MI (Dressler syndrome).
- Classic: sharp pleuritic pain relieved by sitting forward; pericardial friction rub.
- ECG: diffuse saddle-shaped ST elevation + PR depression (pathognomonic pattern).
- Treatment: NSAIDs (or aspirin) + colchicine reduces recurrence; avoid NSAIDs in post-MI pericarditis (use aspirin only).

CASE_ID: 104
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Coarctation of the Aorta

ALIASES:
aortic coarctation
CoA

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 16-year-old boy is referred for evaluation of hypertension found incidentally during a sports physical; he has never had symptoms.
2. He has a history of a bicuspid aortic valve diagnosed at age 5.
3. On exam, blood pressure is 158/an abnormal value in the right arm but 104/an abnormal value in the left leg; femoral pulses are markedly diminished and delayed compared to radial pulses.
4. A harsh systolic murmur is heard over the precordium and over the back between the scapulae; an ejection click is also present.
5. Chest X-ray shows rib notching (erosion of the inferior surface of ribs 3-8 from dilated intercostal collateral arteries) and a figure-3 sign at the aortic knob.
6. MRI angiography confirms a discrete narrowing of the descending aorta just distal to the left subclavian artery; he undergoes percutaneous balloon dilation with stent placement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Congenital narrowing of the descending aorta, usually distal to left subclavian artery.
- Upper extremity hypertension + lower extremity hypotension + diminished femoral pulses.
- Associated with bicuspid aortic valve (most common), Turner syndrome (45,X), berry aneurysms.
- Rib notching (inferior rib erosion) from intercostal collateral arteries; figure-3 sign on CXR.
- Treatment: balloon dilation with stenting or surgical repair.

CASE_ID: 105
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pleural Effusion

ALIASES:
exudative pleural effusion
transudative pleural effusion
pleural fluid

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man with a history of congestive heart failure presents with worsening dyspnea and a nonproductive cough over 2 weeks despite being on diuretics.
2. He recently stopped taking his furosemide because of leg cramps.
3. On exam, breath sounds are absent at the right base with dullness to percussion; tactile fremitus is decreased on the right.
4. Chest X-ray shows blunting of the right costophrenic angle with a meniscus sign; decubitus film confirms the fluid is free-flowing.
5. Thoracentesis yields 800 mL of straw-colored fluid; LDH is 88 U/L (serum LDH 200), protein is 1.8 g/dL (serum protein 6.4 g/dL), and glucose equals serum glucose - consistent with a transudate by Light criteria.
6. After diuresis is resumed and optimized, the effusion resolves; he is counseled on medication adherence.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Light criteria for exudate: pleural fluid/serum LDH >0.6, pleural LDH >2/3 upper limit of normal, pleural fluid/serum protein >0.5.
- Transudates: heart failure (most common), cirrhosis, nephrotic syndrome - systemic causes.
- Exudates: infection (parapneumonic), malignancy, TB, rheumatoid arthritis, PE - local causes.
- Physical exam: absent breath sounds, dullness, decreased fremitus at the base.

CASE_ID: 106
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Mesothelioma

ALIASES:
malignant mesothelioma
pleural mesothelioma
asbestos-related cancer

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old retired shipyard worker presents with a 4-month history of progressive dyspnea, dull right-sided chest pain, and a 20-pound weight loss.
2. He worked with asbestos insulation for an abnormal value before retiring; his last exposure was approximately an abnormal value ago.
3. On exam, breath sounds are markedly decreased over the right hemithorax; there is dullness to percussion and visible chest wall fullness on the right.
4. Chest CT shows a large right pleural effusion with circumferential pleural thickening and nodular pleural masses encasing the right lung.
5. Thoracentesis yields hemorrhagic fluid; cytology is non-diagnostic; video-assisted thoracoscopic surgery (VATS) biopsy shows malignant epithelioid cells positive for calretinin, WT-1, and D2-40 on immunohistochemistry.
6. The diagnosis of malignant pleural mesothelioma is confirmed; he is started on cisplatin plus pemetrexed chemotherapy; prognosis is discussed with the family.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Malignancy of pleural mesothelial cells; strongly associated with asbestos exposure (latency 20-40 years).
- Asbestos fibers: serpentine (chrysotile) and amphibole (crocidolite - most carcinogenic).
- Pleural effusion + pleural thickening on CT; diagnosis requires biopsy (cytology often inadequate).
- IHC markers: calretinin, WT-1, D2-40 (positive); CEA, TTF-1 (negative - helps distinguish from adenocarcinoma).
- Poor prognosis; median survival 12-18 months.

CASE_ID: 107
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Diverticulitis

ALIASES:
acute diverticulitis
colonic diverticulitis
sigmoid diverticulitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old obese man presents with 2 days of left lower quadrant pain, fever, and change in bowel habits; he has had similar milder episodes in the past.
2. He reports a low-fiber diet his entire adult life; he takes ibuprofen regularly for back pain.
3. On exam, temperature is 38.6°C; there is left lower quadrant tenderness with voluntary guarding; no peritoneal signs are present.
4. WBC is 16,400 with left shift; CRP is elevated; CT scan of the abdomen and pelvis shows sigmoid colonic wall thickening with pericolonic fat stranding and a small pericolic abscess.
5. He is started on IV piperacillin-tazobactam and placed on bowel rest with gradual improvement over 48 hours.
6. At 6-week follow-up colonoscopy rules out underlying malignancy; he is counseled on high-fiber diet and is offered elective sigmoid colectomy given his recurrent complicated episodes.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Inflammation of colonic diverticula; sigmoid colon most commonly affected.
- Left lower quadrant pain, fever, leukocytosis - "left-sided appendicitis."
- CT scan is diagnostic modality of choice; do NOT perform colonoscopy during acute attack.
- Uncomplicated: oral antibiotics (or observation for mild cases); complicated (abscess, perforation, fistula, obstruction): IV antibiotics ± drainage ± surgery.
- Diverticulosis: low-fiber diet, constipation; diverticula form at weakest points (where vasa recta penetrate muscularis).

CASE_ID: 108
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Primary Biliary Cholangitis

ALIASES:
PBC
primary biliary cirrhosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old woman presents with a 2-year history of progressive fatigue, intense generalized pruritus that is worst at night, and dry eyes and dry mouth.
2. She has a history of Sjögren syndrome and autoimmune thyroiditis.
3. On exam, she has mild jaundice, xanthelasmas around her eyes, and excoriation marks from scratching; the liver edge is palpable an abnormal value below the costal margin.
4. Labs show markedly elevated alkaline phosphatase at an abnormal value, GGT an abnormal value, mildly elevated bilirubin, and cholesterol an abnormal value; AST and ALT are only mildly elevated.
5. Anti-mitochondrial antibodies (AMA) are positive at a titer of 1:640; anti-M2 subtype is specifically positive.
6. Liver biopsy shows granulomatous destruction of small intrahepatic bile ducts with lymphocytic infiltration; she is started on ursodeoxycholic acid with improvement in liver enzymes over 6 months.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Autoimmune destruction of small intrahepatic bile ducts; predominantly affects middle-aged women.
- Anti-mitochondrial antibodies (AMA, anti-M2) are the hallmark - present in >95%.
- Elevated ALP and GGT >> AST/ALT (cholestatic pattern); pruritus from bile salt accumulation.
- Associated with Sjögren syndrome, CREST, autoimmune thyroiditis.
- Treatment: ursodeoxycholic acid (slows progression); liver transplant for end-stage disease.

CASE_ID: 109
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Zenker Diverticulum

ALIASES:
pharyngoesophageal diverticulum
Zenker's diverticulum
hypopharyngeal diverticulum

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 74-year-old man presents with a 3-year history of progressive difficulty swallowing, regurgitation of undigested food hours after eating, and a gurgling sound in his throat when he swallows.
2. He reports waking up at night with food material in his mouth and has had several episodes of aspiration pneumonia.
3. He has lost 18 pounds over the past year due to fear of eating; he frequently clears his throat.
4. On exam, a soft compressible mass is noted on the left side of his neck that gurgles on palpation (a gurgling neck mass).
5. Barium swallow reveals a posterior outpouching arising from the posterior pharyngeal wall at the level of the Killian triangle (between the inferior pharyngeal constrictor and cricopharyngeus muscle).
6. He undergoes endoscopic cricopharyngeal myotomy with staple-assisted diverticulotomy with resolution of symptoms and no further aspiration events.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Pulsion diverticulum through Killian triangle (Killian dehiscence) from cricopharyngeal muscle dysfunction.
- Classic symptoms: dysphagia, regurgitation of undigested food, halitosis, gurgling, aspiration.
- Most common in elderly men; diagnosed by barium swallow (NOT endoscopy - risk of perforation).
- Boyce sign: neck mass that gurgles on compression.
- Treatment: surgical or endoscopic cricopharyngeal myotomy.

CASE_ID: 110
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Multiple Endocrine Neoplasia Type 1

ALIASES:
MEN1
Werner syndrome
MEN type 1

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 36-year-old woman presents with recurrent kidney stones, peptic ulcer disease refractory to high-dose PPI therapy, and episodes of hypoglycemia.
2. Her father had parathyroid surgery at age 40 and a pituitary tumor removed at age 44.
3. Labs show hypercalcemia (calcium an abnormal value), elevated PTH, and markedly elevated fasting gastrin at 2,200 pg/mL.
4. She also has galactorrhea; MRI of the pituitary shows a an abnormal value macroadenoma; prolactin is elevated at 480 ng/mL.
5. CT of the abdomen shows a 2.1 cm pancreatic head mass; sestamibi scan shows four-gland parathyroid hyperplasia.
6. Genetic testing reveals a germline MEN1 gene mutation (menin tumor suppressor, chromosome 11q13); her children are offered cascade genetic testing.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- MEN1 (3 P's): Parathyroid hyperplasia (most common, hypercalcemia), Pituitary adenoma (prolactinoma most common), Pancreatic islet cell tumors (gastrinoma → Zollinger-Ellison syndrome most common).
- Zollinger-Ellison: gastrinoma → excess gastrin → refractory PUD, diarrhea, esophagitis.
- MEN2A: Medullary thyroid cancer + Pheochromocytoma + Parathyroid (RET mutation).
- MEN2B: MTC + Pheo + Mucosal neuromas + Marfanoid habitus (RET mutation, no parathyroid).
- Autosomal dominant; menin is a tumor suppressor gene on chromosome 11q13.

CASE_ID: 111
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hypernatremia

ALIASES:
high sodium
elevated serum sodium
hypernatremia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. An 84-year-old woman with advanced dementia is brought from her nursing home with altered mental status, lethargy, and decreased responsiveness over 2 days.
2. She has had poor oral intake for an abnormal value and no access to water without assistance; she has no history of kidney disease.
3. On exam, mucous membranes are parched, skin turgor is poor, and she is disoriented and lethargic; no focal neurological deficits are present.
4. Serum sodium is an abnormal value; serum osmolality is 338 mOsm/kg; urine osmolality is 820 mOsm/kg (appropriately concentrated, indicating normal ADH response).
5. BUN is 48 mg/dL and creatinine is 1.8 mg/dL consistent with prerenal azotemia from volume depletion.
6. She is given free water replacement via NG tube targeting sodium correction no faster than 10-12 mEq/L per day; serum sodium normalizes over 48 hours with improvement in mental status.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hypernatremia = serum Na >145; always represents a deficit of free water relative to sodium.
- Most common cause: inadequate water intake (elderly, infants, altered mental status); also excessive water loss (diabetes insipidus, sweating, diarrhea).
- Concentrated urine (Uosm >600) = appropriate ADH response = insufficient water intake or insensible losses.
- Symptoms: lethargy, irritability, muscle twitching, seizures, coma.
- Correction: replace free water deficit slowly (no faster than 10-12 mEq/L per day) to avoid cerebral edema.

CASE_ID: 112
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acromegaly

ALIASES:
growth hormone excess
GH excess
somatotroph adenoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old man presents after his wife noticed over the past 5 years that his face has changed, his hands and feet have grown larger, and his voice has deepened.
2. He reports worsening headaches, bilateral hand numbness and tingling, and excessive sweating; his hat and shoe sizes have both increased by two sizes.
3. On exam, he has coarse facial features with frontal bossing, macroglossia, prognathism, widely spaced teeth, and spade-like hands with soft tissue enlargement.
4. Phalen and Tinel tests are positive bilaterally consistent with carpal tunnel syndrome; blood pressure is 158/an abnormal value.
5. IGF-1 (somatomedin C) is markedly elevated at 4.8 times the upper limit of normal for his age; random GH fails to suppress below 1 ng/mL during a 75g oral glucose tolerance test.
6. MRI of the pituitary reveals a 2.2 cm macroadenoma with suprasellar extension; he undergoes transsphenoidal surgical resection with significant reduction in GH and IGF-1 levels.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- GH-secreting pituitary adenoma; in adults causes acromegaly (not gigantism, as epiphyses are closed).
- Classic features: coarsening of facial features, enlargement of hands/feet, prognathism, macroglossia, carpal tunnel syndrome, hyperglycemia, hypertension, sleep apnea.
- Diagnosis: elevated IGF-1 + failure of GH to suppress during OGTT (gold standard).
- Complications: cardiovascular disease (most common cause of death), sleep apnea, diabetes, colon polyps.
- Treatment: transsphenoidal surgery (first line); octreotide/lanreotide (somatostatin analogs) if surgery fails.

CASE_ID: 113
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Bell Palsy

ALIASES:
facial nerve palsy
idiopathic facial paralysis
CN VII palsy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old woman presents with sudden onset of right-sided facial droop that developed overnight; she noticed it when she tried to smile in the mirror this morning.
2. She had a mild cold an abnormal value ago; she reports mild right ear pain that began yesterday.
3. On exam, she is unable to raise her right eyebrow, close her right eye, smile symmetrically, or puff her cheeks on the right side - involvement of both upper and lower face on the right.
4. The remainder of the neurological exam is normal; taste is absent on the anterior two-thirds of the right tongue.
5. There are no vesicles in the ear canal or on the palate; Lyme serology is negative; blood glucose is normal.
6. She is started on a 10-day course of prednisone plus acyclovir within 72 hours of onset; at 2-month follow-up, nearly complete facial function has returned.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Idiopathic facial nerve (CN VII) palsy; most common cause of unilateral facial weakness.
- Key: COMPLETE ipsilateral facial weakness (forehead spared = upper motor neuron/central lesion).
- Taste loss (anterior 2/3 tongue), hyperacusis, decreased lacrimation can accompany.
- Associated with HSV-1 reactivation; if vesicles in ear = Ramsay Hunt syndrome (VZV).
- Treatment: prednisone within 72 hours (best evidence); add antivirals (acyclovir/valacyclovir).

CASE_ID: 114
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Trigeminal Neuralgia

ALIASES:
tic douloureux
facial pain syndrome
CN V neuralgia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old woman presents with episodic severe right-sided facial pain that she describes as an electric shock or stabbing sensation lasting 1-2 seconds, occurring dozens of times per day.
2. The pain is triggered by chewing, speaking, touching her cheek, and a light breeze on her face; she has stopped eating adequately out of fear of triggering attacks.
3. Between attacks she is completely pain-free; the pain is localized to her right cheek and lower jaw.
4. Neurological exam is completely normal; no sensory loss or motor weakness is detected in the face.
5. MRI of the brain shows a vascular loop from the superior cerebellar artery compressing the right trigeminal nerve at the root entry zone.
6. She is started on carbamazepine with significant reduction in attack frequency; given ongoing symptoms despite medication, she is referred for microvascular decompression surgery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common neuralgia; affects V2 (maxillary) and V3 (mandibular) divisions most commonly.
- Paroxysmal, severe, lancinating facial pain triggered by innocuous stimuli (trigger zones).
- Unilateral, seconds duration, pain-free intervals; neurological exam is NORMAL.
- Most common cause: vascular compression of CN V at root entry zone (superior cerebellar artery).
- Treatment: carbamazepine (first-line); oxcarbazepine; microvascular decompression if refractory.

CASE_ID: 115
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Normal Pressure Hydrocephalus

ALIASES:
NPH
communicating hydrocephalus

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 76-year-old man is brought by his family for a year of progressive walking difficulty, urinary accidents, and declining memory affecting his daily activities.
2. He describes his feet as feeling "glued to the floor" and takes small shuffling steps; he has difficulty initiating movement.
3. On exam, he has a wide-based magnetic gait with en bloc turns; he is unable to tandem walk and has mild executive dysfunction on cognitive testing.
4. He has urinary urgency with several episodes of incontinence per week; MRI brain shows markedly enlarged lateral ventricles out of proportion to sulcal atrophy with an Evans ratio of 0.38.
5. Lumbar puncture opening pressure is 14 cm H2O (normal); removal of 40 mL of CSF results in dramatic improvement in his gait that lasts 6 hours.
6. A ventriculoperitoneal shunt is placed; at 3-month follow-up gait has significantly improved and urinary incontinence has resolved; cognition shows partial improvement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Classic triad (3 W's): Wet (urinary incontinence), Wobbly (magnetic gait), Wacky (dementia).
- Gait disturbance is first and most prominent symptom; best response to shunting.
- Normal opening pressure despite ventriculomegaly (impaired CSF resorption at arachnoid granulations).
- Large-volume LP tap test: improvement confirms shunt candidacy.
- Differentiate from Parkinson (tremor, rigidity, responds to levodopa) and Alzheimer (memory first).

CASE_ID: 116
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hyperkalemia

ALIASES:
high potassium
elevated potassium
potassium toxicity

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man with end-stage renal disease on hemodialysis misses his scheduled session and presents to the ED with muscle weakness and palpitations.
2. He admits to eating bananas, tomatoes, and orange juice over the past 2 days.
3. On exam, he has mild proximal muscle weakness and decreased deep tendon reflexes; he is placed on continuous cardiac monitoring immediately.
4. ECG shows peaked tall T waves in the precordial leads with widening of the QRS to 140 ms; serum potassium is an abnormal value.
5. IV calcium gluconate is administered immediately for cardiac membrane stabilization; insulin plus dextrose and nebulized albuterol are given to shift potassium into cells.
6. He undergoes emergent hemodialysis with definitive potassium removal; post-dialysis potassium is 4.2 mEq/L and ECG normalizes completely.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- ECG changes in order: peaked T waves → PR prolongation → wide QRS → sine wave → VF.
- Most common causes: renal failure, ACE inhibitors/ARBs/K-sparing diuretics, metabolic acidosis, cell lysis.
- Treatment sequence: calcium gluconate (stabilizes membrane, does NOT lower K), insulin+dextrose (shifts K in), albuterol (shifts K in), bicarbonate, kayexalate/patiromer (eliminates K), dialysis (definitive).
- Calcium gluconate is the FIRST treatment when ECG changes are present.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 117
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Nephrolithiasis

ALIASES:
kidney stones
renal calculi
urolithiasis
calcium oxalate stones

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old man presents with sudden onset severe right flank pain that radiates to his groin and right testicle; he is writhing on the stretcher and cannot get comfortable.
2. He reports nausea and vomiting; he notes his urine appeared pink this morning.
3. On exam, he has severe right costovertebral angle tenderness; abdomen is soft without guarding; there is no fever.
4. Urinalysis shows 3+ blood with 50-100 RBCs per high-power field; urine pH is 5.0; no bacteria or WBC casts are present.
5. Non-contrast CT of the abdomen and pelvis shows a 5 mm calcified stone at the right ureterovesical junction with mild proximal hydroureteronephrosis.
6. He is given IV ketorolac and ondansetron with significant pain relief; the stone passes spontaneously over the next 48 hours; stone analysis shows calcium oxalate monohydrate and he is advised to increase fluid intake to >2.5 L/day.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common type: calcium oxalate (75-80%); radiopaque on plain X-ray and CT.
- Classic: acute severe colicky flank pain radiating to groin, hematuria, nausea.
- CT without contrast: most sensitive imaging; ultrasound preferred in pregnant patients.
- Stone types: calcium oxalate/phosphate (radiopaque, most common), struvite (staghorn, infection-related), uric acid (radiolucent, in gout/low urine pH), cystine (genetic, hexagonal crystals).
- Stones <5mm usually pass spontaneously; >10mm usually require intervention.

CASE_ID: 118
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Iron Deficiency Anemia

ALIASES:
IDA
microcytic anemia
iron deficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with fatigue, pallor, and exertional dyspnea over 3 months; she has heavy menstrual periods lasting 8-10 days each cycle.
2. She reports craving ice constantly and occasionally eating clay (pica); she has noticed spoon-shaped nails developing.
3. On exam, she is pale with conjunctival pallor; koilonychia (spoon nails) and angular cheilitis are present; the tongue appears smooth and atrophic.
4. CBC shows hemoglobin 7.8 g/dL, MCV 64 fL (microcytic), elevated RDW 18%; peripheral smear shows hypochromic microcytic cells, target cells, and pencil cells.
5. Iron studies: serum ferritin 3 ng/mL (very low), serum iron low, TIBC elevated, transferrin saturation 6%.
6. Upper and lower endoscopy is performed given her young age and severe anemia to rule out GI blood loss; menorrhagia is confirmed as the cause and she is started on oral ferrous sulfate with iron supplementation and hormonal therapy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common anemia worldwide; low MCV, low ferritin (most specific), low iron, high TIBC.
- Causes: blood loss (most common in adults), poor intake, malabsorption (celiac), increased demand (pregnancy).
- Pica (ice/pagophagia, clay/geophagia), koilonychia, angular cheilitis, Plummer-Vinson syndrome (esophageal web).
- Distinguish from thalassemia: IDA has high RDW (anisocytosis); thalassemia has normal/low RDW with microcytosis.
- Treatment: oral iron; investigate cause; parenteral iron if malabsorption.

CASE_ID: 119
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Aplastic Anemia

ALIASES:
bone marrow failure
pancytopenia
aplastic anaemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 19-year-old man presents with 6 weeks of progressive fatigue, recurrent nosebleeds, and multiple small bruises appearing without trauma.
2. He had hepatitis A infection an abnormal value ago; he takes no medications and has no chemical exposures.
3. On exam, he is pale with petechiae on his lower extremities and oral mucosa; no lymphadenopathy or organomegaly is present.
4. CBC shows pancytopenia: hemoglobin 6.2 g/dL, WBC 1,800 (ANC 400), and platelets 12,000; reticulocyte count is 0.1% (markedly low).
5. Peripheral smear shows normocytic red cells without abnormal cell forms; no blasts are seen; the smear is simply hypocellular.
6. Bone marrow biopsy shows markedly hypocellular marrow (less than 10% cellularity) replaced by fat cells with no dysplasia or malignant infiltration; he is referred for allogeneic stem cell transplant given his age and severe aplasia.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Failure of hematopoietic stem cells → pancytopenia with hypocellular bone marrow.
- Causes: idiopathic (most common), drugs (chloramphenicol, chemotherapy), radiation, viral (hepatitis, parvovirus B19, EBV), autoimmune, PNH.
- Pancytopenia + low reticulocyte count + hypocellular bone marrow = aplastic anemia.
- Distinguish from myelodysplastic syndrome (dysplasia on marrow biopsy) and leukemia (blasts).
- Treatment: young patients → allogeneic HSCT; older patients → immunosuppression (ATG + cyclosporine).

CASE_ID: 120
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Polycythemia Vera

ALIASES:
PV
polycythemia rubra vera
JAK2 mutation erythrocytosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man presents with headaches, dizziness, blurred vision, and intense itching after hot showers that has progressively worsened over 2 years.
2. His face appears unusually ruddy; he has had two episodes of transient visual loss and one deep vein thrombosis in the past year.
3. On exam, facial plethora and injected conjunctivae are notable; splenomegaly extends an abnormal value below the left costal margin.
4. CBC shows hemoglobin 20.8 g/dL, hematocrit 63%, WBC 14,200, and platelets 720,000; serum EPO is undetectable.
5. Bone marrow biopsy shows hypercellularity with trilineage proliferation and decreased iron stores.
6. JAK2 V617F mutation testing is positive, confirming polycythemia vera; he is started on phlebotomy to maintain hematocrit below 45% and low-dose aspirin; hydroxyurea is added given his high thrombotic risk.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Myeloproliferative neoplasm; JAK2 V617F mutation in >95% of cases.
- Aquagenic pruritus after hot shower is nearly pathognomonic (histamine from basophils).
- Low EPO distinguishes from secondary polycythemia (reactive EPO elevation in hypoxia, EPO-secreting tumors).
- Complications: thrombosis (stroke, MI, Budd-Chiari syndrome), hemorrhage, transformation to myelofibrosis or AML.
- Treatment: phlebotomy (first-line to keep Hct <45%); hydroxyurea for high-risk; low-dose aspirin.

CASE_ID: 121
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Herpes Simplex Encephalitis

ALIASES:
HSV encephalitis
herpes encephalitis
HSV-1 encephalitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old woman presents with 4 days of fever, severe headache, and personality changes described by her husband as unusual behavior and speaking incoherently.
2. She had a cold sore on her lip an abnormal value ago; over the past 24 hours she has become increasingly confused and had one brief seizure at home.
3. On exam, temperature is 39.1°C; she is confused, agitated, and has subtle right-sided facial twitching; there is mild nuchal rigidity.
4. CT head is normal; lumbar puncture shows 120 WBCs (lymphocytic pleocytosis), elevated protein at an abnormal value, and normal glucose; opening pressure is mildly elevated.
5. MRI of the brain shows T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in the right temporal lobe and insular cortex with corresponding restricted diffusion - the classic pattern for HSV encephalitis.
6. CSF HSV-1 PCR returns positive; IV acyclovir is started immediately on clinical suspicion (before PCR result) and continued for 14-21 days with full neurological recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause of fatal sporadic encephalitis in the US; HSV-1 in adults, HSV-2 in neonates.
- Triad: fever + altered mental status + focal neurological signs (especially temporal lobe symptoms - personality change, olfactory hallucinations, temporal lobe seizures).
- MRI: temporal lobe and insular cortex involvement (hemorrhagic necrosis).
- CSF: lymphocytic pleocytosis, elevated protein, normal glucose; PCR for HSV is diagnostic.
- START IV ACYCLOVIR EMPIRICALLY - do not wait for PCR confirmation; mortality without treatment is >70%.

CASE_ID: 122
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Cellulitis

ALIASES:
skin infection
soft tissue infection
bacterial cellulitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man with diabetes and peripheral vascular disease presents with 3 days of progressive redness, warmth, and swelling of his left lower leg following a minor abrasion while gardening.
2. The redness has expanded rapidly and he now has a red line tracking up his leg; he is febrile at 38.9°C.
3. On exam, the left lower leg shows a well-demarcated area of erythema, edema, and warmth extending from the ankle to mid-calf; a red streak (lymphangitis) tracks proximally toward the groin.
4. WBC is 18,200; blood cultures are drawn; no fluctuance or crepitus is appreciated; there is no evidence of abscess formation on bedside ultrasound.
5. He is started on IV cefazolin given systemic signs and lymphangitis; blood cultures grow beta-hemolytic Streptococcus pyogenes (Group A Strep).
6. After 48 hours of IV antibiotics with clinical improvement (decreased erythema and fever), he is transitioned to oral cephalexin for a total 14-day course with wound care and leg elevation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common causes: Group A Streptococcus (S. pyogenes) and Staphylococcus aureus.
- Non-purulent cellulitis: streptococcal; purulent (abscess, furuncle): staphylococcal (cover MRSA if needed).
- Lymphangitis (red streak) indicates spread along lymphatics; systemic signs require IV antibiotics.
- Risk factors: skin disruption, edema, diabetes, obesity, tinea pedis, venous insufficiency.
- Mark borders of erythema with pen to monitor progression; failure to improve in 48 hours = consider deeper infection (necrotizing fasciitis).
