CASE_ID: 260
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Achalasia

ALIASES:
esophageal achalasia
primary achalasia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old woman presents with a 2-year history of progressive difficulty swallowing both solids and liquids that has worsened over time.
2. She reports regurgitation of undigested food, occasional chest pain, and an unintentional 15-pound weight loss.
3. On exam, she appears thin but otherwise well; no neck mass or lymphadenopathy is appreciated.
4. Barium swallow shows a dilated esophagus that tapers to a smooth bird-beak narrowing at the lower esophageal sphincter.
5. Upper endoscopy excludes mechanical obstruction or malignancy at the gastroesophageal junction.
6. Esophageal manometry demonstrates absent peristalsis in the distal esophagus and failure of the lower esophageal sphincter to relax with swallowing, and she undergoes pneumatic dilation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Achalasia results from loss of inhibitory neurons in the myenteric plexus of the esophagus, causing failure of LES relaxation and absent peristalsis.
- Hallmark: dysphagia to BOTH solids and liquids from the onset (unlike obstructing lesions, which progress from solids to liquids).
- Barium swallow shows the classic bird-beak (tapered) appearance at the LES.
- High-resolution manometry is the gold standard for diagnosis.
- Treatment options: pneumatic dilation, Heller myotomy, peroral endoscopic myotomy (POEM), or botulinum toxin injection.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 261
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Wilson Disease

ALIASES:
hepatolenticular degeneration
copper overload

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old college student is referred for evaluation of elevated liver enzymes found on routine screening, with mild fatigue.
2. Over the past year his roommate has noticed worsening tremor, slurred speech, and emotional lability; his grades have declined sharply.
3. On exam, he has a resting and intention tremor, dysarthria, and slow movements; slit-lamp examination reveals golden-brown rings at the corneal limbus.
4. Labs show AST 110, ALT 145, low alkaline phosphatase, and a Coombs-negative hemolytic anemia.
5. Serum ceruloplasmin is markedly low at 8 mg/dL and 24-hour urinary copper is elevated at 280 mcg.
6. Liver biopsy shows hepatic copper content greater than 250 mcg/g dry weight, and genetic testing confirms biallelic ATP7B mutations; he is started on penicillamine.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Wilson disease is an autosomal recessive disorder caused by ATP7B gene mutations leading to impaired biliary excretion of copper.
- Copper accumulates in the liver, brain (especially basal ganglia), and cornea.
- Kayser-Fleischer rings (copper deposition in Descemet membrane) are characteristic and best seen on slit-lamp examination.
- Triad: liver disease (hepatitis, cirrhosis), neurologic symptoms (tremor, dystonia, parkinsonism), and psychiatric symptoms.
- Treatment: chelation with penicillamine or trientine; zinc reduces intestinal copper absorption; liver transplant for fulminant or end-stage disease.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 262
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Alpha-1 Antitrypsin Deficiency

ALIASES:
A1AT deficiency
AAT deficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old nonsmoker presents with progressive exertional dyspnea over the past 2 years and a chronic cough.
2. He has no history of occupational exposures but his father had cirrhosis of unknown cause at age 55.
3. On exam, he has decreased breath sounds bilaterally with prolonged expiration; the liver is enlarged and firm to palpation.
4. Pulmonary function tests show an obstructive pattern with significantly decreased FEV1; chest CT reveals panacinar emphysema predominantly in the lower lobes.
5. Liver enzymes are mildly elevated and serum alpha-1 antitrypsin level is low at 35 mg/dL (normal 90-200).
6. Genetic testing confirms homozygous PiZZ phenotype, and liver biopsy reveals PAS-positive, diastase-resistant globules in hepatocytes.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Alpha-1 antitrypsin is a serine protease inhibitor that protects lung tissue from neutrophil elastase.
- Deficiency causes early-onset panacinar emphysema (lower lobe predominant, in contrast to smoking-related upper lobe emphysema).
- The PiZZ phenotype causes misfolded protein accumulation in hepatocytes, leading to liver disease.
- Liver biopsy shows characteristic PAS-positive, diastase-resistant intracytoplasmic globules.
- Treatment: smoking cessation (essential), bronchodilators, intravenous AAT augmentation therapy; liver transplant cures the deficiency.

CASE_ID: 263
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Gilbert Syndrome

ALIASES:
constitutional hepatic dysfunction
familial nonhemolytic unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 21-year-old college student is referred for evaluation of yellow eyes noticed by his roommate during exam week.
2. He recently recovered from a viral upper respiratory infection and reports being stressed and skipping meals during finals.
3. On exam, he has mild scleral icterus but is otherwise asymptomatic and well-appearing; there is no hepatosplenomegaly.
4. Labs show total bilirubin of an abnormal value with direct bilirubin of an abnormal value; AST, ALT, alkaline phosphatase, CBC, and reticulocyte count are all normal.
5. Haptoglobin, LDH, and Coombs test are normal, ruling out hemolysis.
6. Repeat testing after he resumes regular meals shows bilirubin returning toward normal, and genetic testing identifies a homozygous TA insertion in the UGT1A1 promoter consistent with reduced enzyme activity.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Gilbert syndrome is caused by reduced activity of UDP-glucuronosyltransferase 1A1 (UGT1A1), the enzyme responsible for conjugating bilirubin.
- It is a benign autosomal recessive condition affecting 5-10% of the population.
- Presents with mild unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia precipitated by fasting, illness, stress, or dehydration.
- Distinguished from hemolysis by normal CBC, reticulocyte count, LDH, and haptoglobin.
- No treatment is necessary; reassurance is appropriate; important to recognize to avoid unnecessary workup.

CASE_ID: 264
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Diabetes Insipidus

ALIASES:
DI
central diabetes insipidus
nephrogenic diabetes insipidus

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old man presents with 3 weeks of excessive thirst and urination, drinking large amounts of water and getting up multiple times each night to urinate.
2. He sustained a traumatic brain injury in a motor vehicle accident an abnormal value ago and has otherwise recovered well.
3. On exam, vital signs are normal; mucous membranes are mildly dry; neurologic exam is nonfocal.
4. 24-hour urine output is 6.5 L with a urine specific gravity of 1.002; serum sodium is an abnormal value and serum osmolality is 304 mOsm/kg.
5. A water deprivation test fails to concentrate the urine, with urine osmolality remaining at 120 mOsm/kg.
6. After administration of desmopressin, urine osmolality rises by more than 100% to 580 mOsm/kg, confirming central etiology, and MRI shows loss of the normal posterior pituitary bright spot.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Diabetes insipidus is characterized by polyuria, polydipsia, hypernatremia, and dilute urine.
- Central DI: deficient ADH secretion from the posterior pituitary (head trauma, surgery, tumors, idiopathic).
- Nephrogenic DI: renal resistance to ADH (lithium, hypercalcemia, hypokalemia, hereditary).
- Water deprivation test: failure to concentrate urine; desmopressin challenge then distinguishes central (responds) from nephrogenic (no response).
- Treatment: central DI with desmopressin; nephrogenic DI with thiazide diuretics, low-salt/protein diet, and removal of offending agents.

CASE_ID: 265
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hashimoto Thyroiditis

ALIASES:
chronic lymphocytic thyroiditis
autoimmune hypothyroidism
Hashimoto disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 45-year-old woman presents with several months of fatigue, weight gain, cold intolerance, constipation, and dry skin.
2. She reports menstrual irregularities and worsening depression; her mother has vitiligo and her sister has type 1 diabetes.
3. On exam, she has bradycardia, periorbital puffiness, delayed relaxation of deep tendon reflexes, and a firm nontender enlarged thyroid gland with a bumpy texture.
4. Labs show TSH 38 mIU/L (markedly elevated), free T4 of 0.4 ng/dL (low), and elevated LDL cholesterol.
5. Anti-thyroid peroxidase (anti-TPO) and anti-thyroglobulin antibodies are strongly positive.
6. Thyroid ultrasound shows a heterogeneous gland with hypoechoic areas, and fine-needle aspiration would reveal lymphocytic infiltration with germinal centers and Hurthle cells.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hashimoto thyroiditis is the most common cause of hypothyroidism in iodine-sufficient regions.
- Autoimmune destruction of the thyroid mediated by anti-TPO and anti-thyroglobulin antibodies.
- Histology: lymphocytic infiltration with germinal centers and Hurthle (Askanazy) cells.
- Strongly associated with HLA-DR3 and HLA-DR5 and with other autoimmune diseases (type 1 DM, vitiligo, Addison disease).
- Increased risk of primary thyroid lymphoma; treatment is lifelong levothyroxine replacement.

CASE_ID: 266
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Subacute Thyroiditis

ALIASES:
de Quervain thyroiditis
granulomatous thyroiditis
subacute granulomatous thyroiditis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old woman presents with 2 weeks of anterior neck pain that radiates to her jaw, low-grade fever, palpitations, and tremor.
2. She had a viral upper respiratory infection about an abnormal value ago that resolved on its own.
3. On exam, she is tachycardic with a temperature of 38.1°C; the thyroid gland is firm and exquisitely tender to palpation.
4. Labs show suppressed TSH, elevated free T4, an ESR of an abnormal value/hr, and a CRP of 42 mg/L.
5. Radioactive iodine uptake is markedly decreased at less than 2%, distinguishing this from Graves disease.
6. She is treated with NSAIDs and a short course of prednisone with rapid symptomatic improvement, and over weeks transitions through a hypothyroid phase before returning to euthyroid.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Subacute thyroiditis classically follows a viral illness and presents with a painful, tender thyroid gland.
- Triphasic course: hyperthyroid phase (release of preformed hormone), hypothyroid phase, then recovery to euthyroid in most cases.
- Markedly elevated ESR and CRP are characteristic.
- LOW radioactive iodine uptake (gland is not synthesizing hormone, only releasing stored hormone) distinguishes it from Graves disease (high uptake).
- Treatment is supportive: NSAIDs, beta blockers for symptoms, prednisone for severe cases.

CASE_ID: 267
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Carcinoid Syndrome

ALIASES:
neuroendocrine tumor syndrome
serotonin syndrome from tumor

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man presents with episodic facial flushing, diarrhea, and wheezing occurring multiple times per week over the past 6 months.
2. The episodes are sometimes triggered by alcohol, certain foods, or emotional stress; he has lost 10 pounds.
3. On exam, telangiectasias are noted on the cheeks and nose; auscultation reveals expiratory wheezing and a tricuspid regurgitation murmur that increases with inspiration.
4. Labs show elevated 24-hour urinary 5-hydroxyindoleacetic acid (5-HIAA) at 80 mg/24 hr (normal <8) and elevated chromogranin A.
5. CT of the abdomen reveals a 3 cm mass in the terminal ileum with mesenteric lymphadenopathy and multiple hepatic lesions.
6. Octreotide scan shows intense uptake in the liver and ileal mass, and biopsy of a liver lesion confirms a well-differentiated neuroendocrine tumor.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Carcinoid tumors are neuroendocrine tumors that secrete serotonin and other vasoactive substances; most arise in the GI tract (ileum most common).
- Carcinoid SYNDROME requires liver metastases (or extra-portal primary) because the liver normally inactivates serotonin via first-pass metabolism.
- Classic tetrad: flushing, diarrhea, wheezing/bronchospasm, right-sided valvular heart disease (tricuspid regurgitation, pulmonic stenosis).
- Diagnosis: elevated 24-hour urine 5-HIAA (serotonin metabolite); somatostatin receptor imaging (octreotide scan or DOTATATE PET).
- Treatment: surgical resection if possible; octreotide (somatostatin analog) controls symptoms and slows tumor growth.

CASE_ID: 268
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Alzheimer Disease

ALIASES:
AD
Alzheimer dementia
senile dementia of Alzheimer type

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 74-year-old woman is brought in by her daughter who reports a 2-year history of gradual decline in her mother's memory and ability to manage household finances.
2. She has gotten lost while driving familiar routes and repeats the same questions; she has no significant vascular risk factors and no stepwise decline.
3. On exam, she is pleasant but confabulates dates and has difficulty recalling 3 of 3 objects after 5 minutes; cranial nerves and motor exam are normal; gait is normal.
4. MMSE score is 19/30 with prominent deficits in recall and orientation; basic labs, TSH, B12, and RPR are all normal.
5. MRI of the brain shows generalized cortical atrophy with prominent hippocampal and medial temporal lobe atrophy without acute lesions.
6. CSF biomarkers show decreased amyloid-beta 42 and elevated total and phosphorylated tau, supporting a diagnosis of Alzheimer disease.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Alzheimer disease is the most common cause of dementia in older adults; characterized by gradual progressive memory loss with preserved alertness early on.
- Pathology: extracellular amyloid-beta plaques and intracellular neurofibrillary tangles of hyperphosphorylated tau.
- Earliest atrophy is in the hippocampus and entorhinal cortex; APOE-e4 is the strongest genetic risk factor.
- Early-onset familial AD is associated with mutations in APP, presenilin-1, and presenilin-2; trisomy 21 (Down syndrome) causes early AD due to extra APP gene copies.
- Treatment: cholinesterase inhibitors (donepezil, rivastigmine, galantamine) and memantine (NMDA antagonist) for symptoms; anti-amyloid monoclonals for early disease.

CASE_ID: 269
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Ischemic Stroke

ALIASES:
cerebrovascular accident
CVA
acute ischemic stroke

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man with atrial fibrillation is brought to the ED by his wife after she noticed he was suddenly unable to speak clearly and had right-sided facial drooping while watching TV 90 minutes ago.
2. He has a history of poorly controlled hypertension and diabetes; he stopped taking his anticoagulation due to cost.
3. On exam, he has right facial droop sparing the forehead, severely impaired expressive language with intact comprehension, and right arm and leg weakness (4/5 strength).
4. NIH Stroke Scale is 12; fingerstick glucose is an abnormal value; blood pressure is 184/an abnormal value.
5. Non-contrast CT of the head shows no hemorrhage and a hyperdense left middle cerebral artery sign; CT angiography reveals a proximal left M1 occlusion.
6. He receives IV alteplase within the time window and undergoes mechanical thrombectomy with successful recanalization, recovering most function over the following week.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acute ischemic stroke is caused by occlusion of a cerebral artery, most commonly from thromboembolism (atrial fibrillation, carotid disease).
- Time is brain: IV tPA is given within 4.5 hours of symptom onset; mechanical thrombectomy for large vessel occlusion up to 24 hours in selected patients.
- MCA territory stroke causes contralateral face/arm weakness > leg, aphasia (if dominant hemisphere), and hemineglect (if non-dominant).
- Forehead is spared in upper motor neuron facial weakness (bilateral innervation of upper face).
- Non-contrast CT is mandatory before tPA to exclude hemorrhage; blood pressure must be <185/110 for tPA eligibility.

CASE_ID: 270
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Intracerebral Hemorrhage

ALIASES:
ICH
hypertensive hemorrhage
hemorrhagic stroke

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man with poorly controlled hypertension develops sudden severe headache, vomiting, and progressive left-sided weakness while shoveling snow.
2. He has not been taking his antihypertensive medications consistently for several months.
3. On exam, he is lethargic with blood pressure of 218/an abnormal value; he has a left hemiparesis with left facial droop sparing the forehead and conjugate gaze deviation to the right.
4. Non-contrast CT of the head shows a 35 mL hyperdense lesion centered in the right putamen with surrounding edema and mild mass effect.
5. There is no fluid level to suggest coagulopathy and no underlying mass on subsequent MRI.
6. Blood pressure is carefully lowered to a systolic target of 140 mmHg with IV nicardipine, and he is admitted to the neuro-ICU for monitoring of intracranial pressure.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hypertensive intracerebral hemorrhage most commonly occurs in the basal ganglia (putamen most common), thalamus, pons, and cerebellum due to rupture of Charcot-Bouchard microaneurysms.
- Presents with sudden focal neurologic deficits PLUS headache, vomiting, and decreased level of consciousness (less common in ischemic stroke).
- Non-contrast CT shows hyperdense (bright) acute blood; this is the test that distinguishes ischemic from hemorrhagic stroke.
- Other causes: cerebral amyloid angiopathy (lobar hemorrhage in elderly), vascular malformations (younger patients), anticoagulant use, trauma.
- Management: blood pressure control (systolic <140-160), reverse anticoagulation, manage ICP, surgical evacuation for cerebellar hemorrhage >3 cm.

CASE_ID: 271
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Friedreich Ataxia

ALIASES:
Friedreich's ataxia
FRDA
FXN deficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 14-year-old boy is brought in for evaluation of progressive clumsiness, frequent falls, and worsening handwriting over the past 2 years.
2. He was previously athletic but now has difficulty walking and his speech has become slurred; an older sister has similar symptoms.
3. On exam, he has gait ataxia, dysarthria, dysmetria, absent deep tendon reflexes in the lower extremities, extensor plantar responses, and impaired proprioception and vibration sense.
4. He has high-arched feet (pes cavus), hammer toes, and kyphoscoliosis; echocardiogram shows concentric left ventricular hypertrophy.
5. Fasting glucose is 158 mg/dL consistent with diabetes mellitus.
6. Genetic testing reveals a homozygous GAA trinucleotide repeat expansion in intron 1 of the FXN gene, reducing frataxin protein expression.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Friedreich ataxia is an autosomal recessive trinucleotide repeat disorder (GAA expansion in the FXN gene on chromosome 9).
- Frataxin deficiency impairs mitochondrial iron metabolism, affecting tissues with high energy demand.
- Neurologic features: progressive gait ataxia, dysarthria, areflexia, loss of proprioception/vibration, and upgoing toes (combination of dorsal column and corticospinal tract degeneration).
- Systemic features: hypertrophic cardiomyopathy (most common cause of death), diabetes mellitus, pes cavus, kyphoscoliosis.
- One of the few trinucleotide repeat diseases that is AUTOSOMAL RECESSIVE (most are autosomal dominant).

CASE_ID: 272
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Tourette Syndrome

ALIASES:
Gilles de la Tourette syndrome
chronic tic disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. An 11-year-old boy is brought in for evaluation of involuntary blinking, throat-clearing, and shoulder shrugging that have waxed and waned over the past 2 years.
2. He recently started uttering brief inappropriate words at school, causing embarrassment; he has difficulty paying attention in class and washes his hands repeatedly.
3. On exam, he is alert and engaged but exhibits frequent eye blinking, head jerking, and grunting throughout the visit; he can suppress them briefly with effort.
4. The movements are not present during sleep and worsen with stress; they have been present for more than an abnormal value with onset before age 18.
5. Neurologic exam is otherwise normal, and laboratory studies including TSH, ANA, and antistreptolysin O are unremarkable.
6. He is diagnosed clinically based on the combination of multiple motor tics and at least one vocal tic for more than 1 year; comorbid ADHD and OCD are also identified and treated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Tourette syndrome requires multiple motor tics AND at least one vocal tic (need not be concurrent) for more than 1 year, with onset before age 18.
- Tics are sudden, brief, stereotyped movements or vocalizations; can be temporarily suppressed; worsen with stress; usually disappear during sleep.
- Coprolalia (involuntary inappropriate words) is famous but occurs in less than 20% of patients.
- High comorbidity with OCD and ADHD; commonly involves dysfunction of basal ganglia circuits.
- Treatment: behavioral therapy (habit reversal) first-line; medications (alpha-2 agonists like clonidine, antipsychotics like risperidone) for severe symptoms.

CASE_ID: 273
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
IgA Nephropathy

ALIASES:
Berger disease
IgAN

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old man presents with dark urine that began 1 day ago.
2. He has had an upper respiratory infection with sore throat for the past 2 days; he had a similar episode an abnormal value ago that resolved spontaneously.
3. On exam, he is normotensive with no edema; throat is mildly erythematous; the remainder of the exam is unremarkable.
4. Urinalysis shows gross hematuria with red blood cell casts and 2+ proteinuria; serum creatinine is an abnormal value; complement levels (C3 and C4) are normal.
5. Antistreptolysin O titer is normal, distinguishing this from poststreptococcal glomerulonephritis.
6. Renal biopsy shows mesangial proliferation on light microscopy and granular mesangial deposits of IgA on immunofluorescence, confirming the diagnosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- IgA nephropathy is the most common primary glomerulonephritis worldwide.
- Classic presentation: episodic gross hematuria SYNPHARYNGITIC (concurrent with mucosal infection), in contrast to PSGN which occurs 1-3 weeks AFTER infection.
- Normal complement levels distinguish IgAN from PSGN (which has low C3) and lupus nephritis (low C3 and C4).
- Mesangial IgA deposition on immunofluorescence is diagnostic.
- Henoch-Schönlein purpura (IgA vasculitis) is the systemic form; treatment may include ACE inhibitors, fish oil, and immunosuppression for progressive disease.

CASE_ID: 274
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Focal Segmental Glomerulosclerosis

ALIASES:
FSGS
focal segmental glomerular sclerosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old man presents with progressive swelling of his legs and face and frothy urine over the past 2 months.
2. He has a history of HIV infection diagnosed an abnormal value ago, currently on antiretroviral therapy, with otherwise stable disease.
3. On exam, he has periorbital edema, anasarca with 3+ pitting edema, and blood pressure of 152/an abnormal value.
4. Urinalysis shows 4+ proteinuria with oval fat bodies; 24-hour urine protein is 9.2 g; serum albumin is 2.1 g/dL and LDL is an abnormal value.
5. Complement levels are normal; ANA, hepatitis serologies, and other secondary workup are unrevealing.
6. Renal biopsy shows segmental sclerosis affecting some (focal) but not all glomeruli on light microscopy, with effacement of podocyte foot processes on electron microscopy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- FSGS is the most common cause of nephrotic syndrome in adults, particularly in African Americans (linked to APOL1 gene variants).
- Causes: primary (idiopathic), secondary to HIV (collapsing variant), heroin use, obesity, sickle cell, reduced nephron mass.
- Light microscopy: focal (some glomeruli) and segmental (part of glomerulus) sclerosis; immunofluorescence is typically negative.
- Electron microscopy: diffuse foot process effacement.
- Treatment: corticosteroids (primary), ACE inhibitors/ARBs, treatment of underlying cause; less responsive to steroids than minimal change disease.

CASE_ID: 275
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hereditary Spherocytosis

ALIASES:
HS
spherocytic hemolytic anemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 16-year-old girl presents with intermittent fatigue, mild jaundice, and right upper quadrant pain that worsens after meals.
2. She had neonatal jaundice requiring phototherapy; her father had his gallbladder and spleen removed in his 30s for unclear reasons.
3. On exam, she has scleral icterus and palpable splenomegaly; mild jaundice is noted.
4. CBC shows hemoglobin 9.4 g/dL with elevated MCHC, elevated reticulocyte count, and a peripheral smear showing numerous spherocytes lacking central pallor.
5. Total bilirubin is 3.8 mg/dL with predominantly unconjugated bilirubin, LDH is elevated, and haptoglobin is decreased; direct Coombs test is negative.
6. Eosin-5-maleimide (EMA) binding flow cytometry shows decreased binding to red blood cells, and right upper quadrant ultrasound reveals pigmented gallstones.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hereditary spherocytosis is most commonly autosomal dominant, caused by mutations in red cell membrane proteins (ankyrin most common, also spectrin, band 3, protein 4.2).
- Loss of membrane → spherical RBCs that are trapped and destroyed in the spleen → chronic extravascular hemolysis.
- Classic CBC finding: elevated MCHC (RBCs are dehydrated and concentrated).
- Coombs-negative hemolytic anemia distinguishes HS from autoimmune hemolytic anemia (which also has spherocytes).
- Complications: pigmented bilirubin gallstones, aplastic crisis (parvovirus B19), splenomegaly; treatment is splenectomy and folate supplementation; cholecystectomy if symptomatic gallstones.

CASE_ID: 276
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
G6PD Deficiency

ALIASES:
glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase deficiency
favism

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old Mediterranean man presents with sudden onset fatigue, dark urine, and yellow skin 2 days after starting a new medication for a urinary tract infection.
2. He had a similar episode after eating fava beans as a child; family history is notable for similar episodes in maternal uncles.
3. On exam, he has scleral icterus and pallor; spleen tip is palpable; the remainder of the exam is unremarkable.
4. CBC shows hemoglobin of 7.8 g/dL (was 13.5 last month) with elevated reticulocytes; LDH is elevated and haptoglobin is undetectable; indirect bilirubin is an abnormal value.
5. Peripheral smear shows bite cells and Heinz bodies (visible with supravital stain); direct Coombs test is negative.
6. The triggering medication (trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole) is discontinued; quantitative G6PD assay performed weeks after the acute episode is markedly low at 5% of normal, confirming the enzyme defect.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- G6PD deficiency is the most common enzymopathy worldwide; X-linked recessive inheritance.
- G6PD generates NADPH via the pentose phosphate pathway, which is needed to regenerate reduced glutathione that protects RBCs from oxidative damage.
- Triggers: oxidative stressors such as sulfa drugs, antimalarials (primaquine), nitrofurantoin, dapsone, fava beans, infection, and naphthalene (mothballs).
- Episodic hemolytic anemia with bite cells (splenic macrophages bite out denatured hemoglobin) and Heinz bodies (oxidized hemoglobin precipitates).
- Test enzyme levels AFTER the acute episode (false-normal during hemolysis as deficient cells are destroyed); treatment is avoidance of triggers.

CASE_ID: 277
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Beta Thalassemia Major

ALIASES:
Cooley anemia
transfusion-dependent thalassemia
beta-thalassemia major

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 2-year-old child of Mediterranean descent is brought in for evaluation of pallor, poor feeding, and failure to thrive.
2. He has had multiple episodes of severe anemia requiring transfusion since infancy; his parents are first cousins.
3. On exam, he has pallor, mild jaundice, prominent maxillary and frontal bones with widened cheekbones, and a markedly enlarged spleen extending to the iliac crest.
4. CBC shows hemoglobin of 5.4 g/dL with severe microcytic anemia (MCV 58), elevated reticulocytes, and a peripheral smear showing target cells, nucleated RBCs, and severe anisopoikilocytosis.
5. Skull X-ray demonstrates a hair-on-end appearance from marrow expansion.
6. Hemoglobin electrophoresis reveals absent or markedly reduced HbA, with marked elevation of HbF and HbA2, confirming the diagnosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Beta thalassemia results from mutations in the HBB gene reducing or eliminating beta-globin production; autosomal recessive.
- Beta-thalassemia major (homozygous): severe transfusion-dependent anemia presenting after 6 months as HbF declines.
- Ineffective erythropoiesis causes marrow expansion (skull X-ray: hair-on-end; widened maxilla, chipmunk facies) and extramedullary hematopoiesis (hepatosplenomegaly).
- Hemoglobin electrophoresis: increased HbF and HbA2, absent or low HbA (compare to beta-thalassemia minor: mildly elevated HbA2).
- Treatment: lifelong transfusions with iron chelation (deferoxamine, deferasirox) to prevent secondary hemochromatosis; bone marrow transplant is curative.

CASE_ID: 278
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Disseminated Intravascular Coagulation

ALIASES:
DIC
consumption coagulopathy
defibrination syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old woman with sepsis from a urinary source becomes acutely confused with diffuse oozing from her IV sites and nasogastric tube.
2. She has been on broad-spectrum antibiotics and vasopressors for the past 24 hours.
3. On exam, she is tachycardic and hypotensive; petechiae are present on the trunk and extremities; oozing is noted from multiple puncture sites and her gums.
4. Labs show platelet count 38,000, prolonged PT (24 sec) and aPTT (62 sec), markedly elevated D-dimer at 12,000 ng/mL, and low fibrinogen at an abnormal value.
5. Peripheral smear demonstrates schistocytes consistent with microangiopathic hemolytic anemia.
6. Blood cultures grow gram-negative rods; she is supported with FFP, cryoprecipitate, and platelets in addition to source control, with gradual normalization of coagulation parameters.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- DIC is widespread activation of the coagulation cascade leading to simultaneous thrombosis (consumption of clotting factors) and bleeding.
- Triggers: sepsis (especially gram-negative), obstetric complications (amniotic fluid embolism, abruption), malignancy (AML M3), trauma, snake bites.
- Lab findings: thrombocytopenia, prolonged PT and aPTT, elevated D-dimer, LOW fibrinogen, schistocytes on smear.
- Differs from TTP/HUS: TTP/HUS has normal PT/aPTT/fibrinogen; DIC has all abnormal.
- Treatment is primarily of the underlying cause; support with FFP, cryoprecipitate, and platelets for bleeding.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 279
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Immune Thrombocytopenic Purpura

ALIASES:
ITP
idiopathic thrombocytopenic purpura
immune thrombocytopenia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-year-old previously healthy girl is brought in for evaluation of a new petechial rash and easy bruising that appeared over the past 3 days.
2. She had a viral upper respiratory infection an abnormal value ago that resolved without treatment.
3. On exam, she is well-appearing with diffuse petechiae on the trunk and extremities and several ecchymoses on the shins; no hepatosplenomegaly or lymphadenopathy.
4. CBC shows platelet count of 12,000 with normal hemoglobin and white cell count; peripheral smear shows decreased platelets but those present are large; no blasts or schistocytes.
5. Coagulation studies (PT, aPTT, fibrinogen) are all normal, ruling out DIC.
6. Bone marrow aspiration, if performed, would show increased megakaryocytes with normal myeloid and erythroid lines; she is observed without treatment and her count recovers spontaneously over weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- ITP is caused by autoantibodies (usually IgG) against platelet glycoproteins (GPIIb/IIIa), leading to splenic destruction of platelets.
- Acute ITP: common in children after viral illness; usually self-limited within weeks to months.
- Chronic ITP: more common in adults, especially young women; may require treatment.
- Diagnosis of exclusion: isolated thrombocytopenia with otherwise normal CBC, smear, and coagulation; bone marrow shows increased megakaryocytes (peripheral destruction).
- Treatment for symptomatic or severe thrombocytopenia: corticosteroids, IVIG (rapid), anti-D immunoglobulin, rituximab, thrombopoietin receptor agonists, splenectomy for refractory cases.

CASE_ID: 280
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Tumor Lysis Syndrome

ALIASES:
TLS

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 14-year-old boy with newly diagnosed Burkitt lymphoma develops decreased urine output, muscle cramps, and lethargy 24 hours after starting chemotherapy.
2. He had a large abdominal mass and bulky lymphadenopathy at presentation; prophylactic allopurinol and IV fluids were started but he received only one dose before chemotherapy.
3. On exam, he is lethargic with tetany of the hands when his arm is occluded by a blood pressure cuff; ECG shows peaked T waves.
4. Labs show potassium an abnormal value, phosphate an abnormal value, calcium an abnormal value (low), uric acid an abnormal value, and creatinine an abnormal value (baseline 0.7).
5. Urinalysis shows uric acid crystals; LDH is markedly elevated at 4,800 U/L.
6. He is started on aggressive IV hydration, rasburicase for hyperuricemia, and emergent hemodialysis for severe metabolic derangements and acute kidney injury; he recovers fully.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Tumor lysis syndrome occurs with rapid destruction of tumor cells, releasing intracellular contents into the circulation.
- Classic electrolyte tetrad: HIGH potassium, HIGH phosphate, HIGH uric acid, LOW calcium (binds released phosphate).
- Highest risk: hematologic malignancies with high cell turnover (Burkitt lymphoma, ALL, AML), large tumor burden, high LDH.
- Acute kidney injury results from uric acid and calcium phosphate crystal deposition in renal tubules.
- Prevention: aggressive IV hydration, allopurinol (xanthine oxidase inhibitor) or rasburicase (recombinant uricase) before chemotherapy; treatment includes hemodialysis if severe.

CASE_ID: 281
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Polycystic Ovary Syndrome

ALIASES:
PCOS
Stein-Leventhal syndrome
hyperandrogenic anovulation

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old woman presents with irregular menstrual cycles occurring every 60-90 days since menarche, along with progressive weight gain and unwanted facial hair growth.
2. She has been unable to conceive after an abnormal value of trying with her partner; she has noticed darkened velvety skin in her armpits and neck.
3. On exam, BMI is 34, blood pressure is 138/an abnormal value; she has terminal hair on the chin and upper lip, mild facial acne, and acanthosis nigricans of the neck.
4. Labs show elevated free testosterone, LH:FSH ratio of 3:1, fasting insulin elevated with HOMA-IR consistent with insulin resistance, and HbA1c of 6.2%.
5. Pregnancy test, prolactin, TSH, and 17-hydroxyprogesterone are all normal, excluding alternative causes.
6. Transvaginal ultrasound reveals bilateral enlarged ovaries with more than 20 small peripheral follicles arranged in a string-of-pearls pattern; metformin and combined oral contraceptives are started.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- PCOS is diagnosed by Rotterdam criteria (2 of 3): oligo/anovulation, clinical or biochemical hyperandrogenism, polycystic ovarian morphology on ultrasound.
- Pathophysiology involves insulin resistance with hyperinsulinemia driving ovarian androgen production and reducing SHBG.
- Strong association with metabolic syndrome, type 2 diabetes, obstructive sleep apnea, NAFLD, and endometrial hyperplasia/cancer (from unopposed estrogen).
- Acanthosis nigricans and skin tags suggest insulin resistance.
- Treatment: lifestyle modification, combined oral contraceptives (regulate cycles, reduce androgens), metformin (insulin sensitization), letrozole or clomiphene for fertility, spironolactone for hirsutism.

CASE_ID: 282
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Prostate Cancer

ALIASES:
prostate adenocarcinoma
carcinoma of the prostate

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man presents for routine follow-up; he is largely asymptomatic but reports new mid-lower back pain over the past 2 months that worsens at night.
2. He has a family history of his father dying of metastatic disease and African American ancestry.
3. On exam, digital rectal examination reveals a firm asymmetric nodule on the posterior aspect of the prostate; spine is tender to palpation over L3-L4.
4. Serum PSA is 38 ng/mL (markedly elevated); alkaline phosphatase is elevated at an abnormal value.
5. Multiparametric MRI of the prostate shows a PI-RADS 5 lesion in the peripheral zone; bone scan demonstrates multiple osteoblastic lesions in the lumbar spine, pelvis, and ribs.
6. Transrectal ultrasound-guided prostate biopsy of the lesion reveals adenocarcinoma with a Gleason score of 4+5=9, and he is started on androgen deprivation therapy with leuprolide plus abiraterone.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Prostate cancer is the most common non-skin cancer in men; African American ancestry and family history are strong risk factors.
- Most arise in the peripheral zone of the prostate, palpable on DRE; in contrast, BPH affects the transitional/periurethral zone.
- Bone metastases are characteristically OSTEOBLASTIC (sclerotic) on imaging, with elevated alkaline phosphatase.
- Gleason scoring (now grouped into Grade Groups 1-5) assesses histologic grade; higher = more aggressive.
- Treatment depends on stage: active surveillance for low-risk, radical prostatectomy or radiation for localized, androgen deprivation (GnRH agonists/antagonists, antiandrogens) for advanced disease.

CASE_ID: 283
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Breast Cancer

ALIASES:
invasive ductal carcinoma
mammary carcinoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old postmenopausal woman presents after noticing a firm lump in her left breast on self-examination 2 weeks ago.
2. Her mother had a similar diagnosis at age 48 and a maternal aunt died of ovarian disease; she had her first child at age 35 and used hormone replacement therapy for an abnormal value.
3. On exam, there is a an abnormal value firm, irregular, fixed mass in the upper outer quadrant of the left breast with skin dimpling; a an abnormal value hard mobile left axillary lymph node is palpable; no nipple discharge.
4. Diagnostic mammography shows an irregular spiculated mass with pleomorphic microcalcifications; ultrasound confirms a solid hypoechoic lesion with posterior acoustic shadowing.
5. Core needle biopsy reveals invasive ductal carcinoma; immunohistochemistry shows ER positive, PR positive, HER2 negative.
6. MRI staging and PET-CT reveal no distant metastases; she undergoes breast-conserving surgery with sentinel lymph node biopsy followed by radiation, adjuvant chemotherapy, and tamoxifen, and genetic testing identifies a pathogenic BRCA2 mutation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Invasive ductal carcinoma is the most common type of breast cancer (75-80%).
- Risk factors: increasing age, female sex, BRCA1/BRCA2 mutations, family history, early menarche, late menopause, nulliparity or first child after age 30, hormone replacement therapy, obesity.
- Suspicious findings: hard, fixed, irregular mass; skin/nipple changes; spiculated mass with microcalcifications on mammography.
- Receptor status guides treatment: ER/PR positive (endocrine therapy: tamoxifen premenopausal, aromatase inhibitors postmenopausal); HER2 positive (trastuzumab); triple negative (chemotherapy).
- BRCA1: increased risk of breast and ovarian cancer; BRCA2: breast (including male), ovarian, pancreatic, prostate; risk-reducing surgery and screening for carriers.

CASE_ID: 284
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Endometrial Cancer

ALIASES:
endometrial adenocarcinoma
uterine cancer
endometrial carcinoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old obese woman presents with vaginal bleeding that started 6 months after her last menstrual period.
2. She has a history of type 2 diabetes, hypertension, polycystic ovary syndrome, and nulliparity; she took unopposed estrogen briefly for menopausal symptoms.
3. On exam, BMI is 38; pelvic exam reveals a normal-appearing cervix with blood in the vaginal vault; the uterus is mobile and slightly enlarged.
4. Pelvic ultrasound shows an endometrial stripe thickness of an abnormal value (postmenopausal abnormal >an abnormal value).
5. Endometrial biopsy reveals complex atypical hyperplasia with foci of well-differentiated endometrioid adenocarcinoma.
6. CT staging shows disease confined to the uterus; she undergoes total hysterectomy with bilateral salpingo-oophorectomy and pelvic lymphadenectomy, and final pathology confirms FIGO stage IA endometrioid adenocarcinoma.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Postmenopausal bleeding is endometrial cancer until proven otherwise; requires endometrial sampling.
- Risk factors are mostly states of unopposed estrogen: obesity, nulliparity, late menopause, early menarche, PCOS, unopposed estrogen therapy, tamoxifen, Lynch syndrome (HNPCC).
- Type I (endometrioid, 80%): estrogen-dependent, often arises from hyperplasia, better prognosis; Type II (serous, clear cell): non-estrogen-dependent, p53 mutations, worse prognosis.
- Postmenopausal endometrial stripe >4 mm on transvaginal ultrasound warrants biopsy.
- Treatment: total hysterectomy with bilateral salpingo-oophorectomy; radiation and/or chemotherapy for higher stages.

CASE_ID: 285
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Placental Abruption

ALIASES:
abruptio placentae
placental separation

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 31-year-old woman at 34 weeks gestation presents to labor and delivery with sudden onset severe abdominal pain and dark vaginal bleeding that began 2 hours ago.
2. She has a history of hypertension, smokes one pack per day, and was in a minor motor vehicle collision yesterday without immediate symptoms.
3. On exam, blood pressure is 92/an abnormal value with tachycardia at 122; the abdomen is rigid and tender with a firm woody uterus; fetal heart rate shows late decelerations.
4. Hemoglobin has dropped from 11.8 to 9.4 g/dL since her last visit; platelets are 88,000; fibrinogen is an abnormal value; coagulation studies are mildly abnormal.
5. Bedside ultrasound shows a retroplacental hypoechoic collection; the placenta is normally located away from the cervical os.
6. Given the maternal hemodynamic instability and non-reassuring fetal heart tracing, she undergoes emergency cesarean delivery with concurrent blood product resuscitation and delivers a viable infant.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Placental abruption is premature separation of the placenta from the uterine wall before delivery.
- Classic triad: PAINFUL vaginal bleeding (may be concealed if retroplacental) + uterine tenderness/rigidity + fetal distress.
- Risk factors: hypertension (most important), trauma, cocaine use, smoking, prior abruption, advanced maternal age, multiparity, polyhydramnios.
- DIC can develop from release of thromboplastin into maternal circulation.
- Treatment depends on fetal/maternal status: delivery if hemodynamically unstable or non-reassuring fetal tracing; expectant management for select stable cases remote from term.

CASE_ID: 286
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Croup

ALIASES:
laryngotracheobronchitis
viral croup

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 2-year-old boy is brought to the ED at 1 AM with a 1-day history of low-grade fever, runny nose, and now a harsh barking cough that started this evening.
2. He has been increasingly fussy and his cough sounds like a seal barking; symptoms worsen when he is upset.
3. On exam, temperature is 38.4°C; he has inspiratory stridor at rest, mild suprasternal retractions, and a hoarse voice; oxygen saturation is 96% on room air; he is drooling slightly but able to swallow.
4. AP neck X-ray shows subglottic narrowing of the tracheal air column producing a steeple sign.
5. He is given nebulized racemic epinephrine and a single dose of oral dexamethasone with marked improvement in stridor and respiratory effort.
6. He is observed for 4 hours without recurrence of stridor at rest and discharged home with parental education; symptoms gradually resolve over the next several days.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Croup (laryngotracheobronchitis) is caused most commonly by parainfluenza virus type 1; affects children 6 months to 6 years.
- Classic triad: barking (seal-like) cough, inspiratory stridor, hoarse voice; preceded by 1-2 days of URI symptoms.
- "Steeple sign" on AP neck X-ray reflects subglottic edema.
- Distinguish from epiglottitis: croup is gradual onset, viral, child can lie down and tolerate secretions; epiglottitis is acute, "tripod" position, drooling, toxic appearance, "thumbprint sign" on lateral neck X-ray.
- Treatment: humidified air, dexamethasone for all severities, nebulized racemic epinephrine for moderate-to-severe stridor at rest.

CASE_ID: 287
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Down Syndrome

ALIASES:
trisomy 21
Down's syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A newborn male is evaluated immediately after delivery for distinctive facial features and generalized hypotonia.
2. His mother is an abnormal value old; she declined first-trimester screening during pregnancy and the delivery was uncomplicated.
3. On exam, he has upslanting palpebral fissures, epicanthal folds, flat nasal bridge, small ears, a protruding tongue, a single transverse palmar crease bilaterally, and a gap between the first and second toes; a harsh holosystolic murmur is heard at the left lower sternal border.
4. Echocardiogram demonstrates a complete atrioventricular septal defect; abdominal X-ray for bilious vomiting on day 2 shows a double bubble sign.
5. CBC reveals a transient leukemoid reaction with elevated WBC, common in this condition.
6. Karyotype analysis confirms 47,XY,+21 (full trisomy 21), and a comprehensive evaluation including thyroid studies, hearing screen, and ophthalmologic exam is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Down syndrome (trisomy 21) is the most common chromosomal disorder; risk increases with advanced maternal age due to meiotic nondisjunction.
- Three mechanisms: meiotic nondisjunction (95%, maternal age-related), Robertsonian translocation (4%, can be inherited), mosaicism (1%, milder features).
- Cardiac defects (50%): endocardial cushion defects (complete AVSD) most characteristic; also VSD, ASD, PDA, Tetralogy of Fallot.
- GI: duodenal atresia (double bubble sign), Hirschsprung disease, imperforate anus, annular pancreas.
- Other associations: intellectual disability, hypothyroidism, atlantoaxial instability, early Alzheimer disease, increased risk of ALL and AML (especially AMKL).

CASE_ID: 288
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Turner Syndrome

ALIASES:
45,X
monosomy X
gonadal dysgenesis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 14-year-old girl is referred for evaluation of short stature and primary amenorrhea; she has never had a menstrual period.
2. Her parents are average height; she has been below the 5th percentile for height since age 4 and has not had any signs of pubertal development.
3. On exam, she is an abnormal value tall (below 3rd percentile) with a webbed neck, low posterior hairline, broad shield-shaped chest with widely spaced nipples, and cubitus valgus; Tanner stage 1 breast and pubic hair.
4. A continuous machinery-like murmur is heard at the back; blood pressure is higher in the arms than the legs; femoral pulses are diminished.
5. Labs show markedly elevated FSH and LH with low estradiol consistent with hypergonadotropic hypogonadism; renal ultrasound reveals a horseshoe kidney.
6. Karyotype confirms 45,X; echocardiogram identifies a bicuspid aortic valve with coarctation of the aorta, and growth hormone and eventually estrogen replacement therapy are initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Turner syndrome results from complete or partial absence of one X chromosome (most commonly 45,X, also mosaicism 45,X/46,XX).
- Clinical features: short stature, primary amenorrhea (streak ovaries), webbed neck, shield chest with widely spaced nipples, cubitus valgus, low posterior hairline, lymphedema in infancy.
- Cardiovascular: bicuspid aortic valve (most common), coarctation of the aorta (continuous murmur, BP discrepancy), aortic dissection.
- Other: horseshoe kidney, hypothyroidism, primary ovarian failure (hypergonadotropic hypogonadism with elevated FSH/LH).
- Treatment: growth hormone in childhood, estrogen replacement at appropriate age for puberty and bone health.

CASE_ID: 289
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Klinefelter Syndrome

ALIASES:
47,XXY
XXY syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 17-year-old boy is brought in by his mother because he has not undergone normal puberty and has been struggling academically.
2. He is taller than his classmates but has not had a growth of facial hair, deepening of his voice, or development of muscle bulk; his testes have always been small.
3. On exam, he is an abnormal value tall with long arms and legs (eunuchoid body habitus), bilateral gynecomastia, sparse facial and body hair, and very small firm testes (4 mL bilaterally with a normal-sized penis).
4. Labs show elevated FSH and LH with low testosterone, consistent with hypergonadotropic hypogonadism.
5. Semen analysis reveals azoospermia.
6. Karyotype confirms 47,XXY; he is started on testosterone replacement therapy with subsequent development of secondary sexual characteristics.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Klinefelter syndrome (47,XXY) is the most common cause of primary hypogonadism and infertility in men.
- Caused by meiotic nondisjunction; risk increases with advanced parental age.
- Classic features: tall stature with long extremities, small firm testes (<6 mL), gynecomastia, sparse body hair, infertility (azoospermia), low testosterone with elevated FSH/LH.
- Increased risk of breast cancer (related to gynecomastia), osteoporosis, varicose veins, autoimmune disease, and germ cell tumors.
- Treatment: testosterone replacement for virilization and bone health; assisted reproductive technologies (TESE-ICSI) for fertility.

CASE_ID: 290
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Angle-Closure Glaucoma

ALIASES:
acute angle closure
closed-angle glaucoma
angle-closure crisis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old hyperopic woman presents to the ED with sudden onset severe right eye pain, blurred vision, nausea, and vomiting that began while she was in a dark movie theater.
2. She reports seeing halos around lights and her right eye feels rock-hard; she takes diphenhydramine for sleep occasionally.
3. On exam, the right eye is markedly injected with a fixed mid-dilated pupil that does not respond to light, hazy cornea, and shallow anterior chamber; visual acuity is severely reduced.
4. Tonometry reveals intraocular pressure of an abnormal value in the right eye (normal <21); gonioscopy of the left eye shows a narrow anterior chamber angle.
5. Slit-lamp examination of the affected eye shows corneal microcystic edema and a closed iridocorneal angle.
6. She is treated with topical timolol, brimonidine, and pilocarpine, IV acetazolamide, and IV mannitol; pressure decreases, and definitive treatment with laser peripheral iridotomy is performed bilaterally.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acute angle-closure glaucoma is an ophthalmologic emergency caused by sudden closure of the anterior chamber angle and blockage of aqueous humor outflow.
- Risk factors: anatomically narrow angles (hyperopia, Asian ancestry, age >60, female sex), pupillary dilation (dim light, anticholinergics, sympathomimetics).
- Presentation: severe eye pain, headache, blurred vision, halos around lights, nausea/vomiting (parasympathetic response), fixed mid-dilated pupil, rock-hard eye, conjunctival injection.
- IOP markedly elevated (40-80 mmHg); diagnosis confirmed by gonioscopy showing closed angle.
- Treatment: medical lowering of IOP (topical and systemic), then definitive laser peripheral iridotomy (creates a hole in the iris to bypass pupillary block).

CASE_ID: 291
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Age-Related Macular Degeneration

ALIASES:
AMD
ARMD
macular degeneration

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 76-year-old woman presents with progressive blurring and distortion of central vision in her right eye that has been worsening over the past 6 months.
2. She reports that straight lines such as door frames appear wavy and she has difficulty reading and recognizing faces; peripheral vision is preserved.
3. On exam, visual acuity is 20/200 in the right eye and 20/40 in the left eye; an Amsler grid shows central metamorphopsia in the right eye.
4. Fundoscopy of the right eye reveals subretinal hemorrhage, exudates, and elevation of the macula; the left eye shows numerous drusen at the macula.
5. Optical coherence tomography demonstrates subretinal fluid and a choroidal neovascular membrane in the right eye.
6. Intravitreal anti-VEGF (ranibizumab) injections are initiated for the right eye with stabilization of visual acuity; AREDS2 vitamin supplements are recommended for the left eye.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- AMD is the leading cause of irreversible central vision loss in adults over 50.
- Dry (atrophic, non-exudative, 80-90%): gradual central vision loss; characterized by drusen and retinal pigment epithelium atrophy.
- Wet (exudative, 10-20%): rapid central vision loss with metamorphopsia (wavy distortion); choroidal neovascularization with subretinal hemorrhage and exudates.
- Risk factors: age, smoking (most important modifiable), family history, light iris color, female sex.
- Treatment: AREDS2 vitamins (antioxidants, zinc) for intermediate dry AMD; intravitreal anti-VEGF injections (ranibizumab, aflibercept, bevacizumab) for wet AMD.

CASE_ID: 292
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Diabetic Retinopathy

ALIASES:
DR
proliferative diabetic retinopathy
nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 56-year-old man presents for an annual eye exam reporting gradual blurring of vision in both eyes over the past year, with occasional floaters in the right eye.
2. He has had type 2 diabetes for an abnormal value with poor glycemic control (HbA1c 10.2%) and has not had an eye exam in an abnormal value.
3. On exam, best-corrected visual acuity is 20/60 in the right eye and 20/40 in the left eye; intraocular pressure is normal.
4. Dilated fundoscopy of both eyes reveals microaneurysms, dot-blot hemorrhages, hard exudates, and cotton wool spots; the right eye additionally shows new vessels emanating from the optic disc with associated preretinal hemorrhage.
5. Fluorescein angiography demonstrates extensive areas of capillary nonperfusion and leakage from the neovascular vessels.
6. The right eye is treated with panretinal photocoagulation and intravitreal anti-VEGF injection; tighter glycemic and blood pressure control are emphasized.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Diabetic retinopathy is a leading cause of blindness in working-age adults; risk correlates with duration of diabetes and glycemic control.
- Pathophysiology: chronic hyperglycemia damages retinal microvasculature; ischemia drives release of VEGF and neovascularization.
- Nonproliferative DR: microaneurysms, dot-blot hemorrhages, hard exudates, cotton wool spots.
- Proliferative DR: neovascularization (of disc, elsewhere, of iris); complications include vitreous hemorrhage, tractional retinal detachment, and neovascular glaucoma.
- Treatment: optimize glucose/BP/lipids; anti-VEGF intravitreal injections for diabetic macular edema; panretinal photocoagulation for proliferative disease.

CASE_ID: 293
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Cataract

ALIASES:
age-related cataract
nuclear sclerotic cataract
senile cataract

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old woman presents with progressive blurring of vision over the past 2 years that has made night driving particularly difficult.
2. She complains of increased glare from oncoming headlights and the sensation that she is looking through a foggy window; she has noted that colors appear less vibrant.
3. On exam, best-corrected visual acuity is 20/80 in the right eye and 20/60 in the left eye; the red reflex is dim and brownish in both eyes.
4. Slit-lamp examination shows clouding and yellowing of the central lens nucleus bilaterally.
5. Fundoscopy through the cloudy media reveals normal optic discs and maculae bilaterally, indicating that the visual loss is due to lens opacity rather than retinal disease.
6. She undergoes phacoemulsification with intraocular lens implantation in the right eye, with postoperative visual acuity improving to 20/25.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Cataract is opacification of the lens; the most common cause of reversible vision loss worldwide.
- Risk factors: aging (most common), diabetes, smoking, UV light exposure, chronic corticosteroid use, trauma, congenital (TORCH infections, galactosemia).
- Symptoms: gradually progressive painless vision loss, glare/halos around lights (especially at night), color desaturation, second sight (myopic shift may temporarily improve near vision).
- Types: nuclear sclerotic (most common, age-related), cortical, posterior subcapsular (steroid-associated, diabetes).
- Treatment: surgical removal (phacoemulsification) with intraocular lens implantation when vision interferes with daily activities.

CASE_ID: 294
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Appendicitis

ALIASES:
appendicitis
inflamed appendix

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 19-year-old man presents to the ED with 12 hours of progressive abdominal pain that began around his umbilicus and has now migrated to the right lower quadrant.
2. He has anorexia, nausea, has vomited twice, and now has a low-grade fever; he has not had a bowel movement today.
3. On exam, temperature is 38.2°C; there is right lower quadrant tenderness with rebound and guarding at McBurney point; psoas and obturator signs are positive.
4. Labs show WBC of 16,200 with left shift; urinalysis is unremarkable; CRP is elevated.
5. CT of the abdomen and pelvis with IV contrast shows a dilated (12 mm) non-compressible blind-ending tubular structure in the right lower quadrant with surrounding fat stranding and a fecalith at the base.
6. He undergoes urgent laparoscopic appendectomy; the appendix is acutely inflamed without perforation, and pathology confirms acute suppurative appendicitis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acute appendicitis is the most common surgical abdominal emergency, typically resulting from luminal obstruction (fecalith, lymphoid hyperplasia, tumor).
- Classic progression: periumbilical visceral pain (T10 dermatome) → migration to right lower quadrant (somatic, parietal peritoneum) → fever, leukocytosis.
- Physical signs: McBurney point tenderness, Rovsing sign, psoas sign (retrocecal), obturator sign (pelvic location).
- CT is the most sensitive imaging in adults; ultrasound preferred in children and pregnancy to avoid radiation.
- Treatment: appendectomy (laparoscopic preferred); antibiotics-only therapy is an option for uncomplicated cases in selected patients.

CASE_ID: 295
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Cholecystitis

ALIASES:
cholecystitis
gallbladder inflammation

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old obese woman presents with 18 hours of constant right upper quadrant abdominal pain that radiates to her right scapula, with nausea and vomiting.
2. She has had several previous episodes of similar but shorter pain after eating fatty meals; she takes oral contraceptives.
3. On exam, temperature is 38.5°C; there is right upper quadrant tenderness with arrest of inspiration during deep palpation; the gallbladder is not clearly palpable.
4. Labs show WBC 14,800 with left shift, mildly elevated AST/ALT and alkaline phosphatase; total bilirubin is an abnormal value; lipase is normal.
5. Right upper quadrant ultrasound shows a thickened gallbladder wall (6 mm), pericholecystic fluid, and multiple gallstones with a sonographic Murphy sign.
6. HIDA scan, performed because the diagnosis remained uncertain, shows non-visualization of the gallbladder at 4 hours; she undergoes laparoscopic cholecystectomy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acute cholecystitis is most commonly caused by gallstone obstruction of the cystic duct, leading to gallbladder inflammation; less commonly acalculous (critically ill patients).
- Classic patient: "Fat, female, forty, fertile" (4 F's); other risk factors include rapid weight loss, hemolytic anemia (pigmented stones), Native American ancestry.
- Murphy sign (inspiratory arrest with palpation of the right upper quadrant) is highly specific.
- Ultrasound is the first-line imaging: gallstones, thickened wall (>3 mm), pericholecystic fluid, sonographic Murphy sign; HIDA scan is most accurate (non-visualization of gallbladder = positive).
- Treatment: IV antibiotics and laparoscopic cholecystectomy within 72 hours; percutaneous cholecystostomy for unstable patients.

CASE_ID: 296
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Choledocholithiasis

ALIASES:
common bile duct stones
CBD stone
bile duct calculus

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 54-year-old woman presents with intermittent right upper quadrant pain and progressive yellowing of her skin and eyes over the past 3 days; her urine has become dark.
2. She had previous biliary colic but has not undergone cholecystectomy; she is afebrile and not particularly ill-appearing.
3. On exam, scleral icterus is present; there is mild right upper quadrant tenderness without rebound or peritoneal signs; the gallbladder is not enlarged on palpation.
4. Labs show total bilirubin of an abnormal value (direct 5.2), alkaline phosphatase an abnormal value, GGT an abnormal value, AST 180, ALT 220, and a normal lipase.
5. Right upper quadrant ultrasound shows cholelithiasis with a dilated common bile duct measuring 12 mm; magnetic resonance cholangiopancreatography reveals a 9 mm filling defect in the distal CBD.
6. She undergoes ERCP with sphincterotomy and stone extraction, followed by laparoscopic cholecystectomy during the same hospitalization.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Choledocholithiasis is the presence of gallstones in the common bile duct, usually migrated from the gallbladder.
- Presentation: jaundice (conjugated hyperbilirubinemia), cholestatic LFTs (elevated alk phos, GGT), dark urine, pale stools, intermittent right upper quadrant pain.
- Cholestatic pattern: alkaline phosphatase rises more than transaminases (opposite of hepatitis).
- Diagnosis: ultrasound shows dilated CBD (>6 mm normally, >8 mm post-cholecystectomy); MRCP or endoscopic ultrasound for visualization; ERCP for diagnosis AND therapy.
- Complications: ascending cholangitis (Charcot triad: fever, jaundice, RUQ pain), gallstone pancreatitis, secondary biliary cirrhosis.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 297
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Bipolar I Disorder

ALIASES:
manic-depressive illness
bipolar disorder
bipolar affective disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old graduate student is brought in by his roommate because he has not slept for 5 days, has been talking rapidly, and started multiple new business ventures this week.
2. He has a history of two depressive episodes in college that resolved with therapy; his uncle was hospitalized for psychiatric reasons.
3. On exam, he is well-groomed and energetic, speech is rapid and difficult to interrupt, his thoughts jump between topics, and he reports feeling on top of the world; he denies hallucinations but admits to spending $15,000 of credit on a get-rich-quick scheme.
4. Urine drug screen is negative; TSH and basic metabolic panel are normal.
5. The symptoms have been present continuously for 1 week and have caused significant functional impairment, satisfying DSM-5 criteria for a manic episode.
6. He is admitted for safety, started on lithium with adjunctive olanzapine, and stabilizes over 2 weeks; outpatient follow-up and a long-term mood stabilizer plan are arranged.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Bipolar I disorder requires at least one lifetime manic episode (≥1 week of elevated/irritable mood with 3+ symptoms or any duration if hospitalization required); depressive episodes are common but not required.
- Manic symptoms (DIG FAST): Distractibility, Indiscretion (risky behavior), Grandiosity, Flight of ideas, increased Activity/Agitation, decreased Sleep, Talkativeness/pressured speech.
- Bipolar II: hypomania (≥4 days, no marked impairment, no psychosis, no hospitalization) plus at least one major depressive episode.
- High lifetime suicide risk; 50% present first with a depressive episode (important to screen for prior mania before prescribing antidepressants alone, which can trigger mania).
- Treatment: mood stabilizers (lithium first-line, also valproate, lamotrigine, carbamazepine); atypical antipsychotics for acute mania; psychoeducation and adherence are critical.

CASE_ID: 298
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Panic Disorder

ALIASES:
panic attacks
recurrent panic attacks

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents to the ED for the third time in 2 months with sudden onset chest pain, palpitations, shortness of breath, and a feeling that she is about to die.
2. Each episode reaches peak intensity within minutes and resolves within 30 minutes; she has been avoiding driving and large crowds for fear of having another episode.
3. On exam, she appears anxious but is hemodynamically stable; the remainder of the exam is unremarkable.
4. ECG, troponin, D-dimer, TSH, urine drug screen, and basic metabolic panel are all normal.
5. The episodes are unprovoked, recurrent, and unexpected, and she experiences persistent worry between attacks about having more episodes, with associated behavioral change.
6. She is started on sertraline with a short-term course of clonazepam for breakthrough symptoms, and referred for cognitive behavioral therapy with exposure components.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Panic attacks are abrupt surges of intense fear with somatic and cognitive symptoms peaking within minutes; common in many psychiatric and medical conditions.
- Panic disorder requires RECURRENT unexpected panic attacks PLUS at least 1 month of persistent worry about additional attacks OR maladaptive change in behavior.
- Common medical mimics to exclude: arrhythmia, hyperthyroidism, pheochromocytoma, hypoglycemia, stimulant use; first-time presentation requires medical workup.
- Often complicated by agoraphobia (fear/avoidance of situations from which escape would be difficult).
- Treatment: SSRIs or SNRIs first-line (long-term); benzodiazepines for short-term symptom relief; cognitive behavioral therapy with interoceptive exposure is highly effective.

CASE_ID: 299
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Ankylosing Spondylitis

ALIASES:
AS
axial spondyloarthritis
Bechterew disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old man presents with 8 months of progressive lower back pain and morning stiffness lasting over an hour that improves with exercise.
2. He has had recurrent episodes of red painful eye over the past year; his maternal uncle has a similar back condition.
3. On exam, lumbar flexion is decreased with reduced chest expansion (an abnormal value); the Schober test is positive; both sacroiliac joints are tender to palpation.
4. Labs show ESR an abnormal value/hr and CRP 32 mg/L; HLA-B27 is positive; rheumatoid factor and anti-CCP are negative.
5. Pelvic X-ray reveals bilateral sacroiliitis with subchondral sclerosis and erosions; lumbar spine X-ray shows squaring of the vertebral bodies and early syndesmophyte formation.
6. MRI of the sacroiliac joints demonstrates active inflammation with bone marrow edema, confirming the diagnosis, and he is started on an NSAID followed by a TNF-alpha inhibitor.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Ankylosing spondylitis is a chronic inflammatory seronegative spondyloarthropathy affecting the axial skeleton; strong association with HLA-B27.
- Classic features: inflammatory back pain (worse in morning, better with activity, age <40, gradual onset, >3 months), sacroiliitis, enthesitis.
- Extraskeletal manifestations: anterior uveitis (most common, recurrent painful red eye), aortic regurgitation, restrictive lung disease (limited chest expansion), IBD, psoriasis.
- Spine X-ray progression: squaring of vertebral bodies → syndesmophytes → "bamboo spine" (late ankylosis).
- Treatment: NSAIDs first-line; TNF-alpha inhibitors (etanercept, infliximab) and IL-17 inhibitors (secukinumab) for refractory disease; physical therapy critical.

CASE_ID: 300
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Systemic Sclerosis

ALIASES:
scleroderma
systemic scleroderma
diffuse cutaneous systemic sclerosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 46-year-old woman presents with 6 months of fingers that turn white then blue then red in response to cold exposure, along with progressive tightening of the skin on her hands and difficulty swallowing solid foods.
2. She also reports heartburn, shortness of breath on exertion, and several small ulcers on her fingertips that have been slow to heal.
3. On exam, she has thickened shiny skin extending proximally past the metacarpophalangeal joints to the forearms and trunk; telangiectasias are noted on the face; bibasilar fine inspiratory crackles are heard.
4. Labs show ANA positive at 1:1280 with a nucleolar pattern; anti-Scl-70 (anti-topoisomerase I) is positive; anti-centromere antibodies are negative.
5. High-resolution CT of the chest shows bilateral basilar reticular opacities and ground-glass changes consistent with interstitial lung disease; pulmonary function tests show a restrictive pattern with decreased DLCO.
6. Echocardiography reveals an elevated estimated pulmonary artery systolic pressure; she is started on mycophenolate for lung disease, calcium channel blockers for Raynaud, and PPI for reflux.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Systemic sclerosis is an autoimmune connective tissue disease characterized by vasculopathy, autoimmunity, and excessive collagen deposition (fibrosis).
- Two main forms: limited cutaneous (CREST: Calcinosis, Raynaud, Esophageal dysmotility, Sclerodactyly, Telangiectasia; anti-centromere; pulmonary arterial hypertension is feared) vs. diffuse cutaneous (truncal skin involvement; anti-Scl-70; interstitial lung disease, scleroderma renal crisis).
- Raynaud phenomenon (triphasic color change: white-blue-red) is the most common initial manifestation.
- Scleroderma renal crisis: acute malignant hypertension with acute kidney injury, microangiopathic hemolysis; treated with ACE inhibitors (life-saving) even with renal failure.
- Treatment is organ-specific: ACE inhibitors for renal crisis, immunosuppressants (mycophenolate, cyclophosphamide) for ILD, endothelin receptor antagonists/PDE-5 inhibitors for PAH, PPIs for reflux.

CASE_ID: 301
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Atrial Fibrillation

ALIASES:
AF
AFib
irregular irregular rhythm

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old man presents with several hours of palpitations and mild shortness of breath that began while he was watching television.
2. He has a history of hypertension, type 2 diabetes, and obesity; he drinks alcohol heavily on weekends.
3. On exam, his pulse is irregularly irregular at approximately 132 bpm; lungs have mild bibasilar crackles; jugular venous pressure is mildly elevated.
4. ECG shows an irregularly irregular rhythm with no discernible P waves and a narrow QRS at a ventricular rate of 130 bpm.
5. TSH is normal; echocardiogram shows a dilated left atrium with preserved ejection fraction; CHA2DS2-VASc score is 4.
6. He is rate-controlled with metoprolol, started on apixaban for stroke prevention, and undergoes successful electrical cardioversion 4 weeks later after a transesophageal echocardiogram excludes left atrial thrombus.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Atrial fibrillation is the most common sustained arrhythmia; characterized by chaotic atrial electrical activity from multiple ectopic foci, often in the pulmonary veins.
- ECG: irregularly irregular rhythm with absent P waves and variable R-R intervals.
- CHA2DS2-VASc score guides anticoagulation: CHF, Hypertension, Age >=75 (2 pts), Diabetes, Stroke/TIA (2 pts), Vascular disease, Age 65-74, Sex (female).
- Major complications: stroke (from left atrial appendage thrombus), heart failure (loss of atrial kick, tachycardia-induced cardiomyopathy).
- Management has two arms: rate or rhythm control (similar mortality) PLUS anticoagulation based on stroke risk.

CASE_ID: 302
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Heart Failure with Reduced Ejection Fraction

ALIASES:
HFrEF
systolic heart failure
congestive heart failure

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 66-year-old man presents with several months of progressive dyspnea on exertion, orthopnea requiring 3 pillows, and bilateral lower extremity swelling.
2. He had a large anterior myocardial infarction an abnormal value ago and has not been adherent to his medications.
3. On exam, blood pressure is 102/an abnormal value; jugular venous pressure is elevated at an abnormal value; an S3 gallop and bibasilar crackles are present; pitting edema extends to his knees bilaterally.
4. Chest X-ray demonstrates cardiomegaly with pulmonary vascular cephalization, Kerley B lines, and small bilateral pleural effusions; BNP is markedly elevated at 1,850 pg/mL.
5. ECG shows Q waves in leads V1-V4 from prior infarction.
6. Transthoracic echocardiography reveals left ventricular ejection fraction of 25% with anterior wall akinesis, and guideline-directed medical therapy with sacubitril-valsartan, carvedilol, spironolactone, and empagliflozin is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- HFrEF is defined by LVEF less than 40% with signs and symptoms of heart failure.
- Most common cause is ischemic cardiomyopathy (post-MI); other causes include dilated cardiomyopathy, valvular disease, and hypertension.
- Diagnostic findings: elevated BNP/NT-proBNP, S3 gallop (specific), JVD, pulmonary edema on chest X-ray.
- Four pillars of guideline-directed medical therapy (all proven to reduce mortality): ARNI or ACEi/ARB, beta blocker (carvedilol, metoprolol succinate, bisoprolol), MRA (spironolactone, eplerenone), SGLT2 inhibitor.
- ICD for primary prevention of sudden cardiac death when EF remains <=35% despite optimal medical therapy.

CASE_ID: 303
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Aortic Dissection

ALIASES:
aortic dissection
acute aortic dissection
Stanford type A dissection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man presents with sudden onset severe tearing chest pain that radiates to his back between his scapulae and began 90 minutes ago.
2. He has poorly controlled hypertension and uses cocaine recreationally; he is tall and thin with long fingers.
3. On exam, blood pressure is 178/an abnormal value in the right arm but 138/an abnormal value in the left arm; a high-pitched early diastolic murmur is heard at the right upper sternal border.
4. Chest X-ray shows a widened mediastinum and rightward deviation of the trachea.
5. CT angiography of the chest demonstrates an intimal flap extending from the aortic root through the descending thoracic aorta with a false lumen.
6. He is taken emergently to the operating room for ascending aortic graft repair; aggressive blood pressure and heart rate control with IV labetalol are initiated preoperatively.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Aortic dissection is a tear in the intimal layer allowing blood to enter the media, creating a false lumen.
- Stanford A: involves the ascending aorta (surgical emergency); Stanford B: descending only (medical management unless complicated).
- Risk factors: hypertension (most common), connective tissue disease (Marfan, Ehlers-Danlos), bicuspid aortic valve, cocaine use, pregnancy, aortitis.
- Classic findings: severe tearing chest/back pain, blood pressure differential between arms (>20 mmHg), aortic regurgitation murmur, widened mediastinum on chest X-ray.
- Management: rapid heart rate and blood pressure control with beta blockers first (esmolol or labetalol) BEFORE vasodilators to avoid reflex tachycardia; CT angiography is diagnostic.

CASE_ID: 304
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Peripheral Artery Disease

ALIASES:
PAD
peripheral vascular disease
chronic limb ischemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man presents with several months of cramping pain in his right calf that occurs after walking one block and resolves with rest.
2. He has a 50-pack-year smoking history, hypertension, hyperlipidemia, and type 2 diabetes.
3. On exam, the right lower extremity is cool with diminished hair growth, thin shiny skin, and dystrophic nails; the right dorsalis pedis and posterior tibial pulses are not palpable; capillary refill is delayed.
4. Ankle-brachial index is 0.55 on the right (normal 0.9 to 1.3) and 0.85 on the left, consistent with moderate to severe arterial insufficiency.
5. Duplex ultrasound shows a long segment of stenosis in the superficial femoral artery with elevated peak systolic velocities.
6. After failure of conservative management with smoking cessation, exercise therapy, and antiplatelet agents, he undergoes percutaneous transluminal angioplasty with stent placement of the right superficial femoral artery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- PAD is atherosclerotic narrowing of peripheral arteries, most commonly involving the lower extremities.
- Classic intermittent claudication: cramping leg pain with exertion that resolves with rest; advanced disease causes rest pain, non-healing ulcers, and gangrene.
- ABI is the screening test: less than 0.9 is diagnostic; less than 0.4 indicates critical limb ischemia; greater than 1.3 suggests non-compressible calcified vessels (diabetes).
- Strong association with coronary and cerebrovascular disease; PAD patients have a major risk of cardiovascular events.
- Management: smoking cessation (essential), supervised exercise therapy, antiplatelet (aspirin or clopidogrel), statin, cilostazol for symptoms; revascularization for refractory or critical disease.
