CASE_ID: 305
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Constrictive Pericarditis

ALIASES:
chronic constrictive pericarditis
pericardial constriction

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 54-year-old man presents with several months of progressive abdominal swelling, lower extremity edema, and exertional dyspnea.
2. He had treated tuberculosis an abnormal value ago and reports a history of cardiac radiation for Hodgkin lymphoma a decade prior.
3. On exam, jugular venous pressure is elevated with prominent x and y descents and paradoxical rise during inspiration (deep rapid sign); a pericardial knock is heard in early diastole; hepatomegaly and ascites are present.
4. ECG shows low voltage with nonspecific T wave changes; chest X-ray reveals pericardial calcification along the cardiac silhouette.
5. Echocardiography demonstrates a thickened pericardium, septal bounce, and exaggerated respiratory variation in transvalvular flow.
6. Cardiac MRI confirms pericardial thickening and calcification, and right and left heart catheterization show equalization of diastolic pressures with a dip-and-plateau (square-root sign) pattern; he undergoes pericardiectomy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Constrictive pericarditis results from a thickened, fibrotic, or calcified pericardium that impairs ventricular filling.
- Causes: idiopathic/viral, prior cardiac surgery, mediastinal radiation, tuberculosis (most common cause worldwide), connective tissue disease.
- Distinctive features: Kussmaul sign (JVP rises with inspiration), pericardial knock, prominent x and y descents on JVP.
- Hemodynamics: equalization of end-diastolic pressures in all four chambers with dip-and-plateau (square root sign).
- Differs from restrictive cardiomyopathy by enhanced ventricular interdependence on echo and the response to therapy; treatment is pericardiectomy.

CASE_ID: 306
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Spontaneous Pneumothorax

ALIASES:
primary spontaneous pneumothorax
PSP
collapsed lung

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old tall thin man presents with sudden onset right-sided chest pain and shortness of breath that began while he was sitting at his desk.
2. He has no history of lung disease or trauma; he smokes a half pack of cigarettes daily and is otherwise healthy.
3. On exam, he is comfortable with mild tachypnea; breath sounds are diminished on the right with hyperresonance to percussion; the trachea is midline.
4. Vital signs are stable with SpO2 of 94% on room air.
5. Chest X-ray shows a visible visceral pleural line with absent lung markings beyond it on the right, with the lung 25% collapsed.
6. A small-bore chest tube is placed in the right pleural space with re-expansion of the lung on follow-up X-ray, and he is counseled on smoking cessation and the recurrence risk.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Primary spontaneous pneumothorax classically occurs in tall thin young men, often smokers, due to rupture of subpleural apical blebs.
- Secondary spontaneous pneumothorax occurs in patients with underlying lung disease (COPD, cystic fibrosis, infections).
- Symptoms: sudden ipsilateral chest pain and dyspnea; exam shows decreased breath sounds and hyperresonance.
- Distinguish from tension pneumothorax (hemodynamic instability, tracheal deviation, distended neck veins) which requires immediate needle decompression.
- Treatment: observation/oxygen for small (<2-3 cm); needle aspiration or chest tube for larger pneumothoraces; pleurodesis or surgery for recurrent cases.

CASE_ID: 307
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Lung Abscess

ALIASES:
pulmonary abscess
necrotizing pneumonia
anaerobic lung abscess

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 56-year-old man with a history of alcohol use disorder presents with several weeks of fever, productive cough, and weight loss of 12 pounds.
2. He reports the sputum has a foul-smelling, putrid quality; he had a witnessed episode of loss of consciousness after heavy drinking an abnormal value ago with possible vomiting.
3. On exam, he has poor dentition with multiple carious teeth and gingival disease; coarse crackles are heard over the right posterior upper lobe with dullness to percussion.
4. WBC is 18,200 with bandemia; CBC also shows mild anemia of chronic disease.
5. Chest CT reveals a 5 cm thick-walled cavitary lesion with an air-fluid level in the right superior segment of the lower lobe.
6. Bronchoscopy with bronchoalveolar lavage cultures grow multiple anaerobic organisms (Peptostreptococcus, Prevotella, Fusobacterium), and he is treated with prolonged courses of ampicillin-sulbactam with gradual resolution.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Lung abscess is a localized collection of pus within the lung parenchyma, typically due to aspiration of oropharyngeal contents.
- Risk factors: altered consciousness (alcohol intoxication, seizure, stroke, anesthesia), poor dentition or periodontal disease, dysphagia, GERD.
- Most common location: superior segment of the right lower lobe or posterior segment of the right upper lobe (gravity-dependent in supine position).
- Anaerobic bacteria (Peptostreptococcus, Prevotella, Bacteroides, Fusobacterium) are the most common pathogens; foul-smelling putrid sputum is classic.
- Treatment: prolonged antibiotics covering anaerobes (clindamycin, ampicillin-sulbactam, beta-lactam plus metronidazole); percutaneous drainage for nonresponders.

CASE_ID: 308
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Small Cell Lung Cancer

ALIASES:
SCLC
oat cell carcinoma
small cell carcinoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old woman presents with several weeks of cough, mild hemoptysis, fatigue, and a recent unexplained 15-pound weight loss.
2. She has a 50-pack-year smoking history; recently her face has appeared puffy and she has noticed prominent veins on her chest.
3. On exam, there is facial plethora and edema, distended neck and chest wall veins, and dullness to percussion at the right upper lobe; a mass is palpable in the supraclavicular fossa.
4. Labs show sodium of an abnormal value with serum osmolality of 258 mOsm/kg and urine osmolality of 480 mOsm/kg, consistent with SIADH.
5. Chest CT reveals a large central right hilar mass invading the mediastinum with compression of the superior vena cava and bulky mediastinal lymphadenopathy.
6. Endobronchial ultrasound-guided biopsy demonstrates small round blue cells with scant cytoplasm, nuclear molding, and crush artifact; chemotherapy with platinum plus etoposide is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Small cell lung cancer is a highly aggressive neuroendocrine malignancy strongly associated with smoking; typically arises centrally.
- Frequent paraneoplastic syndromes: SIADH (ectopic ADH), Cushing syndrome (ectopic ACTH), Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome (antibodies to voltage-gated calcium channels).
- Superior vena cava syndrome: facial/upper extremity edema, plethora, distended neck and chest wall veins from compression of SVC by central tumor.
- Histology: small round blue cells with scant cytoplasm, nuclear molding, high mitotic rate, neuroendocrine markers (chromogranin, synaptophysin).
- Staged as limited (one hemithorax, one radiation port) vs. extensive; treated with chemotherapy and radiation; very chemosensitive but high relapse rate.

CASE_ID: 309
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Allergic Bronchopulmonary Aspergillosis

ALIASES:
ABPA
hypersensitivity to Aspergillus

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman with cystic fibrosis presents with worsening wheezing, cough productive of brownish mucus plugs, and recurrent episodes of fever over the past 6 months.
2. Her asthma has become poorly controlled despite escalation of inhaled corticosteroids; she has had three pulmonary exacerbations in the past year.
3. On exam, she has diffuse wheezing and crackles with prolonged expiration.
4. Labs show peripheral eosinophilia (1,200 cells/microL) and a markedly elevated total serum IgE at 2,400 IU/mL; specific IgE and IgG to Aspergillus fumigatus are elevated, and the immediate skin prick test to Aspergillus is positive.
5. High-resolution CT of the chest reveals central bronchiectasis predominantly in the upper lobes with mucoid impaction and finger-in-glove opacities.
6. She is treated with systemic corticosteroids and oral itraconazole with marked improvement in symptoms and decline in serum IgE.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- ABPA is a hypersensitivity response to Aspergillus species colonizing the airways, predominantly in patients with asthma or cystic fibrosis.
- Classic criteria include asthma or CF, immediate skin reactivity or specific IgE to Aspergillus, markedly elevated total IgE, peripheral eosinophilia, and central bronchiectasis.
- Imaging: central bronchiectasis with mucoid impaction giving a finger-in-glove appearance; upper lobe predominance.
- Treatment: systemic corticosteroids are the mainstay; itraconazole or voriconazole as adjunct or steroid-sparing agent.
- Distinguish from invasive aspergillosis (immunocompromised hosts with tissue invasion) and aspergilloma (fungal ball in pre-existing cavity).

CASE_ID: 310
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hepatitis A

ALIASES:
HAV
hepatitis A virus infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old man presents with 1 week of fatigue, nausea, low-grade fever, and right upper quadrant discomfort, followed 5 days later by yellowing of the skin and dark urine.
2. He returned from a 3-week trip to rural Mexico an abnormal value ago, where he ate from local food stalls and drank unbottled water; he has no IV drug use or sexual risk factors.
3. On exam, he has scleral icterus and mild tender hepatomegaly; there is no encephalopathy or asterixis.
4. Labs show AST 1,840, ALT 2,210, total bilirubin an abnormal value (direct 6.1), alkaline phosphatase 220, and prolonged PT (15 sec); albumin is 3.6.
5. Hepatitis A IgM antibody is positive; HBsAg, anti-HBc IgM, and HCV antibody are negative.
6. He is managed supportively at home with rest and hydration; symptoms gradually resolve over 6 weeks with normalization of liver enzymes and complete clinical recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hepatitis A is a non-enveloped RNA picornavirus transmitted via the fecal-oral route, commonly through contaminated food or water.
- Self-limited acute hepatitis without chronic carriage; never causes cirrhosis or chronic infection.
- Anti-HAV IgM indicates acute infection; anti-HAV IgG indicates past infection or vaccination (immunity).
- Most patients recover fully within 2-6 weeks; rare fulminant hepatic failure (<1%) is more common in older adults and those with chronic liver disease.
- Prevention: vaccination (recommended for travelers, MSM, IDU, chronic liver disease), good hand hygiene, sanitation; post-exposure prophylaxis with vaccine or immunoglobulin.

CASE_ID: 311
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hepatitis B

ALIASES:
HBV
hepatitis B virus infection
chronic hepatitis B

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old immigrant from East Asia is found to have elevated liver enzymes on a routine pre-employment screen; he is asymptomatic.
2. His mother had liver cancer and a sister has chronic liver disease; he was born in a region with high endemic rates and was never vaccinated.
3. On exam, he is well-appearing; the liver is mildly enlarged with a firm edge; no jaundice, ascites, or signs of portal hypertension.
4. Labs show AST 78, ALT 92, total bilirubin normal, albumin normal, and platelet count 165,000; alpha-fetoprotein is 22 ng/mL.
5. Hepatitis B serology: HBsAg positive, anti-HBs negative, anti-HBc IgG positive, anti-HBc IgM negative, HBeAg positive, anti-HBe negative; HBV DNA viral load is 8 million IU/mL.
6. Liver biopsy reveals chronic hepatitis with moderate fibrosis (stage 2); he is started on tenofovir alafenamide with enrollment in regular HCC surveillance.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hepatitis B is a partially double-stranded DNA hepadnavirus transmitted via blood, sexual contact, and perinatal (most common worldwide).
- Serology key: HBsAg = active infection; anti-HBs = immunity (vaccine or recovery); anti-HBc IgM = acute, IgG = chronic or past; HBeAg = high infectivity/replication.
- Risk of chronicity is inversely related to age at infection: 90% in perinatal infection, <5% in adults.
- Chronic infection: increased risk of cirrhosis, hepatocellular carcinoma (can occur without cirrhosis, unlike most other causes), polyarteritis nodosa, membranous nephropathy.
- Treatment of chronic HBV: nucleos(t)ide analogs (tenofovir, entecavir); pegylated interferon in selected cases; HCC surveillance with US +/- AFP.

CASE_ID: 312
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Alcoholic Hepatitis

ALIASES:
alcohol-associated hepatitis
acute alcoholic hepatitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old man presents with 2 weeks of yellow eyes, low-grade fever, right upper quadrant pain, and abdominal swelling.
2. He drinks at least 8-10 alcoholic beverages daily and has continued this pattern for the past an abnormal value; his last drink was 2 days ago.
3. On exam, he has scleral icterus, tender hepatomegaly, spider angiomata, palmar erythema, and bilateral parotid enlargement; modest ascites is appreciable.
4. Labs show AST 220, ALT 90 (AST/ALT ratio greater than 2), total bilirubin an abnormal value, INR 1.8, albumin 2.4, and an elevated GGT; CBC reveals macrocytic anemia and thrombocytopenia.
5. Ultrasound shows hepatomegaly with fatty infiltration and modest ascites; viral hepatitis serologies and acetaminophen level are negative.
6. With a Maddrey discriminant function of 38, he is treated with prednisolone and supportive care including nutrition and B-vitamin repletion; he commits to alcohol cessation and begins outpatient counseling.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Alcoholic hepatitis is an acute inflammatory liver injury occurring on a background of chronic alcohol use, distinct from steatohepatitis or cirrhosis.
- Classic AST/ALT ratio greater than 2:1 (a memory aid is "scotch and toast" with AST elevated more than ALT); both rarely exceed 500.
- Other clues: macrocytosis (folate deficiency, direct alcohol effect on marrow), elevated GGT, thrombocytopenia (direct marrow toxicity).
- Severity assessment: Maddrey discriminant function (DF) or MELD score; severe disease (DF >32 or MELD >20) may benefit from prednisolone.
- Treatment: alcohol cessation is essential; nutrition support, thiamine and folate; pentoxifylline has fallen out of favor; severe nonresponders may be candidates for liver transplant.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 313
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Cirrhosis

ALIASES:
hepatic cirrhosis
end-stage liver disease
decompensated cirrhosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 56-year-old woman with chronic hepatitis C presents with increasing abdominal girth and confusion over the past week.
2. She has a long history of heavy alcohol use, which she stopped an abnormal value ago; she has had two prior episodes of variceal bleeding.
3. On exam, she has scleral icterus, spider angiomata, palmar erythema, gynecomastia, abdominal distension with shifting dullness, and asterixis on extended wrists; the spleen tip is palpable.
4. Labs show total bilirubin an abnormal value, albumin 2.6 g/dL, INR 1.9, platelet count 62,000, and an elevated ammonia level.
5. Right upper quadrant ultrasound reveals a small nodular liver with surface irregularity, splenomegaly, and ascites; portal vein is patent with reversed flow on Doppler.
6. Diagnostic paracentesis is consistent with portal hypertensive ascites (serum-ascites albumin gradient greater than 1.1), and she is started on lactulose, rifaximin, spironolactone, and propranolol with referral for liver transplant evaluation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Cirrhosis is irreversible fibrosis and nodular regeneration of the liver leading to portal hypertension and hepatic synthetic dysfunction.
- Common causes: chronic hepatitis B and C, alcohol-associated liver disease, MASH/NASH, autoimmune hepatitis, hereditary hemochromatosis, Wilson disease, alpha-1 antitrypsin deficiency, primary biliary cholangitis.
- Manifestations of portal hypertension: ascites, esophageal/gastric varices, splenomegaly, hepatic encephalopathy, caput medusae, hepatorenal syndrome, hepatopulmonary syndrome.
- Compensated cirrhosis can be asymptomatic; decompensation is defined by ascites, variceal bleeding, encephalopathy, or jaundice.
- MELD score predicts mortality and prioritizes liver transplant; nonselective beta blockers reduce variceal bleeding; spontaneous bacterial peritonitis is suspected when ascitic fluid PMN >250/microL.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 314
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hepatocellular Carcinoma

ALIASES:
HCC
primary liver cancer

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old man with longstanding compensated cirrhosis from chronic hepatitis B presents with 3 months of right upper quadrant discomfort and a 15-pound weight loss.
2. He has been adherent to tenofovir and has had no prior decompensation, but has missed his routine screening for an abnormal value.
3. On exam, he has stigmata of chronic liver disease and a firm palpable mass in the right upper quadrant; an arterial bruit is auscultated over the liver.
4. Labs show alpha-fetoprotein of 8,200 ng/mL; AST and ALT are mildly elevated; bilirubin and albumin are normal.
5. Multiphase contrast-enhanced CT of the abdomen demonstrates a 7 cm hepatic mass with arterial hyperenhancement and portal venous washout, plus tumor extension into the right portal vein.
6. The imaging features are diagnostic (LI-RADS 5) without need for biopsy; given portal vein invasion (BCLC stage C), he is offered systemic therapy with atezolizumab plus bevacizumab.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hepatocellular carcinoma is the most common primary liver malignancy; the major risk factor is cirrhosis from any cause.
- Hepatitis B can cause HCC even without cirrhosis (uniquely among hepatotropic viruses).
- AFP is a useful but imperfect tumor marker; not all HCCs produce AFP, and elevations occur in chronic hepatitis without cancer.
- Imaging diagnosis: arterial phase hyperenhancement with washout on portal venous or delayed phases in a cirrhotic liver is virtually diagnostic (LI-RADS 5).
- Surveillance with ultrasound (+/- AFP) every 6 months is recommended for at-risk patients; treatment options include resection, transplant, ablation, embolization (TACE), and systemic therapy (atezolizumab-bevacizumab).

CASE_ID: 315
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus

ALIASES:
T1DM
insulin-dependent diabetes
juvenile diabetes

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 14-year-old boy presents with 3 weeks of polyuria, polydipsia, and a 12-pound unintentional weight loss despite increased appetite.
2. He has a family history of autoimmune thyroid disease; he recently had a viral upper respiratory illness.
3. On exam, he is thin with mild dehydration; vital signs are stable; no acetone odor on breath; abdomen is soft and nontender.
4. Random plasma glucose is an abnormal value; HbA1c is 11.8%; urine ketones are small and urine glucose is 4+; arterial blood gas shows pH 7.36 with HCO3 of 22.
5. Anti-GAD-65, islet cell, and anti-insulin antibodies are positive; C-peptide is undetectable.
6. He is initiated on a basal-bolus insulin regimen with insulin glargine and lispro, started on a continuous glucose monitor, and receives extensive diabetes education with his family.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Type 1 diabetes results from autoimmune T cell-mediated destruction of pancreatic beta cells, leading to absolute insulin deficiency.
- Strongly associated with HLA-DR3 and HLA-DR4; classic autoantibodies: anti-GAD-65, islet cell antibodies, anti-insulin, anti-IA-2, anti-ZnT8.
- Classic presentation: polyuria, polydipsia, polyphagia, weight loss; may present with diabetic ketoacidosis.
- Low or undetectable C-peptide distinguishes T1DM from T2DM (which has normal or elevated C-peptide due to insulin resistance).
- Lifelong insulin replacement is required (basal-bolus regimens with rapid-acting and long-acting analogs, or continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion); associated with other autoimmune conditions.

CASE_ID: 316
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Congenital Adrenal Hyperplasia

ALIASES:
CAH
21-hydroxylase deficiency
classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A newborn female is evaluated for ambiguous genitalia noted at birth; she was born at term to a healthy mother after an uneventful pregnancy.
2. The genitalia appear masculinized with clitoromegaly and partial labial fusion; on day 7 of life she develops poor feeding, vomiting, and lethargy.
3. On exam, she is dehydrated and hypotensive with mottled skin; the labia majora are fused and rugated; no palpable gonads.
4. Labs show sodium an abnormal value, potassium an abnormal value, glucose an abnormal value, and metabolic acidosis; karyotype confirms 46,XX.
5. Serum 17-hydroxyprogesterone is markedly elevated; cortisol is low and ACTH is elevated; serum testosterone is elevated.
6. She is treated with IV fluids, dextrose, and hydrocortisone with rapid improvement; lifelong hydrocortisone and fludrocortisone are initiated, and reconstructive surgical planning is discussed with the family.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Classic congenital adrenal hyperplasia is most often due to 21-hydroxylase deficiency (CYP21A2 gene), autosomal recessive.
- The enzymatic block diverts precursors into androgen synthesis, with deficient cortisol and aldosterone.
- Female infants present with virilization (ambiguous genitalia); male infants are phenotypically normal at birth but develop salt-wasting crisis 1-2 weeks postnatally.
- Salt-wasting crisis: hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, hypovolemia, hypoglycemia, metabolic acidosis from aldosterone deficiency.
- Markedly elevated 17-hydroxyprogesterone is diagnostic; treatment is glucocorticoid replacement (hydrocortisone) with mineralocorticoid (fludrocortisone) for salt wasters; newborn screening is routine.

CASE_ID: 317
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Conn Syndrome

ALIASES:
primary hyperaldosteronism
aldosterone-producing adenoma
primary aldosteronism

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 44-year-old woman is referred for evaluation of difficult-to-control hypertension despite three antihypertensive agents.
2. She reports muscle weakness, cramping, polyuria, and frequent headaches; she has no flushing, palpitations, or sweating spells.
3. On exam, blood pressure is 168/an abnormal value with no peripheral edema; cardiovascular and abdominal exams are unremarkable.
4. Labs show potassium of an abnormal value (despite no diuretic use), sodium 144, bicarbonate 30, and a mild metabolic alkalosis.
5. Plasma aldosterone is 42 ng/dL with suppressed plasma renin activity; aldosterone-to-renin ratio exceeds 30, and a saline infusion test fails to suppress aldosterone.
6. CT of the adrenal glands reveals a 1.8 cm right adrenal adenoma, confirmed as the source by adrenal venous sampling; she undergoes laparoscopic right adrenalectomy with normalization of blood pressure and potassium.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Primary hyperaldosteronism is the most common cause of secondary hypertension; should be considered in resistant hypertension, hypokalemia, or early-onset hypertension.
- Two main causes: bilateral adrenal hyperplasia (most common, treated medically) and unilateral aldosterone-producing adenoma (Conn syndrome, treated surgically).
- Classic biochemistry: hypertension + hypokalemia + metabolic alkalosis (though many patients are normokalemic).
- Screening: aldosterone-to-renin ratio (high aldosterone, low/suppressed renin); confirmatory testing with saline suppression or oral salt loading.
- Treatment: surgical adrenalectomy for unilateral adenoma; mineralocorticoid receptor antagonists (spironolactone, eplerenone) for bilateral disease.

CASE_ID: 318
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hypoglycemia

ALIASES:
low blood sugar
neuroglycopenia
hypoglycemic episode

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old woman with type 2 diabetes presents to the ED after being found confused and diaphoretic at home by her daughter.
2. She takes glyburide and insulin; she had decreased oral intake over the past 24 hours due to nausea from a recent antibiotic prescription for a urinary tract infection.
3. On exam, she is diaphoretic, tremulous, and minimally responsive with a heart rate of 118 bpm and BP 144/an abnormal value; no focal neurologic deficit.
4. Point-of-care glucose is an abnormal value; after IV dextrose her mental status improves within minutes and she becomes alert and conversational.
5. Workup reveals an acute kidney injury contributing to delayed sulfonylurea clearance; insulin and sulfonylurea levels are not used here as the cause is iatrogenic.
6. Glyburide is discontinued and her insulin regimen is adjusted; she is admitted for observation given the risk of recurrent hypoglycemia from sulfonylurea, and her diabetes regimen is simplified.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Whipple triad establishes true hypoglycemia: symptoms consistent with hypoglycemia, documented low blood glucose, and resolution with glucose administration.
- Adrenergic (autonomic) symptoms: tremor, palpitations, diaphoresis, hunger; neuroglycopenic symptoms: confusion, irritability, seizure, coma.
- Common causes in diabetics: insulin or sulfonylurea overdose, missed meals, renal/hepatic failure, alcohol; in non-diabetics consider insulinoma, factitious, adrenal insufficiency, severe illness.
- Sulfonylurea-induced hypoglycemia can be prolonged and recurrent; admit for observation and consider octreotide (suppresses insulin release) in refractory cases.
- C-peptide is elevated in endogenous insulin excess (insulinoma, sulfonylurea) and suppressed in exogenous insulin administration (factitious).

CASE_ID: 319
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Vascular Dementia

ALIASES:
multi-infarct dementia
vascular cognitive impairment
post-stroke dementia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 78-year-old man is brought in by his wife who reports stepwise cognitive decline over the past 2 years with sudden worsening episodes followed by periods of relative stability.
2. He has long-standing hypertension, type 2 diabetes, hyperlipidemia, and a history of two prior strokes with residual right hemiparesis.
3. On exam, he has right-sided weakness with hyperreflexia and an extensor plantar response; gait is slow and shuffling with a wide base; affect is labile.
4. Cognitive testing reveals impairments in executive function and processing speed more than memory; MMSE is 21/30 with prominent deficits in attention and visuospatial domains.
5. Routine workup including TSH, B12, and RPR is unremarkable.
6. MRI of the brain demonstrates multiple lacunar infarcts in the basal ganglia and thalamus along with extensive periventricular white matter ischemic disease, and aggressive risk factor modification with antiplatelet therapy is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Vascular dementia is the second most common cause of dementia (after Alzheimer disease) and frequently coexists with AD as mixed dementia.
- Hallmarks distinguishing from AD: stepwise (not gradual) decline, prominent executive dysfunction (less memory impairment early), focal neurologic deficits, vascular risk factors.
- Risk factors: hypertension, diabetes, atrial fibrillation, smoking, prior stroke or TIA, hyperlipidemia.
- Imaging shows multiple infarcts (lacunar or cortical) and white matter ischemic changes.
- Treatment focuses on secondary stroke prevention (antiplatelets, statins, BP control, glycemic control); cholinesterase inhibitors may provide modest cognitive benefit.

CASE_ID: 320
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Lewy Body Dementia

ALIASES:
DLB
dementia with Lewy bodies
diffuse Lewy body disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 74-year-old man is brought in by his family for evaluation of fluctuating cognitive impairment that varies markedly from day to day and even hour to hour.
2. Over the past an abnormal value he has reported seeing small children and animals in his house that no one else sees, and he has been falling more frequently.
3. On exam, he has a resting tremor of his right hand, cogwheel rigidity, and bradykinesia; his gait is shuffling with reduced arm swing.
4. He acts out his dreams during sleep, sometimes punching the air and falling out of bed (REM sleep behavior disorder); his wife reports this began years before any cognitive symptoms.
5. He was prescribed haloperidol for agitation at another hospital and developed severe muscle rigidity and decreased responsiveness, which improved when the medication was stopped.
6. MRI shows generalized cortical atrophy without hippocampal predominance; DAT scan demonstrates reduced striatal dopamine transporter uptake, supporting the diagnosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Dementia with Lewy bodies is a synucleinopathy with cortical and subcortical Lewy bodies (alpha-synuclein aggregates).
- Core features: fluctuating cognition with marked variation, recurrent visual hallucinations, parkinsonism, REM sleep behavior disorder.
- Visual hallucinations are typically well-formed (people, animals, children) and occur early, in contrast to Alzheimer disease.
- Distinguish from Parkinson disease dementia by the 1-year rule: if cognitive symptoms precede or occur within 1 year of motor symptoms it is DLB; if motor symptoms long precede cognitive symptoms it is PD dementia.
- Severe neuroleptic sensitivity is characteristic and dangerous; avoid first-generation antipsychotics; cholinesterase inhibitors may improve cognitive and behavioral symptoms.

CASE_ID: 321
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Frontotemporal Dementia

ALIASES:
FTD
behavioral variant frontotemporal dementia
Pick disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old previously gentle accountant is brought in by his wife after his progressive personality changes have led to his being fired from his job.
2. He has become increasingly disinhibited, making inappropriate comments to strangers, overeating sweets, and showing little concern for family members who are upset.
3. On exam, he is apathetic and lacks insight into his behavior; his memory and orientation are relatively preserved; he exhibits utilization behavior and perseveration.
4. Neuropsychological testing reveals prominent deficits in executive function and social cognition with relative sparing of memory and visuospatial skills.
5. CSF biomarkers do not show the Alzheimer profile; thyroid, B12, RPR, and HIV testing are unrevealing.
6. MRI of the brain demonstrates striking bilateral frontal and anterior temporal lobe atrophy with sparing of the posterior cortex, and a presumptive diagnosis of behavioral variant FTD is made.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Frontotemporal dementia is the most common cause of dementia in patients under age 60; presents earlier than Alzheimer disease.
- Behavioral variant (most common subtype): early changes in personality and social conduct, disinhibition, apathy, loss of empathy, hyperorality/dietary changes, compulsive behavior.
- Primary progressive aphasia variants: semantic (loss of word meaning) and nonfluent/agrammatic (effortful speech with grammatical errors).
- Pathology: tau-positive (Pick bodies) or TDP-43 inclusions; mutations in MAPT, GRN, C9orf72 in familial forms.
- Imaging: frontal and anterior temporal atrophy in striking contrast to AD (which preferentially affects hippocampus and parietal cortex).

CASE_ID: 322
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Status Epilepticus

ALIASES:
SE
generalized convulsive status epilepticus
prolonged seizure

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old woman with epilepsy is brought to the ED by paramedics after a witnessed seizure that began 30 minutes ago and has not stopped despite ongoing convulsive activity.
2. She ran out of her levetiracetam 3 days ago and has not been able to refill it.
3. On exam, she has rhythmic generalized tonic-clonic movements, eyes deviated to the right, frothing at the mouth, and is unresponsive; oxygen saturation has dropped to 88%.
4. Fingerstick glucose is an abnormal value; vital signs show tachycardia and hyperthermia at 39.2°C.
5. IV lorazepam 4 mg is given without cessation; a second dose is administered and she is loaded with IV levetiracetam, after which her seizures terminate.
6. She is intubated for airway protection given her postictal state, and EEG shows no further electrographic seizures; her antiepileptic regimen is resumed and she is discharged after counseling on medication adherence.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Status epilepticus is defined as a single seizure lasting more than 5 minutes or recurrent seizures without return to baseline between them.
- It is a neurologic emergency: prolonged seizures cause neuronal injury, rhabdomyolysis, aspiration, hyperthermia, and metabolic acidosis.
- Common causes: anticonvulsant nonadherence, alcohol withdrawal, hypoglycemia, hyponatremia, stroke, CNS infection, drug toxicity, brain tumor.
- First-line treatment: IV benzodiazepines (lorazepam preferred); second-line: IV levetiracetam, fosphenytoin, or valproate; refractory cases require general anesthesia (midazolam, propofol, pentobarbital) with EEG monitoring.
- Check finger-stick glucose immediately and give thiamine before glucose if alcohol use is suspected.

CASE_ID: 323
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Membranoproliferative Glomerulonephritis

ALIASES:
MPGN
mesangiocapillary glomerulonephritis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old man with a history of injection drug use presents with frothy urine, leg swelling, and a 1-month history of fatigue.
2. He has known hepatitis C infection that has been untreated; he denies hematuria but acknowledges occasional joint pains and a purpuric rash on his legs.
3. On exam, he has palpable purpura on the lower extremities and bilateral pitting pretibial edema; blood pressure is 158/an abnormal value.
4. Urinalysis shows hematuria with dysmorphic red blood cells and red cell casts and 3+ proteinuria; 24-hour protein is 4.2 g; serum creatinine is an abnormal value.
5. Serum C3 and C4 are both low; rheumatoid factor is positive and cryoglobulins are detectable; HCV RNA is positive.
6. Renal biopsy shows a lobular accentuation of glomeruli with subendothelial deposits and double contours of the basement membrane (tram tracks) on silver stain; he is treated with direct-acting antiviral therapy for HCV with subsequent improvement in renal function.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Membranoproliferative glomerulonephritis presents with a mixed nephritic-nephrotic picture and low complement levels.
- Type I (immune complex MPGN): associated with chronic infections (HCV most common, also HBV, endocarditis), autoimmune disease (SLE), and monoclonal gammopathy; classically with mixed cryoglobulinemia in HCV.
- Type II (dense deposit disease, now a C3 glomerulopathy): associated with C3 nephritic factor (an autoantibody stabilizing C3 convertase).
- Light microscopy: lobular hypercellularity; silver stain shows tram-track double contour of the glomerular basement membrane from mesangial cell interposition.
- Treatment targets the underlying cause; immunosuppression for idiopathic disease.

CASE_ID: 324
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Fanconi Syndrome

ALIASES:
proximal renal tubular dysfunction
generalized proximal tubular dysfunction
de Toni-Fanconi syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 9-year-old boy is brought in for evaluation of poor growth, polyuria, and bone pain that has been worsening over the past year.
2. His family is Ashkenazi Jewish; he was diagnosed with cystinosis at age 2 but has been inconsistently treated.
3. On exam, he is below the 3rd percentile for height and weight; he has rachitic changes at the wrists and bowing of his legs; corneal slit-lamp exam shows fine crystalline deposits.
4. Labs show hypokalemia (potassium 2.9), hypophosphatemia (an abnormal value), normal anion gap metabolic acidosis (HCO3 16) with low serum bicarbonate, and elevated alkaline phosphatase; serum creatinine is mildly elevated.
5. Urine studies show glucosuria with normal serum glucose, generalized aminoaciduria, phosphaturia, and a urine pH inappropriately above 5.5 in the setting of acidemia.
6. Confirmation of impaired proximal tubular reabsorption supports the diagnosis; he is started on aggressive electrolyte and bicarbonate replacement, vitamin D and phosphate supplementation, and cysteamine therapy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Fanconi syndrome is a generalized defect of proximal tubular reabsorption causing urinary loss of bicarbonate, glucose, amino acids, phosphate, urate, and potassium.
- Findings: type 2 (proximal) renal tubular acidosis, glucosuria with normal blood glucose, aminoaciduria, hypophosphatemia, hypokalemia, hypouricemia.
- Causes: in children, inherited (cystinosis is most common, Wilson disease, Lowe syndrome, hereditary fructose intolerance, galactosemia, tyrosinemia); in adults, acquired (multiple myeloma with light chain disease, tenofovir, ifosfamide, valproate, heavy metals).
- Hypophosphatemia from urinary phosphate wasting causes rickets in children and osteomalacia in adults.
- Treatment is supportive: replace bicarbonate, potassium, phosphate, vitamin D; cysteamine for cystinosis.

CASE_ID: 325
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hypocalcemia

ALIASES:
low calcium
hypocalcemic tetany

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 56-year-old woman presents to the ED 24 hours after total thyroidectomy for differentiated thyroid cancer with perioral tingling and cramping in her hands and feet.
2. She is also describing twitching of her face when she chews and a tight sensation in her throat.
3. On exam, tapping over the facial nerve produces ipsilateral facial contraction (Chvostek sign); inflating a blood pressure cuff above systolic pressure for 3 minutes elicits carpal spasm (Trousseau sign).
4. Labs show total calcium an abnormal value, ionized calcium 0.84 mmol/L, magnesium 1.8, phosphorus an abnormal value, and intact PTH less than 5 pg/mL (markedly low); albumin is normal.
5. ECG shows prolongation of the QT interval to 510 ms.
6. She is treated with IV calcium gluconate followed by oral calcium carbonate and calcitriol; PTH and calcium are monitored over weeks as her residual parathyroid tissue does not recover, and she remains on lifelong replacement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hypocalcemia can be ionized or related to a low albumin level; correct total calcium for albumin or measure ionized calcium directly.
- Symptoms: paresthesias (perioral and acral), muscle cramps, tetany, laryngospasm, seizures, prolonged QT interval, hypotension.
- Chvostek sign: facial muscle contraction on tapping over CN VII; Trousseau sign: carpal spasm after BP cuff inflation (more sensitive and specific).
- Common causes: hypoparathyroidism (post-surgical is the most common iatrogenic cause), severe hypomagnesemia (impairs PTH secretion and action), vitamin D deficiency, chronic kidney disease, acute pancreatitis, blood transfusion (citrate).
- Treatment: IV calcium for severe or symptomatic hypocalcemia; oral calcium and active vitamin D (calcitriol) for chronic management; replace magnesium if low.

CASE_ID: 326
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Myeloid Leukemia

ALIASES:
AML
acute myelogenous leukemia
acute nonlymphocytic leukemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man presents with several weeks of progressive fatigue, easy bruising, bleeding gums, and a recent febrile illness.
2. He has a history of myelodysplastic syndrome diagnosed an abnormal value ago and was exposed to benzene during his earlier career as a chemical worker.
3. On exam, he is pale with diffuse petechiae on the lower extremities and ecchymoses on his arms; gingival hypertrophy is noted; no significant lymphadenopathy and only mild splenomegaly.
4. CBC shows WBC of 64,000 with 78% blasts, hemoglobin 7.2 g/dL, and platelet count 18,000; peripheral smear demonstrates large blasts containing pink-red needle-like cytoplasmic inclusions (Auer rods).
5. Bone marrow aspirate is markedly hypercellular with greater than 60% myeloblasts; flow cytometry confirms myeloid lineage (MPO, CD13, CD33 positive); cytogenetics reveals a complex karyotype with -7.
6. The diagnosis is AML evolving from MDS; given his age and high-risk cytogenetics, he is initiated on azacitidine plus venetoclax with consideration of allogeneic stem cell transplantation in remission.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- AML is a clonal proliferation of myeloid blasts with maturation arrest; diagnosis requires >=20% blasts in the bone marrow or peripheral blood (or specific genetic abnormalities).
- Risk factors: prior MDS or MPN, prior chemotherapy/radiation, benzene exposure, Down syndrome (megakaryoblastic AML), Fanconi anemia.
- Auer rods (rod-shaped azurophilic granules) are pathognomonic of AML and are particularly prominent in acute promyelocytic leukemia (APL, M3).
- APL (t(15;17), PML-RARA fusion) presents with DIC and is treated with all-trans retinoic acid (ATRA) and arsenic trioxide; highly curable.
- General treatment for AML: induction chemotherapy (cytarabine + anthracycline, "7+3") for fit patients, less intensive regimens (azacitidine + venetoclax) for older or unfit; allogeneic stem cell transplant for high-risk disease.

CASE_ID: 327
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Megaloblastic Anemia

ALIASES:
B12 deficiency anemia
pernicious anemia
folate deficiency anemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old woman presents with fatigue, paresthesias in her hands and feet, and unsteadiness on her feet over the past several months.
2. She has a long-standing history of autoimmune hypothyroidism and vitiligo; she follows a strict vegan diet and takes no supplements.
3. On exam, she has glossitis with a beefy red tongue, decreased proprioception and vibration sense in the lower extremities, and a positive Romberg sign with a wide-based gait.
4. CBC shows hemoglobin 9.6 g/dL with MCV 118 fL; peripheral smear demonstrates hypersegmented neutrophils (>5 lobes) and oval macrocytes; LDH is markedly elevated and haptoglobin is low (intramedullary hemolysis).
5. Serum vitamin B12 level is 92 pg/mL (low); methylmalonic acid and homocysteine are both elevated; intrinsic factor and parietal cell antibodies are positive.
6. She is started on intramuscular cyanocobalamin loading doses followed by monthly maintenance; her hematologic and neurologic deficits gradually improve.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Megaloblastic anemia results from impaired DNA synthesis due to vitamin B12 or folate deficiency, with normal cytoplasmic maturation, producing large RBCs.
- B12 deficiency: pernicious anemia (autoimmune destruction of gastric parietal cells, lack of intrinsic factor), terminal ileal disease (Crohn, resection), strict vegan diet, Diphyllobothrium latum, metformin, PPIs.
- Folate deficiency: dietary deficiency (most common), alcoholism, pregnancy/lactation, hemolysis, methotrexate, phenytoin, trimethoprim.
- Both produce macrocytic anemia and hypersegmented neutrophils; only B12 deficiency causes neurologic symptoms (subacute combined degeneration affecting dorsal columns and lateral corticospinal tracts).
- Methylmalonic acid is elevated only in B12 deficiency; homocysteine is elevated in both; replace B12 BEFORE folate to avoid worsening neurologic damage.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 328
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Anemia of Chronic Disease

ALIASES:
anemia of inflammation
anemia of chronic inflammation

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old woman with long-standing rheumatoid arthritis presents for routine follow-up; she reports baseline fatigue without acute change.
2. Her arthritis is moderately controlled on methotrexate and a TNF inhibitor; she has no history of GI bleeding, heavy menses, or recent surgery.
3. On exam, she has chronic synovitis of the metacarpophalangeal and proximal interphalangeal joints; mucous membranes are mildly pale; no hepatosplenomegaly.
4. CBC shows hemoglobin 10.2 g/dL with MCV 81 fL (normocytic to mildly microcytic), normal WBC and platelets; reticulocyte count is inappropriately low.
5. Iron studies show low serum iron, low TIBC, low transferrin saturation but normal-to-elevated ferritin at 220 ng/mL; CRP is elevated at 18 mg/L.
6. Inflammatory markers and disease activity are addressed; iron is not supplemented as the workup excludes true iron deficiency, and her hemoglobin remains stable.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Anemia of chronic disease (now often called anemia of inflammation) is the second most common anemia after iron deficiency.
- Mechanism: inflammatory cytokines (IL-6) increase hepcidin, which blocks iron absorption and traps iron in macrophages, reducing erythropoiesis.
- Lab pattern: low serum iron, LOW TIBC (vs. high in iron deficiency), low transferrin saturation, NORMAL or HIGH ferritin (acute phase reactant).
- Usually normocytic but can be mildly microcytic; reticulocyte count is low (hypoproliferative).
- Treatment is of the underlying disease; ESAs in selected refractory cases (e.g., CKD); avoid unnecessary iron supplementation if true deficiency is excluded.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 329
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Sideroblastic Anemia

ALIASES:
ringed sideroblast anemia
sideroblastic syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man presents with progressive fatigue and dyspnea on exertion that has worsened over 2 months.
2. He has a history of alcohol use disorder and is on isoniazid for latent tuberculosis; he takes no vitamin supplements.
3. On exam, he has conjunctival pallor; the remainder of the exam is unremarkable; there is no hepatosplenomegaly.
4. CBC shows hemoglobin 8.4 g/dL with MCV 72 fL (microcytic) and a wide RDW; peripheral smear shows a dimorphic population of microcytic hypochromic and normocytic red cells with basophilic stippling.
5. Iron studies reveal high serum iron, high transferrin saturation, high ferritin, and a low to normal TIBC, distinguishing this from iron deficiency.
6. Bone marrow biopsy with Prussian blue stain reveals more than 15% erythroid precursors with iron-laden mitochondria forming a ring around the nucleus (ringed sideroblasts); his isoniazid is supplemented with pyridoxine and he commits to alcohol cessation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Sideroblastic anemia results from impaired heme synthesis with mitochondrial iron loading, producing ringed sideroblasts in the bone marrow.
- Hereditary X-linked sideroblastic anemia: ALAS2 gene mutation (defective ALA synthase, the first and rate-limiting enzyme of heme synthesis).
- Acquired causes: alcohol (most common reversible cause), lead poisoning, isoniazid (B6 antagonist), chloramphenicol, copper deficiency, MDS (clonal sideroblastic anemia).
- Lab clues: microcytic anemia with iron overload pattern (high iron, high ferritin) and dimorphic RBC population on smear; basophilic stippling.
- Treatment: pyridoxine (B6) for hereditary and INH-induced forms; address underlying cause; transfusions and iron chelation in chronic cases.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 330
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Renal Cell Carcinoma

ALIASES:
RCC
clear cell renal carcinoma
hypernephroma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man presents with intermittent painless blood in his urine over the past 2 months and dull left flank discomfort.
2. He has a 40-pack-year smoking history, obesity, and long-standing hypertension; he reports a 12-pound unintentional weight loss and recurrent low-grade fevers.
3. On exam, a left-sided varicocele is noted that does not decompress when the patient is supine, raising concern for venous obstruction.
4. Labs show hematuria on urinalysis, hemoglobin 17.8 g/dL (polycythemia), elevated calcium of an abnormal value, and elevated alkaline phosphatase; ESR is high.
5. CT of the abdomen and pelvis demonstrates a heterogeneous 8 cm enhancing mass arising from the upper pole of the left kidney with extension of tumor thrombus into the left renal vein and inferior vena cava.
6. He undergoes radical left nephrectomy with IVC thrombectomy; pathology confirms clear cell renal cell carcinoma, and he is started on adjuvant pembrolizumab.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Renal cell carcinoma is the most common primary kidney cancer in adults, arising from proximal tubular epithelium; clear cell is the most common subtype.
- Risk factors: smoking (most important), obesity, hypertension, acquired cystic kidney disease (dialysis patients), von Hippel-Lindau disease, hereditary papillary RCC, tuberous sclerosis.
- Classic triad (only present in 10%): flank pain, hematuria, palpable abdominal mass; most are now found incidentally on imaging.
- Paraneoplastic syndromes: erythrocytosis (ectopic EPO), hypercalcemia (PTHrP), hypertension (renin), Stauffer syndrome (cholestasis without metastasis), fever, eosinophilia.
- Left-sided varicocele that does not decompress when supine suggests left renal vein obstruction by tumor; surgery is the main treatment, with checkpoint inhibitors and TKIs for advanced/metastatic disease.

CASE_ID: 331
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Colorectal Cancer

ALIASES:
colon cancer
CRC
adenocarcinoma of the colon

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 66-year-old man presents with several months of fatigue, decreased exercise tolerance, and a recent 10-pound unintentional weight loss.
2. He has a family history of his father dying of a similar disease at age 70; he has not had any screening colonoscopy.
3. On exam, he has conjunctival pallor; abdominal exam reveals no palpable masses or hepatomegaly; rectal exam reveals brown guaiac-positive stool without palpable masses.
4. Labs show iron deficiency anemia with hemoglobin 8.4 g/dL, MCV 72, low ferritin, and elevated CEA at 84 ng/mL.
5. Colonoscopy reveals a circumferential ulcerated mass at the splenic flexure causing partial luminal narrowing; biopsies confirm moderately differentiated adenocarcinoma.
6. Staging CT shows three hepatic metastases without other distant disease, and he undergoes laparoscopic left hemicolectomy followed by FOLFOX chemotherapy; KRAS, NRAS, and BRAF testing guide targeted therapy decisions.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Colorectal cancer is among the most common cancers and a leading cause of cancer mortality; arises mostly from adenomatous polyps via the adenoma-carcinoma sequence (APC -> KRAS -> p53).
- Right-sided lesions: iron deficiency anemia, occult blood loss, fatigue; left-sided lesions: obstruction, change in bowel habits, hematochezia.
- Risk factors: age, family history, IBD (especially long-standing UC), Lynch syndrome (HNPCC), familial adenomatous polyposis (APC mutation), diet (red/processed meat), smoking, obesity.
- CEA is useful for monitoring recurrence after treatment but not for screening (low sensitivity/specificity).
- Screening starts at age 45 for average-risk adults: colonoscopy every 10 years, FIT yearly, FIT-DNA every 3 years, or CT colonography every 5 years.

CASE_ID: 332
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Bladder Cancer

ALIASES:
urothelial carcinoma
transitional cell carcinoma of the bladder
TCC

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man presents with 2 weeks of painless visible blood in his urine without dysuria or fever.
2. He has a 50-pack-year smoking history and worked for an abnormal value in a dye manufacturing plant; he denies prior trauma or kidney stones.
3. On exam, he is afebrile; abdomen is soft without palpable masses; rectal exam reveals a small benign-feeling prostate.
4. Urinalysis confirms gross hematuria with rare red cell casts and no proteinuria; urine culture is negative; serum creatinine is normal.
5. CT urogram demonstrates a 2.8 cm enhancing soft tissue mass arising from the right lateral wall of the bladder.
6. Cystoscopy reveals a papillary lesion that is biopsied, and pathology shows high-grade urothelial carcinoma invading the lamina propria but not the muscularis propria (T1); he undergoes transurethral resection and intravesical BCG therapy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Urothelial (transitional cell) carcinoma is the most common bladder cancer in developed countries; squamous cell carcinoma is associated with chronic inflammation/infection (Schistosoma haematobium worldwide, chronic catheterization).
- Painless gross hematuria in an older smoker is bladder cancer until proven otherwise.
- Risk factors: smoking (#1, accounts for ~50%), occupational exposure to aromatic amines (dye, rubber, leather, paint), cyclophosphamide, pelvic radiation, chronic UTI/stones.
- Workup: cystoscopy with biopsy (gold standard), CT urogram, urine cytology; staging guides treatment.
- Treatment: TURBT +/- intravesical BCG for non-muscle invasive; cystectomy +/- chemotherapy for muscle-invasive; immunotherapy or chemotherapy for metastatic.

CASE_ID: 333
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Osteosarcoma

ALIASES:
osteogenic sarcoma
primary bone sarcoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 15-year-old boy presents with several weeks of progressive pain and swelling in his right knee that started without an inciting injury.
2. The pain is worse at night and has been waking him from sleep; he plays soccer but has not had any specific trauma; he has had a recent growth spurt.
3. On exam, there is firm tender swelling over the distal right femur with warmth and limited knee flexion; no overlying skin changes or fluctuance.
4. Plain X-ray of the right femur shows a destructive mixed lytic and sclerotic lesion in the metaphysis with periosteal elevation (Codman triangle) and a sunburst pattern of new bone formation.
5. Labs show alkaline phosphatase 680 U/L and LDH 720 U/L; chest CT reveals two pulmonary nodules concerning for metastasis.
6. Open biopsy demonstrates malignant osteoid produced by anaplastic spindle cells, confirming the diagnosis; he begins neoadjuvant chemotherapy (MAP regimen) followed by limb-sparing surgical resection.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Osteosarcoma is the most common primary bone malignancy in adolescents/young adults, with a peak incidence around the growth spurt.
- Classic locations: metaphysis of long bones around the knee (distal femur, proximal tibia) and proximal humerus.
- Radiographic features: lytic/sclerotic destructive lesion, periosteal reaction with Codman triangle (elevation of periosteum), sunburst pattern from spiculated new bone.
- Risk factors: rapid bone growth (adolescent peak), hereditary retinoblastoma (RB1 mutation), Li-Fraumeni syndrome (TP53), Paget disease (in older adults), prior radiation.
- Treatment: neoadjuvant chemotherapy (MAP: methotrexate, doxorubicin, cisplatin) followed by limb-sparing resection or amputation, then adjuvant chemotherapy; pulmonary metastasectomy when isolated.

CASE_ID: 334
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Ewing Sarcoma

ALIASES:
Ewing's sarcoma
PNET of bone
EWSR1-FLI1 sarcoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 12-year-old boy presents with several weeks of worsening pain and swelling in the mid-shaft of his right femur, low-grade fevers, and fatigue.
2. He has had no specific injury; the pain is constant and worse at night; he reports a 5-pound unintentional weight loss.
3. On exam, there is tender warm swelling over the right thigh midshaft with mild overlying erythema; a soft tissue mass is palpable.
4. CBC shows leukocytosis with mild anemia; ESR is an abnormal value/hr and LDH is an abnormal value.
5. Plain X-ray of the right femur reveals a permeative lytic lesion in the diaphysis with a laminated "onion-skin" periosteal reaction and an associated soft tissue mass; MRI confirms a large soft tissue component.
6. Biopsy shows small round blue cells positive for CD99 (MIC2) on immunohistochemistry; FISH identifies the t(11;22) translocation producing the EWSR1-FLI1 fusion gene, and multimodal therapy with chemotherapy, surgery, and radiation is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Ewing sarcoma is the second most common primary bone malignancy in children and adolescents.
- Classic location: diaphysis of long bones (especially femur) and flat bones (pelvis, ribs); contrasts with osteosarcoma which favors metaphysis.
- Radiographic findings: lytic permeative bone destruction with characteristic "onion-skin" laminated periosteal reaction; often a prominent soft tissue mass.
- Histology: small round blue cell tumor (differential includes lymphoma, rhabdomyosarcoma, neuroblastoma, Wilms); CD99 positive; defined by t(11;22) EWSR1-FLI1 fusion.
- Treatment: chemotherapy plus local control (surgery and/or radiation); highly responsive but high-risk cases require aggressive multimodal therapy.

CASE_ID: 335
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Rhabdomyolysis

ALIASES:
muscle breakdown syndrome
exertional rhabdomyolysis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old man is brought to the ED after collapsing during the final mile of a marathon on a hot day.
2. He had been training for months and reports diffuse severe muscle pain and very dark, almost tea-colored urine in the past few hours.
3. On exam, he is dehydrated and tachycardic; his thigh and back muscles are exquisitely tender and feel firm; he has scant urine output despite IV fluids.
4. Labs show creatine kinase of 48,an abnormal value, AST 1,200, ALT 280, potassium an abnormal value, phosphate 7.2, calcium 7.1 (low), and creatinine an abnormal value (baseline 0.9); urinalysis shows large blood on dipstick with no red blood cells on microscopy.
5. ECG demonstrates peaked T waves with mild QRS widening.
6. He is treated with aggressive IV normal saline targeting urine output of 200-300 mL/hr, calcium gluconate for hyperkalemic ECG changes, insulin-dextrose; his CK trends down and renal function gradually normalizes over a week.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Rhabdomyolysis is the breakdown of skeletal muscle with release of intracellular contents (myoglobin, K+, phosphate, CK, LDH) into the circulation.
- Causes: crush injury, prolonged immobilization, intense exertion, seizures, drugs/toxins (statins, cocaine, alcohol, neuroleptic malignant syndrome), hyperthermia, infections.
- Classic findings: muscle pain, weakness, dark "cola-colored" or "tea-colored" urine, markedly elevated CK (often >10,000 to 100,000+).
- Key labs: elevated CK, hyperkalemia, hyperphosphatemia, hypocalcemia (binds to released phosphate), AKI; dipstick positive for blood but no RBCs on microscopy (myoglobin gives false-positive heme reaction).
- Treatment: aggressive IV crystalloid hydration to maintain urine output, treat hyperkalemia, monitor for compartment syndrome and DIC; alkalinization with bicarbonate is controversial.

CASE_ID: 336
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Giant Cell Arteritis

ALIASES:
GCA
temporal arteritis
cranial arteritis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 74-year-old woman presents with 3 weeks of new constant right-sided headache, jaw pain when chewing tough foods, and brief episodes of right-sided vision loss this morning.
2. She has had stiffness and pain in her shoulders and hips most mornings for the past an abnormal value, with significant difficulty getting out of bed; she has lost 6 pounds.
3. On exam, the right temporal artery is prominent, tender, and pulseless; visual acuity is 20/40 in the affected eye with a relative afferent pupillary defect; bilateral shoulder girdle tenderness with reduced range of motion.
4. Labs show ESR of an abnormal value/hr, CRP of 84 mg/L, and a mild normocytic anemia; alkaline phosphatase is mildly elevated.
5. Ophthalmologic exam reveals optic disc pallor and edema concerning for anterior ischemic optic neuropathy.
6. High-dose IV methylprednisolone is started immediately without waiting for biopsy; temporal artery biopsy performed within 2 weeks shows granulomatous inflammation with multinucleated giant cells and intimal thickening, confirming the diagnosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Giant cell arteritis is a large- and medium-vessel granulomatous vasculitis affecting the cranial branches of the external carotid artery in patients over 50.
- Classic features: new-onset temporal headache, jaw claudication, scalp tenderness, visual symptoms (amaurosis fugax, AION), constitutional symptoms.
- Strongly associated with polymyalgia rheumatica (proximal muscle stiffness/pain, ~50% of GCA patients have PMR).
- ESR is classically very high (>50, often >100); CRP elevated; temporal artery biopsy is gold standard (skip lesions, take 1-2 cm specimen).
- Treatment with corticosteroids should NOT be delayed for biopsy if GCA is suspected (vision loss is irreversible); tocilizumab is steroid-sparing.

CASE_ID: 337
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Granulomatosis with Polyangiitis

ALIASES:
GPA
Wegener granulomatosis
c-ANCA vasculitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old man presents with months of persistent sinusitis, recurrent epistaxis, chronic ear pain, and now hemoptysis.
2. He also reports fatigue, arthralgias, and frothy urine; his symptoms have failed multiple courses of antibiotics.
3. On exam, the nasal septum has a saddle deformity from cartilage destruction; sclerae are injected; lungs have scattered crackles; mild lower extremity edema is present.
4. Urinalysis reveals hematuria with red blood cell casts and 2+ proteinuria; serum creatinine is an abnormal value (baseline 0.9); CBC shows leukocytosis and normocytic anemia.
5. Chest CT demonstrates multiple bilateral pulmonary nodules with cavitation; c-ANCA (anti-PR3) is positive at a high titer.
6. Renal biopsy reveals pauci-immune crescentic glomerulonephritis without significant immune complex deposition; he is treated with high-dose corticosteroids plus rituximab with disease remission.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Granulomatosis with polyangiitis is a small- and medium-vessel vasculitis with necrotizing granulomatous inflammation.
- Classic triad: upper airway (sinusitis, nasal ulcers, saddle nose), lower airway (cavitary lung nodules, hemoptysis), and renal involvement (pauci-immune glomerulonephritis).
- c-ANCA (anti-proteinase 3) is positive in >90% of cases with active systemic disease.
- Renal biopsy: pauci-immune (scant or no immune complex deposits) crescentic glomerulonephritis.
- Treatment: induction with high-dose corticosteroids plus rituximab or cyclophosphamide; maintenance with rituximab, azathioprine, or methotrexate.

CASE_ID: 338
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Eosinophilic Granulomatosis with Polyangiitis

ALIASES:
EGPA
Churg-Strauss syndrome
allergic granulomatosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old woman with poorly controlled asthma presents with 6 weeks of worsening dyspnea, fatigue, foot drop, and a painful purpuric rash on her lower extremities.
2. She has a long history of allergic rhinitis and nasal polyps; her asthma began in her 40s and has been escalating over the past year.
3. On exam, she has expiratory wheezing, palpable purpura on both legs, and right foot drop with diminished pinprick sensation in the L5 distribution; sinuses are tender.
4. Labs show marked peripheral eosinophilia (4,200 cells/microL), elevated ESR and CRP, and mild elevation in creatinine.
5. p-ANCA (anti-MPO) is positive; chest CT reveals migratory ground-glass opacities and patchy consolidations.
6. Sural nerve biopsy demonstrates necrotizing vasculitis with extravascular eosinophil-rich granulomas, confirming the diagnosis; she is started on high-dose corticosteroids plus mepolizumab.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Eosinophilic granulomatosis with polyangiitis is a small/medium-vessel vasculitis associated with asthma and eosinophilia.
- Three sequential phases: prodromal (asthma, allergic rhinitis), eosinophilic (peripheral eosinophilia, eosinophilic infiltrates), vasculitic (systemic vasculitis).
- Mononeuritis multiplex (e.g., foot drop) is a hallmark; cardiac involvement (myocarditis) is the leading cause of death.
- p-ANCA (anti-MPO) is positive in ~40-60%; peripheral eosinophilia is universal; biopsy shows eosinophil-rich vasculitis and extravascular granulomas.
- Treatment: corticosteroids; cyclophosphamide for severe organ involvement; mepolizumab (anti-IL-5) for relapsing/refractory disease.

CASE_ID: 339
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Takayasu Arteritis

ALIASES:
pulseless disease
aortic arch syndrome
Takayasu's arteritis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old Japanese woman presents with several months of fatigue, low-grade fevers, weight loss, and intermittent left arm pain when carrying groceries.
2. She has noticed lightheadedness when standing quickly and that her left arm feels cool compared to the right.
3. On exam, the left radial pulse is markedly diminished; blood pressure is 78/an abnormal value in the left arm and 142/an abnormal value in the right; a vascular bruit is heard over the left carotid artery.
4. Labs show ESR of an abnormal value/hr, CRP of 32 mg/L, and a mild normocytic anemia.
5. MR angiography reveals long-segment circumferential wall thickening and luminal narrowing of the aortic arch with stenosis of the left subclavian and left common carotid arteries.
6. She is started on high-dose corticosteroids with methotrexate as a steroid-sparing agent; serial imaging is planned to monitor for progression and possible revascularization.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Takayasu arteritis is a large-vessel granulomatous vasculitis affecting the aorta and its major branches, predominantly in young Asian women under 40.
- Classic features: pulse deficits ("pulseless disease"), blood pressure asymmetry between arms (>10 mmHg), arterial bruits, claudication of the extremities, vascular hypertension.
- Two phases: prepulseless (systemic inflammation: fever, weight loss, arthralgias) and pulseless (vascular: ischemic symptoms).
- Imaging: MR angiography or CT angiography shows long-segment circumferential wall thickening and stenosis of the aortic arch and branches.
- Treatment: corticosteroids first-line; methotrexate or other steroid-sparing agents; biologics (tocilizumab) for refractory disease; revascularization for critical stenosis.

CASE_ID: 340
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Polyarteritis Nodosa

ALIASES:
PAN
medium-vessel vasculitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old man presents with 2 months of fevers, weight loss of 15 pounds, abdominal pain after meals, and new pain and weakness in his right foot.
2. He was diagnosed with chronic hepatitis B an abnormal value ago and has been inconsistent with antiviral therapy; he denies upper airway symptoms.
3. On exam, blood pressure is 168/an abnormal value, livedo reticularis is present on the lower extremities, and there are tender subcutaneous nodules along the calves; right foot drop is noted with loss of pinprick sensation in the L5 distribution.
4. Labs show elevated ESR (88) and CRP (62), normocytic anemia, mildly elevated creatinine; ANCA is negative; hepatitis B surface antigen is positive.
5. Mesenteric angiography reveals multiple small saccular and fusiform aneurysms ("rosary bead" appearance) with segmental narrowing of medium-sized arteries.
6. Skin biopsy of a nodule shows transmural fibrinoid necrosis of medium-sized arteries with neutrophilic infiltration; he is treated with corticosteroids, antiviral therapy for HBV, and plasma exchange.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Polyarteritis nodosa is a necrotizing vasculitis of medium-sized muscular arteries that SPARES the lungs and glomeruli (no pulmonary or glomerular involvement).
- Strongly associated with chronic hepatitis B infection.
- Classic findings: constitutional symptoms, mononeuritis multiplex, livedo reticularis, skin nodules, hypertension (renal artery), abdominal angina/mesenteric ischemia, testicular pain.
- Angiography: microaneurysms and segmental stenoses in renal, mesenteric, and hepatic arteries.
- ANCA-NEGATIVE (distinguishes from GPA, EGPA, MPA); treatment is steroids plus immunosuppression; in HBV-associated PAN, treat with antivirals plus plasma exchange and a short steroid course.

CASE_ID: 341
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Sjogren Syndrome

ALIASES:
sicca syndrome
Sjogren's syndrome
primary Sjogren disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old woman presents with 2 years of progressive dryness in her eyes (described as a foreign body sensation) and dry mouth that has caused difficulty swallowing dry foods and accelerated dental caries.
2. She also reports painless bilateral parotid swelling, fatigue, and arthralgias; she had a kidney stone last year.
3. On exam, the parotid glands are bilaterally enlarged and nontender; the oral mucosa is dry without saliva pooling; multiple dental caries are visible; the cornea shows fluorescein-stained punctate epithelial defects.
4. Schirmer test demonstrates less than an abnormal value of wetting in 5 minutes bilaterally (abnormal); labs show ANA positive at 1:640 speckled pattern, anti-Ro/SSA and anti-La/SSB positive, mildly elevated ESR, and a normal anion gap metabolic acidosis (RTA type 1).
5. Minor salivary gland biopsy of the lower lip reveals focal lymphocytic sialadenitis with a focus score of 2.
6. She is treated with artificial tears, cyclosporine eye drops, and pilocarpine for sicca symptoms; hydroxychloroquine is added for fatigue and arthralgias.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Sjogren syndrome is an autoimmune disorder with lymphocytic infiltration of exocrine glands, causing dry eyes (keratoconjunctivitis sicca) and dry mouth (xerostomia).
- Can be primary or secondary (associated with RA, SLE, systemic sclerosis).
- Autoantibodies: ANA (often speckled pattern), anti-Ro/SSA and anti-La/SSB; rheumatoid factor often positive.
- Extra-glandular manifestations: arthralgias, interstitial lung disease, distal renal tubular acidosis (type 1), peripheral neuropathy, cryoglobulinemic vasculitis.
- Significantly increased risk of mucosa-associated lymphoid tissue (MALT) lymphoma (most often parotid); monitor for persistent parotid swelling.

CASE_ID: 342
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Polymyositis

ALIASES:
PM
idiopathic inflammatory myopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 46-year-old woman presents with several months of progressive symmetric weakness, especially difficulty climbing stairs, standing from a chair, and lifting heavy objects overhead.
2. She denies muscle pain or skin rashes; she reports mild dysphagia for solids, has had a 6-pound weight loss, and gets short of breath walking up hills.
3. On exam, proximal muscle strength is 3/5 in the deltoids and hip flexors with relative sparing of distal strength; no rash, no Gottron papules, no heliotrope rash; mild bibasilar dry crackles.
4. Labs show CK 4,an abnormal value, AST 220, ALT 180, aldolase elevated, and LDH 580; ANA positive 1:320, anti-Jo-1 antibodies positive.
5. EMG demonstrates a myopathic pattern with short-duration polyphasic motor unit potentials and spontaneous activity (fibrillations).
6. Muscle biopsy reveals CD8+ T-cell endomysial inflammation surrounding non-necrotic muscle fibers without perifascicular atrophy or perimysial involvement; she is treated with prednisone plus methotrexate.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Polymyositis is an autoimmune inflammatory myopathy presenting with symmetric proximal muscle weakness without rash (distinguishes from dermatomyositis).
- Pathology: CD8+ T-cell mediated endomysial inflammation surrounding non-necrotic muscle fibers; in contrast, dermatomyositis shows CD4+ T cells with perimysial inflammation and perifascicular atrophy.
- Antisynthetase syndrome (anti-Jo-1 most common): myositis + interstitial lung disease + arthritis + mechanic's hands + Raynaud + fever.
- Elevated muscle enzymes (CK, aldolase, LDH, AST, ALT) reflect muscle injury; EMG is helpful but biopsy is definitive.
- Treatment: high-dose corticosteroids with steroid-sparing agents (methotrexate, azathioprine, mycophenolate); IVIG for refractory disease.

CASE_ID: 343
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Antiphospholipid Syndrome

ALIASES:
APS
antiphospholipid antibody syndrome
Hughes syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old woman with systemic lupus erythematosus presents with sudden onset right leg swelling and pain that began 2 days ago.
2. She has had three first-trimester miscarriages and one fetal death at an abnormal value; she also recalls an episode of transient blindness in her left eye last year that she did not investigate.
3. On exam, the right calf is warm and swollen with positive Homan sign; livedo reticularis is noted on the thighs; superficial thrombophlebitis is visible on the left arm; multiple small superficial ulcers are present on the toes.
4. Doppler ultrasound confirms an extensive deep vein thrombosis in the right femoral vein extending into the iliac system.
5. Labs show prolonged aPTT that fails to correct with mixing studies (suggesting an inhibitor); lupus anticoagulant testing is positive, anticardiolipin IgG antibodies are positive at high titer, and anti-beta-2-glycoprotein I antibodies are positive.
6. The antibody profile is confirmed positive on repeat testing 12 weeks later, satisfying diagnostic criteria; she is started on lifelong warfarin with a target INR of 2-3.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Antiphospholipid syndrome is an acquired thrombophilia defined by venous or arterial thrombosis and/or pregnancy morbidity PLUS persistent antiphospholipid antibodies.
- Three diagnostic antibodies: lupus anticoagulant, anticardiolipin IgG/IgM, anti-beta-2-glycoprotein I IgG/IgM; must be persistently positive >=12 weeks apart.
- Paradoxical prolongation of aPTT in vitro that doesn't correct with mixing (lupus anticoagulant) despite a HYPERCOAGULABLE state in vivo.
- Can be primary or secondary to SLE; associated with thrombocytopenia, livedo reticularis, recurrent pregnancy loss, valvular vegetations (Libman-Sacks endocarditis).
- Treatment: long-term warfarin for thrombotic APS (DOACs less effective, especially for triple-positive); LMWH plus aspirin in pregnancy.

CASE_ID: 344
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Behcet Disease

ALIASES:
Behcet syndrome
Behcet's disease
Silk Road disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old man presents with recurrent painful mouth ulcers and intermittent genital sores over the past year.
2. He has also had episodes of eye irritation and a tender nodular rash on the legs that come and go.
3. Examination shows aphthous oral lesions, healed genital ulceration, and inflammatory skin findings without a clear infectious source.
4. The pathergy test is positive (papule formation 24-48 hours after sterile needle prick).
5. Slit-lamp examination demonstrates anterior uveitis with hypopyon in the right eye.
6. Workup excludes HIV, syphilis, herpes simplex, and inflammatory bowel disease; treatment is initiated with topical corticosteroids for oral lesions, colchicine, and infliximab for ocular involvement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Behcet disease is a variable-vessel vasculitis with strong geographic distribution along the ancient Silk Road (Turkey, Middle East, East Asia).
- Diagnostic criteria require recurrent oral ulcers PLUS two of: recurrent genital ulcers, ocular lesions (uveitis), skin lesions (erythema nodosum, pseudofolliculitis), or positive pathergy test.
- Strong association with HLA-B51.
- Posterior uveitis can cause irreversible blindness; vascular involvement (venous thrombosis, arterial aneurysms) is potentially life-threatening.
- Treatment depends on organ involvement: colchicine, topical steroids, apremilast for mucocutaneous disease; immunosuppressants (azathioprine, cyclosporine) and biologics (anti-TNF, anti-IL-1) for major organ involvement.
