CASE_ID: 345
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Cocaine Intoxication

ALIASES:
cocaine toxicity
sympathomimetic toxidrome
crack intoxication

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old man is brought to the ED by his friends after he became extremely agitated and developed chest pain at a party 1 hour ago.
2. He has no significant past medical history but admits to having used a recreational substance at the party; he is sweating profusely.
3. On exam, he is agitated and paranoid with widely dilated pupils that respond to light; heart rate 142 bpm, blood pressure 198/an abnormal value, temperature 39.2°C, mucous membranes are dry, and his skin is flushed and diaphoretic.
4. Initial assessment suggests a medication- or toxin-related syndrome, and confirmatory testing is obtained.
5. CT of the head is unremarkable, with no signs of stroke or intracerebral hemorrhage.
6. He is treated with IV benzodiazepines (lorazepam), aggressive cooling, IV fluids, and aspirin; nitrates and phentolamine are used for blood pressure and chest pain; beta blockers are AVOIDED to prevent unopposed alpha stimulation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Cocaine inhibits reuptake of dopamine, norepinephrine, and serotonin, producing sympathetic activation and euphoria; also a potent local anesthetic and sodium channel blocker.
- Sympathomimetic toxidrome: agitation, mydriasis, tachycardia, hypertension, hyperthermia, diaphoresis (contrast with anticholinergic toxidrome which has dry skin).
- Cardiovascular complications: vasospasm and thrombosis causing MI (even in young patients), aortic dissection, arrhythmias, cardiomyopathy.
- Other complications: stroke (ischemic and hemorrhagic), seizures, rhabdomyolysis, intestinal ischemia, nasal septal perforation, levamisole-induced agranulocytosis.
- Treatment: benzodiazepines first-line for agitation and to lower BP/HR; AVOID beta blockers (unopposed alpha causes paradoxical vasoconstriction); phentolamine for hypertension; aspirin and nitrates for ACS; active cooling for hyperthermia.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 346
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Alcohol Withdrawal

ALIASES:
ethanol withdrawal
delirium tremens
DTs

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old man hospitalized for cellulitis develops tremor, anxiety, and tachycardia 36 hours after admission; over the next 24 hours he becomes increasingly agitated and confused.
2. He acknowledges drinking 12 beers daily for the past an abnormal value with his last drink the morning of admission; he had a similar episode after a prior hospitalization.
3. On exam, he is diaphoretic, tachycardic at 128 bpm with BP 168/96, has a coarse tremor of the outstretched hands, is disoriented to time and place, and reports seeing insects crawling on the walls.
4. Labs show macrocytic anemia (MCV 108), elevated GGT, AST/ALT ratio of 2.4, mildly low magnesium and potassium, and a CIWA-Ar score of 28 (severe).
5. ECG and chest imaging are unrevealing for other causes of his presentation.
6. He is started on a symptom-triggered IV lorazepam protocol along with IV thiamine before any glucose, multivitamins, and aggressive electrolyte repletion; symptoms gradually subside over 4 days.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Alcohol withdrawal results from CNS hyperexcitability after chronic GABA receptor down-regulation and NMDA up-regulation are unmasked by ethanol cessation.
- Timeline: mild withdrawal/tremor (6-24 hrs), withdrawal seizures (12-48 hrs), alcoholic hallucinosis (12-48 hrs, visual hallucinations with otherwise clear sensorium), delirium tremens (48-96 hrs, altered mental status + autonomic instability + hallucinations).
- DTs is the most severe form with significant mortality; characterized by gross confusion, severe autonomic instability, and visual hallucinations.
- Treatment: benzodiazepines are the cornerstone (lorazepam, chlordiazepoxide, diazepam); symptom-triggered protocols using CIWA-Ar.
- Always give thiamine BEFORE glucose to prevent precipitating Wernicke encephalopathy; replete magnesium (helps prevent seizures) and other electrolytes.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 347
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Opioid Withdrawal

ALIASES:
opiate withdrawal
heroin withdrawal
narcotic withdrawal

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old man presents to the ED with severe muscle and bone aches, nausea, vomiting, diarrhea, and intense anxiety that began 12 hours after his last use.
2. He has a history of heroin use for an abnormal value and was recently incarcerated for 2 days, where he had no access to substances.
3. On exam, he is restless and yawning; pupils are widely dilated and reactive; piloerection is visible on his arms; he has rhinorrhea, lacrimation, and excessive sweating; pulse is 108 bpm and blood pressure 152/94.
4. Initial assessment suggests a medication- or toxin-related syndrome, and confirmatory testing is obtained.
5. He has no respiratory depression or altered mental status.
6. He is started on buprenorphine-naloxone after sufficient withdrawal symptoms are demonstrated to avoid precipitated withdrawal; clonidine and antiemetics are given for symptomatic relief, and outpatient maintenance therapy is arranged.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Opioid withdrawal is uncomfortable and distressing but NOT life-threatening (unlike alcohol or benzodiazepine withdrawal).
- Symptoms (opposite of opioid effects): mydriasis (vs. miosis), piloerection ("cold turkey"), yawning, rhinorrhea, lacrimation, abdominal cramps, diarrhea, anxiety, restless legs, muscle aches.
- Onset depends on the half-life of the opioid: short-acting (heroin: 6-12 hrs); long-acting (methadone: 24-72 hrs).
- Treatment options: methadone (full mu agonist, slow taper or maintenance), buprenorphine (partial mu agonist, less risk of overdose), clonidine (alpha-2 agonist for autonomic symptoms), supportive antiemetics and antidiarrheals.
- Buprenorphine must be initiated when withdrawal is moderate (COWS >=10-12) to prevent precipitated withdrawal; naltrexone for maintenance after detoxification.

CASE_ID: 348
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Methemoglobinemia

ALIASES:
methemoglobin toxicity
acquired methemoglobinemia
oxidant-induced methemoglobinemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 56-year-old man undergoing routine outpatient endoscopy develops sudden dyspnea and bluish discoloration of his lips and fingertips 20 minutes after a topical anesthetic was applied to his pharynx.
2. He has otherwise been healthy with no respiratory or cardiac complaints; the procedure had not yet begun and he received no sedation.
3. On exam, he is anxious and centrally cyanotic with bluish-gray discoloration of the lips, tongue, and nail beds; pulse oximetry reads 85% on supplemental oxygen yet does not improve with increased FiO2.
4. Arterial blood drawn into a syringe appears chocolate brown and does not turn red with exposure to air; arterial blood gas reveals PaO2 of an abnormal value with calculated saturation of 99% (saturation gap with measured SpO2).
5. Co-oximetry reveals a methemoglobin level of 38%; the local anesthetic used was benzocaine spray.
6. He is treated with IV methylene blue 1 mg/kg with rapid resolution of cyanosis and normalization of the methemoglobin level over the next hour.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Methemoglobinemia occurs when iron in heme is oxidized from ferrous (Fe2+) to ferric (Fe3+), which cannot bind oxygen and causes a leftward shift of the oxygen dissociation curve in remaining normal hemoglobin.
- Common oxidant triggers: topical anesthetics (benzocaine, lidocaine, prilocaine), dapsone, nitrates/nitrites, sulfonamides, antimalarials, aniline dyes, well water (infants).
- Classic clinical clues: cyanosis disproportionate to symptoms, "chocolate brown" blood that does not pink with oxygen, "saturation gap" between SpO2 (low) and PaO2-calculated saturation (normal); pulse oximeter reads erroneously around 85%.
- Severity correlates with methemoglobin level: <15% asymptomatic, 15-30% cyanosis, 30-50% headache/dyspnea, >50% altered mental status, >70% lethal.
- Treatment: methylene blue (reduces methemoglobin via NADPH-methemoglobin reductase); G6PD deficient patients cannot generate NADPH and may worsen with methylene blue (use ascorbic acid or exchange transfusion instead).
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 349
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Iron Overdose

ALIASES:
acute iron toxicity
ferrous sulfate overdose
iron poisoning

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 3-year-old girl is brought to the ED by her mother who found her with an empty bottle of prenatal vitamins; the child has vomited 4 times in the last hour.
2. The mother estimates that approximately 30 tablets are missing; the child has been having intermittent abdominal pain and one episode of bloody diarrhea.
3. On exam, the child is lethargic and pale; vital signs show heart rate 138 bpm, BP 78/an abnormal value, and capillary refill of 4 seconds; her abdomen is tender diffusely.
4. Labs show metabolic acidosis with pH 7.22, lactate 5.6 mmol/L, glucose an abnormal value, AST 184, and a serum iron level of 580 mcg/dL at 4 hours post-ingestion.
5. Abdominal X-ray reveals multiple radiopaque tablets in the stomach and small intestine.
6. Whole bowel irrigation is initiated and IV deferoxamine chelation therapy is administered, with gradual improvement in metabolic status; the child develops "vin rose" colored urine and recovers.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Iron overdose is a leading cause of pediatric poisoning death; toxicity occurs at doses >40-60 mg/kg of elemental iron.
- Five clinical stages: I (0-6 hrs: GI - vomiting, diarrhea, hematemesis, abdominal pain), II (6-24 hrs: latent/recovery phase - deceptive), III (12-48 hrs: shock, metabolic acidosis, multiorgan failure), IV (2-5 days: hepatotoxicity), V (4-6 weeks: gastric outlet/pyloric scarring).
- Mechanism: free iron generates oxygen radicals damaging cells; mitochondrial dysfunction leads to lactic acidosis; corrosive to GI mucosa.
- Diagnosis: serum iron level at 4-6 hours post-ingestion; >500 mcg/dL is severe toxicity; abdominal X-ray shows radiopaque tablets (iron is radiodense).
- Treatment: whole bowel irrigation (no activated charcoal - doesn't bind iron); IV deferoxamine chelation produces orange-red "vin rose" urine when binding iron.

CASE_ID: 350
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Lead Poisoning

ALIASES:
plumbism
lead toxicity
chronic lead poisoning

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 4-year-old boy is brought in for evaluation of irritability, decreased appetite, abdominal pain, and developmental regression noted by his mother.
2. The family recently moved into an older house built in the 1950s that has chipping paint, and the child likes to chew on windowsills.
3. On exam, the child is pale and irritable; abdominal exam reveals mild diffuse tenderness without organomegaly; a bluish-grey discoloration is visible at the gum margins (Burton line).
4. Labs show hemoglobin 8.2 g/dL with MCV 68, basophilic stippling on peripheral smear, and elevated red cell zinc protoporphyrin; venous blood lead level is 68 mcg/dL.
5. X-ray of the long bones reveals dense metaphyseal lines (lead lines); abdominal X-ray shows radiopaque flakes within the bowel.
6. The child is admitted, the home is professionally remediated, and chelation therapy with succimer (oral DMSA) is initiated; blood lead levels are monitored over months with environmental control.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Lead poisoning in children most commonly comes from ingestion of lead-based paint chips (in homes built before 1978) or dust; in adults usually occupational (battery manufacturing, smelting, demolition).
- Lead inhibits ferrochelatase and ALA dehydratase in heme synthesis, causing accumulation of protoporphyrins and ALA; impairs sulfhydryl-containing enzymes throughout the body.
- Pediatric features: behavioral and cognitive impairment (irreversible), abdominal pain, microcytic anemia with basophilic stippling, lead lines on gums (Burton) and long-bone metaphyses, peripheral neuropathy.
- Adult features: abdominal pain, peripheral neuropathy (wrist drop, foot drop), encephalopathy, nephropathy (proximal tubular dysfunction, gouty nephropathy), hypertension.
- Treatment: remove exposure (essential); chelation with succimer (DMSA) for moderate; EDTA + dimercaprol (BAL) for severe (>70 mcg/dL or encephalopathy); BAL given first to prevent intracellular shift.

CASE_ID: 351
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Autism Spectrum Disorder

ALIASES:
ASD
autistic disorder
pervasive developmental disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 3-year-old boy is brought in for evaluation because he has not yet spoken any meaningful words and rarely makes eye contact with his parents.
2. He prefers to play alone, lines up his toy cars in straight rows for hours, becomes extremely upset if his routine is changed, and engages in repetitive hand-flapping when excited; he was healthy at birth with normal milestones until an abnormal value.
3. On exam, he avoids eye contact, does not respond to his name, and shows minimal social referencing; he plays in a rigid stereotyped manner without imaginative play or pointing; head circumference is at the 90th percentile.
4. Audiologic testing is normal, ruling out hearing impairment as a cause of language delay.
5. Modified Checklist for Autism in Toddlers (M-CHAT) is markedly positive; chromosomal microarray and fragile X testing are obtained as part of genetic workup.
6. He meets DSM-5 criteria for autism spectrum disorder; early intervention with applied behavioral analysis, speech therapy, and occupational therapy is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Autism spectrum disorder is a neurodevelopmental disorder defined by (1) persistent deficits in social communication and interaction and (2) restricted, repetitive patterns of behavior, interests, or activities, with onset in early childhood.
- Symptoms must be present in early development and cause significant functional impairment.
- Prevalence is increasing (~1 in 36 children); 4 times more common in males; high genetic heritability.
- Associated conditions: intellectual disability (in some), epilepsy, fragile X syndrome, tuberous sclerosis, Rett syndrome, sleep disorders, ADHD.
- Treatment: early intensive behavioral intervention (applied behavioral analysis), speech and occupational therapy, special education; risperidone and aripiprazole for irritability/aggression; no medication for core symptoms.

CASE_ID: 352
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Fragile X Syndrome

ALIASES:
fragile X
FXS
Martin-Bell syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 5-year-old boy is brought in for evaluation of developmental delay, learning difficulties, and behavioral problems including hand flapping and avoidance of eye contact.
2. His mother reports a maternal uncle with similar features who lived in a group home; his older sister has mild learning difficulties.
3. On exam, he has a long narrow face with prominent forehead and large protruding ears, hyperflexible joints (especially the fingers), and a single transverse palmar crease; he avoids eye contact and has stereotypic hand-flapping movements.
4. Genitourinary exam at age 5 shows normal external genitalia, but his older brother (age 12) has macroorchidism on routine exam.
5. Comprehensive metabolic, lead, and thyroid testing are unrevealing.
6. Molecular genetic testing reveals more than 200 CGG trinucleotide repeats in the FMR1 gene on the X chromosome with abnormal methylation, confirming fragile X syndrome.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Fragile X syndrome is the most common inherited cause of intellectual disability and the most common monogenic cause of autism spectrum disorder.
- Caused by CGG trinucleotide repeat expansion in the FMR1 gene on the X chromosome, leading to hypermethylation and silencing of FMR1 protein expression.
- Classic features: long narrow face, prominent forehead and jaw, large protruding ears, macroorchidism (especially post-pubertal), hyperflexible joints; intellectual disability with autistic features.
- X-linked dominant with variable penetrance; affects males more severely; female carriers may have milder cognitive issues, premature ovarian insufficiency, and FXTAS in older age.
- Diagnosis by PCR or Southern blot showing >200 CGG repeats; treatment is supportive with educational and behavioral interventions.

CASE_ID: 353
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Rett Syndrome

ALIASES:
Rett's syndrome
RTT
MECP2 disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 3-year-old girl is brought in for evaluation of loss of previously acquired skills; she had been developing normally until 18 months of age when she began losing language and motor skills.
2. Her parents have noticed that she has stopped using her hands purposefully and instead engages in continuous hand-wringing movements; she walked at an abnormal value but now has an unsteady gait.
3. On exam, head circumference has fallen from the 50th percentile at an abnormal value to below the 5th percentile; she makes minimal eye contact and engages in stereotypic hand-wringing and hand-mouthing; her gait is wide-based and ataxic; she has episodes of hyperventilation followed by breath-holding.
4. Neurologic exam reveals diffuse hypotonia with brisk reflexes and occasional myoclonic jerks; she has bruxism and intermittent staring spells.
5. EEG demonstrates background slowing with epileptiform discharges; MRI is largely unremarkable.
6. Molecular genetic testing confirms a pathogenic mutation in the MECP2 gene on the X chromosome; multidisciplinary care including PT, OT, speech therapy, and anti-seizure medications is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Rett syndrome is an X-linked dominant neurodevelopmental disorder caused by mutations in the MECP2 gene; almost exclusively affects females (lethal in most males in utero).
- Classic course: normal development for 6-18 months, then regression with loss of purposeful hand use and language, deceleration of head growth (acquired microcephaly), and emergence of stereotyped hand movements.
- Hallmark: stereotyped hand-wringing or hand-washing movements; loss of purposeful hand use is the cardinal feature.
- Other features: breathing abnormalities (hyperventilation, breath-holding, apnea), seizures, gait abnormalities, autistic features, growth failure.
- Treatment is supportive: physical, occupational, and speech therapy; anti-seizure medications; trofinetide is the first FDA-approved disease-targeted therapy.

CASE_ID: 354
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Phenylketonuria

ALIASES:
PKU
phenylalanine hydroxylase deficiency
classical PKU

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 4-month-old infant is referred after newborn screening showed elevated phenylalanine; the family had recently emigrated from a country without universal newborn screening.
2. Although the infant initially appeared well, the parents have noted he has developed a musty body odor and is now less interactive than his cousins of the same age.
3. On exam, he has fair skin, blue eyes, and blonde hair (lighter than his parents); head circumference has slowed; he has eczema on the cheeks and arms and a peculiar musty odor.
4. Plasma phenylalanine is an abnormal value (severely elevated; normal <2); tyrosine is low; the BH4 cofactor loading test does not significantly lower phenylalanine.
5. Genetic testing confirms biallelic pathogenic mutations in the PAH gene, consistent with classic phenylketonuria.
6. He is immediately started on a strict phenylalanine-restricted diet with special medical formula; phenylalanine levels are monitored closely, and developmental assessments at age 2 show normal cognitive development.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Phenylketonuria is an autosomal recessive disorder caused by deficiency of phenylalanine hydroxylase (or rarely the BH4 cofactor), leading to accumulation of phenylalanine and its metabolites (phenylketones).
- Without treatment, untreated PKU causes severe intellectual disability, seizures, microcephaly, eczema, fair complexion, and a characteristic musty/mousy odor (from phenylacetate in sweat/urine).
- Diagnosed by newborn screening (universal in US); confirmed by quantitative plasma amino acids.
- Treatment: strict lifelong phenylalanine-restricted diet (avoid aspartame and high-protein foods); supplement with tyrosine (becomes essential); sapropterin (BH4) for responsive patients.
- Maternal PKU syndrome: a pregnant woman with PKU who is not on a strict diet exposes her fetus to high phenylalanine, causing congenital heart defects, microcephaly, and intellectual disability in the (heterozygous) child.

CASE_ID: 355
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Galactosemia

ALIASES:
classic galactosemia
GALT deficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-day-old neonate is admitted with poor feeding, vomiting, lethargy, and jaundice that has worsened over the past 3 days.
2. He was born at term after an uncomplicated pregnancy and initially appeared well; he is being exclusively breastfed.
3. On exam, he has jaundice, hepatomegaly, and a bulging anterior fontanelle; cataracts are noted on ophthalmologic examination.
4. Labs show direct hyperbilirubinemia, elevated transaminases, hypoglycemia, prolonged INR, and a positive urine reducing substance test (Clinitest) but negative urine glucose dipstick.
5. Blood culture grows E. coli, and he is started on broad-spectrum antibiotics.
6. Enzymatic assay confirms markedly low galactose-1-phosphate uridyltransferase (GALT) activity in red blood cells; lactose-free formula (soy-based) is substituted with rapid clinical improvement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Classic galactosemia is an autosomal recessive deficiency of GALT (galactose-1-phosphate uridyltransferase), causing accumulation of galactose-1-phosphate.
- Presents in the first days to weeks of life with feeding difficulties, vomiting, jaundice, hepatomegaly, hypoglycemia, cataracts (galactitol in lens), and predisposition to E. coli sepsis.
- The positive urine Clinitest (detects reducing substances) with negative urine glucose dipstick is a classic clue (dipstick detects glucose specifically; Clinitest detects all reducing sugars).
- Diagnosed by newborn screening (low GALT activity); confirmed by enzyme assay or genetic testing.
- Treatment: complete elimination of galactose and lactose from the diet (lactose-free formula such as soy-based); despite dietary management, long-term complications include speech apraxia, intellectual disability, and ovarian failure.

CASE_ID: 356
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Tay-Sachs Disease

ALIASES:
GM2 gangliosidosis
hexosaminidase A deficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 9-month-old infant of Ashkenazi ancestry is brought in for evaluation of developmental regression; she previously sat unsupported but has lost this ability over the past 2 months.
2. Her parents report she is increasingly easily startled by loud sounds with an exaggerated reaction; her vision appears to be failing.
3. On exam, she has hypotonia with brisk reflexes, profound hyperacusis with exaggerated startle, and visual inattention; her head circumference is increased relative to her body.
4. Fundoscopic examination reveals a cherry-red spot at the macula in both eyes; there is no hepatosplenomegaly.
5. Genetic testing identifies biallelic pathogenic variants in the HEXA gene; enzymatic testing confirms markedly reduced beta-hexosaminidase A activity in leukocytes.
6. The family is counseled regarding the progressive nature of the disease, expected death by 3-5 years, and reproductive risks for future pregnancies including preimplantation genetic testing.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Tay-Sachs disease is an autosomal recessive lysosomal storage disease caused by HEXA gene mutations leading to deficiency of beta-hexosaminidase A and accumulation of GM2 ganglioside in neurons.
- Highly enriched among Ashkenazi Jewish (carrier frequency ~1 in 30), French Canadian, and Cajun populations.
- Infantile onset: developmental regression starting around 6 months, hyperacusis (exaggerated startle), cherry-red macular spot, hypotonia followed by spasticity, blindness, seizures; death by 3-5 years.
- Distinguish from Niemann-Pick disease (also cherry-red spot but with hepatosplenomegaly; sphingomyelinase deficiency); Tay-Sachs has NO hepatosplenomegaly.
- No effective treatment; carrier screening and prenatal/preimplantation diagnosis are critical for at-risk populations.

CASE_ID: 357
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Maple Syrup Urine Disease

ALIASES:
MSUD
branched-chain ketoaciduria

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 5-day-old neonate is brought to the ED with poor feeding, lethargy, vomiting, and intermittent opisthotonus over the past 24 hours.
2. He was born at term and appeared well at discharge on day 2; the parents are first cousins from a Mennonite community.
3. On exam, he is lethargic with hypertonia and posturing episodes; the urine and earwax have a distinctive sweet smell reminiscent of maple syrup or burnt sugar.
4. Labs show metabolic acidosis with an increased anion gap, positive urine ketones, hypoglycemia, and elevated plasma branched-chain amino acids (leucine, isoleucine, valine) with markedly elevated alloisoleucine.
5. MRI of the brain shows symmetric white matter edema in the brainstem and cerebellum.
6. He is treated with hemodialysis to remove toxic metabolites, followed by a strict low-protein diet with special branched-chain amino acid-restricted formula; long-term management requires lifelong dietary control.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Maple syrup urine disease is an autosomal recessive defect in the branched-chain alpha-ketoacid dehydrogenase complex, causing accumulation of branched-chain amino acids (leucine, isoleucine, valine) and their toxic alpha-ketoacid derivatives.
- The dehydrogenase complex requires thiamine (B1) as a cofactor; some forms are thiamine-responsive.
- Classic presentation: poor feeding, vomiting, lethargy, ketosis, and characteristic maple syrup odor in urine, sweat, and earwax in the first days of life.
- Untreated cases progress to seizures, coma, cerebral edema, and death.
- Treatment: dietary restriction of branched-chain amino acids, special medical formula, frequent monitoring; acute crises require hemodialysis; liver transplantation can be curative.

CASE_ID: 358
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Glycogen Storage Disease Type I

ALIASES:
von Gierke disease
glucose-6-phosphatase deficiency
GSD Ia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-month-old infant is brought in for evaluation of recurrent episodes of severe morning hypoglycemia, especially after longer overnight intervals between feedings.
2. The parents have noticed the infant's abdomen has become progressively distended and that he is smaller than peers his age.
3. On exam, the abdomen is protuberant with massive hepatomegaly extending well below the umbilicus; the kidneys are also palpable bilaterally; he has doll-like facial features with fat cheeks and thin extremities.
4. Labs during an episode show hypoglycemia (glucose an abnormal value), severe lactic acidosis (lactate 12 mmol/L), hyperuricemia, hypertriglyceridemia, and elevated transaminases; ketones are mildly increased.
5. A glucagon stimulation test fails to raise blood glucose normally and instead worsens the lactic acidosis.
6. Liver biopsy demonstrates enzymatically absent glucose-6-phosphatase activity; treatment with frequent meals, uncooked cornstarch, and nighttime continuous gastric feeds is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Von Gierke disease (GSD I) is caused by deficiency of glucose-6-phosphatase (Ia, most common) or glucose-6-phosphate translocase (Ib).
- The defect prevents both glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis from producing free glucose, leading to severe fasting hypoglycemia and accumulation of glucose-6-phosphate (driving glycolysis to lactate and shunting to lipid/uric acid pathways).
- Hallmark biochemical findings: fasting hypoglycemia, lactic acidosis, hyperuricemia, hyperlipidemia (xanthomas in older children).
- Massive hepatomegaly and nephromegaly; doll-like facial features; growth failure; bleeding tendency from platelet dysfunction.
- Treatment: frequent feeding, uncooked cornstarch (slow-release glucose), continuous nocturnal gastric feeds; avoid fructose and galactose; allopurinol for hyperuricemia.

CASE_ID: 359
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Epiglottitis

ALIASES:
acute epiglottitis
supraglottitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 4-year-old unvaccinated child is brought to the ED with rapid onset high fever, severe sore throat, and difficulty breathing over the past 4 hours.
2. The child has not had any cough or coryza; the parents report he is refusing to swallow, even his own saliva.
3. On exam, the child is sitting upright in a tripod position with his neck extended and jaw protruded; he is drooling and has muffled "hot potato" voice; he appears toxic with a temperature of 39.8°C and inspiratory stridor.
4. No attempt is made to examine the oropharynx with a tongue depressor given the risk of precipitating airway obstruction.
5. Lateral neck X-ray shows a swollen epiglottis (thumbprint sign) and dilated hypopharynx; a controlled intubation is performed in the operating room.
6. Blood cultures grow Haemophilus influenzae type b, and he is treated with IV ceftriaxone with extubation after 48 hours and full recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Epiglottitis is a life-threatening infection of the epiglottis and supraglottic structures that can cause rapid airway obstruction.
- Classic patient: unvaccinated child or adult with abrupt onset of fever, dysphagia, drooling, muffled voice, and tripod positioning; "the 4 D's" - dysphagia, drooling, distress, dysphonia.
- Historically caused by Haemophilus influenzae type B (Hib); now less common due to vaccination; other organisms include Streptococcus pneumoniae, S. pyogenes, S. aureus.
- "Thumbprint sign" on lateral neck X-ray (swollen epiglottis); contrasts with the "steeple sign" of croup (subglottic narrowing on AP view).
- Management: secure the airway FIRST in a controlled setting (OR with anesthesia and ENT); avoid agitating the child or examining the oropharynx; IV antibiotics (ceftriaxone +/- vancomycin); rifampin prophylaxis for close contacts in Hib cases.

CASE_ID: 360
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Sudden Infant Death Syndrome

ALIASES:
SIDS
crib death
cot death

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A previously healthy 3-month-old male infant is brought to the ED in cardiac arrest after being found unresponsive in his crib by his mother.
2. He was put to sleep prone on a couch with soft bedding and a stuffed animal; he was born at an abnormal value gestation and had a recent mild upper respiratory infection; his mother smoked during pregnancy.
3. On arrival the infant is unresponsive, apneic, and pulseless; cyanosis and dependent lividity are noted; resuscitation efforts are unsuccessful.
4. Postmortem investigation including thorough scene investigation, review of the medical history, and complete autopsy reveals no abnormalities; toxicology and metabolic screening are unrevealing.
5. There is no evidence of trauma, infection, or congenital anomaly to explain the death.
6. The cause of death is certified as SIDS; the family receives bereavement counseling, and prevention education is provided to neighbors and family members about safe sleep practices.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- SIDS is the sudden unexplained death of an infant less than 1 year of age that remains unexplained after a thorough investigation including autopsy, scene examination, and review of the clinical history.
- Peak incidence: 2-4 months of age; rare before 2 weeks or after 6 months.
- Modifiable risk factors: prone or side sleeping position, soft bedding/sleep surfaces, bed sharing, maternal smoking (prenatal and postnatal), overheating, lack of breastfeeding.
- Non-modifiable risk factors: preterm birth, low birth weight, male sex, recent URI, family history of SIDS.
- Prevention (ABCs of safe sleep): Alone (separate sleep surface), on the Back, in a Crib (firm flat surface); avoid soft bedding, overheating, and tobacco smoke; pacifier use during sleep is protective.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 361
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Wernicke-Korsakoff Syndrome

ALIASES:
Wernicke encephalopathy
Korsakoff psychosis
thiamine deficiency encephalopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old homeless man with chronic alcohol use disorder is brought to the ED after he was found wandering in the street, unable to walk steadily and confused about his surroundings.
2. He has been drinking heavily for an abnormal value and has not been eating regularly; his last alcohol-related hospitalization was an abnormal value ago when he was discharged AMA before receiving any vitamin supplementation.
3. On exam, he is disoriented to time and place but alert; horizontal nystagmus is present on lateral gaze and there is bilateral lateral rectus palsy with diplopia; gait is wide-based and ataxic; vital signs are stable.
4. Labs show macrocytic anemia (MCV 110), elevated GGT, and a low albumin; ethanol level is undetectable.
5. After IV thiamine is administered, the ophthalmoplegia resolves within hours and the ataxia improves over days, but he is later noted to have profound anterograde and retrograde amnesia with confabulation, while remaining alert.
6. MRI shows hyperintensities in the mamillary bodies and periaqueductal gray matter, consistent with the progression to Korsakoff psychosis; long-term care in a supervised setting with nutritional support is arranged.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Wernicke encephalopathy is an acute neurologic emergency caused by thiamine (B1) deficiency; classic triad: encephalopathy/confusion, oculomotor dysfunction (nystagmus, ophthalmoplegia, especially CN VI palsy), and gait ataxia.
- Most commonly seen in chronic alcohol use disorder, but also in hyperemesis gravidarum, malabsorption (post-bariatric surgery), eating disorders, and prolonged TPN without thiamine.
- ALWAYS give parenteral thiamine BEFORE glucose in at-risk patients to prevent precipitating Wernicke encephalopathy.
- Korsakoff psychosis is the chronic, often irreversible memory disorder following untreated Wernicke encephalopathy: anterograde amnesia, retrograde amnesia, confabulation, and apathy in an alert patient.
- Pathology: degeneration of mamillary bodies, dorsomedial thalamus, and periaqueductal gray; treatment is high-dose IV/IM thiamine followed by oral maintenance.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 362
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Conduct Disorder

ALIASES:
conduct disorder of childhood
childhood-onset conduct disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 15-year-old boy is brought in by his mother and a court-appointed counselor after he was caught setting fire to a neighbor's shed.
2. Over the past an abnormal value he has been suspended from school multiple times for fighting and bullying classmates, has been caught stealing from stores on three occasions, and was reported for cruelty to a neighbor's cat.
3. On exam, he is sullen and uncooperative, displays no remorse for his behavior, and blames others for his troubles; he denies hallucinations, delusions, or substance use; he is alert and oriented with intact cognition.
4. School records confirm a pattern of aggressive behavior, frequent absences (truancy), and property destruction extending back to age 11; he has run away from home twice and stayed out overnight without permission multiple times.
5. There is a strong family history of antisocial behavior; he has been exposed to repeated household violence.
6. He meets DSM-5 criteria for conduct disorder, childhood-onset type; multimodal treatment including parent management training, multisystemic therapy, and a structured therapeutic school placement is recommended.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Conduct disorder is a persistent pattern of behavior that violates the basic rights of others or major societal norms, with onset before age 18.
- DSM-5 requires at least 3 of 15 behaviors in 4 categories over 12 months (at least one in past 6 months): aggression to people/animals, destruction of property, deceitfulness/theft, serious rule violations.
- Childhood-onset (before age 10) has a worse prognosis and higher progression to antisocial personality disorder than adolescent-onset.
- Risk factors: harsh/inconsistent parenting, exposure to violence, parental antisocial behavior, low socioeconomic status, neurobiological factors.
- Treatment: parent management training, multisystemic therapy, cognitive-behavioral therapy; medications target comorbid conditions (ADHD, mood disorders); cannot diagnose antisocial personality disorder until age 18.

CASE_ID: 363
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Oppositional Defiant Disorder

ALIASES:
ODD

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 9-year-old boy is brought in by his parents for evaluation of frequent temper outbursts and arguing with adults that has been going on for at least 8 months.
2. He often deliberately annoys his younger sister, blames others for his mistakes, refuses to comply with reasonable requests from his parents and teachers, and has been described as "easily angered" and "spiteful.".
3. On exam, he is alert and verbally interactive but argumentative; he denies hallucinations, suicidal ideation, or substance use; cognitive screening is normal.
4. He is achieving academically appropriately for his grade level; his behavior is similar at home and school; importantly, he does not show physical aggression toward people or animals, does not steal, and does not destroy property maliciously.
5. There are no symptoms of mood disorder, psychosis, or substance use; ADHD screening is negative.
6. He meets DSM-5 criteria for oppositional defiant disorder of moderate severity; parent management training is initiated, with individual therapy emphasizing problem-solving skills and emotional regulation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Oppositional defiant disorder is a pattern of angry/irritable mood, argumentative/defiant behavior, and vindictiveness lasting at least 6 months.
- Symptoms must include at least 4 behaviors from 3 categories (angry/irritable mood, argumentative/defiant behavior, vindictiveness) with at least one non-sibling.
- Differs from conduct disorder: ODD does NOT include the more severe violations seen in CD (physical aggression to people/animals, property destruction, theft, serious rule violations).
- Strong association with ADHD, mood disorders, anxiety disorders; up to 30% may progress to conduct disorder.
- Treatment: parent management training is first-line; individual therapy (CBT, problem-solving); school-based interventions; medications target comorbid conditions only.

CASE_ID: 364
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Optic Neuritis

ALIASES:
demyelinating optic neuritis
inflammatory optic neuropathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with 5 days of progressively blurred vision in her right eye and dull pain behind the eye that worsens with eye movement.
2. She also reports an episode of right arm weakness and numbness an abnormal value ago that resolved spontaneously after several weeks.
3. On exam, visual acuity is 20/200 in the right eye and 20/20 in the left; there is a relative afferent pupillary defect (Marcus Gunn pupil) in the right eye; color vision is markedly impaired in the right eye (red desaturation); fundoscopy of the affected eye is normal early in the course.
4. Visual field testing reveals a central scotoma in the right eye.
5. MRI of the brain and orbits demonstrates enhancement of the right optic nerve; multiple periventricular white matter T2 hyperintensities are also seen with one enhancing lesion.
6. She is treated with IV methylprednisolone with gradual improvement in vision; further evaluation supports a diagnosis of clinically isolated syndrome with high risk for multiple sclerosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Optic neuritis is inflammation of the optic nerve; most commonly idiopathic or demyelinating (associated with multiple sclerosis).
- Classic presentation: subacute monocular visual loss with eye pain that worsens with movement, central scotoma, color desaturation (especially red), and afferent pupillary defect.
- Fundoscopy is normal in retrobulbar optic neuritis ("the patient sees nothing and the doctor sees nothing"); papillitis (disc swelling) occurs in anterior optic neuritis.
- Up to 50% of patients with optic neuritis develop multiple sclerosis within 15 years; MRI brain stratifies risk.
- Treatment: IV high-dose methylprednisolone (does not improve final visual outcome but speeds recovery); ORAL prednisone alone is contraindicated as it increases recurrence; evaluate for and treat underlying MS.

CASE_ID: 365
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hordeolum

ALIASES:
stye
external hordeolum
internal hordeolum

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old woman presents with a painful red bump on her right upper eyelid that developed over the past 3 days and has been progressively enlarging.
2. She wears contact lenses and reports recently rubbing her eyes frequently due to seasonal allergies; she has a history of similar lesions in the past.
3. On exam, there is a tender, erythematous, well-circumscribed nodule near the lid margin of the right upper eyelid with a small yellowish center pointing externally; the conjunctiva is mildly injected; visual acuity is unaffected.
4. There is no preseptal cellulitis, no fever, and no associated lymphadenopathy.
5. Slit-lamp examination shows the lesion arising at the base of an eyelash with surrounding inflammation; no corneal involvement or hypopyon.
6. She is treated with warm compresses several times per day and topical erythromycin ointment; the lesion drains spontaneously within a week with complete resolution.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hordeolum is an acute bacterial infection (usually Staphylococcus aureus) of the eyelid glands.
- External hordeolum (stye): involves the gland of Zeis or Moll at the base of an eyelash; points outward toward the skin.
- Internal hordeolum: involves the meibomian glands within the tarsal plate; points inward toward the conjunctiva; more painful and may take longer to resolve.
- Distinguish from chalazion: chronic, granulomatous, non-tender obstruction of a meibomian gland (sterile lipogranuloma).
- Treatment: warm compresses 4 times daily, lid hygiene; topical antibiotics for external lesions or systemic for periorbital cellulitis; incision and drainage by ophthalmology if persistent.

CASE_ID: 366
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Orbital Cellulitis

ALIASES:
postseptal cellulitis
orbital infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 7-year-old boy is brought to the ED with 2 days of worsening eyelid swelling, eye pain, and double vision following a sinus infection.
2. He had nasal congestion and facial pain for the past week that was being treated with oral antibiotics; he now also has a high fever.
3. On exam, the right eye shows marked eyelid swelling and erythema, proptosis, restricted and painful eye movements in all directions, and decreased visual acuity (20/80 from baseline 20/20); fever of 39.2°C.
4. Labs show WBC of 18,400 with bandemia; blood cultures are drawn before initiating broad-spectrum antibiotics.
5. CT scan of the orbits with contrast reveals fat stranding within the right orbit posterior to the orbital septum, opacification of the ethmoid sinuses, and a small subperiosteal abscess along the medial orbital wall.
6. He is admitted for IV vancomycin and ampicillin-sulbactam; ophthalmology and ENT consult for possible surgical drainage; symptoms improve significantly within 48 hours.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Orbital cellulitis is an infection of soft tissue POSTERIOR to the orbital septum and is a sight- and life-threatening emergency.
- Must be distinguished from preseptal (periorbital) cellulitis: preseptal lacks proptosis, ophthalmoplegia, painful eye movements, and vision changes.
- Most commonly arises from contiguous spread from ethmoid sinusitis in children; other sources include trauma, dental infection, hematogenous spread.
- Common pathogens: Staphylococcus aureus (including MRSA), Streptococcus species, Haemophilus influenzae, anaerobes; polymicrobial.
- Complications: subperiosteal abscess, cavernous sinus thrombosis, meningitis, vision loss; treatment with IV broad-spectrum antibiotics and surgical drainage if abscess or no improvement within 24-48 hours.

CASE_ID: 367
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
HIV/AIDS

ALIASES:
acquired immunodeficiency syndrome
human immunodeficiency virus infection
AIDS-defining illness

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old man presents with several weeks of fever, weight loss of 18 pounds, oral thrush, and white patches on the lateral tongue that cannot be scraped off.
2. He has had multiple male sexual partners without consistent condom use over the past an abnormal value and has never been tested for sexually transmitted infections.
3. On exam, he has temporal wasting, oropharyngeal pseudomembranous candidiasis, white corrugated plaques on the lateral tongue, and generalized lymphadenopathy; lung auscultation reveals fine bibasilar crackles with mild hypoxemia (SpO2 91%).
4. Labs show pancytopenia, an elevated LDH, and a CD4 count of 88 cells/microL; HIV-1/2 antigen/antibody combination test is positive with high viral load (482,000 copies/mL).
5. Chest CT shows diffuse bilateral ground-glass opacities with a perihilar distribution.
6. Bronchoalveolar lavage with silver stain reveals cup-shaped cysts of Pneumocystis jirovecii; he is treated with TMP-SMX and corticosteroids, with initiation of antiretroviral therapy with bictegravir-emtricitabine-tenofovir alafenamide after 2 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- HIV is an RNA retrovirus that infects CD4+ T cells via gp120 binding to CD4 and CCR5/CXCR4 coreceptors; progressive CD4 decline leads to immunodeficiency and opportunistic infections.
- AIDS is defined by CD4 <200 cells/microL OR an AIDS-defining illness regardless of CD4 count.
- Stages: acute retroviral syndrome (mononucleosis-like, 2-4 wks after infection), clinical latency (asymptomatic, years), AIDS (opportunistic infections, malignancies).
- Key opportunistic infections by CD4 count: PCP (<200), toxoplasmosis (<100), MAC and CMV (<50), cryptococcal meningitis, esophageal candidiasis.
- Diagnosis: HIV-1/2 antigen/antibody combination test (4th generation); confirmation with viral load and CD4; treat all HIV patients with ART (combination of integrase inhibitor + 2 NRTIs typically).

CASE_ID: 368
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Chlamydia trachomatis Infection

ALIASES:
chlamydia
chlamydial cervicitis
chlamydial urethritis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old sexually active woman presents for evaluation of intermittent post-coital bleeding and increased vaginal discharge over the past month.
2. She has had two new sexual partners in the past an abnormal value and uses oral contraceptives; she does not use condoms consistently.
3. On exam, the cervix appears friable with mucopurulent endocervical discharge; bimanual exam reveals no adnexal or cervical motion tenderness; vaginal pH is 4.0 with no fishy odor.
4. Wet mount shows numerous WBCs without trichomonads or clue cells; KOH prep is negative.
5. Nucleic acid amplification test (NAAT) of a vaginal swab is positive for Chlamydia trachomatis and negative for Neisseria gonorrhoeae.
6. She is treated with doxycycline for 7 days; her sexual partners are notified for expedited partner therapy; she is counseled about reinfection risk, screened for other STIs, and educated about prevention.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Chlamydia trachomatis is the most common bacterial sexually transmitted infection in the United States; an obligate intracellular gram-negative-like bacterium that lacks peptidoglycan.
- Often ASYMPTOMATIC, especially in women; screening recommended annually in sexually active women under 25 and in older women with risk factors.
- Manifestations: cervicitis, urethritis, PID, salpingitis (with tubal factor infertility, ectopic pregnancy), reactive arthritis (HLA-B27), Fitz-Hugh-Curtis syndrome (perihepatitis), neonatal conjunctivitis and pneumonia from perinatal transmission.
- LGV (serovars L1-L3): tropical disease with inguinal buboes and proctocolitis (especially in MSM).
- Treatment: doxycycline for 7 days (preferred); azithromycin single dose (alternative, used in pregnancy); test of cure in 3 months for reinfection.

CASE_ID: 369
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Trichomoniasis

ALIASES:
trich
Trichomonas vaginalis infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with a 2-week history of frothy, foul-smelling yellow-green vaginal discharge accompanied by vulvar itching and burning with urination.
2. She has multiple new sexual partners over the past an abnormal value and does not consistently use condoms.
3. On exam, copious frothy yellow-green discharge is noted in the vaginal vault; the cervix has scattered punctate hemorrhages (strawberry cervix); vaginal pH is 6.0; KOH "whiff test" is positive.
4. Wet mount microscopy reveals motile flagellated organisms approximately the size of a leukocyte; numerous WBCs are present.
5. NAAT confirms Trichomonas vaginalis and is negative for chlamydia and gonorrhea; HIV testing is offered and pending.
6. She is treated with a single 2-gram oral dose of metronidazole and counseled to abstain from alcohol for 72 hours; her sexual partners are also treated to prevent reinfection.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Trichomonas vaginalis is a sexually transmitted protozoan (a flagellated anaerobic protozoan with characteristic motility); the most common non-viral STI worldwide.
- Classic findings: frothy yellow-green malodorous discharge, vulvovaginitis, dysuria, post-coital bleeding; strawberry cervix (colpitis macularis) is specific but not sensitive.
- Vaginal pH typically >4.5; wet mount shows motile trichomonads; NAAT is most sensitive.
- Often asymptomatic in men (urethritis) but can transmit to partners; increases risk of HIV acquisition and adverse pregnancy outcomes (preterm birth).
- Treatment: metronidazole or tinidazole (single dose or 7-day course); treat sexual partners simultaneously; avoid alcohol with metronidazole (disulfiram-like reaction).

CASE_ID: 370
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Bacterial Vaginosis

ALIASES:
BV
Gardnerella vaginitis
nonspecific vaginitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 31-year-old woman presents with a 10-day history of thin grayish vaginal discharge with a strong fishy odor that is most noticeable after sexual intercourse.
2. She has been in a stable monogamous relationship and is using an IUD for contraception; she recently started using a new scented feminine wash.
3. On exam, there is a thin homogeneous gray-white discharge coating the vaginal walls; no significant erythema, edema, or inflammation; no cervical motion tenderness or adnexal tenderness.
4. Vaginal pH is 5.5 (elevated); the whiff test (10% KOH on the discharge) produces a strong amine fishy odor.
5. Wet mount microscopy reveals clue cells (vaginal epithelial cells with adherent bacteria obscuring their borders) comprising more than 20% of the epithelial cells, with a paucity of lactobacilli; no WBCs or trichomonads.
6. She meets Amsel criteria (3 of 4: thin discharge, pH >4.5, positive whiff test, clue cells); she is treated with oral metronidazole for 7 days with resolution of symptoms.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Bacterial vaginosis is a polymicrobial overgrowth of anaerobic and facultative bacteria (Gardnerella vaginalis, Atopobium, Prevotella) with loss of normal lactobacilli; NOT considered an STI but associated with sexual activity.
- Amsel criteria (3 of 4 required): thin homogeneous discharge, vaginal pH >4.5, positive whiff test with KOH, clue cells on microscopy.
- Distinguish from other causes of vaginitis: candidiasis (curd-like discharge, normal pH, pseudohyphae), trichomoniasis (frothy yellow-green, pH >4.5, strawberry cervix, motile trichomonads).
- Complications: increased risk of preterm labor in pregnancy, post-procedural infections, increased susceptibility to STIs including HIV.
- Treatment: oral or vaginal metronidazole or clindamycin; treatment of male partners NOT recommended (does not reduce recurrence).

CASE_ID: 371
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Lymphogranuloma Venereum

ALIASES:
LGV
chlamydia trachomatis L serovar
tropical bubo

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old man who has sex with men presents with 2 weeks of right groin swelling and pain, accompanied by anorectal pain, mucopurulent discharge, and tenesmus.
2. He returned an abnormal value ago from a 1-month trip to West Africa where he had unprotected receptive anal intercourse with multiple partners; he had a transient painless genital ulcer an abnormal value ago that resolved without treatment.
3. On exam, several markedly enlarged tender right inguinal and femoral lymph nodes are present, divided by the inguinal ligament (groove sign); some nodes are fluctuant.
4. Anoscopy reveals friable rectal mucosa with mucopurulent discharge consistent with proctocolitis.
5. NAAT for Chlamydia trachomatis on a rectal swab is positive; genotyping confirms an L serovar; HIV testing is negative.
6. He is treated with doxycycline for 21 days; his sexual partners are notified, and screening for other STIs is performed.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Lymphogranuloma venereum is caused by Chlamydia trachomatis serovars L1, L2, and L3 (different from the D-K serovars causing typical chlamydia).
- Three stages: primary (small painless genital ulcer that often goes unnoticed, heals spontaneously), secondary (painful inguinal lymphadenopathy with buboes; "groove sign" - lymphadenopathy above and below the inguinal ligament), tertiary (chronic proctocolitis, strictures, fistulas, elephantiasis).
- Increasingly recognized as causing proctocolitis in men who have sex with men, mimicking inflammatory bowel disease.
- Diagnosis: NAAT for C. trachomatis with L serovar genotyping (when available).
- Treatment: doxycycline for 21 days (longer course than uncomplicated chlamydia); bubo aspiration may be required.

CASE_ID: 372
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Stevens-Johnson Syndrome

ALIASES:
SJS
toxic epidermal necrolysis
SJS/TEN spectrum

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old man presents with 3 days of fever, malaise, sore throat, and now a rapidly spreading painful red rash with blistering involving his face, trunk, and mouth.
2. He was started on lamotrigine an abnormal value ago for newly diagnosed epilepsy; the dose was titrated faster than recommended.
3. On exam, temperature is 38.8°C; there is a confluent erythematous rash with flaccid bullae on the trunk and proximal extremities, with skin sloughing covering approximately 8% of body surface area; the lips, oral mucosa, and conjunctivae are eroded and crusted; skin shearing with gentle pressure is positive.
4. Labs show leukopenia, elevated transaminases, and electrolyte abnormalities; SCORTEN score predicts moderate-to-severe disease.
5. Skin biopsy reveals full-thickness epidermal necrosis with minimal dermal infiltrate and subepidermal blister formation.
6. Lamotrigine is discontinued, and he is transferred to a burn unit for supportive care; aggressive fluid resuscitation, wound care, nutrition, and ophthalmology consultation for ocular involvement are initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- SJS and toxic epidermal necrolysis (TEN) are severe mucocutaneous reactions on a spectrum: SJS involves <10% body surface area, SJS/TEN overlap 10-30%, TEN >30%.
- Most commonly drug-induced (within 1-3 weeks of starting): sulfonamides, anticonvulsants (lamotrigine, carbamazepine, phenytoin), allopurinol, NSAIDs, antibiotics; less commonly infections (Mycoplasma).
- Hallmark: painful skin with rapid progression, mucosal involvement (at least 2 sites - eyes, mouth, genitals), positive Nikolsky sign (skin sloughs with lateral pressure), full-thickness epidermal necrosis.
- HLA-B*15:02 (Han Chinese, Southeast Asian) increases risk of carbamazepine SJS; HLA-B*58:01 increases risk of allopurinol SJS.
- Treatment: discontinue the culprit drug immediately; burn unit or ICU level care; supportive (fluids, wound care, infection prevention, nutrition, ophthalmologic care); IVIG or cyclosporine in severe cases.

CASE_ID: 373
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pityriasis Rosea

ALIASES:
PR
herald patch rash

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 21-year-old college student presents with a 2-week history of an itchy rash that began as a single oval salmon-colored patch on her trunk and then spread to multiple smaller patches a week later.
2. She had a mild upper respiratory illness an abnormal value ago that resolved without treatment; she has not started any new medications, soaps, or detergents.
3. On exam, multiple oval pink macules and patches are present on the trunk, oriented along the lines of skin cleavage and arranged in a Christmas tree pattern on the back; a larger pre-existing patch is visible on the right side of the upper abdomen; fine collarette scale is seen at the edges of the lesions.
4. The face and acral surfaces are largely spared; she has no mucosal involvement, no genital lesions.
5. KOH preparation and RPR are negative, excluding tinea and secondary syphilis.
6. She is reassured that the eruption is self-limited; symptomatic treatment with low-potency topical corticosteroids and oral antihistamines is provided, and the rash resolves spontaneously over 6 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Pityriasis rosea is a self-limited papulosquamous eruption most likely associated with reactivation of human herpesvirus 6 or 7.
- Classic course: a single larger "herald patch" (2-5 cm, oval, scaly) precedes the generalized eruption by days to weeks.
- The widespread rash consists of small oval pink salmon-colored patches with fine collarette scale, oriented along lines of skin cleavage, classically described as a "Christmas tree" pattern on the back.
- ALWAYS rule out secondary syphilis (RPR) and tinea (KOH); palmar/plantar involvement, lymphadenopathy, or mucosal lesions argue against pityriasis rosea.
- Self-limited resolution in 6-8 weeks; treatment is symptomatic (antihistamines, low-potency topical steroids); reassure that recurrence is rare.

CASE_ID: 374
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Drug Reaction with Eosinophilia and Systemic Symptoms

ALIASES:
DRESS syndrome
drug hypersensitivity syndrome
DIHS

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old woman presents with 5 days of fever, a widespread morbilliform rash, facial swelling, and increasing fatigue.
2. She started phenytoin an abnormal value ago after a new seizure disorder; she also recently started allopurinol for gout.
3. On exam, temperature is 39.4°C; there is a confluent macular and papular erythematous rash involving more than 50% of the body surface area with marked facial edema; cervical lymphadenopathy is present; oral mucosa is mildly affected without significant erosion.
4. Labs show WBC of 18,400 with 22% eosinophils (absolute eosinophil count 4,000); AST 1,840, ALT 1,720; creatinine elevated to an abnormal value; atypical lymphocytes are present on peripheral smear; HHV-6 reactivation is suspected and confirmed serologically.
5. Skin biopsy shows superficial perivascular lymphocytic infiltrate with eosinophils and dyskeratotic keratinocytes.
6. Phenytoin and allopurinol are both discontinued; she is started on systemic corticosteroids with gradual taper over weeks; rash, hepatitis, and renal injury slowly resolve, but she requires long-term monitoring for delayed autoimmune sequelae.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- DRESS syndrome is a severe systemic hypersensitivity drug reaction with delayed onset (2-8 weeks after starting the offending drug).
- Hallmark features (RegiSCAR criteria): fever, lymphadenopathy, peripheral eosinophilia, atypical lymphocytes, internal organ involvement (most commonly liver), and a widespread morbilliform rash.
- Common culprits: aromatic anticonvulsants (phenytoin, carbamazepine, lamotrigine), allopurinol, sulfasalazine, dapsone, vancomycin, antiretrovirals (abacavir).
- Pathogenesis involves drug-specific T-cell reactivity and reactivation of herpes viruses (HHV-6 most commonly, also EBV, CMV).
- Treatment: immediate discontinuation of the offending drug; systemic corticosteroids (slow taper over weeks); IVIG or cyclosporine for refractory cases; long-term surveillance for autoimmune sequelae (thyroiditis, type 1 diabetes).

CASE_ID: 375
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Pilonidal Disease

ALIASES:
pilonidal cyst
pilonidal abscess
pilonidal sinus

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old man presents with 3 days of severe pain and swelling near the top of his gluteal cleft, with intermittent drainage of pus over the past 24 hours.
2. He is a long-distance truck driver, sits for many hours each day, has dense body hair, and reports two prior similar episodes that resolved with antibiotic courses.
3. On exam, there is a tender, fluctuant an abnormal value erythematous fluctuant mass located in the midline of the upper gluteal cleft, approximately an abnormal value above the anus; multiple midline pits are visible with tufts of hair protruding; a small amount of purulent drainage is expressed with gentle palpation.
4. He is afebrile and otherwise well; surrounding cellulitis extends approximately an abnormal value beyond the fluctuant area.
5. Bedside ultrasound confirms a subcutaneous fluid collection without communication with the rectum or sacrum.
6. He undergoes incision and drainage of the abscess with packing of the cavity; oral antibiotics covering skin flora are prescribed, and he is counseled on hair removal in the area and definitive surgical excision after the acute infection resolves.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Pilonidal disease is a chronic inflammatory condition of the natal cleft caused by ingrown hairs creating sinus tracts in the subcutaneous tissue.
- Risk factors: young adult males, dense body hair, obesity, prolonged sitting (truckers, students), poor hygiene, hyperhidrosis.
- Classic location: midline of the upper gluteal cleft (sacrococcygeal area) with characteristic midline pits often containing protruding hairs.
- Spectrum: acute abscess (presents with painful fluctuant mass), chronic sinus disease, complex recurrent disease.
- Management: incision and drainage for acute abscess; definitive excision of sinus tracts after acute infection resolves; meticulous hygiene and hair removal in the area to prevent recurrence.

CASE_ID: 376
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Cholera

ALIASES:
Vibrio cholerae infection
cholera gravis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old man presents with profuse watery diarrhea and vomiting that began suddenly 12 hours ago and has not stopped.
2. He returned 3 days ago from a relief trip to a region experiencing flooding and a diarrheal outbreak, where he drank from local water sources.
3. On exam, he is markedly dehydrated with sunken eyes, poor skin turgor, tachycardia, and hypotension; he has passed more than 6 liters of stool since onset.
4. The stool is described as painless, odorless, and containing flecks of mucus with the appearance of cloudy water.
5. Labs show a non-anion gap metabolic acidosis, hypokalemia, and acute kidney injury from volume depletion; stool dark-field microscopy reveals darting motile curved gram-negative rods.
6. He is treated with aggressive oral and IV rehydration with electrolyte solutions and a single dose of doxycycline, with rapid clinical recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Cholera is caused by Vibrio cholerae, a comma-shaped oxidase-positive gram-negative rod transmitted via contaminated water or food.
- The cholera toxin (an AB toxin) permanently activates Gs, increasing cAMP and driving massive chloride and water secretion into the gut lumen.
- Hallmark: voluminous painless "rice-water" stool leading to rapid, severe dehydration and hypovolemic shock.
- Diagnosis is largely clinical in an outbreak; stool microscopy shows darting motility; culture on TCBS agar.
- Treatment centers on aggressive rehydration (oral rehydration solution exploits intact glucose-coupled sodium absorption); antibiotics (doxycycline, azithromycin) shorten the course.

CASE_ID: 377
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Typhoid Fever

ALIASES:
enteric fever
Salmonella typhi infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old woman presents with one week of progressively rising fever, headache, abdominal pain, and constipation followed by diarrhea.
2. She returned 10 days ago from visiting family in South Asia, where she ate street food and did not receive any pre-travel vaccinations.
3. On exam, her temperature is 39.6°C with a relatively slow pulse of 78 bpm; she has mild hepatosplenomegaly and blanching rose-colored macules on her trunk.
4. Labs show leukopenia with a left shift, mildly elevated transaminases, and anemia.
5. Blood cultures drawn in the first week grow a motile gram-negative rod; bone marrow culture is more sensitive.
6. She is treated with ceftriaxone with gradual defervescence; she is counseled on the risk of becoming a chronic gallbladder carrier.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Typhoid (enteric) fever is caused by Salmonella enterica serotype Typhi, transmitted by the fecal-oral route via contaminated food or water.
- Classic features: stepwise rising fever, relative bradycardia (Faget sign), abdominal pain, rose spots (blanching macules on trunk), hepatosplenomegaly.
- Bowel pattern often shifts from constipation early to "pea soup" diarrhea later; complications include intestinal perforation and hemorrhage in the third week.
- Diagnosis: blood culture (positive early); bone marrow culture is the most sensitive; stool culture positive later.
- Chronic carriers harbor the organism in the gallbladder (associated with gallstones); the classic example is "Typhoid Mary"; treatment with fluoroquinolones or ceftriaxone, with cholecystectomy for chronic carriers.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 378
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Leptospirosis

ALIASES:
Weil disease
Leptospira infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 29-year-old man presents with abrupt onset high fever, severe muscle aches especially in the calves, headache, and notably red eyes.
2. He participated in a triathlon involving a freshwater swim two weeks ago and also cleans out a rodent-infested barn on his property.
3. On exam, he has conjunctival suffusion without discharge, calf tenderness, mild jaundice, and hepatosplenomegaly.
4. Labs show elevated bilirubin with relatively modest transaminase elevation, acute kidney injury, thrombocytopenia, and elevated creatine kinase.
5. Microscopic agglutination testing and PCR confirm Leptospira infection.
6. He is treated with IV penicillin (with monitoring for a Jarisch-Herxheimer reaction) and supportive care for his renal dysfunction, with full recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Leptospirosis is a zoonotic spirochetal infection acquired through contact with water or soil contaminated by the urine of infected animals (especially rodents).
- Risk activities: freshwater exposure (triathletes, kayakers), farming, sewage work, tropical travel.
- Anicteric phase: flu-like illness with conjunctival suffusion (red eyes without discharge) and severe myalgias (classically calf muscles).
- Weil disease (severe form): jaundice with disproportionately mild transaminase elevation, acute kidney injury, hemorrhage, and pulmonary involvement.
- Diagnosis by serology (microscopic agglutination test) or PCR; treatment with penicillin or doxycycline; watch for Jarisch-Herxheimer reaction.

CASE_ID: 379
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Brucellosis

ALIASES:
undulant fever
Malta fever
Brucella infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 45-year-old man presents with several weeks of intermittent fevers that rise and fall, drenching night sweats, fatigue, and joint pains.
2. He works as a goat farmer and makes his own unpasteurized cheese, which he consumes regularly.
3. On exam, he has a low-grade fever, hepatosplenomegaly, and tenderness over the sacroiliac joints; testicular swelling is also noted.
4. Labs show mild pancytopenia and elevated transaminases; blood cultures held for prolonged incubation grow small gram-negative coccobacilli.
5. Serum agglutination titers for Brucella are markedly elevated.
6. He is treated with combination therapy of doxycycline plus rifampin for 6 weeks with resolution of symptoms.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Brucellosis is a zoonosis caused by Brucella species (small gram-negative coccobacilli), acquired from unpasteurized dairy products or contact with infected livestock.
- Classic "undulant fever": fevers that rise and fall in waves, with night sweats described as having a peculiar musty odor.
- Has a predilection for the reticuloendothelial system and bone/joints: sacroiliitis, spondylitis, hepatosplenomegaly, epididymo-orchitis.
- A facultative intracellular organism that survives in macrophages, causing chronic and relapsing infection; blood cultures require prolonged incubation.
- Treatment requires combination antibiotics (doxycycline plus rifampin or an aminoglycoside) for a prolonged course to prevent relapse.

CASE_ID: 380
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Histoplasmosis

ALIASES:
Histoplasma capsulatum infection
Ohio River Valley fever

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old man presents with several weeks of fever, dry cough, fatigue, and weight loss; he has advanced HIV with a CD4 count of 45.
2. He lives in the Mississippi River Valley and recently explored caves with large bat populations as a hobby.
3. On exam, he has hepatosplenomegaly, diffuse lymphadenopathy, and oral ulcers; scattered crackles are heard in both lungs.
4. Labs show pancytopenia and markedly elevated ferritin and LDH; chest imaging reveals diffuse reticulonodular infiltrates with mediastinal lymphadenopathy.
5. Urine Histoplasma antigen is positive; peripheral blood smear and bone marrow show small intracellular yeast within macrophages.
6. He is treated with liposomal amphotericin B followed by itraconazole maintenance, along with optimization of his antiretroviral therapy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Histoplasma capsulatum is a dimorphic fungus endemic to the Ohio and Mississippi River Valleys; found in soil enriched with bird or bat droppings (caves, chicken coops).
- Inhaled microconidia convert to yeast at body temperature and are engulfed by macrophages; appears as small oval yeast inside macrophages.
- Immunocompetent hosts: usually asymptomatic or a self-limited flu-like illness; can mimic tuberculosis with cavitary lung disease.
- Immunocompromised hosts (especially AIDS with CD4 <150): disseminated disease with hepatosplenomegaly, pancytopenia, mucosal ulcers, and adrenal involvement.
- Diagnosis via urine/serum antigen; treatment with amphotericin B for severe disease followed by itraconazole.

CASE_ID: 381
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Coccidioidomycosis

ALIASES:
Valley fever
San Joaquin Valley fever
Coccidioides infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old woman presents with three weeks of fever, cough, pleuritic chest pain, fatigue, and painful red nodules on her shins.
2. She recently moved to Arizona and started a job at a construction site with significant dust exposure.
3. On exam, she has tender erythematous nodules on the anterior shins (erythema nodosum) and migratory arthralgias; lungs have scattered crackles.
4. Labs show peripheral eosinophilia; chest X-ray reveals a focal infiltrate with hilar lymphadenopathy and a thin-walled cavity.
5. Serologic testing for Coccidioides is positive; biopsy of a lung lesion would show large spherules containing endospores.
6. Given her immunocompetent status and moderate symptoms, she is treated with fluconazole and monitored for resolution.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Coccidioidomycosis is caused by Coccidioides immitis/posadasii, dimorphic fungi endemic to the southwestern US (Arizona, California's San Joaquin Valley).
- Acquired by inhaling arthroconidia from disrupted soil (dust storms, construction, archaeology); converts to spherules filled with endospores in tissue.
- Primary infection: often asymptomatic or self-limited pneumonia; "desert rheumatism" triad of fever, arthralgias, and erythema nodosum or multiforme.
- Disseminated disease (higher risk in pregnant women, immunocompromised, and certain ethnic groups): meningitis, bone, and skin involvement.
- Diagnosis via serology or tissue showing spherules; treatment with azoles (fluconazole, itraconazole); amphotericin B for severe or disseminated disease, lifelong fluconazole for meningitis.

CASE_ID: 382
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Blastomycosis

ALIASES:
Blastomyces dermatitidis infection
North American blastomycosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 50-year-old man presents with several weeks of cough, fever, weight loss, and well-demarcated skin lesions on his face and arms.
2. He is an avid hunter and camper in Wisconsin who frequently spends time along riverbanks and in wooded areas.
3. On exam, he has verrucous, crusted skin lesions with heaped-up borders and microabscesses; lung auscultation reveals focal crackles.
4. Chest X-ray shows alveolar infiltrates with mass-like consolidation that mimics malignancy; a lytic bone lesion is noted on imaging of a painful rib.
5. Sputum and skin biopsy reveal broad-based budding yeast with thick refractile walls.
6. He is treated with itraconazole for mild-to-moderate disease, with amphotericin B reserved for severe or CNS involvement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Blastomyces dermatitidis is a dimorphic fungus endemic to the Great Lakes, Ohio, and Mississippi River regions; associated with moist soil and decaying wood.
- Inhaled conidia convert to broad-based budding yeast with thick walls in tissue (a key morphologic identifier).
- Classic triad of involvement: lungs (pneumonia, can mimic malignancy), skin (verrucous or ulcerated lesions), and bone (osteomyelitis).
- Distinguish morphologically: Blastomyces has broad-based buds; Cryptococcus has narrow-based buds with a capsule; Histoplasma is small and intracellular.
- Treatment: itraconazole for mild-moderate; amphotericin B for severe, disseminated, or CNS disease.

CASE_ID: 383
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Giardiasis

ALIASES:
Giardia lamblia infection
beaver fever

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 31-year-old man presents with two weeks of foul-smelling greasy diarrhea, bloating, excessive flatulence, and crampy abdominal pain.
2. He went backpacking in the mountains last month and drank from a stream without filtering or boiling the water.
3. On exam, he has mild diffuse abdominal tenderness and appears to have lost a few pounds; there is no fever or blood in the stool.
4. Stool studies show no blood or leukocytes; a stool ova and parasite exam and stool antigen test are performed.
5. Stool antigen testing (ELISA) is positive for Giardia; microscopy reveals pear-shaped trophozoites with two nuclei resembling a face.
6. He is treated with a course of tinidazole or metronidazole with resolution of his symptoms.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Giardiasis is caused by Giardia lamblia (duodenalis), a flagellated protozoan transmitted via the fecal-oral route, classically from contaminated freshwater.
- Risk factors: backpackers/hikers drinking unfiltered stream water, daycare attendees, travelers, men who have sex with men.
- Causes malabsorptive, fatty (steatorrhea), foul-smelling diarrhea with bloating and flatulence; no blood or fever (non-invasive).
- Trophozoites have a characteristic pear shape with two nuclei (a "face"); stool antigen ELISA is more sensitive than microscopy.
- Treatment: tinidazole (single dose) or metronidazole; nitazoxanide is an alternative.

CASE_ID: 384
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Amebiasis

ALIASES:
Entamoeba histolytica infection
amebic dysentery
amebic liver abscess

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 40-year-old man presents with several weeks of bloody, mucoid diarrhea, abdominal pain, and now right upper quadrant pain with fever.
2. He immigrated from a tropical region an abnormal value ago and has had intermittent loose stools since arrival.
3. On exam, he is febrile with tender hepatomegaly and right upper quadrant tenderness; bowel sounds are active.
4. Labs show leukocytosis without eosinophilia and elevated alkaline phosphatase; ultrasound reveals a single large hypoechoic lesion in the right lobe of the liver.
5. Stool antigen testing and serology are positive for Entamoeba histolytica; aspiration of the abscess would reveal "anchovy paste" material.
6. He is treated with metronidazole for the invasive infection followed by a luminal agent (paromomycin) to eradicate intestinal cysts.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Amebiasis is caused by Entamoeba histolytica, transmitted via the fecal-oral route by ingestion of cysts from contaminated food or water.
- Intestinal disease: bloody diarrhea (dysentery) with flask-shaped colonic ulcers; can mimic inflammatory bowel disease (do NOT give steroids).
- Amebic liver abscess: typically a single lesion in the right lobe with "anchovy paste" contents; serology is highly sensitive.
- Trophozoites with ingested red blood cells are diagnostic; distinguish from non-pathogenic Entamoeba dispar.
- Treatment: metronidazole or tinidazole for invasive disease, ALWAYS followed by a luminal agent (paromomycin or diloxanide) to clear cysts and prevent relapse.

CASE_ID: 385
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Cryptosporidiosis

ALIASES:
Cryptosporidium infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 36-year-old man with AIDS presents with several weeks of profuse, watery, non-bloody diarrhea, abdominal cramping, and weight loss.
2. His most recent CD4 count was 35 and he has been inconsistent with antiretroviral therapy.
3. On exam, he is thin and dehydrated with diffuse mild abdominal tenderness; there is no fever or blood in the stool.
4. He is passing more than 2 liters of watery stool daily, raising concern for severe volume loss.
5. Modified acid-fast staining of the stool reveals small red oocysts; stool antigen testing confirms Cryptosporidium.
6. He is treated with aggressive rehydration and nitazoxanide, but the key intervention is immune reconstitution by restarting effective antiretroviral therapy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Cryptosporidium is an intracellular protozoan transmitted via the fecal-oral route, often through contaminated water (resistant to chlorination; outbreaks from pools and municipal water).
- In immunocompetent hosts: self-limited watery diarrhea.
- In AIDS patients (CD4 <100): chronic, profuse, life-threatening watery diarrhea with severe dehydration and wasting.
- Diagnosis: modified acid-fast stain shows oocysts; stool antigen immunoassays are sensitive.
- Treatment: nitazoxanide and supportive rehydration, but immune reconstitution with antiretroviral therapy is the definitive treatment in AIDS.
