CASE_ID: 386
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Cytomegalovirus Infection

ALIASES:
CMV infection
cytomegalovirus retinitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 41-year-old man with AIDS presents with several days of progressive blurry vision and floaters in his right eye.
2. His CD4 count is 28 and he has not been taking antiretroviral therapy or prophylaxis.
3. On exam, dilated funduscopy reveals fluffy yellow-white retinal infiltrates with associated hemorrhages along the vascular arcades in a "pizza pie" or "cheese and ketchup" appearance.
4. He also reports odynophagia, and endoscopy reveals large, deep, linear esophageal ulcers.
5. Biopsy of the esophageal ulcer shows enlarged cells with intranuclear "owl eye" inclusions; CMV PCR is markedly positive.
6. He is treated with IV ganciclovir (or valganciclovir) and started on antiretroviral therapy with ophthalmology co-management to prevent retinal detachment.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- CMV is a herpesvirus that establishes lifelong latency; reactivation causes disease primarily in immunocompromised hosts (AIDS with CD4 <50, transplant recipients).
- CMV retinitis: the most common cause of vision loss in AIDS; "pizza pie" retinopathy (hemorrhages and exudates); a sight-threatening emergency.
- Other manifestations: esophagitis/colitis (large linear ulcers), pneumonitis (especially post-transplant), and congenital CMV (most common congenital infection).
- Histology: cytomegaly with "owl eye" intranuclear inclusions.
- Treatment: ganciclovir/valganciclovir first-line; foscarnet for resistant disease or ganciclovir-induced myelosuppression; restore immune function.

CASE_ID: 387
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Progressive Multifocal Leukoencephalopathy

ALIASES:
PML
JC virus encephalopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 44-year-old woman with multiple sclerosis on natalizumab presents with several weeks of progressive right-sided weakness, visual field loss, and difficulty speaking.
2. She has otherwise been stable on her infusions for the past an abnormal value and had a positive JC virus antibody at her last screening.
3. On exam, she has a right homonymous hemianopia, expressive aphasia, and right hemiparesis without fever or signs of meningismus.
4. MRI of the brain reveals multifocal, asymmetric, non-enhancing white matter lesions without mass effect, predominantly in the subcortical regions.
5. CSF PCR is positive for JC virus DNA.
6. Natalizumab is discontinued and plasma exchange is used to remove the drug; immune reconstitution is monitored carefully for inflammatory worsening (IRIS).

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy is caused by reactivation of JC polyomavirus, leading to lytic infection of oligodendrocytes and demyelination.
- Occurs almost exclusively in immunosuppressed patients: AIDS (CD4 <200), and those on immunomodulators (especially natalizumab, rituximab).
- Presents with subacute progressive focal neurologic deficits (weakness, visual loss, cognitive decline) WITHOUT fever or signs of mass effect.
- MRI shows multifocal asymmetric white matter lesions that do NOT enhance and have no mass effect.
- Diagnosis: JC virus PCR in CSF; treatment is restoration of immune function (stop immunosuppressant, ART in HIV); no specific antiviral exists.

CASE_ID: 388
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Tetanus

ALIASES:
Clostridium tetani infection
lockjaw

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old farmer presents with progressive muscle stiffness and painful spasms over several days.
2. He recently had a deep puncture wound that was cleaned at home, and his vaccination history is incomplete.
3. Examination shows trismus, neck rigidity, and stimulus-triggered muscle spasms while mental status remains intact.
4. He is alert and oriented throughout, with no fever initially; autonomic instability with labile blood pressure and tachycardia develops.
5. The diagnosis is clinical; the puncture wound shows minimal inflammation but is the presumed source.
6. He is treated with wound debridement, human tetanus immunoglobulin, metronidazole, benzodiazepines for spasms, and supportive care in the ICU; he also receives the tetanus toxoid vaccine.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Tetanus is caused by Clostridium tetani, an anaerobic spore-forming gram-positive rod found in soil; spores germinate in deep, contaminated wounds (low oxygen).
- Tetanospasmin (an AB exotoxin) travels retrograde up motor neurons and blocks release of inhibitory neurotransmitters (GABA and glycine) from Renshaw cells, causing unopposed muscle contraction.
- Classic findings: trismus (lockjaw), risus sardonicus, opisthotonos, generalized spasms triggered by stimuli; patient remains conscious.
- Autonomic instability is a major cause of death in severe cases.
- Management: wound debridement, tetanus immunoglobulin (neutralizes unbound toxin), metronidazole, benzodiazepines, supportive care; prevention via vaccination is paramount.

CASE_ID: 389
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Botulism

ALIASES:
Clostridium botulinum intoxication
infant botulism

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 4-month-old infant is brought in for several days of poor feeding, weak cry, constipation, and progressive floppiness.
2. The parents recently started giving the infant honey as a soother; the baby was previously developing normally.
3. On exam, the infant is hypotonic ("floppy baby") with poor head control, ptosis, sluggishly reactive pupils, and a diminished gag reflex; sensation and alertness appear intact.
4. The weakness is symmetric and descending, beginning with the cranial nerves and progressing caudally.
5. Stool studies detect Clostridium botulinum toxin and organisms; the infant is monitored closely for respiratory failure.
6. The infant is treated with human-derived botulism immunoglobulin (BIG-IV) and supportive care including respiratory monitoring, with gradual recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Botulism is caused by botulinum toxin from Clostridium botulinum, an anaerobic spore-forming gram-positive rod.
- The toxin blocks presynaptic release of acetylcholine at the neuromuscular junction, causing flaccid paralysis (contrast with tetanus, which blocks inhibitory signals causing rigid paralysis).
- Infant botulism: ingestion of spores (classically from honey) that germinate in the immature gut; presents as a "floppy baby" with constipation, weak cry, poor feeding, and descending paralysis.
- Foodborne botulism (improperly canned foods): symmetric descending paralysis, diplopia, dysphagia, dysarthria; the "4 D's"; no fever, intact sensation.
- Treatment: infants receive human-derived immunoglobulin (BIG-IV); adults receive equine antitoxin; supportive respiratory care; do NOT give honey to infants under 1 year.

CASE_ID: 390
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Scabies

ALIASES:
Sarcoptes scabiei infestation
human itch mite

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old college student presents with two weeks of intense itching that is markedly worse at night and is disrupting his sleep.
2. Several of his roommates have developed similar symptoms, and he recently returned from a crowded hostel during travel.
3. On exam, there are small erythematous papules and thin serpiginous gray lines in the finger webs, flexor wrists, and around the waistline; excoriations are widespread.
4. The genitals show pruritic nodules, while the face and scalp are spared in this immunocompetent adult.
5. Skin scraping of a burrow examined under microscopy reveals mites, eggs, and fecal pellets (scybala).
6. He is treated with topical permethrin 5% applied from neck to toe overnight and repeated in one week; all close contacts are treated simultaneously and bedding is laundered in hot water.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Scabies is caused by the mite Sarcoptes scabiei, which burrows into the stratum corneum; transmitted by prolonged skin-to-skin contact.
- Hallmark: intense pruritus worse at night, with burrows in finger webs, wrists, axillae, waistline, and genitals; face and scalp spared in adults (but involved in infants).
- The itching is a delayed hypersensitivity reaction, so it can take weeks to appear in a first infestation.
- Crusted (Norwegian) scabies: hyperinfestation with thick scaly plaques in immunocompromised patients; highly contagious.
- Diagnosis by skin scraping showing mites, eggs, or feces; treatment with topical permethrin (or oral ivermectin); treat all close contacts and decontaminate fomites.

CASE_ID: 391
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Tinea Versicolor

ALIASES:
pityriasis versicolor
Malassezia infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old man presents with several months of slightly scaly discolored patches on his upper back and chest that became more noticeable after summer sun exposure.
2. He lives in a hot, humid climate and is otherwise healthy; the patches are mildly itchy but largely asymptomatic.
3. On exam, there are numerous well-demarcated, oval, hypopigmented and light-brown macules with a fine scale on the trunk and shoulders; gently scraping produces a powdery scale.
4. The patches fail to tan with sun exposure, making them more apparent against tanned skin.
5. Wood lamp examination shows a yellow-green fluorescence; KOH preparation reveals short hyphae and round yeast in a "spaghetti and meatballs" pattern.
6. He is treated with topical selenium sulfide and topical ketoconazole; he is counseled that repigmentation takes weeks to months even after successful treatment.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Tinea versicolor is a superficial fungal infection caused by Malassezia (formerly Pityrosporum), part of the normal skin flora that overgrows in hot, humid conditions.
- Despite the name "tinea," it is caused by yeast, not dermatophytes.
- Presents with hypopigmented or hyperpigmented scaly macules on the trunk; the yeast produces azelaic acid that inhibits melanocytes (causing hypopigmentation that does not tan).
- KOH prep shows the classic "spaghetti and meatballs" appearance (short hyphae and spores); Wood lamp may show yellow-green fluorescence.
- Treatment: topical antifungals (selenium sulfide, ketoconazole, zinc pyrithione); oral azoles for extensive disease; recurrence is common.

CASE_ID: 392
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Impetigo

ALIASES:
nonbullous impetigo
school sores

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-year-old boy is brought in with several itchy sores around his nose and mouth that began as small red bumps and then developed crusts over a few days.
2. He attends a daycare where a few other children have similar lesions; he is otherwise well and afebrile.
3. On exam, there are clustered erythematous lesions with golden-yellow honey-colored crusts on the perioral and perinasal skin; some lesions have ruptured to leave moist erosions.
4. There is mild regional lymphadenopathy but no systemic signs of infection.
5. Bacterial culture of the lesion exudate grows Staphylococcus aureus and group A Streptococcus.
6. He is treated with topical mupirocin for the localized lesions and counseled on hygiene to prevent spread; the family is warned about the small risk of post-streptococcal glomerulonephritis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Impetigo is a contagious superficial bacterial skin infection caused by Staphylococcus aureus and/or group A Streptococcus (Streptococcus pyogenes).
- Nonbullous impetigo (most common): honey-colored crusted lesions, often on the face; spreads easily among children.
- Bullous impetigo: caused by S. aureus exfoliative toxin, producing flaccid bullae that rupture.
- A potential complication of streptococcal impetigo is post-streptococcal glomerulonephritis (not rheumatic fever, which follows pharyngitis).
- Treatment: topical mupirocin for localized disease; oral antibiotics (cephalexin, dicloxacillin) for widespread or bullous disease.

CASE_ID: 393
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hidradenitis Suppurativa

ALIASES:
acne inversa
HS

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with recurrent painful boils in her armpits and groin that have been occurring for several years and intermittently drain foul-smelling pus.
2. She is obese, smokes cigarettes, and reports that the lesions flare cyclically and have left scarring; she has tried multiple antibiotic courses with only temporary relief.
3. On exam, there are inflamed nodules, abscesses, and interconnected sinus tracts with rope-like scarring in both axillae and the inguinal folds; open and double-headed comedones are present.
4. The lesions are localized to intertriginous, apocrine gland-bearing areas; there is no involvement of the face or trunk.
5. The diagnosis is clinical based on the characteristic lesions, location, and recurrent chronic course.
6. She is started on topical clindamycin, oral antibiotics, and lifestyle modification (smoking cessation, weight loss); adalimumab is added for moderate-to-severe disease.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hidradenitis suppurativa is a chronic inflammatory disorder of hair follicles in apocrine gland-bearing skin (axillae, groin, perineum, inframammary folds).
- Pathogenesis involves follicular occlusion and rupture with secondary inflammation; it is NOT primarily an infection.
- Risk factors: obesity, smoking, family history, female sex, and metabolic syndrome.
- Hallmark: recurrent painful nodules and abscesses progressing to sinus tracts and scarring; double-headed comedones are characteristic.
- Treatment is staged (Hurley staging): topical/oral antibiotics, anti-inflammatory agents, TNF inhibitors (adalimumab) for moderate-severe disease, and surgery for refractory tracts.

CASE_ID: 394
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Vitiligo

ALIASES:
vitiligo vulgaris
acquired depigmentation

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 30-year-old woman presents with progressively enlarging white patches on her hands, around her eyes, and on her knees over the past year.
2. She has a personal history of autoimmune hypothyroidism and a family history of similar skin changes; the patches are asymptomatic.
3. On exam, there are several well-demarcated, completely depigmented (chalk-white) macules and patches with a symmetric distribution over the dorsal hands, periorbital skin, and extensor knees.
4. Wood lamp examination accentuates the depigmented areas, confirming complete absence of melanin.
5. Thyroid function tests reveal elevated TSH consistent with her known autoimmune thyroiditis.
6. She is started on topical corticosteroids and topical calcineurin inhibitors with consideration of narrowband UVB phototherapy; she is counseled on sun protection and the chronic relapsing nature of the condition.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Vitiligo is an acquired autoimmune disorder characterized by destruction of melanocytes, leading to depigmented (not merely hypopigmented) macules.
- Strongly associated with other autoimmune diseases: autoimmune thyroid disease (most common), type 1 diabetes, pernicious anemia, Addison disease, alopecia areata.
- Lesions are chalk-white, well-demarcated, often symmetric, favoring the face, hands, and bony prominences; Wood lamp accentuates them.
- Distinguish from tinea versicolor (scaly, partial hypopigmentation) and pityriasis alba (ill-defined, in children with atopy).
- Treatment: topical corticosteroids, topical calcineurin inhibitors, phototherapy (narrowband UVB), and JAK inhibitors (ruxolitinib cream); depigmentation therapy for extensive disease.

CASE_ID: 395
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Actinic Keratosis

ALIASES:
solar keratosis
senile keratosis
precancerous keratosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old fair-skinned man presents with several rough, scaly spots on his face, scalp, and the back of his hands that he describes as feeling like sandpaper.
2. He worked outdoors as a roofer for an abnormal value and has a history of multiple blistering sunburns; some lesions are tender.
3. On exam, there are multiple erythematous macules and papules with adherent yellow-brown scale, primarily on sun-exposed areas; the lesions are often easier to feel than to see.
4. One lesion on the lower lip has become thickened and indurated, raising concern for progression.
5. Biopsy of the indurated lip lesion shows atypical keratinocytes confined to the lower epidermis with parakeratosis, confirming actinic keratosis without invasion.
6. He is treated with cryotherapy for individual lesions and topical 5-fluorouracil for field treatment; he is counseled on rigorous sun protection and regular skin surveillance.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Actinic keratosis is a premalignant lesion caused by chronic ultraviolet (sun) damage to keratinocytes.
- It is a precursor to squamous cell carcinoma; a small percentage progress to invasive SCC over time.
- Classic description: rough, scaly, "sandpaper-like" papules on sun-exposed skin (face, scalp, ears, dorsal hands) that are often more easily palpated than seen.
- Actinic cheilitis is the variant on the lower lip; induration or ulceration suggests progression to SCC.
- Treatment: cryotherapy for isolated lesions; field therapy (topical 5-FU, imiquimod, photodynamic therapy) for multiple lesions; sun protection for prevention.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 396
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pheochromocytoma Crisis

ALIASES:
catecholamine crisis
adrenal medullary tumor crisis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old woman presents to the ED with the sudden onset of a pounding headache, palpitations, profuse sweating, and a feeling of impending doom.
2. She reports recurrent similar episodes over the past several months, each lasting about 20 minutes; her blood pressure has been difficult to control on multiple agents.
3. On exam, her blood pressure is 224/an abnormal value with a heart rate of 132; she is diaphoretic, pale, and anxious; the episode was triggered shortly after she received a dose of metoclopramide.
4. Plasma free metanephrines and 24-hour urinary fractionated metanephrines are markedly elevated.
5. CT of the abdomen reveals a 4 cm heterogeneous, enhancing mass in the right adrenal gland.
6. She is started on phenoxybenzamine for alpha blockade first, and only after adequate alpha blockade is a beta blocker added; she undergoes laparoscopic adrenalectomy after appropriate preoperative preparation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Pheochromocytoma is a catecholamine-secreting tumor of adrenal medullary chromaffin cells; follows the "rule of 10s" (10% bilateral, 10% extra-adrenal, 10% malignant, 10% familial).
- Classic episodic triad: headache, palpitations, and diaphoresis, with paroxysmal hypertension.
- Associated syndromes: MEN 2A and 2B (RET mutation), von Hippel-Lindau, neurofibromatosis type 1.
- Diagnosis: elevated plasma free metanephrines and urinary fractionated metanephrines; imaging to localize; MIBG scan for extra-adrenal/metastatic.
- Treatment: alpha blockade FIRST (phenoxybenzamine) to prevent hypertensive crisis, THEN beta blockade; never give beta blockers alone (unopposed alpha causes severe hypertension); surgical resection is definitive.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 397
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Multiple Endocrine Neoplasia Type 2

ALIASES:
MEN 2
MEN2A
RET-associated syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 35-year-old man presents with episodes of headache, palpitations, and sweating, and is found to have a thyroid nodule and an elevated calcium level on routine labs.
2. His father died of a thyroid malignancy and his sister had an adrenal tumor removed; he reports a family history of similar problems across generations.
3. On exam, he has a firm thyroid nodule, hypertension, and otherwise unremarkable findings; there are no mucosal neuromas or marfanoid features.
4. Labs show elevated calcitonin, elevated plasma metanephrines, hypercalcemia, and an elevated PTH.
5. Genetic testing identifies a germline RET proto-oncogene mutation; fine-needle aspiration of the thyroid nodule confirms medullary thyroid carcinoma.
6. The pheochromocytoma is addressed first with alpha blockade and adrenalectomy, followed by total thyroidectomy for the medullary carcinoma and management of his primary hyperparathyroidism.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- MEN 2 is an autosomal dominant syndrome caused by germline mutations in the RET proto-oncogene.
- MEN 2A: medullary thyroid carcinoma + pheochromocytoma + primary hyperparathyroidism.
- MEN 2B: medullary thyroid carcinoma + pheochromocytoma + mucosal neuromas and a marfanoid habitus (no hyperparathyroidism).
- Medullary thyroid carcinoma (arising from calcitonin-secreting parafollicular C cells) is present in nearly all patients and is the major cause of mortality.
- When both a pheochromocytoma and another tumor are present, ALWAYS treat the pheochromocytoma first; prophylactic thyroidectomy is recommended for RET mutation carriers.

CASE_ID: 398
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Prolactinoma

ALIASES:
prolactin-secreting pituitary adenoma
lactotroph adenoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with several months of irregular menses progressing to absent periods, milky nipple discharge, and difficulty conceiving.
2. She also reports decreased libido and recent headaches; she is not pregnant and takes no medications known to raise prolactin.
3. On exam, expressible galactorrhea is present bilaterally; visual field testing by confrontation suggests a deficit in the temporal fields.
4. Labs show a markedly elevated serum prolactin of 240 ng/mL; pregnancy test, TSH, and renal function are normal.
5. MRI of the pituitary reveals a 1.2 cm sellar mass (macroadenoma) compressing the optic chiasm.
6. She is started on a dopamine agonist (cabergoline), which lowers prolactin, restores menses, and shrinks the tumor, avoiding the need for surgery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Prolactinoma is the most common functional pituitary adenoma.
- Hyperprolactinemia suppresses GnRH, causing hypogonadism: in women, amenorrhea, galactorrhea, and infertility; in men, decreased libido, erectile dysfunction, and gynecomastia.
- Macroadenomas (>1 cm) can cause mass effect: bitemporal hemianopia (optic chiasm compression) and headache.
- Always exclude other causes of elevated prolactin: pregnancy, hypothyroidism (elevated TRH stimulates prolactin), dopamine antagonists (antipsychotics, metoclopramide), renal failure, and stalk compression.
- First-line treatment is medical with dopamine agonists (cabergoline, bromocriptine), which both lower prolactin and shrink the tumor; surgery (transsphenoidal) is reserved for refractory or intolerant cases.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 399
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acromegaly Complications

ALIASES:
growth hormone excess
somatotroph adenoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old man presents because his wedding ring and shoes no longer fit and his facial appearance has gradually coarsened over several years.
2. He complains of headaches, excessive sweating, joint pains, and new tingling in both hands; old photographs confirm the slow change in his features.
3. On exam, he has enlarged hands and feet, a prominent jaw with widely spaced teeth, frontal bossing, an enlarged tongue, and a positive Tinel sign at both wrists; blood pressure is elevated.
4. Labs show an elevated insulin-like growth factor 1 (IGF-1) for age and an elevated fasting glucose; growth hormone fails to suppress after an oral glucose load.
5. MRI of the pituitary reveals a 1.5 cm macroadenoma.
6. He undergoes transsphenoidal resection of the adenoma; somatostatin analogs (octreotide) are used for residual disease, and he is screened for colon polyps and sleep apnea.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acromegaly results from excess growth hormone, almost always from a pituitary somatotroph adenoma, occurring in adults after epiphyseal closure (gigantism if before closure).
- Clinical features: enlargement of hands, feet, and facial bones; coarse features, macroglossia, prognathism, carpal tunnel syndrome, skin tags, hyperhidrosis.
- Systemic complications drive mortality: cardiomyopathy and heart failure, hypertension, diabetes/insulin resistance, obstructive sleep apnea, and increased risk of colon polyps and colorectal cancer.
- Diagnosis: elevated IGF-1 (best screening test); confirm with failure of GH to suppress during an oral glucose tolerance test (random GH is unreliable due to pulsatility).
- Treatment: transsphenoidal surgery (first-line); medical therapy with somatostatin analogs, GH receptor antagonist (pegvisomant), or dopamine agonists; radiation for refractory disease.

CASE_ID: 400
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Thyroid Storm

ALIASES:
thyrotoxic crisis
accelerated thyrotoxicosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 36-year-old woman with poorly controlled Graves disease is brought to the ED with high fever, agitation, and a racing heartbeat that began after she developed a urinary tract infection.
2. She had stopped taking her methimazole a few weeks ago because she was feeling better.
3. On exam, her temperature is 40.2°C, heart rate is 168 bpm and irregular, and blood pressure is 158/64; she is confused and agitated with a fine tremor, warm moist skin, lid lag, and a diffuse goiter with a bruit.
4. ECG reveals atrial fibrillation with rapid ventricular response; labs show suppressed TSH and markedly elevated free T4 and T3.
5. The Burch-Wartofsky score is high, supporting the clinical diagnosis of thyroid storm.
6. She is treated with a beta blocker (propranolol), a thionamide (propylthiouracil), iodine solution given AFTER the thionamide, hydrocortisone, and aggressive cooling and supportive care, with treatment of the precipitating infection.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Thyroid storm is a life-threatening, decompensated state of severe thyrotoxicosis, often precipitated by infection, surgery, trauma, iodine load, or thionamide withdrawal.
- Clinical hallmarks: hyperpyrexia, tachyarrhythmias (often atrial fibrillation), heart failure, agitation/delirium, and GI symptoms; the Burch-Wartofsky score aids diagnosis.
- It is a CLINICAL diagnosis; thyroid hormone levels are not necessarily higher than in uncomplicated hyperthyroidism.
- Treatment sequence (the order matters): beta blocker (propranolol also blocks peripheral T4-to-T3 conversion), then a thionamide (PTU blocks synthesis and peripheral conversion), then iodine (Wolff-Chaikoff effect) AFTER the thionamide to avoid fueling synthesis, plus glucocorticoids.
- Glucocorticoids reduce peripheral T4-to-T3 conversion and treat possible relative adrenal insufficiency; identify and treat the precipitant.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 401
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Rheumatic Fever

ALIASES:
acute rheumatic fever
rheumatic heart disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 9-year-old boy is brought in with fever, migratory joint pain affecting his knees and then his ankles, and involuntary jerking movements of his hands and face.
2. He had a sore throat about three weeks ago that was not treated with antibiotics.
3. On exam, he has a temperature of 38.5°C, swollen and tender large joints, a new high-pitched blowing holosystolic murmur at the apex, painless subcutaneous nodules over the elbows, and a non-pruritic rash with pink rings on his trunk.
4. Labs show elevated ESR and CRP, and an elevated anti-streptolysin O titer; throat culture is now negative but serology confirms recent streptococcal infection.
5. ECG shows a prolonged PR interval.
6. He is treated with anti-inflammatory therapy, penicillin to eradicate residual streptococci, and is started on long-term antibiotic prophylaxis to prevent recurrence.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acute rheumatic fever is an immune-mediated sequela of untreated group A streptococcal pharyngitis, occurring 2-4 weeks after infection (molecular mimicry).
- Diagnosis uses the Jones criteria: evidence of preceding strep infection PLUS 2 major or 1 major and 2 minor criteria.
- Major criteria (JONES): Joints (migratory polyarthritis), heart (carditis, including murmurs), Nodules (subcutaneous), Erythema marginatum, Sydenham chorea.
- Mitral valve is most commonly affected; chronic rheumatic heart disease causes mitral stenosis years later.
- Treatment: anti-inflammatories for symptoms, penicillin to clear streptococci, and long-term antibiotic prophylaxis to prevent recurrent attacks; prevention is treating strep pharyngitis promptly.

CASE_ID: 402
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Ventricular Septal Defect

ALIASES:
VSD
interventricular septal defect

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 2-month-old infant is brought in for a routine checkup, and the pediatrician notes poor weight gain and a heart murmur; the parents report the baby sweats and tires during feeding.
2. The pregnancy and delivery were uncomplicated and the baby appeared well at birth.
3. On exam, there is a loud, harsh holosystolic murmur best heard at the left lower sternal border with a palpable thrill; the infant is tachypneic with mild hepatomegaly.
4. Chest X-ray shows cardiomegaly with increased pulmonary vascular markings; ECG shows biventricular hypertrophy.
5. Echocardiography reveals a moderate-sized defect in the membranous interventricular septum with left-to-right shunting.
6. The infant is started on diuretics and high-calorie feeds for heart failure symptoms; given the size and symptoms, surgical closure is planned, as small defects often close spontaneously.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Ventricular septal defect is the most common congenital heart defect; produces a left-to-right shunt.
- Classic finding: a harsh holosystolic murmur at the left lower sternal border; smaller defects paradoxically produce louder murmurs.
- Small VSDs are often asymptomatic and frequently close spontaneously; large defects cause heart failure in infancy (failure to thrive, tachypnea, diaphoresis with feeds).
- Untreated large shunts can lead to pulmonary hypertension and eventual shunt reversal (Eisenmenger syndrome) with cyanosis.
- Management: small defects observed; symptomatic or large defects managed with diuretics and surgical/catheter closure; endocarditis prophylaxis considerations.

CASE_ID: 403
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Myocarditis

ALIASES:
viral myocarditis
inflammatory cardiomyopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old man presents with several days of progressive shortness of breath, chest discomfort, and palpitations following a recent flu-like illness.
2. He was previously healthy and active with no cardiac history; the upper respiratory symptoms occurred about ten days ago.
3. On exam, he is tachycardic with an S3 gallop, bibasilar crackles, and mild jugular venous distention; blood pressure is low-normal.
4. Labs show an elevated troponin without obstructive coronary disease on subsequent angiography, and elevated BNP; ECG shows diffuse nonspecific ST-T changes and sinus tachycardia.
5. Echocardiography reveals globally reduced left ventricular function with an ejection fraction of 35%; cardiac MRI shows patchy late gadolinium enhancement in a non-coronary distribution.
6. He is managed with guideline-directed heart failure therapy and supportive care; he is counseled to avoid strenuous exercise during recovery, and most function returns over months.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Myocarditis is inflammation of the myocardium, most commonly caused by viruses (classically coxsackievirus B, also parvovirus B19, HHV-6, adenovirus, and SARS-CoV-2).
- Other causes: other infections, autoimmune disease, hypersensitivity reactions, and certain drugs (including immune checkpoint inhibitors).
- Presentation ranges from subclinical to fulminant heart failure, arrhythmias, and sudden cardiac death (an important cause in young athletes).
- Elevated troponin with non-obstructive coronaries and non-coronary late gadolinium enhancement on cardiac MRI support the diagnosis; endomyocardial biopsy is the gold standard but is used selectively.
- Treatment is largely supportive with heart failure therapy; avoid exercise during the acute phase; immunosuppression for specific causes (e.g., giant cell myocarditis).

CASE_ID: 404
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Abdominal Aortic Aneurysm Rupture

ALIASES:
ruptured AAA
aortic aneurysm rupture

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old man is brought to the ED with the sudden onset of severe abdominal and back pain followed by near-syncope.
2. He has a long history of smoking, hypertension, and known but unmonitored aortic enlargement; a brother also had vascular surgery.
3. On exam, he is hypotensive with a heart rate of 124; there is a pulsatile, tender mass palpable in the mid-abdomen, and he has flank ecchymosis.
4. He becomes increasingly pale and diaphoretic, with a rapidly dropping hemoglobin on point-of-care testing.
5. Bedside ultrasound shows a large infrarenal aortic aneurysm with surrounding free fluid; he is too unstable for CT.
6. He is taken emergently to the operating room for open or endovascular repair with massive transfusion support, as a ruptured aneurysm is rapidly fatal without surgery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- An abdominal aortic aneurysm is a focal dilation of the aorta (>3 cm), most commonly infrarenal, driven by atherosclerosis and degeneration of the media.
- Risk factors: age, male sex, smoking (strongest modifiable factor), hypertension, family history; connective tissue disease in younger patients.
- Classic rupture triad: sudden severe abdominal/back pain, hypotension, and a pulsatile abdominal mass; rupture has very high mortality.
- Most are asymptomatic and found incidentally; one-time screening ultrasound is recommended for men aged 65-75 who have ever smoked.
- Elective repair is indicated at >=5.5 cm, rapid expansion, or symptoms; rupture requires emergent open or endovascular repair.

CASE_ID: 405
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Obesity Hypoventilation Syndrome

ALIASES:
Pickwickian syndrome
OHS

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old man with severe obesity presents with chronic daytime sleepiness, morning headaches, and progressive shortness of breath.
2. His wife reports he snores loudly and stops breathing during sleep, and he has been falling asleep at work; he has lower extremity swelling.
3. On exam, his BMI is 48; he has a plethoric face, jugular venous distention, and bilateral lower extremity edema; oxygen saturation is 88% while awake.
4. Arterial blood gas while awake and breathing room air shows a chronic respiratory acidosis with an elevated bicarbonate (compensated hypercapnia); the hematocrit is elevated.
5. Pulmonary function testing shows a restrictive pattern; a sleep study confirms both obstructive apneas and sustained nocturnal hypoventilation.
6. He is started on nocturnal positive airway pressure therapy and supplemental oxygen, with weight loss counseling and management of cor pulmonale.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Obesity hypoventilation syndrome is defined by obesity (BMI >=30), daytime hypercapnia (PaCO2 >45 mmHg), and sleep-disordered breathing, after excluding other causes of hypoventilation.
- Mechanism: excess weight increases the work of breathing and reduces chest wall compliance, while blunted central respiratory drive leads to chronic CO2 retention.
- Distinguished from pure obstructive sleep apnea by the presence of awake, chronic daytime hypercapnia.
- Chronic respiratory acidosis is compensated by renal bicarbonate retention; chronic hypoxemia can cause secondary polycythemia and pulmonary hypertension with cor pulmonale.
- Treatment: positive airway pressure therapy (often BiPAP for hypoventilation), weight loss (including bariatric surgery), and avoidance of sedatives.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 406
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Silicosis

ALIASES:
silica pneumoconiosis
occupational silica lung disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man presents with several years of progressive exertional dyspnea and a dry cough.
2. He worked for an abnormal value in sandblasting and stone cutting without consistent respiratory protection.
3. On exam, he has decreased breath sounds and fine crackles; there is no clubbing.
4. Chest X-ray shows multiple small rounded opacities predominantly in the upper lobes with calcified hilar lymph nodes that have a peripheral "eggshell" pattern.
5. Pulmonary function tests reveal a restrictive pattern with reduced diffusing capacity; a tuberculin skin test is obtained given his elevated risk.
6. He is counseled that there is no specific treatment beyond removing exposure and supportive care, and he is screened regularly for tuberculosis and lung cancer.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Silicosis is a fibrotic pneumoconiosis caused by inhalation of crystalline silica dust (sandblasting, mining, stone cutting, foundry work).
- Silica is ingested by macrophages, triggering fibrosis predominantly in the upper lobes.
- Classic imaging: upper lobe nodular opacities and "eggshell" calcification of hilar lymph nodes; progressive massive fibrosis in advanced disease.
- Silica impairs macrophage function, markedly increasing the risk of tuberculosis (silicotuberculosis); also increases the risk of lung cancer.
- No specific therapy exists; management is exposure cessation, supportive care, TB screening/treatment, and vaccination.

CASE_ID: 407
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Asbestosis

ALIASES:
asbestos pneumoconiosis
asbestos-related lung disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 66-year-old man presents with progressive dyspnea on exertion and a dry cough developing over several years.
2. He worked in shipbuilding and as a pipe insulator from the 1970s to the 1990s; he is a former smoker.
3. On exam, he has fine bibasilar inspiratory crackles and digital clubbing.
4. Chest X-ray shows reticular opacities predominantly in the lower lobes and bilateral calcified pleural plaques along the diaphragm.
5. Pulmonary function tests reveal a restrictive pattern with reduced diffusing capacity; sputum analysis shows ferruginous bodies.
6. He is counseled on smoking cessation (synergistic cancer risk), monitored for lung cancer and mesothelioma, and managed supportively.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Asbestosis is a fibrotic pneumoconiosis from inhaled asbestos fibers (shipbuilding, insulation, construction, roofing, brake linings).
- Unlike silica and coal, asbestos causes LOWER lobe predominant fibrosis.
- Pleural plaques (often calcified) are a marker of asbestos exposure but are themselves benign.
- Asbestos bodies (ferruginous bodies): asbestos fibers coated with iron-protein, seen in tissue/sputum.
- Increases the risk of bronchogenic carcinoma (the most common asbestos-associated cancer, markedly synergistic with smoking) and malignant mesothelioma (specific but less common); no specific treatment.

CASE_ID: 408
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Whipple Disease

ALIASES:
Tropheryma whipplei infection
intestinal lipodystrophy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old man presents with months of chronic diarrhea, significant weight loss, migratory joint pains, and abdominal pain.
2. He is a farmer and reports the arthralgias preceded the GI symptoms by years; he has also noticed memory problems and abnormal eye movements.
3. On exam, he is cachectic with hyperpigmentation, generalized lymphadenopathy, low-grade fever, and a peculiar combination of eye and jaw muscle movements.
4. Labs show malabsorption with low albumin, iron deficiency, and fat in the stool.
5. Small bowel biopsy reveals foamy macrophages in the lamina propria that stain strongly with periodic acid-Schiff (PAS-positive); PCR confirms Tropheryma whipplei.
6. He is treated with an initial course of IV ceftriaxone followed by prolonged oral trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole for about one year to prevent relapse and CNS disease.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Whipple disease is a rare systemic infection caused by Tropheryma whipplei, a gram-positive actinomycete; classically affects middle-aged white men.
- Multisystem involvement: malabsorptive diarrhea and weight loss, migratory arthralgias (often preceding GI symptoms by years), cardiac, and neurologic disease.
- A classic neurologic sign is oculomasticatory myorhythmia (simultaneous eye and jaw movements), which is pathognomonic.
- Diagnosis: small bowel biopsy shows PAS-positive foamy macrophages in the lamina propria; confirmed by PCR.
- Treatment requires prolonged antibiotics (ceftriaxone induction followed by long-term TMP-SMX) because of the high relapse rate and CNS involvement.

CASE_ID: 409
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Lactose Intolerance

ALIASES:
lactase deficiency
lactose malabsorption

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old woman of East Asian descent presents with recurrent bloating, abdominal cramps, flatulence, and watery diarrhea that occur about an hour after consuming dairy products.
2. Her symptoms began in her late teens and reliably follow ice cream or milk, but she tolerates lactose-free alternatives without issue.
3. On exam, the abdomen is soft with mild diffuse tenderness and active bowel sounds; there is no weight loss, blood in the stool, or systemic signs.
4. Routine labs and inflammatory markers are normal; stool studies show no blood, leukocytes, or pathogens.
5. A hydrogen breath test after a lactose load shows a significant rise in breath hydrogen; stool osmotic gap is elevated and stool pH is low.
6. She is advised to reduce dietary lactose, use lactase enzyme supplements, and choose lactose-free products, with complete symptom resolution.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Lactose intolerance results from deficiency of intestinal lactase (brush-border enzyme), leaving undigested lactose to be fermented by colonic bacteria.
- Primary (most common): genetically programmed decline in lactase after childhood, highly prevalent in East Asian, African, and Native American populations.
- Secondary: temporary loss of lactase from mucosal injury (viral gastroenteritis, celiac disease, Crohn disease).
- Produces an OSMOTIC diarrhea: bloating, flatulence, cramps, and watery stools after dairy; elevated stool osmotic gap, low stool pH, positive hydrogen breath test.
- Management is dietary lactose restriction and lactase enzyme supplementation; symptoms resolve with avoidance (contrast with secretory diarrhea, which persists with fasting).

CASE_ID: 410
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Gastrointestinal Stromal Tumor

ALIASES:
GIST
KIT-positive mesenchymal tumor

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 60-year-old man presents with fatigue and dark, tarry stools; he reports vague early satiety and mild upper abdominal discomfort.
2. He has no NSAID use or known ulcer disease; he has lost some weight over the past few months.
3. On exam, he is pale with mild epigastric fullness; rectal exam reveals melena.
4. Labs show iron deficiency anemia; upper endoscopy reveals a submucosal mass in the stomach with central ulceration and oozing.
5. CT shows a well-circumscribed, enhancing exophytic gastric mass; biopsy demonstrates spindle cells that stain positive for KIT (CD117) and DOG1.
6. He undergoes surgical resection, and because the tumor is large with a high mitotic rate, adjuvant imatinib is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Gastrointestinal stromal tumors arise from the interstitial cells of Cajal (the gut's pacemaker cells) and are the most common mesenchymal tumor of the GI tract.
- Most occur in the stomach; they often present as submucosal masses with GI bleeding, anemia, or early satiety.
- The majority harbor activating mutations in the KIT (CD117) or PDGFRA genes; immunohistochemistry is positive for KIT (CD117) and DOG1.
- Associated with neurofibromatosis type 1 and Carney triad.
- Treatment: surgical resection for localized disease; tyrosine kinase inhibitors (imatinib) for unresectable, metastatic, or high-risk tumors (targeting the KIT mutation).

CASE_ID: 411
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Zollinger-Ellison Syndrome

ALIASES:
gastrinoma
ZES

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 45-year-old man presents with recurrent, refractory peptic ulcers, abdominal pain, and chronic diarrhea despite high-dose acid-suppressing therapy.
2. He has had multiple ulcers including some in unusual locations beyond the duodenal bulb; he also reports symptoms of kidney stones.
3. On exam, he has epigastric tenderness; there are no specific external findings, but his family history includes endocrine tumors.
4. Labs show a markedly elevated fasting serum gastrin level; an elevated calcium prompts evaluation for associated endocrine disease.
5. A secretin stimulation test paradoxically increases gastrin (rather than suppressing it); imaging localizes a tumor in the duodenal wall.
6. He is treated with high-dose proton pump inhibitors to control acid hypersecretion, surgical resection of the gastrinoma, and is evaluated for MEN 1 given his hypercalcemia.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Zollinger-Ellison syndrome is caused by a gastrin-secreting tumor (gastrinoma), most often located in the duodenum or pancreas (the "gastrinoma triangle").
- Excess gastrin drives massive gastric acid secretion, causing multiple, recurrent, and refractory peptic ulcers, including ulcers distal to the duodenal bulb.
- Chronic diarrhea results from acid inactivating pancreatic enzymes and damaging the mucosa; steatorrhea can occur.
- About 25% of gastrinomas are associated with MEN 1 (parathyroid, pituitary, pancreatic tumors); hypercalcemia is a clue.
- Diagnosis: elevated fasting gastrin and a positive secretin stimulation test (gastrin rises paradoxically); treat with high-dose PPIs and tumor resection.

CASE_ID: 412
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Creutzfeldt-Jakob Disease

ALIASES:
CJD
prion disease
spongiform encephalopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man is brought in for rapidly progressive cognitive decline over the past 3 months, with prominent memory loss, personality change, and frequent startle-like jerks.
2. His decline has been strikingly fast compared with typical dementia, and he has become unable to care for himself within weeks.
3. On exam, he has profound dementia, prominent myoclonus that is provoked by startle, cerebellar ataxia, and visual disturbances.
4. MRI of the brain with diffusion-weighted imaging shows cortical ribboning and increased signal in the basal ganglia; EEG reveals periodic sharp wave complexes.
5. CSF analysis is positive for the 14-3-3 protein and RT-QuIC, supporting the diagnosis.
6. The family is counseled that the disease is uniformly fatal with no effective treatment, and that the typical course is death within a year; care focuses on comfort.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Creutzfeldt-Jakob disease is a fatal neurodegenerative prion disease caused by misfolded prion protein (PrPSc) that templates conversion of normal protein, causing spongiform change.
- Hallmark: rapidly progressive dementia (over weeks to months) with myoclonus (often startle-induced), ataxia, and visual or cerebellar signs.
- Forms: sporadic (most common), familial, and acquired (iatrogenic from contaminated instruments/grafts, or variant CJD from bovine spongiform encephalopathy).
- Diagnostic support: MRI cortical ribboning and basal ganglia signal, periodic sharp waves on EEG, CSF 14-3-3 protein and RT-QuIC.
- No effective treatment; uniformly fatal, usually within 1 year; prions are notoriously resistant to standard sterilization.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 413
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Syringomyelia

ALIASES:
spinal cord syrinx
syrinx

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 30-year-old man presents with several years of progressive loss of pain and temperature sensation in both arms, along with painless burns on his hands that he did not feel.
2. He notes weakness and wasting of his hand muscles; he has a history of a Chiari malformation noted in childhood.
3. On exam, there is a "cape-like" distribution of loss of pain and temperature sensation across the shoulders and arms, with preserved light touch and proprioception; the small muscles of the hands are atrophic with absent upper extremity reflexes.
4. Lower extremity strength and sensation are relatively preserved early in the course.
5. MRI of the cervical spine reveals a fluid-filled cavity (syrinx) within the central cord, associated with a Chiari I malformation.
6. He is referred for neurosurgical evaluation; treatment targets the underlying cause (posterior fossa decompression for Chiari) to halt progression.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Syringomyelia is a fluid-filled cavity (syrinx) within the spinal cord, most often in the cervical region.
- Strongly associated with Chiari I malformation; other causes include prior trauma, tumors, and infection.
- The expanding central syrinx first damages the crossing spinothalamic fibers, causing a "cape-like" loss of pain and temperature sensation with preservation of dorsal column functions (light touch, vibration, proprioception) - a dissociated sensory loss.
- As it expands, it affects anterior horn cells (lower motor neuron signs, hand muscle atrophy) and later the corticospinal tracts.
- Diagnosis by MRI; treatment addresses the underlying cause (e.g., posterior fossa decompression) and may include shunting of the syrinx.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 414
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Brown-Sequard Syndrome

ALIASES:
spinal cord hemisection syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 27-year-old man is brought to the ED after a stab wound to the right side of his mid-back during an altercation.
2. He reports weakness in his right leg and a loss of the ability to feel temperature and pain on the opposite (left) side of his body below the injury.
3. On exam, below the level of the lesion he has right-sided weakness with hyperreflexia and an extensor plantar response, loss of vibration and proprioception on the right, and loss of pain and temperature sensation on the left.
4. There is a band of complete sensory loss at the level of the lesion on the right side.
5. MRI of the spine confirms hemisection of the cord on the right at the level of injury.
6. He is managed with surgical stabilization and supportive care; the dissociated pattern of deficits allows precise localization of the lesion.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Brown-Sequard syndrome results from hemisection (one-sided damage) of the spinal cord, classically from penetrating trauma.
- Three key deficits below the lesion: ipsilateral loss of motor function and dorsal column sensation (vibration, proprioception), and CONTRALATERAL loss of pain and temperature.
- The contralateral pain/temperature loss occurs because spinothalamic fibers cross within 1-2 levels of entering the cord (so damage above the crossing affects the opposite side).
- At the level of the lesion, there is ipsilateral complete sensory loss and lower motor neuron signs.
- It is a classic teaching lesion for understanding spinal cord tract anatomy; treatment is of the underlying cause with supportive rehabilitation.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 415
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Tabes Dorsalis

ALIASES:
neurosyphilis dorsal column disease
tertiary syphilis spinal cord disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man presents with several years of progressive difficulty walking, especially in the dark, along with sharp, shooting "lightning" pains in his legs.
2. He has a history of untreated syphilis decades ago and reports episodes of severe abdominal pain and difficulty with bladder control.
3. On exam, he has a wide-based, stomping gait; loss of vibration and proprioception in the legs; absent ankle and knee reflexes; and a strongly positive Romberg sign.
4. His pupils are small, irregular, and constrict to accommodation but do not react to light (Argyll Robertson pupils).
5. Serum and CSF testing confirm neurosyphilis with reactive treponemal tests and CSF pleocytosis.
6. He is treated with high-dose IV penicillin G; some sensory deficits are irreversible, but treatment halts progression.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Tabes dorsalis is a form of tertiary neurosyphilis caused by degeneration of the dorsal columns and dorsal roots from chronic Treponema pallidum infection.
- Classic features: sensory ataxia (positive Romberg, stomping gait), lancinating "lightning" pains, loss of proprioception and vibration, areflexia, and neurogenic bladder.
- Argyll Robertson pupils ("prostitute's pupils"): accommodate but do not react to light; classically associated with neurosyphilis.
- Charcot joints (neuropathic arthropathy) can develop from loss of proprioceptive feedback.
- Diagnosis with treponemal and nontreponemal serologies plus CSF analysis; treatment is IV penicillin G; deficits acquired before treatment may be permanent.

CASE_ID: 416
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Hydatidiform Mole

ALIASES:
complete hydatidiform mole
molar pregnancy gestational trophoblastic disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old woman at an estimated 12 weeks gestation presents with vaginal bleeding, severe nausea and vomiting, and passage of grape-like tissue.
2. Her uterus feels much larger than expected for her dates, and she reports symptoms suggesting hyperthyroidism such as tremor and heat intolerance.
3. On exam, the uterus is markedly enlarged for gestational age, no fetal heart tones are detected, and her blood pressure is elevated despite the early gestational age.
4. Serum beta-hCG is extraordinarily elevated, far above expected for gestational age.
5. Pelvic ultrasound reveals a "snowstorm" or "cluster of grapes" appearance with no identifiable fetus or amniotic fluid.
6. She undergoes suction dilation and curettage, with histology confirming a complete mole; serial beta-hCG levels are monitored to detect persistent gestational trophoblastic neoplasia.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hydatidiform mole is a form of gestational trophoblastic disease with abnormal trophoblast proliferation.
- Complete mole (46,XX, all paternal; empty egg fertilized): no fetal tissue, "snowstorm" ultrasound, very high hCG, higher risk of progression to choriocarcinoma.
- Partial mole (69,XXX/XXY triploid; two sperm fertilize one egg): some fetal tissue present, lower hCG, lower malignant potential.
- Markedly elevated hCG can cause early preeclampsia, theca lutein cysts, and hyperthyroidism (hCG has structural similarity to TSH).
- Treatment: suction curettage with serial beta-hCG monitoring to detect persistent disease or choriocarcinoma; contraception during monitoring.

CASE_ID: 417
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Choriocarcinoma

ALIASES:
gestational choriocarcinoma
trophoblastic malignancy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 27-year-old woman presents with vaginal bleeding, shortness of breath, and hemoptysis a few months after a molar pregnancy was evacuated.
2. Her post-molar beta-hCG levels had been declining but then plateaued and began to rise again.
3. On exam, she has an enlarged uterus and scattered crackles in the lungs; she appears pale.
4. Labs show a markedly and rising beta-hCG; chest imaging reveals multiple round pulmonary nodules ("cannonball" metastases).
5. There is no evidence of an intrauterine pregnancy on ultrasound.
6. She is treated with chemotherapy (this tumor is highly chemosensitive, classically responsive to methotrexate), with excellent cure rates even in metastatic disease.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Choriocarcinoma is a malignant gestational trophoblastic neoplasm; it most often follows a hydatidiform mole but can follow any pregnancy (including normal or ectopic).
- It produces beta-hCG, so a plateauing or rising hCG after pregnancy evacuation suggests persistent disease.
- It spreads hematogenously and early; the lungs are the most common metastatic site ("cannonball" metastases), and brain and liver involvement can occur.
- Histology shows malignant trophoblasts WITHOUT chorionic villi.
- It is remarkably chemosensitive (methotrexate-based regimens) with high cure rates even when metastatic; non-gestational choriocarcinoma (from germ cells) is more aggressive.

CASE_ID: 418
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Preterm Premature Rupture of Membranes

ALIASES:
PPROM
preterm prelabor rupture of membranes

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old woman at 30 weeks gestation presents with a sudden gush of clear fluid from the vagina followed by continued leakage.
2. She denies regular contractions but reports the constant dampness; she has a history of a prior preterm birth and is a smoker.
3. On exam, sterile speculum examination reveals pooling of clear fluid in the posterior vaginal fornix; no active labor is evident, and there is no foul odor or maternal fever.
4. The pooled fluid turns nitrazine paper blue (alkaline) and shows a ferning pattern when dried on a slide.
5. Ultrasound confirms reduced amniotic fluid volume (oligohydramnios) with a viable fetus.
6. She is admitted for expectant management with latency antibiotics, antenatal corticosteroids for fetal lung maturity, and magnesium sulfate for neuroprotection, with close monitoring for infection.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Premature rupture of membranes is rupture before the onset of labor; PPROM is rupture before 37 weeks gestation.
- Risk factors: prior PROM/preterm birth, intra-amniotic infection, smoking, short cervix, multiple gestation, and polyhydramnios.
- Diagnosis by sterile speculum exam: pooling of fluid, positive nitrazine test (amniotic fluid is alkaline and turns paper blue), and ferning on microscopy; avoid digital exams to reduce infection risk.
- The major risks are intra-amniotic infection (chorioamnionitis), cord prolapse, placental abruption, and complications of prematurity.
- Management depends on gestational age: latency antibiotics, antenatal corticosteroids (24-34 weeks), magnesium for neuroprotection (<32 weeks), and delivery if infection, fetal compromise, or term is reached.

CASE_ID: 419
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Cryptorchidism

ALIASES:
undescended testis
undescended testicle

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 9-month-old boy is brought in for a well-child visit, and the parents are concerned that they cannot feel one of his testicles in the scrotum.
2. He was born at an abnormal value gestation; at his newborn exam the right testis was noted to be undescended, and it has still not descended.
3. On exam, the left testis is palpable in the scrotum, but the right scrotum is empty and the right testis is palpable in the inguinal canal; it cannot be brought into the scrotum.
4. There are no other genital abnormalities, and the rest of the exam is normal.
5. The testis remains undescended at this age, beyond the window for spontaneous descent.
6. He is referred for orchiopexy (surgical fixation of the testis into the scrotum), ideally performed between 6 and 18 months of age to reduce later risks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Cryptorchidism is failure of one or both testes to descend into the scrotum; more common in premature infants.
- Most undescended testes descend spontaneously within the first 6 months; persistence beyond this warrants intervention.
- Untreated cryptorchidism increases the risk of infertility and testicular germ cell cancer (the risk is increased even in the normally descended contralateral testis).
- Other complications: testicular torsion and inguinal hernia.
- Treatment is orchiopexy (surgical mobilization and fixation into the scrotum), ideally by 18 months; this improves fertility and allows easier monitoring for malignancy (though it does not fully eliminate cancer risk).

CASE_ID: 420
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Necrotizing Enterocolitis

ALIASES:
NEC
neonatal necrotizing enterocolitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A premature infant born at 28 weeks, now 10 days old, develops feeding intolerance, abdominal distention, and bloody stools.
2. The infant had been started on formula feeds and was previously stable in the neonatal ICU.
3. On exam, the abdomen is distended, tense, and tender with visible erythema of the abdominal wall; the infant is lethargic with temperature instability and bradycardia.
4. Labs show thrombocytopenia, metabolic acidosis, and a left-shifted white count; the infant appears septic.
5. Abdominal X-ray reveals pneumatosis intestinalis (gas within the bowel wall) and portal venous gas; a later film shows free air indicating perforation.
6. Feeds are stopped, a nasogastric tube is placed for decompression, broad-spectrum antibiotics are started, and surgery is consulted for the perforation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Necrotizing enterocolitis is a life-threatening intestinal emergency primarily affecting premature, low-birth-weight infants.
- Risk factors: prematurity (the most important), formula feeding (breast milk is protective), bowel ischemia, and bacterial colonization.
- Presentation: feeding intolerance, abdominal distention, bloody stools, and signs of sepsis, typically in the first weeks of life.
- The pathognomonic radiographic finding is pneumatosis intestinalis (gas in the bowel wall); portal venous gas and free air (perforation) indicate severe disease.
- Management: stop feeds, bowel rest with nasogastric decompression, IV fluids and broad-spectrum antibiotics; surgery for perforation or clinical deterioration.

CASE_ID: 421
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Kwashiorkor

ALIASES:
protein malnutrition
edematous malnutrition

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 2-year-old child from a region experiencing food insecurity is brought in with swelling of the legs and abdomen, irritability, and failure to grow.
2. The child was weaned abruptly onto a starchy, low-protein diet after a sibling was born.
3. On exam, there is pitting edema of the lower extremities, a distended abdomen with hepatomegaly, thin reddish-tinged hair, and flaky, peeling skin; the child is apathetic and irritable.
4. Despite the edema and protuberant belly, there is muscle wasting; weight may appear deceptively preserved due to fluid.
5. Labs show markedly low serum albumin, anemia, and fatty infiltration of the liver.
6. The child is refed cautiously to avoid refeeding syndrome, with gradual protein and calorie repletion, micronutrient supplementation, and treatment of infections.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Kwashiorkor is a severe protein malnutrition with relatively preserved calorie intake, classically after weaning onto a carbohydrate-rich, protein-poor diet.
- Hallmark: edema (from hypoalbuminemia and reduced oncotic pressure), a distended belly, and fatty liver (impaired lipoprotein synthesis to export fat).
- Other features: skin changes (dermatosis, "flaky paint"), hair changes (depigmentation, the "flag sign"), apathy/irritability, and immune dysfunction.
- Contrast with marasmus: total calorie deficiency causing severe muscle and fat wasting WITHOUT edema (an emaciated, "skin and bones" appearance).
- Treatment: gradual nutritional rehabilitation to avoid refeeding syndrome (watch for hypophosphatemia), micronutrients, and treatment of concurrent infection.

CASE_ID: 422
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Retinopathy of Prematurity

ALIASES:
ROP
retrolental fibroplasia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A premature infant born at 26 weeks gestation, who required prolonged supplemental oxygen and mechanical ventilation, undergoes routine ophthalmologic screening in the neonatal ICU.
2. The infant has had a complicated NICU course with several episodes requiring high oxygen concentrations.
3. On dilated retinal examination, the ophthalmologist notes a sharp demarcation line between vascularized and avascular peripheral retina with abnormal new vessel growth at the border.
4. There are no other ocular abnormalities, and the anterior segment is normal.
5. The findings are graded by stage and zone; the infant is monitored with serial exams as the disease can progress to retinal detachment.
6. Treatment with laser photocoagulation (or anti-VEGF injection) of the avascular peripheral retina is performed for threshold disease to prevent progression to detachment and blindness.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Retinopathy of prematurity is abnormal retinal blood vessel development in premature, low-birth-weight infants.
- Pathophysiology is biphasic: hyperoxia (often from supplemental oxygen) suppresses VEGF and halts normal vessel growth, then relative hypoxia later drives excessive VEGF and pathologic neovascularization.
- Risk factors: prematurity, low birth weight, and high supplemental oxygen exposure.
- Untreated severe ROP can cause traction retinal detachment and blindness; it is a leading cause of childhood blindness.
- Prevention includes careful titration of oxygen; screening exams in at-risk infants; treatment with laser photocoagulation or intravitreal anti-VEGF for advanced disease.

CASE_ID: 423
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Transposition of the Great Arteries

ALIASES:
TGA
d-transposition of the great arteries

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A full-term newborn becomes progressively cyanotic within the first hours of life, with bluish discoloration that does not improve with supplemental oxygen.
2. The pregnancy was notable for poorly controlled maternal diabetes; the delivery was otherwise uncomplicated.
3. On exam, the infant is markedly cyanotic and tachypneic; a single loud S2 is heard, and there may be no significant murmur if the ventricular septum is intact.
4. Pulse oximetry shows severe hypoxemia with minimal response to 100% oxygen (a failed hyperoxia test).
5. Chest X-ray shows an "egg on a string" appearance (narrow mediastinum with an egg-shaped heart); echocardiography confirms the aorta arising from the right ventricle and the pulmonary artery from the left ventricle.
6. A prostaglandin E1 infusion is started to maintain ductal patency, and a balloon atrial septostomy is performed to improve mixing before definitive arterial switch surgery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- In transposition of the great arteries, the aorta arises from the right ventricle and the pulmonary artery from the left ventricle, creating two parallel circulations.
- It is incompatible with life unless there is mixing between the circulations (via a patent ductus arteriosus, ASD, or VSD).
- It is a cause of early cyanosis (within hours to days of birth) that does not improve with oxygen; associated with maternal diabetes.
- Classic chest X-ray: "egg on a string" appearance.
- Initial management: prostaglandin E1 to keep the ductus arteriosus open (maintaining mixing) and balloon atrial septostomy; definitive treatment is the arterial switch operation.

CASE_ID: 424
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Duchenne Muscular Dystrophy

ALIASES:
DMD
dystrophinopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 4-year-old boy is brought in because he is slower than his peers, falls frequently, has difficulty climbing stairs, and walks on his toes.
2. He has trouble rising from the floor and uses his hands to "walk up" his own legs to stand; there is a family history of a maternal uncle who used a wheelchair from a young age.
3. On exam, he has enlarged, firm calf muscles, proximal muscle weakness greater than distal, a waddling gait, and a positive Gowers sign; deep tendon reflexes are diminished.
4. Labs show a markedly elevated creatine kinase (in the thousands) and elevated transaminases of muscle origin.
5. Genetic testing reveals a frameshift deletion in the dystrophin gene, confirming the diagnosis (muscle biopsy would show absent dystrophin).
6. He is started on corticosteroids to slow progression, with multidisciplinary care including cardiac and pulmonary monitoring, as cardiomyopathy and respiratory failure are the leading causes of death.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Duchenne muscular dystrophy is an X-linked recessive disorder caused by a frameshift mutation in the dystrophin gene, resulting in absent dystrophin (a cytoskeletal protein that anchors muscle fibers).
- Presents in early childhood with proximal muscle weakness, a waddling gait, and the Gowers sign (using the arms to "climb up" the legs to stand).
- Pseudohypertrophy of the calves (muscle replaced by fat and connective tissue) is characteristic; CK is markedly elevated.
- Becker muscular dystrophy is the milder allelic disorder (non-frameshift mutation with partially functional dystrophin and later onset).
- Death typically results from dilated cardiomyopathy and respiratory failure; corticosteroids slow progression, with newer exon-skipping and gene therapies emerging.

CASE_ID: 425
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Neuroblastoma

ALIASES:
sympathetic nervous system tumor
adrenal medullary tumor of childhood

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 2-year-old child is brought in because of a firm abdominal mass noticed during bathing, along with poor appetite, weight loss, and irritability.
2. The parents also report the child has had episodes of bluish bumps on the skin and unusual eye movements.
3. On exam, there is a firm, irregular abdominal mass that crosses the midline; the child has periorbital ecchymoses ("raccoon eyes"), bluish subcutaneous nodules, and rapid dancing eye movements with myoclonic jerks.
4. Labs show elevated urinary catecholamine metabolites (homovanillic acid and vanillylmandelic acid).
5. Imaging reveals an adrenal mass with calcifications that displaces the kidney without arising from it; biopsy shows small round blue cells forming rosettes.
6. Treatment is risk-stratified, ranging from observation (some tumors regress spontaneously) to surgery, chemotherapy, and other modalities; MYCN amplification portends a worse prognosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Neuroblastoma is the most common extracranial solid tumor of childhood and the most common cancer in infants; it arises from neural crest cells of the sympathetic nervous system (often the adrenal medulla).
- Presents as a firm, irregular abdominal mass that can cross the midline (contrast with Wilms tumor, which is smooth and usually does not cross midline).
- Secretes catecholamines, so urinary HVA and VMA are elevated.
- Paraneoplastic features: opsoclonus-myoclonus ("dancing eyes, dancing feet") and periorbital ecchymoses ("raccoon eyes") from orbital metastases.
- Histology shows small round blue cells with Homer-Wright rosettes; MYCN oncogene amplification is associated with poor prognosis; some low-risk tumors regress spontaneously.

CASE_ID: 426
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hemophilia A

ALIASES:
factor VIII deficiency
classic hemophilia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 14-month-old boy is brought in after developing a swollen, warm, painful knee following a minor fall while learning to walk.
2. He has a history of prolonged bleeding after a heel stick as a newborn and large bruises with minor bumps; a maternal uncle had a lifelong bleeding disorder.
3. On exam, there is a tense, swollen knee (hemarthrosis) with limited range of motion and overlying warmth; scattered ecchymoses are noted.
4. Labs show a prolonged aPTT that corrects on mixing studies, with a normal PT, normal platelet count, and normal bleeding time.
5. Factor VIII activity is markedly reduced; factor IX is normal.
6. He is treated with factor VIII replacement for the acute bleed, and a long-term prophylaxis plan is established to prevent recurrent hemarthroses and joint damage.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hemophilia A is an X-linked recessive deficiency of clotting factor VIII (hemophilia B is factor IX deficiency, clinically indistinguishable).
- Classic presentation: bleeding into joints (hemarthroses) and muscles, easy bruising, and prolonged bleeding after procedures, usually in males.
- Coagulation profile: prolonged aPTT (intrinsic pathway) that CORRECTS with mixing studies, with normal PT, platelet count, and bleeding time.
- Recurrent hemarthroses lead to chronic arthropathy and joint destruction if untreated.
- Treatment: factor replacement (recombinant factor VIII), desmopressin for mild disease (releases stored factor VIII), and emicizumab for prophylaxis; monitor for the development of inhibitors.

CASE_ID: 427
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hairy Cell Leukemia

ALIASES:
HCL
leukemic reticuloendotheliosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 55-year-old man presents with fatigue, recurrent infections, and a sensation of abdominal fullness.
2. He has noticed easy bruising and has had two episodes of pneumonia in the past year.
3. On exam, there is massive splenomegaly without significant lymphadenopathy.
4. Labs show pancytopenia (anemia, thrombocytopenia, and notably monocytopenia); the peripheral smear reveals lymphoid cells with fine cytoplasmic projections.
5. Flow cytometry shows a B-cell clone expressing CD11c, CD25, CD103, and CD123; the cells are tartrate-resistant acid phosphatase (TRAP) positive, and a BRAF V600E mutation is identified.
6. He is treated with a purine analog (cladribine), which produces durable remissions.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hairy cell leukemia is an indolent B-cell malignancy of older adults, classically men.
- Presents with pancytopenia (including characteristic MONOCYTOPENIA), recurrent infections, and massive splenomegaly with little or no lymphadenopathy.
- The malignant cells have hair-like cytoplasmic projections; they are TRAP-positive and express CD11c, CD25, CD103, and CD123.
- The BRAF V600E mutation is characteristic and a potential therapeutic target.
- A "dry tap" on bone marrow aspiration occurs due to marrow fibrosis; treatment with purine analogs (cladribine, pentostatin) achieves durable remissions.

CASE_ID: 428
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Waldenstrom Macroglobulinemia

ALIASES:
lymphoplasmacytic lymphoma
IgM monoclonal gammopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man presents with fatigue, blurred vision, headaches, mucosal bleeding, and confusion.
2. He has also noticed numbness in his hands and feet and that his fingers turn white in the cold.
3. On exam, he has hepatosplenomegaly and lymphadenopathy; funduscopy reveals dilated, tortuous "sausage-link" retinal veins with hemorrhages.
4. Labs show a normocytic anemia with marked rouleaux formation on smear, an elevated total protein with a monoclonal IgM spike on serum protein electrophoresis, and a high serum viscosity.
5. Bone marrow biopsy shows infiltration by lymphoplasmacytic cells; an MYD88 L265P mutation is identified.
6. The acute hyperviscosity is treated with plasmapheresis, followed by chemoimmunotherapy (rituximab-based) for the underlying malignancy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Waldenstrom macroglobulinemia is a lymphoplasmacytic lymphoma that secretes monoclonal IgM.
- The large pentameric IgM increases serum viscosity, causing hyperviscosity syndrome: visual changes ("sausage-link" retinal veins), neurologic symptoms (headache, confusion), and mucosal bleeding.
- Unlike multiple myeloma, it typically does NOT cause lytic bone lesions or significant hypercalcemia/renal failure (IgM, not IgG/IgA, and a different disease).
- IgM can also cause cold agglutinin disease, peripheral neuropathy, and type I cryoglobulinemia (Raynaud-like symptoms); rouleaux formation is seen on smear.
- The MYD88 L265P mutation is characteristic; acute hyperviscosity is treated with plasmapheresis, and the lymphoma is treated with rituximab-based regimens.

CASE_ID: 429
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Primary Myelofibrosis

ALIASES:
myelofibrosis
agnogenic myeloid metaplasia
chronic idiopathic myelofibrosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 66-year-old woman presents with progressive fatigue, early satiety, drenching night sweats, weight loss, and left upper quadrant fullness.
2. Her symptoms have developed gradually over the past year, and she bruises easily.
3. On exam, she has massive splenomegaly and conjunctival pallor.
4. Labs show anemia and a leukoerythroblastic peripheral smear with teardrop-shaped red cells (dacrocytes), nucleated red cells, and immature myeloid forms.
5. Bone marrow aspiration yields a "dry tap"; biopsy reveals marrow fibrosis with atypical megakaryocytes; a JAK2 V617F mutation is identified.
6. She is managed based on risk: a JAK inhibitor (ruxolitinib) for symptoms and splenomegaly, supportive transfusions, and consideration of allogeneic stem cell transplant in eligible patients.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Primary myelofibrosis is a myeloproliferative neoplasm characterized by clonal proliferation with marrow fibrosis and extramedullary hematopoiesis.
- Driver mutations include JAK2 (most common), CALR, and MPL.
- Marrow fibrosis causes a "dry tap" on aspiration and forces hematopoiesis into the spleen and liver, causing massive splenomegaly.
- The peripheral smear shows a leukoerythroblastic picture with teardrop cells (dacrocytes), nucleated RBCs, and immature granulocytes.
- Management is risk-adapted: JAK inhibitors (ruxolitinib) for symptom control and splenomegaly; allogeneic stem cell transplant is the only curative option.

CASE_ID: 430
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Langerhans Cell Histiocytosis

ALIASES:
LCH
histiocytosis X
eosinophilic granuloma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 3-year-old child is brought in with a chronic draining ear, a scaly seborrhea-like scalp rash, and increased thirst and urination.
2. The parents also report bone pain and a soft swelling over the skull.
3. On exam, there is a lytic skull lesion, a recalcitrant seborrheic rash, chronic otitis media, and hepatosplenomegaly; the increased urination prompts concern for diabetes insipidus.
4. Labs and a water deprivation test confirm central diabetes insipidus from pituitary stalk involvement.
5. Biopsy of a skin or bone lesion reveals cells with grooved, "coffee bean" nuclei that stain positive for CD1a and S-100; electron microscopy shows tennis-racket-shaped Birbeck granules.
6. Treatment is determined by the extent of involvement, ranging from local therapy for single-system disease to chemotherapy for multisystem disease.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Langerhans cell histiocytosis is a clonal proliferation of Langerhans cells (specialized dendritic antigen-presenting cells) and is frequently driven by BRAF V600E mutations.
- Classic features span multiple systems: lytic bone lesions (especially skull), a seborrheic/eczematous rash, chronic ear drainage, and pituitary involvement causing central diabetes insipidus.
- The classic triad of Hand-Schuller-Christian disease: skull lesions, diabetes insipidus, and exophthalmos.
- Diagnostic histology: Birbeck granules ("tennis racket" shape) on electron microscopy; cells are CD1a and S-100 positive with grooved "coffee bean" nuclei.
- Treatment ranges from observation/local therapy for single lesions to systemic chemotherapy for multisystem disease.
