CASE_ID: 431
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Renal Artery Stenosis

ALIASES:
renovascular disease
RAS

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man with diffuse atherosclerosis presents with difficult-to-control hypertension despite four antihypertensive medications.
2. His renal function recently worsened sharply after starting an ACE inhibitor, and he has had episodes of sudden pulmonary edema ("flash pulmonary edema").
3. On exam, his blood pressure is 178/an abnormal value, and an abdominal bruit is auscultated.
4. Labs show a rise in serum creatinine after the ACE inhibitor was started; there is mild hypokalemia.
5. Duplex ultrasound and CT angiography demonstrate a tight stenosis of the right renal artery at its origin from atherosclerotic plaque.
6. He is managed with optimal medical therapy and risk-factor modification; revascularization (angioplasty/stenting) is reserved for refractory hypertension or deteriorating renal function.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Renal artery stenosis is a major cause of secondary hypertension; reduced renal perfusion activates the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system.
- Two main causes: atherosclerosis (older patients, men, ostial lesions) and fibromuscular dysplasia (younger women, "string of beads" mid-vessel appearance).
- Clues: resistant hypertension, an abdominal bruit, an acute rise in creatinine after starting an ACE inhibitor or ARB (efferent arteriole dilation drops GFR in a kidney dependent on angiotensin II), and recurrent flash pulmonary edema.
- Diagnosis: duplex ultrasound, CT or MR angiography; catheter angiography is the gold standard.
- Treatment: medical therapy and risk-factor control are first-line; angioplasty with stenting (or surgery) for refractory cases, with fibromuscular dysplasia responding well to angioplasty.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 432
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Rhabdomyolysis-Induced Acute Kidney Injury

ALIASES:
pigment nephropathy
myoglobinuric acute kidney injury

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. An 80-year-old woman is found on the floor of her home after lying there for two days following a fall, unable to get up.
2. She was immobile for a prolonged period and is now confused with decreased urine output.
3. On exam, she is dehydrated with a tender, swollen left thigh where she had been lying; her urine is dark brown.
4. Initial renal studies support intrinsic kidney involvement and guide further evaluation.
5. The urine sediment shows pigmented granular casts.
6. She is treated with aggressive IV fluid resuscitation to maintain urine output and clear myoglobin, along with management of her hyperkalemia.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Rhabdomyolysis releases myoglobin, which is filtered by the glomerulus and causes acute kidney injury via three mechanisms: tubular obstruction by casts, direct tubular toxicity, and renal vasoconstriction.
- Common causes: prolonged immobilization/"found down," crush injury, intense exertion, seizures, and certain drugs/toxins.
- The urine dipstick is positive for blood (myoglobin cross-reacts with the heme assay) but microscopy shows few or no red blood cells - a key clue.
- Associated electrolyte disturbances: hyperkalemia, hyperphosphatemia, hypocalcemia, and elevated creatine kinase; pigmented granular casts on urinalysis.
- The cornerstone of treatment is early, aggressive IV fluid resuscitation to maintain urine flow and limit pigment-induced injury; manage hyperkalemia urgently.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 433
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hyponatremia

ALIASES:
low serum sodium
hypotonic hyponatremia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old woman presents with several days of nausea, headache, lethargy, and confusion that has been worsening.
2. She was recently started on a thiazide diuretic for hypertension and has been drinking large amounts of water; she has no edema.
3. On exam, she is euvolemic with normal jugular venous pressure, no edema, and no orthostatic changes; she is disoriented and has a single witnessed seizure.
4. Labs show a serum sodium of an abnormal value with a low serum osmolality; urine osmolality is inappropriately high and urine sodium is elevated.
5. Thyroid and cortisol testing are normal, and the clinical picture is consistent with a thiazide effect plus excess free water intake.
6. Because she is symptomatic with seizures, she is treated cautiously with hypertonic saline, correcting the sodium slowly to avoid osmotic demyelination.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hyponatremia is most often hypotonic; the workup uses volume status and urine studies to find the cause.
- Hypovolemic (diuretics, GI/renal losses), euvolemic (SIADH, hypothyroidism, adrenal insufficiency, primary polydipsia), and hypervolemic (heart failure, cirrhosis, nephrotic syndrome) categories guide management.
- Symptoms relate to cerebral edema: nausea, headache, confusion, seizures, and coma in severe or rapidly developing cases.
- Severe symptomatic hyponatremia is treated with hypertonic (3%) saline.
- Correct sodium SLOWLY (generally <8-10 mEq/L per 24 hours) to avoid osmotic demyelination syndrome (central pontine myelinolysis); overly rapid correction is the classic pitfall.

CASE_ID: 434
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Chronic Kidney Disease

ALIASES:
CKD
chronic renal failure

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man with long-standing poorly controlled diabetes and hypertension presents with fatigue, poor appetite, itching, and lower extremity swelling.
2. He has not seen a physician in several years and reports decreased urine output and nocturia.
3. On exam, he is hypertensive with pallor, periorbital and lower extremity edema, and excoriations from scratching.
4. Labs show an elevated creatinine with an eGFR of 22 mL/min, hyperkalemia, a high phosphate with low calcium and elevated PTH, a normocytic anemia, and a metabolic acidosis; urinalysis shows significant proteinuria.
5. Renal ultrasound reveals small, echogenic kidneys bilaterally, consistent with chronic disease.
6. He is managed with blood pressure and glucose control, an ACE inhibitor for proteinuria, phosphate binders, treatment of anemia, and dietary modification, with planning for eventual renal replacement therapy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Chronic kidney disease is defined by a sustained reduction in GFR (<60 for >=3 months) or evidence of kidney damage (e.g., albuminuria); diabetes and hypertension are the leading causes.
- Complications reflect lost kidney functions: anemia (decreased erythropoietin), renal osteodystrophy and secondary hyperparathyroidism (decreased active vitamin D, phosphate retention, low calcium), metabolic acidosis, hyperkalemia, and volume overload.
- Uremic symptoms (fatigue, anorexia, pruritus, pericarditis, encephalopathy) appear with advanced disease.
- Small, echogenic kidneys on ultrasound suggest chronicity (versus normal/large kidneys in acute injury or diabetic nephropathy early on).
- Management slows progression (BP control, ACE inhibitor/ARB for proteinuria, glycemic control, SGLT2 inhibitors) and treats complications; dialysis or transplant for end-stage disease.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 435
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Osteomalacia

ALIASES:
adult rickets
defective bone mineralization

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old woman who emigrated from a northern climate and wears concealing clothing presents with diffuse bone pain, muscle weakness, and a waddling gait.
2. She has a history of celiac disease and limited sun exposure; she has had two low-impact fractures in the past year.
3. On exam, she has proximal muscle weakness, bony tenderness over the ribs and pelvis, and a waddling gait.
4. Labs show low vitamin D, low-normal calcium, low phosphate, elevated alkaline phosphatase, and an elevated PTH.
5. X-rays reveal diffuse osteopenia and pseudofractures (Looser zones) at the medial femoral necks and pubic rami.
6. She is treated with vitamin D and calcium supplementation, with management of her underlying malabsorption, and her symptoms gradually improve.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Osteomalacia is defective mineralization of osteoid in mature bone (rickets is the equivalent in children with open growth plates).
- The most common cause is vitamin D deficiency (poor intake, limited sun exposure, malabsorption such as celiac disease, or chronic kidney/liver disease).
- Vitamin D deficiency lowers calcium and phosphate absorption, causing secondary hyperparathyroidism, low phosphate, and high alkaline phosphatase.
- Classic findings: diffuse bone pain, proximal muscle weakness, and pseudofractures (Looser zones) on imaging.
- Treatment is repletion of vitamin D and calcium and addressing the underlying cause; contrast with osteoporosis, which has normal mineralization but reduced bone mass and normal labs.

CASE_ID: 436
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Osteogenesis Imperfecta

ALIASES:
OI
brittle bone disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 3-year-old child is brought in after sustaining a femur fracture from a minor fall; this is the fourth fracture in two years.
2. The parents are under investigation for possible child abuse, but the family history reveals a parent with frequent fractures and hearing loss.
3. On exam, the child has blue-tinged sclerae, mildly loose joints, and small, discolored, brittle teeth; the limbs show evidence of prior healed fractures with mild deformity.
4. There is conductive and mixed hearing loss noted on screening.
5. Radiographs show multiple fractures at different stages of healing and generalized osteopenia with thin cortices.
6. Genetic testing identifies a mutation in a type I collagen gene; management is supportive with bisphosphonates, physical therapy, and fracture care, with awareness that this can mimic non-accidental trauma.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Osteogenesis imperfecta is most often caused by autosomal dominant mutations in type I collagen genes (COL1A1, COL1A2), the major structural protein of bone.
- Hallmark: recurrent fractures with minimal trauma due to brittle bones.
- Classic associated findings: blue sclerae (thin sclera revealing underlying choroid), dental abnormalities (dentinogenesis imperfecta), and early conductive/mixed hearing loss.
- It is an important mimic of non-accidental trauma (child abuse) and must be considered in children with multiple fractures.
- Treatment is supportive: bisphosphonates to increase bone density, physical therapy, surgical management of fractures and deformities, and genetic counseling.

CASE_ID: 437
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Marfan Syndrome

ALIASES:
Marfan's syndrome
fibrillin-1 disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 19-year-old man is referred after a pre-participation sports physical noted that he is unusually tall and thin with long limbs and a heart murmur.
2. He reports decreasing vision and a family history of a relative who died suddenly of a "torn aorta" at a young age.
3. On exam, he is tall with an arm span exceeding his height, long slender fingers (arachnodactyly), a positive thumb and wrist sign, pectus excavatum, a high-arched palate, and hyperflexible joints; a diastolic murmur of aortic regurgitation is heard.
4. Slit-lamp examination reveals upward (superotemporal) lens dislocation (ectopia lentis).
5. Echocardiography shows dilation of the aortic root.
6. He is started on a beta blocker (or an ARB) to slow aortic dilation, restricted from contact and high-intensity sports, and monitored with serial echocardiograms for prophylactic aortic surgery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Marfan syndrome is an autosomal dominant connective tissue disorder caused by mutations in the fibrillin-1 (FBN1) gene, a component of elastic fibers.
- Skeletal features: tall stature, long extremities, arachnodactyly, pectus deformities, scoliosis, joint hypermobility, and a high-arched palate.
- Ocular: superotemporal (upward) lens dislocation - ectopia lentis (contrast with homocystinuria, which causes downward dislocation).
- The major cause of mortality is aortic root dilation leading to dissection or rupture; mitral valve prolapse and aortic regurgitation also occur.
- Management: beta blockers or ARBs to reduce aortic stress, activity restriction, serial imaging, and prophylactic aortic root replacement; genetic counseling.

CASE_ID: 438
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Ehlers-Danlos Syndrome

ALIASES:
EDS
hereditary connective tissue hypermobility

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old woman presents with chronic joint pain, frequent joint dislocations, and skin that bruises very easily.
2. She reports poor wound healing with widened scars and a family history of similar joint and skin problems.
3. On exam, she has marked joint hypermobility (she can hyperextend her fingers and elbows well beyond normal), skin that stretches excessively and recoils, and thin atrophic "cigarette paper" scars.
4. There are no immediate signs of vascular catastrophe, but a careful history and family history are obtained given the possibility of the vascular subtype.
5. The clinical features fit a hypermobility/classical pattern; genetic testing is used to identify high-risk subtypes (the vascular type, due to type III collagen defects).
6. Management is supportive with physical therapy, joint protection, and skin care; patients with the vascular subtype require vigilant monitoring for arterial and organ rupture.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Ehlers-Danlos syndrome is a group of inherited connective tissue disorders affecting collagen; multiple subtypes exist with different genes and severities.
- Common features: joint hypermobility and recurrent dislocations, hyperextensible skin, easy bruising, and poor/abnormal wound healing with atrophic scars.
- The vascular subtype (classically type III collagen, COL3A1) is the most dangerous, predisposing to spontaneous rupture of arteries, bowel, and the gravid uterus.
- Classic EDS involves type V collagen defects; the hypermobility type is the most common but least understood genetically.
- Management is largely supportive (physical therapy, joint and skin protection); the vascular subtype requires avoidance of trauma and careful surgical/obstetric planning.

CASE_ID: 439
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Plantar Fasciitis

ALIASES:
plantar fasciopathy
heel pain syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 45-year-old recreational runner presents with sharp pain in the bottom of his heel that is worst with the first steps in the morning.
2. The pain improves after walking for a while but returns after prolonged standing; he recently increased his running mileage and is overweight.
3. On exam, there is point tenderness at the medial calcaneal tubercle (the origin of the plantar fascia), and dorsiflexion of the toes reproduces the pain.
4. There is no numbness, no swelling, and a normal neurovascular exam; the rest of the foot is unremarkable.
5. The diagnosis is clinical; imaging is not required but a heel spur may be incidentally seen on X-ray (not the cause of pain).
6. He is treated with stretching exercises, supportive footwear with arch supports, activity modification, and NSAIDs, with most cases resolving over months.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Plantar fasciitis is the most common cause of heel pain, resulting from degeneration and microtears of the plantar fascia at its calcaneal origin.
- Classic feature: sharp heel pain that is worst with the first steps in the morning (or after periods of rest) and improves with activity.
- Risk factors: running and high-impact activity, obesity, prolonged standing, and tight calf muscles or pes planus/cavus.
- Diagnosis is clinical (point tenderness at the medial calcaneal tubercle); incidental heel spurs are common and not the source of pain.
- Treatment is conservative: stretching, supportive footwear/orthotics, activity modification, and NSAIDs; most cases resolve, with injections or surgery rarely needed.

CASE_ID: 440
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Schizoaffective Disorder

ALIASES:
schizoaffective illness

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 30-year-old woman is brought in by her family for evaluation of bizarre behavior and mood disturbances over the past year.
2. They report that for the past several months she has had persistent auditory hallucinations and paranoid delusions, and that during this time she also had a 6-week period of severe depression and, separately, a period of elevated mood.
3. On exam, she is guarded with a flat affect, describes hearing voices commenting on her actions, and holds fixed paranoid beliefs; her thought process is tangential.
4. Critically, she reports that the hallucinations and delusions have persisted even during weeks when her mood was completely normal.
5. Urine toxicology is negative, and medical causes are excluded.
6. She is treated with an antipsychotic and a mood stabilizer or antidepressant as appropriate, with psychosocial support.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Schizoaffective disorder requires a major mood episode (depressive or manic) concurrent with criteria for schizophrenia, PLUS at least 2 weeks of psychotic symptoms (delusions or hallucinations) in the ABSENCE of a major mood episode.
- The presence of psychosis without mood symptoms distinguishes it from a mood disorder with psychotic features (where psychosis occurs only during mood episodes).
- Mood symptoms must be present for the majority of the total illness duration (distinguishing it from schizophrenia, where mood symptoms are brief if present).
- Subtypes: bipolar type (manic episodes occur) and depressive type.
- Treatment combines antipsychotics with mood stabilizers or antidepressants; prognosis is generally better than schizophrenia but worse than a primary mood disorder.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 441
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Body Dysmorphic Disorder

ALIASES:
BDD
dysmorphophobia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old man presents at the urging of his family, who are concerned about the hours he spends examining his appearance in the mirror.
2. He is convinced that his nose is grossly deformed and ugly, although others reassure him it looks completely normal; he has consulted multiple plastic surgeons.
3. On exam, his nose appears entirely normal; he describes spending more than 4 hours a day checking mirrors, comparing himself to others, and avoiding social situations because of his perceived flaw.
4. His preoccupation has caused him to drop out of school and avoid dating; he has had thoughts of self-harm related to his appearance.
5. There is no evidence of an eating disorder (his concern is not about weight), and reality testing about the defect is poor (he is nearly delusional in his conviction).
6. He is treated with a high-dose SSRI and cognitive behavioral therapy; cosmetic procedures are discouraged as they rarely satisfy and may worsen the disorder.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Body dysmorphic disorder is a preoccupation with one or more perceived defects in physical appearance that are not observable or appear slight to others.
- Patients perform repetitive behaviors (mirror checking, grooming, reassurance seeking, comparing) in response to the appearance concerns, causing significant distress or impairment.
- It is classified in the obsessive-compulsive and related disorders spectrum.
- It is distinct from an eating disorder (where the concern is specifically about weight and body shape).
- Treatment: SSRIs (often at higher doses) and cognitive behavioral therapy; cosmetic/surgical interventions are generally ineffective and not recommended; screen for suicidality.

CASE_ID: 442
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Narcolepsy

ALIASES:
narcolepsy with cataplexy
hypocretin deficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 19-year-old college student presents with two years of overwhelming daytime sleepiness and falling asleep involuntarily during classes despite adequate nighttime sleep.
2. He describes sudden episodes of muscle weakness in his knees and face triggered by laughter, vivid dream-like experiences as he falls asleep, and occasional inability to move upon waking.
3. On exam, the neurologic examination is normal between episodes; a witnessed bout of laughter produces transient buckling of his knees with preserved consciousness.
4. A multiple sleep latency test shows a short sleep latency with rapid entry into REM sleep on multiple naps.
5. Cerebrospinal fluid analysis reveals low hypocretin (orexin) levels.
6. He is treated with scheduled naps and sleep hygiene plus a wakefulness-promoting agent (modafinil); cataplexy is managed with sodium oxybate or certain antidepressants.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Narcolepsy is a chronic disorder of sleep-wake regulation, with type 1 characterized by loss of hypocretin (orexin)-producing neurons in the hypothalamus.
- The classic tetrad: excessive daytime sleepiness, cataplexy (sudden loss of muscle tone triggered by emotion, especially laughter, with preserved consciousness), sleep paralysis, and hypnagogic/hypnopompic hallucinations.
- Cataplexy is highly specific and defines type 1 narcolepsy; it reflects intrusion of REM-sleep atonia into wakefulness.
- Diagnosis: multiple sleep latency test showing short sleep latency and sleep-onset REM periods; low CSF hypocretin supports type 1; strong HLA-DQB1*06:02 association.
- Treatment: scheduled naps and good sleep hygiene, wakefulness-promoting agents (modafinil) for sleepiness, and sodium oxybate or certain antidepressants for cataplexy.

CASE_ID: 443
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Open-Angle Glaucoma

ALIASES:
primary open-angle glaucoma
POAG
chronic glaucoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man is found to have an abnormality during a routine eye examination; he has noticed some difficulty with peripheral vision while driving.
2. He has a family history of glaucoma and a history of type 2 diabetes; he has had no eye pain or redness.
3. On exam, visual acuity is preserved centrally, but visual field testing reveals bilateral peripheral (especially arcuate/nasal) field loss.
4. Tonometry shows elevated intraocular pressure bilaterally; gonioscopy reveals an open anterior chamber angle.
5. Funduscopy demonstrates increased optic disc cupping with an elevated cup-to-disc ratio bilaterally.
6. He is started on topical prostaglandin analog drops to lower intraocular pressure, with the goal of slowing progressive optic nerve damage and preserving remaining vision.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Primary open-angle glaucoma is a chronic, progressive optic neuropathy associated with elevated intraocular pressure and an OPEN anterior chamber angle.
- It is typically painless and insidious, causing gradual loss of PERIPHERAL vision first; central vision is preserved until late (contrast with the acute, painful presentation of angle-closure glaucoma).
- Risk factors: increased age, African ancestry, family history, elevated IOP, diabetes, and thin corneas.
- Funduscopy shows increased optic disc cupping (elevated cup-to-disc ratio); diagnosis integrates IOP, visual fields, and optic disc appearance.
- Treatment lowers IOP: topical prostaglandin analogs (first-line), beta blockers, alpha agonists, carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; laser trabeculoplasty or surgery for refractory cases.

CASE_ID: 444
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Central Retinal Vein Occlusion

ALIASES:
CRVO
retinal vein thrombosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man presents with sudden, painless loss of vision in his right eye that occurred over a few hours upon waking.
2. He has hypertension, diabetes, and a history of glaucoma; he takes no anticoagulants.
3. On exam, visual acuity in the right eye is severely reduced; there is a relative afferent pupillary defect; the eye is not red or painful.
4. Dilated funduscopy of the affected eye reveals diffuse retinal hemorrhages in all four quadrants, dilated tortuous veins, cotton wool spots, and optic disc edema - the classic "blood and thunder" fundus.
5. The contralateral eye is normal, and there is no evidence of arterial occlusion.
6. He is referred urgently to ophthalmology; treatment focuses on managing macular edema (anti-VEGF injections) and neovascular complications, along with control of vascular risk factors.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Central retinal vein occlusion causes sudden, painless monocular vision loss from thrombosis of the central retinal vein, leading to venous congestion and retinal ischemia.
- The classic funduscopic appearance is "blood and thunder": diffuse retinal hemorrhages in all quadrants, dilated tortuous veins, cotton wool spots, and disc edema.
- Risk factors: hypertension, diabetes, hyperlipidemia, glaucoma, and hypercoagulable states (consider the latter in younger patients).
- Contrast with central retinal ARTERY occlusion: a pale retina with a cherry-red spot, "box-carring," and a sudden painless loss treated as a stroke-equivalent emergency.
- Major complications are macular edema and neovascularization (which can cause neovascular glaucoma); treatment includes anti-VEGF therapy and managing risk factors.

CASE_ID: 445
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Limb Compartment Syndrome

ALIASES:
compartment syndrome
acute compartment syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old man sustained a closed tibial shaft fracture in a soccer injury and now, several hours after casting, reports severe and worsening leg pain that is out of proportion to the injury.
2. The pain is not relieved by opioids and is dramatically worsened by passive movement of his toes.
3. On exam, the leg compartment is tense and swollen; passive stretch of the muscles elicits excruciating pain; he reports paresthesias in the foot, and the compartment feels firm.
4. Distal pulses are still palpable (pulses are often preserved until very late and should not be falsely reassuring).
5. Compartment pressure measurement is elevated, with a small difference between the compartment pressure and the diastolic blood pressure.
6. He is taken emergently for fasciotomy to relieve the pressure and prevent irreversible muscle and nerve necrosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acute compartment syndrome occurs when increased pressure within a closed fascial compartment compromises perfusion, leading to ischemia of muscles and nerves.
- Common causes: long bone fractures (especially tibia), crush injuries, reperfusion after vascular injury, and tight casts/dressings.
- The earliest and most reliable sign is PAIN OUT OF PROPORTION to the injury and pain with passive stretch of the involved muscles.
- The classic "6 P's" (pain, paresthesia, pallor, paralysis, pulselessness, poikilothermia) are misleading: pulselessness and paralysis are LATE findings; do not wait for them.
- It is a surgical emergency treated with emergent fasciotomy; delay risks Volkmann ischemic contracture, rhabdomyolysis, and limb loss.

CASE_ID: 446
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Perforated Peptic Ulcer

ALIASES:
perforated viscus
gastric perforation

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man presents with the sudden onset of severe, diffuse abdominal pain that began abruptly while he was at home.
2. He has a long history of using NSAIDs for arthritis and has had intermittent epigastric pain; the pain is now constant and worsened by any movement.
3. On exam, he is lying very still, is tachycardic and febrile, and has a rigid, board-like abdomen with diffuse rebound tenderness and absent bowel sounds.
4. Labs show leukocytosis; an upright chest X-ray reveals free air under the diaphragm (pneumoperitoneum).
5. The clinical picture is consistent with a perforated viscus causing chemical and then bacterial peritonitis.
6. He is resuscitated with IV fluids, given broad-spectrum antibiotics and a proton pump inhibitor, and taken emergently to the operating room for surgical repair.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Perforation is a serious complication of peptic ulcer disease, classically from NSAID use or H. pylori; most commonly an anterior duodenal ulcer.
- It presents with the sudden onset of severe abdominal pain followed by signs of peritonitis: a rigid "board-like" abdomen, rebound tenderness, and a patient who lies still to avoid movement.
- Free air under the diaphragm (pneumoperitoneum) on an upright chest X-ray is the classic finding; CT is more sensitive.
- Spillage of gastric/duodenal contents causes chemical peritonitis followed by bacterial peritonitis and sepsis.
- Management: aggressive resuscitation, broad-spectrum antibiotics, acid suppression, and emergent surgical repair (often with an omental/Graham patch).

CASE_ID: 447
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Primary Sclerosing Cholangitis

ALIASES:
PSC
sclerosing cholangitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old man with ulcerative colitis presents with several months of fatigue, intermittent itching, and right upper quadrant discomfort.
2. He has noticed darkening of his urine and intermittent mild jaundice; his colitis has been relatively quiescent.
3. On exam, he has mild scleral icterus and excoriations from scratching; the liver edge is mildly enlarged.
4. Labs show a cholestatic pattern with markedly elevated alkaline phosphatase and GGT, mildly elevated bilirubin, and a positive p-ANCA; antimitochondrial antibody is negative.
5. Magnetic resonance cholangiopancreatography reveals multifocal strictures and dilations of the intrahepatic and extrahepatic bile ducts, producing a "beaded" appearance.
6. He is managed with surveillance for complications; he is counseled on the markedly increased risk of cholangiocarcinoma and colorectal cancer, and liver transplantation is the only definitive treatment for end-stage disease.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Primary sclerosing cholangitis is a chronic cholestatic disease with inflammation and fibrosis of the intra- and extrahepatic bile ducts.
- It has a strong association with inflammatory bowel disease, especially ulcerative colitis (most PSC patients have IBD).
- Classic imaging (MRCP or ERCP): multifocal strictures alternating with dilations producing a "beaded" or "string of beads" appearance.
- Labs show a cholestatic pattern (high alkaline phosphatase and GGT); p-ANCA is often positive; antimitochondrial antibody is typically negative (distinguishing it from primary biliary cholangitis, which affects middle-aged women and is AMA-positive).
- Major complications include cholangiocarcinoma, colorectal cancer (in those with IBD), and biliary cirrhosis; liver transplantation is the definitive treatment.

CASE_ID: 448
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Osteomyelitis from Hematogenous Spread

ALIASES:
acute hematogenous osteomyelitis
bone infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. An 8-year-old boy presents with several days of fever and worsening pain in his right lower leg, refusing to bear weight.
2. He had a minor skin infection a couple of weeks ago; there is no history of penetrating trauma to the leg.
3. On exam, he is febrile with focal tenderness, warmth, and swelling over the distal femoral metaphysis; range of motion of the adjacent knee is limited by pain.
4. Labs show leukocytosis with markedly elevated ESR and CRP; blood cultures are drawn and grow gram-positive cocci in clusters.
5. Early plain films are normal, but MRI reveals bone marrow edema and a developing subperiosteal collection in the metaphysis.
6. He is treated with prolonged IV antibiotics targeting the organism, with surgical drainage reserved for abscess or failure to respond.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acute hematogenous osteomyelitis is most common in children and typically affects the metaphysis of long bones (rich blood supply with sluggish flow).
- Staphylococcus aureus is the most common overall cause; consider Salmonella in sickle cell disease, Pseudomonas in puncture wounds through shoes and IV drug users, and group B strep in neonates.
- Early plain X-rays are often normal (changes lag 1-2 weeks); MRI is the most sensitive early imaging and shows marrow edema.
- ESR and CRP are sensitive markers; bone biopsy/culture or blood cultures identify the organism to guide therapy.
- Treatment is prolonged antibiotics (often several weeks) directed at the organism, with surgical debridement for abscess, necrotic bone, or failure to improve.

CASE_ID: 449
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Adrenal Crisis

ALIASES:
acute adrenal insufficiency
addisonian crisis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 44-year-old woman with known primary adrenal insufficiency presents to the ED with severe weakness, vomiting, abdominal pain, and near-syncope after several days of a febrile gastrointestinal illness.
2. She admits she stopped taking her hydrocortisone because she could not keep anything down.
3. On exam, she is hypotensive (BP 78/44), tachycardic, and lethargic with diffuse abdominal tenderness; her skin shows diffuse hyperpigmentation including the palmar creases.
4. Labs reveal hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, hypoglycemia, and a mild metabolic acidosis; a random cortisol is inappropriately low.
5. The clinical picture, combined with the precipitating illness and missed steroid doses, is diagnostic of adrenal crisis.
6. She is treated immediately with IV hydrocortisone and aggressive IV fluids with dextrose, without waiting for confirmatory testing, with rapid hemodynamic improvement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Adrenal crisis is a life-threatening emergency caused by an acute deficiency of cortisol, often precipitated by stress (infection, surgery, trauma) or abrupt cessation of glucocorticoids in a steroid-dependent patient.
- Hallmarks: hypotension/shock refractory to fluids and vasopressors, often with abdominal pain, vomiting, and altered mental status.
- In primary adrenal insufficiency (Addison disease), aldosterone deficiency also causes hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, and volume depletion; hyperpigmentation reflects elevated ACTH/MSH.
- Treatment must not be delayed for diagnostic confirmation: give IV hydrocortisone (stress-dose glucocorticoid) and aggressive IV fluids with dextrose immediately.
- Prevention: patient education on stress-dosing during illness, never abruptly stopping chronic steroids, and carrying emergency injectable glucocorticoid.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 450
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Beta-Blocker Toxicity

ALIASES:
beta-adrenergic antagonist overdose
beta blocker overdose

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 55-year-old man is brought to the ED after intentionally ingesting a large quantity of his cardiac medication about an hour ago; he is now lightheaded and confused.
2. He has a history of hypertension and depression and takes propranolol; an empty pill bottle was found beside him.
3. On exam, he is bradycardic at 38 bpm and hypotensive at 80/50; he is diaphoretic and has cool extremities, with one episode of a seizure and a fingerstick glucose that is low.
4. ECG shows sinus bradycardia with a prolonged PR interval and a widened QRS (from propranolol's sodium-channel-blocking, membrane-stabilizing effect).
5. The bradycardia and hypotension are initially refractory to atropine and IV fluids.
6. He is treated with high-dose glucagon (which bypasses the beta receptor to increase cardiac cAMP), along with IV fluids, vasopressors, and high-dose insulin-dextrose therapy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Beta-blocker overdose causes bradycardia and hypotension by blocking beta-adrenergic receptors and reducing cardiac cAMP.
- Lipophilic agents (propranolol) can cause CNS effects (seizures, coma) and, with membrane-stabilizing activity, QRS widening; beta-blockade can also cause hypoglycemia (especially in children).
- Bradycardia and hypotension are frequently refractory to atropine and fluids alone.
- The classic antidote is GLUCAGON, which activates adenylate cyclase via a separate receptor, raising cardiac cAMP independent of the beta receptor.
- Additional therapies: IV fluids, vasopressors, high-dose insulin-euglycemia therapy, and lipid emulsion or pacing for refractory cases; contrast with calcium channel blocker toxicity, which causes similar hemodynamics but with hyperglycemia.
================================================== 
CASE_ID: 451
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Cardiac Amyloidosis

ALIASES:
amyloid cardiomyopathy
ATTR amyloidosis
AL amyloidosis cardiac

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old man presents with six months of progressive exertional dyspnea and bilateral lower extremity edema.
2. He has bilateral carpal tunnel syndrome from a decade ago and his previously well-controlled hypertension has resolved without any medication changes.
3. Exam reveals an elevated jugular venous pressure, an S4 gallop, pitting pretibial edema, and faint periorbital purpura after Valsalva.
4. ECG shows low-voltage limb leads despite a wall thickness of an abnormal value on echo; troponin is mildly and chronically elevated.
5. Echocardiography demonstrates concentric LV hypertrophy with a "sparkling" granular myocardium, biatrial enlargement, and a relative apical sparing pattern on strain imaging.
6. Technetium-99m pyrophosphate scintigraphy shows grade 3 myocardial uptake, serum and urine immunofixation are negative for a monoclonal protein, and tafamidis is initiated for confirmed ATTR cardiac amyloidosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Cardiac amyloidosis classically causes HFpEF with a voltage-mass discrepancy on ECG (low voltage despite thick walls) and a "sparkling" myocardium.
- Bilateral carpal tunnel syndrome and spontaneous resolution of prior hypertension are important non-cardiac clues that should prompt evaluation.
- PYP scintigraphy with grade 2-3 uptake distinguishes ATTR from AL when monoclonal proteins are absent; tafamidis stabilizes the transthyretin tetramer.

CASE_ID: 452
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Arrhythmogenic Right Ventricular Cardiomyopathy

ALIASES:
ARVC
ARVD
arrhythmogenic right ventricular dysplasia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old male collegiate soccer player collapses during a match and is resuscitated from ventricular fibrillation.
2. He has had months of palpitations and presyncope on exertion that he dismissed as anxiety; his uncle died suddenly at age 35.
3. He is hemodynamically stable on arrival; cardiac auscultation reveals a soft systolic murmur at the lower left sternal border.
4. A 12-lead ECG shows a terminal notch on the QRS in V1-V3 and T-wave inversions in the right precordial leads.
5. Cardiac MRI demonstrates a dilated, hypokinetic right ventricle with fibrofatty replacement of the free wall and regional wall motion abnormalities.
6. Genetic testing identifies a pathogenic plakophilin-2 (PKP2) desmosomal mutation, and an ICD is implanted given his aborted sudden cardiac death.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- ARVC causes fibrofatty replacement of the right ventricular myocardium and presents with exercise-induced ventricular arrhythmias and sudden death in young athletes.
- The epsilon wave (terminal QRS notch in V1-V3) and right precordial T-wave inversions are characteristic ECG findings.
- It is autosomal dominant with desmosomal gene mutations (PKP2 most common); management is exercise restriction plus ICD in high-risk patients.

CASE_ID: 453
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Brugada Syndrome

ALIASES:
Brugada
SCN5A channelopathy
sudden unexplained nocturnal death syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old man of Southeast Asian descent is evaluated after a nighttime syncopal event with gasping respirations witnessed by his wife.
2. His brother died unexpectedly in his sleep at age 38 and an uncle drowned without explanation.
3. Exam is unremarkable; he is in sinus rhythm with a structurally normal heart on echocardiography.
4. A baseline ECG shows incomplete right bundle branch block; during a febrile illness, the ECG develops a coved ST elevation greater than an abnormal value in V1-V2.
5. A procainamide challenge unmasks a type 1 Brugada pattern across the right precordial leads.
6. Genetic testing reveals a pathogenic SCN5A loss-of-function mutation, and an ICD is implanted given his arrhythmic syncope.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Brugada syndrome is an inherited sodium channelopathy classically affecting young men of Asian descent and presenting with nocturnal arrhythmic death.
- Type 1 ECG pattern is a coved ST elevation greater than 2 mm with T-wave inversion in V1-V2; fever and sodium channel blockers can unmask it.
- SCN5A mutations are the most common cause; ICD is indicated for survivors of cardiac arrest or those with arrhythmic syncope plus a type 1 pattern.

CASE_ID: 454
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Takotsubo Cardiomyopathy

ALIASES:
stress cardiomyopathy
apical ballooning syndrome
broken heart syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old postmenopausal woman presents with acute substernal chest pain and dyspnea that began an hour after learning of her husband's sudden death.
2. She has no history of coronary disease and her only medication is a thiazide.
3. Vitals show mild hypotension; lung auscultation reveals bibasilar crackles and her ECG shows new anterior T-wave inversions with QT prolongation.
4. Troponin is only mildly elevated and is disproportionately low for the degree of wall-motion abnormality; brain natriuretic peptide is markedly elevated.
5. Bedside echocardiography demonstrates apical akinesis with hypercontractile basal segments and an LV ejection fraction of 30%.
6. Coronary angiography shows no obstructive disease and a ventriculogram captures the characteristic apical ballooning; repeat echo at six weeks demonstrates fully normalized LV function.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Takotsubo cardiomyopathy mimics anterior MI with chest pain, ECG changes, and modest troponin elevation, classically triggered by intense emotional or physical stress in postmenopausal women.
- The defining feature is transient apical ballooning with preserved basal contraction in the absence of obstructive coronary disease.
- It is mediated by catecholamine surge and usually resolves over weeks with supportive care; watch for LVOT obstruction, mural thrombus, and arrhythmias.

CASE_ID: 455
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Lymphangioleiomyomatosis

ALIASES:
LAM
pulmonary lymphangioleiomyomatosis
TSC-associated LAM

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old woman presents with her third spontaneous pneumothorax in two years and progressive dyspnea on exertion.
2. She has no smoking history; she has had mild hemoptysis and small facial angiofibromas across her nose and cheeks.
3. Exam reveals decreased breath sounds at the apices and a palpable left flank mass.
4. Pulmonary function testing reveals an obstructive pattern with reduced DLCO; abdominal imaging incidentally reveals bilateral renal angiomyolipomas.
5. High-resolution chest CT shows numerous thin-walled, round cysts diffusely distributed throughout both lungs.
6. Serum VEGF-D returns at 1200 pg/mL and genetic testing confirms a TSC2 mutation; sirolimus is initiated with stabilization of lung function.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- LAM is a rare cystic lung disease almost exclusively in women of reproductive age, causing recurrent pneumothorax and progressive obstruction.
- It is associated with tuberous sclerosis complex (sporadic LAM also occurs) and features renal angiomyolipomas and chylothorax.
- Diagnosis: characteristic HRCT cysts plus elevated VEGF-D or compatible features; mTOR inhibitors (sirolimus) are the mainstay of treatment.

CASE_ID: 456
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pulmonary Alveolar Proteinosis

ALIASES:
PAP
alveolar proteinosis
GM-CSF autoantibody PAP

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 40-year-old man presents with several months of progressive dyspnea on exertion and a dry cough.
2. He has no significant occupational exposures and is a never-smoker; he has had several recent low-grade respiratory infections.
3. Exam reveals diffuse fine inspiratory crackles bilaterally and an oxygen saturation of 88% on room air; clubbing is absent.
4. Pulmonary function tests show restrictive physiology with reduced DLCO and serum LDH is markedly elevated.
5. High-resolution chest CT demonstrates bilateral ground-glass opacities with superimposed interlobular septal thickening producing a "crazy-paving" pattern.
6. Bronchoalveolar lavage returns opaque, milky fluid with PAS-positive amorphous eosinophilic material, anti-GM-CSF antibodies are detected in serum, and whole-lung lavage produces marked clinical improvement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Pulmonary alveolar proteinosis results from accumulation of surfactant-derived lipoproteinaceous material in alveoli.
- Most adult cases are autoimmune, caused by anti-GM-CSF antibodies impairing macrophage clearance; secondary forms occur with hematologic disease or silica exposure.
- HRCT shows "crazy-paving," BAL fluid is milky and PAS-positive, and whole-lung lavage plus inhaled GM-CSF are mainstays of treatment.

CASE_ID: 457
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pulmonary Langerhans Cell Histiocytosis

ALIASES:
PLCH
pulmonary histiocytosis X
eosinophilic granuloma of the lung

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old man presents with a spontaneous right-sided pneumothorax and several months of dry cough and dyspnea on exertion.
2. He has smoked a pack of cigarettes daily since age 16 and has new polyuria with nocturia.
3. Exam reveals diminished breath sounds on the right and clubbing is absent; he is afebrile.
4. Pulmonary function testing shows a mixed obstructive-restrictive pattern with reduced DLCO; a water deprivation test demonstrates central diabetes insipidus.
5. High-resolution chest CT shows numerous bizarre, irregularly shaped cysts and small nodules predominantly in the upper and middle lung zones, sparing the costophrenic angles.
6. Surgical lung biopsy reveals stellate bronchiolocentric nodules infiltrated by CD1a- and S100-positive Langerhans cells, and smoking cessation produces stabilization of his lung function.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Pulmonary Langerhans cell histiocytosis is a smoking-related interstitial lung disease in young to middle-aged adults, with bizarre upper-lobe-predominant cysts and nodules that spare the costophrenic angles.
- Extrapulmonary involvement (bone lytic lesions, central diabetes insipidus from pituitary stalk infiltration) supports the diagnosis.
- Diagnosis is by biopsy showing CD1a/CD207/S100-positive Langerhans cells; smoking cessation is the cornerstone of treatment and often halts progression.

CASE_ID: 458
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Eosinophilic Esophagitis

ALIASES:
EoE
eosinophilic esophagitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old man presents with three years of intermittent solid food dysphagia and a recent food impaction with a piece of meat at a restaurant.
2. He has childhood asthma, seasonal allergies, and atopic dermatitis; an 8-week PPI trial has not relieved his symptoms.
3. Exam is unremarkable and his weight is stable.
4. Routine labs are unremarkable apart from a mild peripheral eosinophilia.
5. Upper endoscopy reveals esophageal rings ("trachealization"), longitudinal furrows, scattered white exudates, and a narrow-caliber lumen.
6. Esophageal biopsies from proximal and distal segments show greater than 60 eosinophils per high-power field with eosinophilic microabscesses; topical swallowed budesonide and an empiric six-food elimination diet are initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Eosinophilic esophagitis is a chronic, immune-mediated esophageal disease driven by food and aeroallergen sensitization, common in atopic young men.
- Endoscopic clues include rings, furrows, exudates, and strictures; biopsy showing greater than 15 eosinophils per HPF on PPI therapy confirms diagnosis.
- Treatment is the "3 Ds": diet (empiric elimination), drugs (topical swallowed corticosteroids, PPIs, dupilumab), and dilation for strictures.

CASE_ID: 459
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Plummer-Vinson Syndrome

ALIASES:
Paterson-Brown-Kelly syndrome
sideropenic dysphagia
Plummer-Vinson

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old white woman presents with progressive solid food dysphagia over the past year and a "burning" tongue.
2. She has long-standing heavy menstrual bleeding and chronic fatigue; she avoids meat in her diet.
3. Exam reveals pallor, angular cheilitis, atrophic glossitis, and brittle spoon-shaped nails.
4. Labs show microcytic anemia with Hb 8.4, MCV 72, ferritin 4, and a transferrin saturation of 5%.
5. Barium swallow demonstrates a thin web in the postcricoid region of the upper esophagus.
6. Upper endoscopy confirms a postcricoid esophageal web that is dilated, biopsies rule out malignancy, and IV iron replacement plus dietary correction resolves her dysphagia.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Plummer-Vinson syndrome is the triad of iron deficiency anemia, dysphagia, and esophageal webs, classically in middle-aged white women.
- It is a recognized risk factor for squamous cell carcinoma of the upper esophagus and hypopharynx, warranting surveillance.
- Treatment is iron repletion and endoscopic dilation of the web; symptoms typically resolve with restoration of iron stores.

CASE_ID: 460
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Bartter Syndrome

ALIASES:
Bartter's syndrome
classic Bartter syndrome
NKCC2 channelopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 16-year-old girl presents with chronic fatigue, polyuria, polydipsia, and muscle cramps; she has always been below the 5th percentile for height.
2. She has had several episodes of unexplained hypokalemia attributed to dieting and is not on diuretics.
3. Exam reveals a thin, mildly dehydrated adolescent with normal blood pressure of 102/64 and no edema.
4. Labs show K+ 2.6, HCO3- 32, Cl- 88, Mg 1.5, and a urine chloride of an abnormal value; her urinary calcium is elevated.
5. Plasma renin and aldosterone are both markedly elevated; a urine diuretic screen is negative.
6. Genetic testing reveals a homozygous mutation in the NKCC2 (SLC12A1) gene, and she is started on potassium-sparing diuretics, indomethacin, and oral potassium with improvement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Bartter syndrome mimics chronic loop diuretic use: hypokalemic, hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis with normotension and hyperreninemia/hyperaldosteronism.
- Hypercalciuria distinguishes Bartter from Gitelman syndrome (which causes hypocalciuria and hypomagnesemia, thiazide-like).
- Causative genes encode NKCC2, ROMK, ClC-Kb, or barttin (thick ascending limb proteins); treat with potassium-sparing diuretics, NSAIDs, and electrolyte repletion.

CASE_ID: 461
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Type 4 Renal Tubular Acidosis

ALIASES:
type IV RTA
hyperkalemic RTA
hypoaldosteronism RTA

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man with long-standing diabetes is found to have persistent hyperkalemia despite a normal GFR and no medication changes.
2. He has diabetic nephropathy with stage 3a CKD; his medications include an ACE inhibitor and a beta-blocker.
3. Exam is unremarkable apart from mild peripheral neuropathy; he is normotensive.
4. Labs show K+ 6.1, Na+ 138, Cl- 110, HCO3- 18 with a normal anion gap, urine pH 5.2 (appropriately acidic), and urine sodium 60.
5. The urine anion gap is positive (Na + K > Cl), and both aldosterone and plasma renin are suppressed.
6. A fludrocortisone trial corrects the hyperkalemia and metabolic acidosis, confirming hyporeninemic hypoaldosteronism; diabetic management is intensified and the ACE inhibitor is reduced.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Type 4 RTA is a hyperkalemic, normal anion gap metabolic acidosis caused by aldosterone deficiency or resistance, classically in diabetic nephropathy.
- Urine pH is appropriately low (often less than 5.5) because hydrogen secretion is preserved, distinguishing it from type 1 (distal) RTA.
- Treatment: low-potassium diet, loop diuretics, fludrocortisone, and addressing contributing medications (ACE inhibitors, NSAIDs, potassium-sparing diuretics).

CASE_ID: 462
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Atypical Hemolytic Uremic Syndrome

ALIASES:
aHUS
complement-mediated TMA
atypical HUS

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 27-year-old woman presents three days postpartum with fatigue, dark urine, and decreased urine output.
2. The delivery was uncomplicated, but her aunt died of "kidney failure" in her 30s.
3. Exam shows pallor, scleral icterus, and minimal lower extremity edema; she is afebrile with normal mental status.
4. Labs show Hb 7.2, platelets 38,000, creatinine 4.1, LDH 1800, and indirect hyperbilirubinemia; peripheral smear shows numerous schistocytes.
5. ADAMTS13 activity is 75% (well above the TTP threshold), stool studies are negative for Shiga toxin, and complement testing shows low C3 with normal C4.
6. Genetic testing reveals a pathogenic complement factor H (CFH) mutation, and she is started on eculizumab with hematologic and renal recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- aHUS is a complement-mediated thrombotic microangiopathy with microangiopathic hemolytic anemia, thrombocytopenia, and AKI; pregnancy/postpartum is a classic trigger.
- Distinguish from typical HUS (Shiga-toxin E. coli, often diarrhea-positive) and TTP (severely low ADAMTS13, neurologic symptoms).
- Most cases involve mutations in complement regulators (CFH, CFI, MCP) or anti-CFH antibodies; treat with C5 inhibitor eculizumab or ravulizumab.

CASE_ID: 463
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Cold Agglutinin Disease

ALIASES:
cold agglutinin syndrome
cold autoimmune hemolytic anemia
IgM mediated AIHA

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old woman presents in winter with fatigue, exertional dyspnea, and dusky discoloration of her fingertips and nose tip whenever she goes outside.
2. She has had recurrent pneumonia in the past year and has noted dark urine on cold mornings.
3. Exam reveals pallor, scleral icterus, and acrocyanosis of the digits and pinnae that resolves with rewarming.
4. Labs show Hb 8.2, MCV 110 (spuriously elevated), reticulocyte count 8%, LDH 850, undetectable haptoglobin, and indirect hyperbilirubinemia; the peripheral smear shows striking red cell agglutination at room temperature.
5. The direct antiglobulin test is positive for C3d and negative for IgG, and the cold agglutinin titer is 1:2048.
6. Serum protein electrophoresis identifies a small IgM kappa monoclonal protein, bone marrow biopsy reveals a low-grade lymphoplasmacytic infiltrate, and rituximab is initiated with hematologic improvement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Cold agglutinin disease is an IgM-mediated autoimmune hemolytic anemia in which agglutinins bind erythrocytes at low temperatures and activate complement, causing extravascular (and sometimes intravascular) hemolysis.
- Classic features: cold-induced acrocyanosis of distal extremities and nose, hemolytic anemia worse in winter, and red-cell agglutination on a room-temperature smear with a spuriously high MCV.
- DAT is positive for C3d (not IgG); primary disease is associated with a clonal IgM gammopathy, while secondary causes include Mycoplasma pneumoniae, EBV, and B-cell lymphomas; rituximab is first-line, and avoid cold exposure.

CASE_ID: 464
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Promyelocytic Leukemia

ALIASES:
APL
APML
AML-M3

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old woman presents with two weeks of bruising, gum bleeding, fatigue, and new heavy menstrual bleeding.
2. She has no recent illnesses, medications, or toxin exposures and is otherwise healthy.
3. Exam reveals pallor, scattered petechiae over the legs, and oozing from venipuncture sites; there is no hepatosplenomegaly or lymphadenopathy.
4. CBC shows WBC 1.2, Hb 7.4, and platelets 18,000; coagulation studies show PT 22, aPTT 48, fibrinogen 90, and markedly elevated D-dimer, consistent with DIC.
5. Peripheral smear reveals hypergranular promyelocytes with bundles of Auer rods ("faggot cells") and bilobed nuclei.
6. FISH on bone marrow demonstrates the t(15;17)(q22;q21) PML-RARA fusion, and emergent ATRA plus arsenic trioxide produce rapid resolution of coagulopathy and remission.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- APL (AML-M3) is defined by the t(15;17) PML-RARA fusion and presents with severe coagulopathy (DIC plus hyperfibrinolysis) and hypergranular blasts.
- "Faggot cells" (bundles of Auer rods) on the smear are pathognomonic; start ATRA immediately on clinical suspicion to reduce early hemorrhagic death.
- ATRA plus arsenic trioxide has made APL the most curable acute leukemia; watch for differentiation syndrome (fever, dyspnea, weight gain, hypotension).

CASE_ID: 465
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Mantle Cell Lymphoma

ALIASES:
MCL
cyclin D1 lymphoma
t(11;14) lymphoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 66-year-old man presents with generalized painless lymphadenopathy, night sweats, and a 15-pound weight loss over four months.
2. He has had progressive abdominal bloating and early satiety; his stools have recently become more frequent and loose.
3. Exam reveals diffuse non-tender lymphadenopathy, marked splenomegaly, and tonsillar enlargement; he has multiple small polypoid lesions seen on rectal exam.
4. CBC shows a leukocytosis with a lymphocytosis of 22,000; peripheral smear shows medium-sized irregular lymphocytes with cleaved nuclei.
5. Excisional lymph node biopsy reveals a diffuse proliferation of small to medium-sized lymphocytes effacing the nodal architecture, and immunohistochemistry is positive for CD5, CD20, and cyclin D1 while negative for CD23.
6. FISH demonstrates the t(11;14)(q13;q32) translocation, colonoscopy reveals multiple lymphomatous polyps (lymphomatous polyposis), and he is started on rituximab plus high-dose cytarabine-containing induction with planned autologous stem cell transplant.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Mantle cell lymphoma is an aggressive B-cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma defined by the t(11;14) translocation, which juxtaposes cyclin D1 (CCND1) with the IgH locus, driving overexpression.
- Immunophenotype: CD20+, CD5+, CD23- (vs CLL, which is CD23+), cyclin D1+; lymphomatous polyposis of the GI tract is a characteristic feature.
- Treatment usually involves intensive chemoimmunotherapy plus autologous stem cell transplant for younger patients, with BTK inhibitors (ibrutinib, acalabrutinib) for relapsed disease.

CASE_ID: 466
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology/oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acquired Hemophilia A

ALIASES:
acquired factor VIII deficiency
autoimmune hemophilia
factor VIII inhibitor

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old woman with no personal or family history of bleeding presents with extensive spontaneous bruising and a large painful right thigh hematoma.
2. She has no recent trauma, is on no anticoagulants, and was recently diagnosed with breast cancer awaiting treatment.
3. Exam reveals widespread large ecchymoses across the trunk and limbs, a tense and tender right thigh hematoma, and pallor.
4. Labs show Hb 7.6, a markedly prolonged aPTT of 86 seconds, a normal PT, and a normal platelet count.
5. A mixing study fails to correct the prolonged aPTT after a 2-hour incubation, and factor VIII activity is less than 1%.
6. A Bethesda assay confirms an anti-factor VIII inhibitor at 18 Bethesda units; she receives recombinant activated factor VII for active bleeding and is started on prednisone plus rituximab to eradicate the inhibitor.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acquired hemophilia A is an autoantibody-mediated factor VIII deficiency in adults with no prior bleeding history, presenting with spontaneous mucocutaneous and soft-tissue hemorrhage.
- The key lab finding is an isolated prolonged aPTT that does NOT correct on mixing study (with delayed incubation), reflecting an inhibitor rather than a factor deficiency.
- Associations include the postpartum state, malignancy, autoimmune disease, and drugs; treat acute bleeding with bypassing agents (recombinant FVIIa, aPCC, or recombinant porcine FVIII) and eradicate the inhibitor with immunosuppression (steroids plus rituximab or cyclophosphamide).

CASE_ID: 467
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Babesiosis

ALIASES:
Babesia microti infection
babesiosis
piroplasmosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 70-year-old asplenic man presents with two weeks of fevers, chills, drenching sweats, and progressive fatigue.
2. He spends summers on Nantucket and recalls multiple tick bites a month ago.
3. Exam shows fever to 39.4 C, tachycardia, scleral icterus, and a midline splenectomy scar; no rash is present.
4. Labs show Hb 8.6, platelets 64,000, total bilirubin 3.8 with indirect predominance, undetectable haptoglobin, and LDH 720, consistent with intravascular hemolysis.
5. Peripheral smear reveals intraerythrocytic ring forms, some in a tetrad ("Maltese cross") configuration, and PCR is positive for Babesia microti.
6. He develops respiratory failure requiring partial exchange transfusion, IV clindamycin plus quinine clear his parasitemia, and convalescent serology confirms cure.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Babesiosis is a tick-borne (Ixodes scapularis) intraerythrocytic parasitic infection causing fever and hemolytic anemia, endemic to the Northeast US.
- Asplenic, elderly, or immunocompromised hosts develop severe disease with high parasitemia, ARDS, DIC, and renal failure.
- Diagnose with smear (rings, "Maltese cross" tetrads) and PCR; treat with atovaquone plus azithromycin (mild) or clindamycin plus quinine (severe), with exchange transfusion for high parasitemia.

CASE_ID: 468
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Mucormycosis

ALIASES:
zygomycosis
rhinocerebral mucormycosis
Rhizopus infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old woman in diabetic ketoacidosis presents with facial pain, headache, and a black eschar on her hard palate.
2. She has poorly controlled type 1 diabetes and is on no immunosuppression.
3. Exam reveals proptosis, ophthalmoplegia, decreased vision in the right eye, and a necrotic-appearing turbinate on rhinoscopy.
4. Early studies support an infectious process, and organism-directed testing is sent.
5. Tissue biopsy from the necrotic turbinate shows broad, pauciseptate, ribbon-like hyphae branching at right angles and invading vessels.
6. Culture grows Rhizopus species, and she undergoes emergent surgical debridement with liposomal amphotericin B and aggressive correction of her acidosis and hyperglycemia.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Mucormycosis is an angioinvasive fungal infection by Rhizopus, Mucor, or Rhizomucor; classic hosts are DKA patients and the iron-overloaded (deferoxamine).
- Rhinocerebral form spreads from nose/sinuses to orbit and brain; black necrotic eschar reflects tissue infarction.
- Histology shows broad, ribbon-like, non-septate hyphae with right-angle branching (vs Aspergillus: septate, acute angle); treat with emergent surgical debridement, amphotericin, and reversal of predisposing factors.

CASE_ID: 469
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Subacute Sclerosing Panencephalitis

ALIASES:
SSPE
Dawson encephalitis
late measles encephalitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 12-year-old boy presents with several months of declining school performance, personality change, and recent onset of myoclonic jerks.
2. He emigrated from a country with limited immunization coverage and had measles as an infant before his second birthday.
3. Exam reveals cognitive impairment, brisk reflexes, ataxia, and periodic massive myoclonic jerks every 5 to 10 seconds.
4. CSF shows mild lymphocytic pleocytosis with a markedly elevated CSF-to-serum measles IgG index and oligoclonal bands.
5. EEG demonstrates periodic high-voltage Radermecker complexes synchronized with the myoclonic jerks.
6. Brain MRI shows symmetric white matter signal abnormalities, and brain biopsy reveals intranuclear inclusions with positive measles virus antigen staining, confirming SSPE.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- SSPE is a fatal late complication of measles, typically 6 to 15 years after infection (highest risk if measles occurred before age 2).
- Stages: behavioral/cognitive decline, myoclonic jerks with characteristic periodic EEG complexes, decerebrate rigidity, and death.
- High CSF anti-measles IgG with oligoclonal bands is diagnostic; prevention is through measles vaccination, since no curative therapy exists.

CASE_ID: 470
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Anti-NMDA Receptor Encephalitis

ALIASES:
anti-NMDAR encephalitis
NMDA receptor antibody encephalitis
paraneoplastic encephalitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 23-year-old woman is brought to the ED with new psychiatric symptoms including paranoid delusions, agitation, and disorganized speech over two weeks.
2. She had a brief flu-like prodrome before the symptoms began and has no prior psychiatric history.
3. Exam reveals orofacial dyskinesias, mutism alternating with screaming, autonomic instability with episodic hyperthermia, and abnormal limb posturing.
4. Routine labs are unremarkable, brain MRI is normal, and EEG shows generalized slowing with an "extreme delta brush" pattern.
5. CSF reveals mild lymphocytic pleocytosis with oligoclonal bands and IgG antibodies against the NR1 subunit of the NMDA receptor.
6. Pelvic ultrasound identifies a left ovarian teratoma; she undergoes tumor resection, receives IV methylprednisolone, IVIG, and rituximab, with gradual neurologic recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Anti-NMDA receptor encephalitis is an autoimmune encephalitis often paraneoplastic in young women (associated with ovarian teratomas).
- Classic stages: viral prodrome, psychiatric symptoms, then movement disorders/orofacial dyskinesias, autonomic instability, and coma; "extreme delta brush" on EEG is characteristic.
- Diagnose with CSF anti-NMDAR antibodies; treat with tumor removal plus immunotherapy (steroids, IVIG, rituximab, cyclophosphamide).

CASE_ID: 471
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Posterior Reversible Encephalopathy Syndrome

ALIASES:
PRES
reversible posterior leukoencephalopathy
hypertensive encephalopathy syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old woman G1 at 32 weeks gestation presents with severe headache, blurry vision, and a generalized seizure.
2. She had rising blood pressures and proteinuria at recent prenatal visits and is on no medications.
3. Exam reveals BP 198/118, brisk reflexes with clonus, cortical blindness, and 3+ pretibial edema.
4. Urinalysis shows 3+ protein; LDH and uric acid are elevated; platelets are 110,000.
5. Brain MRI demonstrates symmetric T2/FLAIR hyperintensities in the bilateral parieto-occipital subcortical white matter without restricted diffusion.
6. Magnesium sulfate is given for seizure prophylaxis, IV labetalol controls her blood pressure, and emergent cesarean delivery is performed; imaging at 6 weeks shows complete radiographic resolution.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- PRES typically presents with headache, seizures, visual disturbances, and altered mental status in the setting of hypertensive emergency, eclampsia, immunosuppressants, or cytotoxic chemotherapy.
- Posterior (parieto-occipital) vasogenic edema on MRI is characteristic and reversible; restricted diffusion suggests evolving infarction.
- Treatment is rapid blood pressure control, seizure management, and removal of offending agents; in eclampsia, magnesium and timely delivery are key.

CASE_ID: 472
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Cerebral Venous Sinus Thrombosis

ALIASES:
CVST
cerebral venous thrombosis
dural sinus thrombosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman on combined oral contraceptives presents with two weeks of progressive headache, now with new left-sided weakness and a focal seizure.
2. She is a smoker and is an abnormal value postpartum; she has no history of migraines or prior strokes.
3. Exam reveals papilledema, mild left hemiparesis, and a partial sixth nerve palsy; she is afebrile.
4. Non-contrast CT shows a hyperdense "cord sign" along the right transverse sinus, and D-dimer is elevated.
5. MR venography demonstrates absent flow in the superior sagittal and right transverse sinuses, and CT brain shows a small right parietal hemorrhagic venous infarct.
6. She is anticoagulated with low-molecular-weight heparin bridging to warfarin, and thrombophilia workup reveals a heterozygous factor V Leiden mutation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- CVST presents with progressive headache, focal neurologic deficits, seizures, and signs of elevated ICP; risk factors include pregnancy/postpartum, OCPs, hypercoagulable states, dehydration, and infection.
- MR venography or CT venography is the imaging test of choice; CT may show a "cord sign" or "empty delta sign" in the superior sagittal sinus.
- Anticoagulation (heparin then warfarin/DOAC) is the mainstay even in the presence of venous hemorrhagic infarction; treat for 3 to 12 months and screen for thrombophilias.

CASE_ID: 473
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Sheehan Syndrome

ALIASES:
postpartum hypopituitarism
Sheehan's syndrome
postpartum pituitary necrosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old woman presents 8 months postpartum with persistent fatigue, inability to breastfeed since delivery, and amenorrhea.
2. Her delivery was complicated by severe postpartum hemorrhage requiring multiple units of blood, and she has progressively lost axillary and pubic hair.
3. Exam reveals dry skin, bradycardia, mild hypotension, and loss of secondary sexual hair; she has cold intolerance and constipation.
4. Labs show low free T4 with inappropriately normal TSH, morning cortisol 2 mcg/dL, low FSH and LH, prolactin 2 ng/mL, and low IGF-1.
5. ACTH stimulation test demonstrates a blunted cortisol response, confirming secondary adrenal insufficiency.
6. Pituitary MRI shows an empty sella with absent pituitary tissue; she is started on hydrocortisone first, then levothyroxine, and combined hormone replacement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Sheehan syndrome is postpartum hypopituitarism due to ischemic necrosis of the hyperplastic pituitary after severe postpartum hemorrhage.
- Earliest manifestations are often failure to lactate (low prolactin) and persistent amenorrhea; later, panhypopituitarism (adrenal, thyroid, gonadal, growth axes) develops.
- Replace cortisol BEFORE thyroid hormone to avoid precipitating adrenal crisis; MRI shows empty sella in chronic cases.

CASE_ID: 474
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Primary Hyperaldosteronism

ALIASES:
Conn syndrome
primary aldosteronism
aldosterone-producing adenoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old man presents with refractory hypertension despite three antihypertensive agents and complains of muscle cramps and frequent urination.
2. He has had several episodes of mild leg weakness; he denies licorice ingestion or diuretic use.
3. Exam reveals BP 168/98 on combination therapy, with no edema, abdominal bruits, or stigmata of Cushing syndrome.
4. Labs show K+ 2.9, Na+ 145, HCO3- 30 with metabolic alkalosis, plasma renin activity suppressed at 0.2 ng/mL/hr, and plasma aldosterone 32 ng/dL.
5. Aldosterone-to-renin ratio is greater than 30, and a confirmatory saline infusion test fails to suppress aldosterone below 10 ng/dL.
6. CT adrenals shows a 1.4-cm left adrenal adenoma, adrenal venous sampling confirms lateralization to the left, and laparoscopic left adrenalectomy normalizes his blood pressure and potassium.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Primary hyperaldosteronism causes hypertension, hypokalemia (variably), and metabolic alkalosis; it is an under-recognized cause of resistant hypertension.
- Aldosterone-to-renin ratio (ARR) is the screening test; confirm with saline infusion or oral salt-loading test demonstrating non-suppressible aldosterone.
- CT plus adrenal venous sampling distinguishes unilateral adenoma (Conn's, treat surgically) from bilateral adrenal hyperplasia (treat medically with spironolactone or eplerenone).

CASE_ID: 475
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Adult-Onset Still Disease

ALIASES:
AOSD
adult Still disease
systemic juvenile idiopathic arthritis adult form

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with five weeks of quotidian high fevers, sore throat, and a faint salmon-colored rash on her trunk that comes and goes with the fevers.
2. She has had progressively worsening polyarthralgia, and an extensive infectious and malignancy workup has been negative.
3. Exam reveals temperature 39.6 C, evanescent macular rash on the trunk during fever spikes, generalized lymphadenopathy, and bilateral wrist effusions.
4. Labs show WBC 22,000 with neutrophilic predominance, normocytic anemia, AST 240, ALT 290, ferritin 22,000, and a low glycosylated ferritin of 12%.
5. ANA, RF, and anti-CCP are all negative; blood cultures, viral serologies, and tuberculosis testing are negative; bone marrow biopsy excludes hematologic malignancy.
6. Yamaguchi criteria are met (major: fever, arthralgia, rash, leukocytosis; minor: lymphadenopathy, hepatic dysfunction), and high-dose corticosteroids with subsequent anakinra (IL-1 inhibitor) lead to remission.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Adult-onset Still disease is a systemic autoinflammatory disorder with quotidian (daily) fevers, evanescent salmon-pink rash, arthritis, sore throat, and prominent leukocytosis.
- Markedly elevated ferritin (often more than 5 times normal) with a low glycosylated ferritin fraction (under 20%) is a useful diagnostic clue.
- Diagnosis by Yamaguchi criteria after excluding infection and malignancy; treat with NSAIDs, corticosteroids, and IL-1 (anakinra) or IL-6 (tocilizumab) inhibitors.
